### AC-1 — Policy and Procedures (Control)

This requirement is about making sure everyone in the organisation understands the rules for who is allowed to access systems and information, and how those rules are applied in practice. Without clear, documented guidance, the wrong people could gain access, access might not be approved or removed when it should be, and sensitive information could be misused or exposed. This could lead to financial loss, disruption, and legal or regulatory breaches.

The organisation must create and publish an organisation-wide access policy and supporting procedures. These must be written in plain language for all relevant roles, including all employees, contractors, and privileged users. They must cover the purpose of the policy, what it includes and excludes, roles and responsibilities, leadership commitment, how different parts of the organisation work together, and how the organisation will ensure compliance. They must also align with applicable laws and the organisation’s own rules.

The Chief Information Security Officer must be responsible for producing, maintaining, and sharing these documents. The policy and procedures must be reviewed at least once every year and updated after any significant change, security incident or breach, audit finding, major organisational change, or change to relevant laws and regulations.

##### CISO-owned access control policy & procedures with review triggers

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Create and maintain an organisation-level **Access Control Policy** and supporting **Access Control Procedures** that explain how access controls are put into practice across the organisation (including information technology (IT) and, where applicable, operational technology/industrial control systems (OT/ICS) interfaces).

###### WHY (control requirement)
Requirement AC-1 says the organisation must have **access control policy and procedures** that cover the access control requirements implemented in systems and organisations. These must be based on the organisation’s **risk management strategy** and developed together with the **security and privacy programme**. The policy/procedures must be **owned by an authorised official** (the **Chief Information Security Officer (CISO)**), shared with the relevant personnel/roles, and **reviewed and updated at least every year** and after specific events (for example: incidents or breaches, audit findings, major organisational changes, and changes to relevant laws/regulations). The guidance also states that simply repeating the access control requirements is not enough—procedures must explain **how** the policy and controls are implemented.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Write the Access Control Policy** as an organisation-level document (or a small set of linked documents) including the required sections: purpose, scope, what is included/excluded, roles and responsibilities, management commitment, coordination between the security and privacy programmes, and compliance assurance.
2. **Write Access Control Procedures** as practical “how-to” instructions aligned to the access control areas (for example: managing user identities over time, requesting and approving access, handling privileged access, access reviews, handling exceptions, emergency access, contractor access, and removing access when no longer needed). Make sure the procedures describe step-by-step actions for the people responsible for carrying them out (system owners, network administrators, OT/ICS engineers, and identity and access management (IAM) administrators).
3. **Set governance and ownership**: appoint the **CISO** as the authorised owner responsible for managing the access control policy and procedures.
4. **Set who receives what**:
   - Share the **policy** with **all personnel, contractors, and privileged users**.
   - Share the **procedures** with **system owners, network administrators, OT/ICS engineers, and IAM administrators**.
5. **Use a controlled document process**: include version control, an approval workflow, and a clear record of changes. Add specific review dates and triggers for updating the documents when events occur.
6. **Put review and update triggers into practice**:
   - Review and update the **policy** **every year** and after any **material change**; also after **security incidents or breaches, audit findings, major organisational changes, or changes to relevant laws/regulations**.
   - Review and update the **procedures** **every year** and after any **material change**; also after **security incidents or breaches, audit findings, major system changes, or changes to relevant laws/regulations**.

###### WHO (roles responsible)
- **CISO**: owner of the policy/procedures and final approver.
- **Security programme**: leads drafting and ensures alignment to the access control requirements.
- **Privacy programme**: checks the policy/procedures for privacy impact.
- **IAM administrators / system owners / network administrators / OT/ICS engineers**: provide input to the procedures and confirm the implementation steps are correct.
- **Legal/Compliance** (as needed): checks alignment with relevant laws/regulations.

###### Acceptance criteria
- An organisation-level **Access Control Policy** exists in a controlled repository, has a named **CISO** owner, includes the required governance content (purpose/scope/roles/responsibilities/coordination/compliance), and is shared with **all personnel, contractors, and privileged users**.
- An organisation-level set of **Access Control Procedures** exists with clear implementation steps (not just restating the access control requirements), is shared with **system owners, network administrators, OT/ICS engineers, and IAM administrators**, and is version-controlled.
- Evidence of the document process shows **annual review timing** and **event-based update triggers** that match the defined events for both the policy and the procedures.
- Evidence of **collaboration between the security and privacy programmes** is available (for example, documented review and sign-off within the approval workflow).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Draft an organisation-wide access control policy, owned by the Chief Information Security Officer (CISO), including its purpose, scope, roles, responsibilities, coordination arrangements, and how compliance will be checked.
- [ ] Draft access control procedures that set out step-by-step instructions for managing user identities, handling access requests and approvals, managing privileged access, carrying out access reviews, managing exceptions, and providing emergency access.
- [ ] Get written review and approval (sign-off) from both the security programme and the privacy programme for the policy and procedures.
- [ ] Publish and share the policy with all staff, contractors, and privileged users through approved channels, and keep proof that it was distributed.
- [ ] Share and distribute the procedures with system owners, network administrators, operational technology/industrial control system (OT/ICS) engineers, and identity and access management (IAM) administrators through approved channels, with proof that they were sent.
- [ ] Set up a controlled document process with version numbers, an approval step before changes take effect, and a clearly stated review date every year.
- [ ] Record when event-driven updates are triggered, and make sure the relevant policies and procedures are updated after incidents or breaches, audit findings, major organisational or system changes, or changes to the laws and regulations that apply.


---

### AC-2 — Account Management (Control)

This expectation is about making sure that every person’s (and approved service’s) access to your system is set up correctly, clearly recorded, and removed quickly when it is no longer needed. If this is not done, the wrong people may keep access after leaving or changing roles, accounts could be created without proper checks, and permissions may be broader than necessary—raising the risk of unauthorised access and misuse of data.

The organisation must define and record which types of accounts are allowed, and must specifically forbid shared, group, emergency, anonymous, temporary, and guest accounts. It must also appoint an account manager, and record who is authorised, which groups or roles they belong to, and the exact permissions for each account. This includes which parts of the system or data they can access, whether extra identity checks are required, and any time limits.

New accounts must be approved by the system owner and the senior information security officer. Accounts must be created and changed only through a documented process that includes a request record, approval, and an audit trail. Any account changes triggered by events must be disabled within 24 hours.

Account use must be monitored and reviewed at least quarterly. The account manager and system owner must be notified within 24 hours when accounts are no longer required, when staff are terminated or transferred, or when someone’s “need to know” changes. If shared or group accounts are used, login details must be changed when members leave, and account actions must align with the organisation’s termination and transfer processes.

##### IAM-enforced account lifecycle with prohibited account types

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up an identity and access management (IAM) process for the application’s accounts so that only authorised **individual** and **service** accounts can be created and used. Block any prohibited account types. Define the account details for each account, including what the account can access, what permissions it has, whether multi-factor authentication (MFA) is required, and any time-based limits (such as allowed times and days). Ensure every action to create, change, disable, or remove an account goes through an approval-based, auditable process, with accounts disabled promptly and the right people notified.

###### WHY (control requirement)
Control AC-2 requires the organisation to manage system accounts by:
- defining **authorised system users** and their **access privileges** (including account-specific details),
- setting out which **account types** are allowed, and (as guidance) blocking higher-risk types such as **shared, group, emergency, anonymous, temporary, and guest**,
- ensuring account creation, enabling, changes, and removal follow documented procedures, with the right approvals and oversight for privileged or administrative access,
- disabling accounts when they are no longer needed and notifying account managers within required timeframes,
- reviewing accounts at a defined frequency.

This solution meets the organisation’s defined requirements:
- **ac-02_odp.01** (only individual and service accounts; prohibit shared/group/emergency/anonymous/temporary/guest),
- **ac-02_odp.02** (account-specific privileges, access scope, MFA, and time restrictions),
- **ac-02_odp.03** (approvers: system owner and senior agency information security officer),
- **ac-02_odp.04–ac-02_odp.08** (documented IAM process; disable within 24 hours; notify within 24 hours for accounts no longer needed, termination/transfer, and changes to who needs access),
- **ac-02_odp.09** (need-to-know and least privilege),
- **ac-02_odp.10** (quarterly review).

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Allow only approved account types in IAM**: Configure the IAM setup so that account provisioning is limited to **individual** and **service** accounts only. Explicitly block provisioning for **shared, group, emergency, anonymous, temporary, and guest** account types (ac-02_odp.01).
2. **Define what each application role allows**: Create an access/role catalogue for each application role that specifies:
   - **access scope** (systems/data),
   - **permission set** (least privilege, need-to-know),
   - whether **MFA** is required,
   - any **time-of-day/day-of-week** restrictions where needed (ac-02_odp.02, ac-02_odp.09).
3. **Use an approval-based process for account changes**: Link the IAM process to the organisation’s ticketing/change system so that requests to create, enable, change, disable, or remove accounts require approval from the **system owner** and the **senior agency information security officer** (ac-02_odp.03, ac-02_odp.04). Ensure the process records the request details, approvals, and the resulting IAM/application access.
4. **Automate account disablement and enforce 24-hour disablement**: Connect human resources (HR) events (such as termination or transfer) and any “need-to-know changed” signals to IAM account removal so affected accounts are **disabled within 24 hours** of the trigger (ac-02_odp.04). Ensure the application uses the IAM status (for example through single sign-on (SSO) and system for cross-domain identity management (SCIM)) so disablement takes effect across the application.
5. **Send notifications within 24 hours**: Configure notifications so the **account manager** (and, where required, the system owner) is informed within **24 hours** when accounts are no longer needed, when users are terminated/transferred, and when access needs change (ac-02_odp.05–ac-02_odp.08).
6. **Run quarterly account reviews with evidence**: Produce quarterly reports of all active application accounts and their access rights, and require confirmation/review against the approved role catalogue and least-privilege rules (ac-02_odp.10). Keep the review evidence for audit.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **IAM/Identity Engineer**: Sets up IAM configuration, provisioning rules, the access/entitlement model, and automation.
- **Application Owner/System Owner**: Owns the application role catalogue and approves account requests.
- **Senior Agency Information Security Officer (or designated equivalent)**: Provides required approvals for account creation and privileged access.
- **Account Manager**: Receives notifications and carries out or coordinates quarterly reviews.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The IAM provisioning policy prevents creating/enrolling prohibited account types (**shared/group/emergency/anonymous/temporary/guest**) for this application (evidence: IAM policy configuration and test results).
- For every active application account, the recorded access details include: permission set, access scope (systems/data), MFA requirement, and any time-of-day/day-of-week restrictions where applicable (evidence: IAM account export/report).
- Account create/enable/modify/disable/remove actions are carried out only through the approved process, with recorded approvals from the **system owner** and **senior agency information security officer** (evidence: ticket/workflow logs).
- When HR termination/transfer or “need-to-know changed” triggers occur, the account is disabled within **24 hours** and notifications are sent within **24 hours** (evidence: trigger-to-disablement timestamps and notification logs).
- Quarterly account review reports and confirmations exist for the application (evidence: review package and sign-off records).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Set up identity and access management (IAM) provisioning rules so that only individual user accounts and service accounts are allowed for this application. Block all shared, group, emergency, anonymous, temporary, and guest account types.
- [ ] Define the application role catalogue, listing what each role is allowed to access. For each role, specify: the access scope (which systems and data), the minimum necessary permissions (least privilege), whether multi-factor authentication (MFA) is required, and any limits on when access is allowed (time of day and day of week).
- [ ] Link identity and access management (IAM) account changes to the ticketing system, so that creating, enabling, changing, disabling, or removing an account requires approval from the system owner and the senior agency information security officer.
- [ ] Set up automated triggers for changes related to hiring, leaving employment, and transfers, and for “need to know” updates, so that any affected user accounts are disabled within 24 hours. Also ensure the application follows the current identity access settings, including single sign-on (SSO) and system for cross-domain identity management (SCIM).
- [ ] Set up automated notifications to the account manager (and the system owner where required) within 24 hours for changes that are no longer needed, termination or transfer actions, and any changes that affect who needs to know or how the system is used.
- [ ] Schedule quarterly reviews of each account and keep proof (attestations) showing that active accounts match the approved role catalogue.


---

#### AC-2.1 — Automated System Account Management (Enhancement)

This requirement ensures that system accounts are created, updated, paused, and removed in a controlled, automated way, and that the right people are informed immediately. Without this, staff could accidentally leave accounts active after someone changes role or leaves, or unauthorised access could go undetected for too long, and unusual account use could be missed.

The organisation is expected to use built-in system features to manage the full account life cycle (create, enable, change, disable, remove), automatically monitor how these accounts are used, and automatically send email notifications to the account managers whenever an account is created, enabled, changed, disabled, or removed, and when a user is terminated or transferred. It should also automatically flag unexpected account activity and report it to the responsible account managers, keep records of what happened and when, and document the procedures, responsibilities, system design, and settings that make these automated actions and notifications work.

##### Event-driven system account lifecycle with automated alerts

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up automated processes to manage **system accounts** across the application environment. This includes **creating, enabling, changing, disabling, and removing** accounts. When these events happen, the system must automatically **notify the relevant account managers**. Also add automated monitoring to **detect and report unusual (atypical) use** of system accounts.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) **AC-2(1)** enhancement requires the organisation to support system account management using **automated processes**. This includes:
- automating the full account lifecycle (create/enable/modify/disable/remove)
- **notifying account managers** about lifecycle events, and about **user termination/transfer** events where they affect system account ownership or use
- **monitoring** system account use
- **reporting unusual (atypical) use**

The organisation-defined parameter **ac-02.01_odp** states that automated processes include **internal system functions** and **automated email notifications** to account managers.

###### HOW (specific approach)
###### 1) Define system-account ownership and notification targets
- Keep a record that links each system account to its **account manager(s)** (for example, a directory field, a configuration management database (CMDB) owner field, or an application configuration register).
- Make sure this mapping is the official source of truth and is kept under version control.

###### 2) Implement automated lifecycle workflows (internal system functions)
- Use the application’s built-in identity and access features (for example, identity provider (IdP) or directory provisioning, service-account management interfaces, or internal admin automation) to carry out lifecycle actions:
  - **Create**: set up the account with the required roles/permissions and the correct environment scope.
  - **Enable/Disable**: switch the account on or off based on workflow triggers (for example, deployment readiness, an approved change, or scheduled time windows).
  - **Modify**: update account details/roles/entitlements using the same workflow.
  - **Remove**: revoke credentials/entitlements and either delete or quarantine the account according to retention rules.
- Ensure lifecycle actions are carried out **only** through the automated workflow, not through one-off manual steps.

###### 3) Send automated email notifications for each lifecycle event
- Connect the workflow to lifecycle events (or have the internal system functions raise events) and send **standard (templated) email notifications** to the mapped account manager(s) for:
  - accounts **created, enabled, modified, disabled, removed**
  - **user termination or transfer** events that affect system account ownership or use (where this applies in your environment)
- Each email must include: account identifier, event type, date/time, the workflow/change reference that triggered it, and a link/reference to the audit record.

###### 4) Monitor system account usage and report unusual activity
- Send system account authentication and activity logs into the enterprise monitoring system, including security information and event management (SIEM).
- Create automated detection rules for unusual use (examples based on available information):
  - logins from unusual sources or at unusual times
  - access outside approved maintenance windows
  - changes to privileges/roles outside expected workflows
- Send alerts to the security operations centre (SOC)/security operations (SecOps) and also to the relevant account manager(s) by email (or through an existing ticketing integration). Alerts must be linked back to the account lifecycle audit record.

###### 5) Ensure auditability and keep evidence
- For every lifecycle event, record an auditable entry that links together:
  - the lifecycle action (which workflow, who/what triggered it, and when)
  - the account identifier
  - the notification details (message ID/correlation ID)
  - the monitoring/alert link (if unusual use was detected)
- Keep logs and audit records according to the organisation’s logging retention standards.

###### WHO
- **Application Owner / Identity and Access Management (IAM) Engineer**: defines the system-account ownership mapping and designs the lifecycle workflows.
- **Security Operations (SOC/SecOps)**: checks that monitoring detections and alert routing are correct.
- **Platform/Infrastructure Engineer**: builds the integrations with the identity provider/directory and the SIEM/log data pipelines.
- **Change Manager / Approver**: confirms that workflow triggers align with approved change governance.

###### Acceptance criteria (audit-ready)
1. For every system account lifecycle event (create/enable/modify/disable/remove), the automation performs the action using internal system functions and produces an auditable record.
2. For every lifecycle event, an automated email is sent to the mapped account manager(s) including at least: account identifier, event type, timestamp, and a reference that links to the audit record.
3. Where a user termination/transfer trigger applies to system account ownership or use, the workflow disables/removes or updates the affected system account(s) and sends the corresponding notification.
4. System account usage data is monitored in the SIEM (or equivalent). Detections of unusual use must generate alerts that are linked to the account and sent to the account manager(s).
5. The evidence package includes: workflow configuration/export, notification template/configuration, sample audit records for each lifecycle event, sample notification emails, and sample evidence for unusual-usage alerts.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and keep up to date an official record that maps each system account to its account manager.
- [ ] Set up automated processes for system accounts to handle their full lifecycle (create, enable, change, disable, and remove) using internal system functions.
- [ ] Set up event-triggered actions so that every stage of the lifecycle produces a correlation identifier, to support auditability.
- [ ] Set up automated email notifications, using templates, to account managers for every stage of the customer lifecycle and for any termination or transfer events.
- [ ] Send system account authentication and activity logs into the security information and event management (SIEM) system, and set up alerts to detect unusual usage patterns.
- [ ] Set up alert routing so unusual-usage notifications are sent to the relevant account manager(s), linked to the corresponding lifecycle audit records.
- [ ] Test the full process from start to finish using test cases that cover every stage of the lifecycle, plus at least one unusual way the system might be used.


---

#### AC-2.2 — Automated Temporary and Emergency Account Management (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure short-term and emergency user accounts are not left active longer than planned. If temporary or emergency access remains enabled after the agreed end time, it increases the risk that someone could continue using it by mistake or misuse it, even after the original need has ended.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must automatically disable any temporary and emergency accounts after 24 hours. This should be done using built-in automated account-management features, rather than relying on an administrator to turn them off manually.

The organisation should also have clear account-management rules and procedures, with documented system settings and design. It must be able to show system-generated records that list which temporary and emergency accounts were disabled and the time they were disabled. These records should be supported by audit records and relevant internal documentation.

People responsible for account management and system administration must ensure the automated process is in place and working as intended.

##### Automate 24-hour disablement for temporary/emergency accounts

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Automatically disable (or revoke sign-in for) temporary and emergency accounts exactly after the organisation-defined time period (default: **24 hours**).

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) **AC-2(2)** enhancement requires that temporary and emergency accounts are **automatically** removed or disabled after **{{ param, ac-02.02_odp.02 }}** (the time period), rather than being left active until an administrator chooses to act. This provides consistent enforcement and reduces the risk of accounts staying active beyond their intended purpose.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
Set up an identity lifecycle automation process that records an **account enablement time** when the account is created or activated, and then enforces an **expiry time = enablement time + 24 hours**.

1. **Standardise account types**: Define “temporary” and “emergency” access as specific identity items (for example, a privileged role assignment that expires, a group membership that expires, or a time-limited account) so the system can reliably identify what should expire.
2. **Set automated lifecycle rules** in the enterprise identity provider (IdP) / identity governance and administration (IGA) platform:
   - When the account or access is created/activated, set the expiry to **24 hours**.
   - When the expiry time is reached, automatically **disable the account** (or block sign-in) without any human action.
3. **Use one trusted source for expiry time**:
   - The process that grants the access must write the expiry time into the IdP/IGA record.
   - Avoid manual changes; any updates must go through the same process.
4. **Create evidence that can be audited**:
   - Turn on logging for lifecycle events (account/access created, expiry set, account disabled/sign-in revoked).
   - Provide a report/query showing which temporary/emergency accounts were disabled and the time they were disabled.
5. **Test and confirm timing accuracy**:
   - Run a controlled test in a non-production environment to confirm the account is disabled within an acceptable window (for example, within a few minutes of the 24-hour point) and that no manual step is needed.
6. **Put monitoring in place**:
   - Add an automated check (scheduled job) that highlights any temporary/emergency accounts whose expiry is more than 24 hours ago and confirms they are disabled.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) / Identity Governance and Administration (IGA) Engineer**: Implements IdP/IGA lifecycle rules, the process that stamps expiry details, and the evidence/reporting.
- **Security Operations / Security Operations Centre (SOC) Analyst**: Reviews logs and monitors for accounts that have passed their expiry time.
- **System Owner / Application Owner**: Confirms that the authentication routes covered by temporary/emergency access are included.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Temporary and emergency accounts are created/activated with an expiry time set to **24 hours** from enablement.
- Accounts are **automatically disabled or sign-in is revoked** at expiry without administrator action.
- Audit evidence exists showing: (a) the account/access was created with an enablement time, (b) the expiry time, and (c) the disablement/revocation event with its time.
- A non-production test confirms correct behaviour at the 24-hour point.
- Monitoring/reporting can produce a list of disabled temporary/emergency accounts and the corresponding disablement times.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define temporary and emergency access as identity records (for example, a role, group, or account that lasts for a set time) that all use a consistent identifier and automatically expire.
- [ ] Set up the enterprise identity provider (IdP) and identity governance and administration (IGA) workflow to record the enablement time and set the access expiry time to 24 hours after enablement.
- [ ] Set up automatic disablement (or block sign-in) when temporary or emergency accounts or objects reach their expiry date.
- [ ] Turn on and keep audit logs for key lifecycle events (creation, setting an expiry date, and disabling or revoking) and check that the logs are complete.
- [ ] Create an audit report that lists all temporary or emergency accounts that have been disabled, including the date and time when each account was disabled.
- [ ] Run a timing test in a non-production environment to confirm that the disablement happens automatically at the 24-hour mark.
- [ ] Set up a scheduled check to identify any temporary or emergency accounts that have passed their expiry date, and confirm that they are disabled.


---

#### AC-2.3 — Disable Accounts (Enhancement)

This requirement is about quickly switching off old or suspicious access accounts, so they can’t be used after they should no longer be active. If expired accounts, accounts that are no longer linked to a real person, accounts found to have broken organisational rules, or accounts that have been unused for 90 days are left running, they can become an easy route for unauthorised access or misuse—even if nobody is actively using them.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must identify these accounts and disable them within 24 hours of the trigger being confirmed. The trigger could be the account expiry date, the point it becomes unlinked from a user or person, the point a policy breach is identified, or the point 90 days of inactivity is reached.

The organisation should also keep clear records showing when each account was disabled and the reason why, so it can demonstrate that the 24-hour timing was consistently followed.

##### Automate account disablement within 24h using IdP/IGA triggers

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Set up an automated process that turns off (disables) user accounts within the organisation-defined **time period** (ac-02.03_odp.01, default **24 hours**) when accounts are **expired, inactive for 90 days, orphaned/unlinked, or flagged as breaking policy/behaving unusually**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The AC-2(3) enhancement requires that accounts meeting these conditions are **disabled within the defined time period** to reduce the organisation’s exposure to attack and to support “least privilege/least functionality”. The guidance specifically links disabling expired, inactive, and unusual accounts to reducing risk.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the official “triggers” and their dates/times**:  
   Use the organisation’s main identity records for expiry/termination, use identity governance and administration (IGA) checks to detect unlinked/orphaned accounts, use policy and exception records for policy-violating/anomalous accounts, and use identity provider (IdP) sign-in/activity records to detect inactivity.
2. **Set up IdP/IGA account rules** to disable accounts when:
   - the **expiry date** is reached,
   - the account becomes **unlinked/orphaned** (for example, it is no longer connected to an active person/user in human resources (HR)/IGA),
   - a **policy exception/violation** flag is raised for the account,
   - the account reaches **90 days of inactivity** (ac-02.03_odp.02).
3. **Apply the 24-hour service level agreement (SLA) in the workflow**:  
   Make the disable action part of the automated lifecycle job so the workflow records both the **trigger time** and the **disablement time**, and raises an alert/fails if the SLA is at risk.
4. **Remove active access as part of disablement**:  
   Ensure the disable action also ends or invalidates any active sessions and access tokens (for example, revoke refresh tokens and/or force re-authentication) so the account cannot still be used after disablement.
5. **Create audit-ready proof**:  
   Store the workflow run identifier and the link between **trigger reason → trigger timestamp → disablement timestamp** in IdP/IGA logs (and send to security information and event management (SIEM) for retention).
6. **Run exception handling operationally**:  
   If disablement is delayed (for example, due to a dependency outage), require an automated incident/ticket record that states the reason and shows disablement still happens within the defined time period.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Identity and access management (IAM)/IGA Engineer**: implement and fine-tune IdP/IGA automation and inactivity/orphan detection rules.
- **Application Security/Platform Owner**: confirm the application uses the IdP for authentication and that session/token revocation works as intended.
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC)/Security Operations**: monitor SLA alerts and check the audit evidence in SIEM.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each trigger type (expired, orphaned/unlinked, policy-violating/anomalous, and 90-days inactivity), test results show the account is **disabled within 24 hours** of the trigger timestamp.
- After disablement, tests confirm the account cannot authenticate and that any active sessions/tokens are revoked/invalidated.
- Audit evidence is available for each test case showing **trigger timestamp, disablement timestamp, and disablement reason**.
- SLA monitoring/alerting is in place and produces an auditable record when disablement is carried out or delayed.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Identify and record the official data sources and the specific date/time fields used to determine: when something expires, whether it is orphaned or not linked, whether it has any policy violations, and whether it has been inactive for 90 days.
- [ ] Set up identity provider (IdP) and identity governance and administration (IGA) lifecycle automation to automatically disable user accounts as soon as each trigger condition is met.
- [ ] Set up workflow controls to ensure account disablement happens within the **ac-02.03_odp.01** time limit (24 hours), and raise alerts when there is a risk of missing the service level agreement (SLA).
- [ ] When a user is enabled or disabled, automatically end any active sessions for the application and make any existing access tokens unusable.
- [ ] Integrate identity provider (IdP) and identity governance and administration (IGA) disablement logs with security information and event management (SIEM) to keep auditable records showing the time the action was triggered, the reason for it, and the time it was disabled.
- [ ] For each type of trigger, run and record test cases to prove that disablement happens within 24 hours and that access is blocked after disablement.


---

#### AC-2.4 — Automated Audit Actions (Enhancement)

This requirement ensures that every time a person’s account is created, updated, enabled, disabled, or deleted, the system automatically logs what changed, when it changed, and who (or what system) made the change. This is important because account changes are a common way for unauthorised access to be gained or for legitimate access to be misused. Without reliable records, it is difficult to spot errors, investigate suspicious activity, or confirm what was done and by whom.

To meet this expectation, your organisation must ensure the system automatically creates audit records for all account-management actions in a consistent format, keeps those records for the period your organisation has decided, and then regularly reviews, analyses, and reports on them in line with your internal audit review and reporting procedures.

##### Automate audit events for account lifecycle changes to SIEM

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Automatically create audit records for **account creation, modification, enabling, disabling, and removal** events. Each record must capture **who or what carried out the action**, **when it happened**, and **which account was affected**. Then ensure these records are **sent automatically** to the organisation’s central audit log system in a **consistent format**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-2(4)** (Enhancement) requires the system to **automatically record** account lifecycle actions (creation, modification, enabling, disabling, removal). It also states that account management audit records must follow **AU-02**, and that they must be **reviewed, analysed, and reported** in line with **AU-06**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Standardise the account lifecycle “source of truth”**: make sure the application’s user provisioning and deprovisioning processes (for example, through an enterprise identity provider and System for Cross-domain Identity Management (SCIM), an identity and access management (IAM) workflow, or admin application programming interfaces (APIs)) are the only approved ways to change account lifecycle status.
2. **Turn on and use audit event feeds from the identity/access layer**: configure the identity provider (IdP) and IAM connections (or use application admin APIs if the application is the system of record) so that each lifecycle action produces an event for:
   - account **created**
   - account **modified** (changes to attributes, roles, or groups)
   - account **enabled**
   - account **disabled**
   - account **removed/deleted**
3. **Convert events into the organisation’s AU-02 audit record format**: add a log conversion and mapping step in the security information and event management (SIEM) intake process so each event includes, at minimum:
   - **event time**
   - **action type** (created/modified/enabled/disabled/removed)
   - **target account identifier** (username/user ID)
   - **actor identity** (admin user/service account/process) and **actor type**
   - **source system** (IdP/IAM/application) and **correlation identifiers** where available
4. **Ensure automatic, tamper-evident delivery**: send events to the SIEM using the organisation-approved logging agent or connector with **append-only, tamper-evident** storage characteristics. Also ensure **time synchronisation** (Network Time Protocol (NTP)) across identity and logging components.
5. **Check coverage with automated tests**: run a repeatable test suite that carries out each lifecycle action in a controlled environment and confirms that the correct audit event is generated, correctly mapped, and received by the SIEM without manual steps.
6. **Make events available for AU-06 review/analysis**: set up SIEM searches and detections, and ensure the events are included in the same review process used for account-management audit records, so AU-06 review, analysis, and reporting can be done consistently.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Owner / IAM Engineer**: implement and maintain the provisioning and audit integration, and the SIEM mapping.
- **Security Operations (SOC) / SIEM Engineer**: implement intake, conversion, and ensure AU-06 review workflows and evidence outputs.
- **Change Manager**: ensure changes to audit mappings and connectors follow the organisation’s change management process.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each lifecycle action (**create, modify, enable, disable, remove**) carried out through the approved provisioning/admin routes, the system produces an **audit record automatically**.
- Each audit record is **converted to the organisation’s AU-02-defined format** and includes **actor identity**, **target account**, **action type**, and **time**.
- Audit events are **automatically sent into the central SIEM** (no manual log entry) and can be retrieved for evidence.
- Automated tests show **100% event coverage** for the five action types in at least one representative environment (for example, a production-like test environment).
- Events are available for **AU-06** review and analysis (for example, SIEM queries/dashboards or detection rules return the expected account lifecycle events).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Identify and enforce the supported account lifecycle routes for the application, using identity provider (IdP) and identity and access management (IAM) provisioning and administration programming interfaces (APIs).
- [ ] Set up identity provider (IdP), identity and access management (IAM), and application audit logs to record when an account is created, changed, enabled, disabled, or removed.
- [ ] Set up security information and event management (SIEM) data processing so that every event is matched to the organisation’s AU-02 audit record fields: actor, target, action, timestamp, and source.
- [ ] Set up automatic, tamper-evident delivery of logs to the central security information and event management (SIEM) system, and confirm that all components have synchronised time using the Network Time Protocol (NTP).
- [ ] Create security information and event management (SIEM) searches and detections, and make sure account lifecycle events are included in the AU-06 review workflow.
- [ ] Run automated end-to-end tests that carry out every step in the process, and confirm that the related audit records are received and can be retrieved.


---

#### AC-2.5 — Inactivity Logout (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure people do not leave their accounts logged in when they step away.

If a user’s session remains open after 15 minutes with no activity, another person could potentially use that unattended access to view or change information. This could lead to financial loss, exposure of data, or disruption to services.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly define and communicate a simple rule to users: after 15 minutes of inactivity, users must log out (sign out) if they expect they will be inactive for longer than 15 minutes. Users should do this using the normal log-out option, rather than relying on automatic timeouts.

The organisation must also:
- include this rule in its account and user guidance
- make sure the system is set up to support user log-out
- document the approach
- keep records showing that users logged out when they followed the 15-minute expectation

##### Expectation-based manual logout guidance and reliable sign-out flow

- Category: Software
- Priority: High

###### WHAT
Set a user-facing requirement and ensure reliable sign-out behaviour so that, **when users believe they will be inactive for longer than the defined period**, they **log out** using the application/identity provider sign-out option.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-2(5)** (enhancement) requires: **“Require that users log out when {{ insert: param, ac-02.05_odp }}.”** The organisation-defined parameter **ac-02.05_odp** states: **“After 15 minutes of inactivity, users must log out when they expect to be inactive longer than this period.”**

The guidance clarifies this is **based on user behaviour or policy**, and that **users must take action to log out** when they expect to be inactive longer than the defined period. **Automatic inactivity-based logout is covered by AC-11**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the expectation-based rule** in the organisation’s access control / acceptable use policy and link it from the application’s security help.
   - The wording must match the parameter: **after 15 minutes of inactivity, users must log out when they expect to be inactive longer than 15 minutes**.
2. **Ensure the application’s “Log out/Sign out” reliably ends the user session** by integrating logout with the enterprise identity provider (IdP) (for example, OpenID Connect (OIDC)/Security Assertion Markup Language (SAML) sign-out) so that selecting logout ends access and clears relevant tokens/session information.
3. **Share expectation-based user guidance in at least two places**:
   - onboarding materials (for example, security induction / access request pack)
   - in-product help/footer banner, or a short “Step away safely” message near the logout control
4. **Make sign-out auditable** by ensuring logout/sign-out events from the IdP and/or application are sent to the security information and event management (SIEM) system with enough details for evidence (user, application, time, event type).
5. **Put evidence handling and exceptions into practice**:
   - create a lightweight security operations centre (SOC)/helpdesk process to record and investigate cases where users report being left logged in, or where audit evidence suggests the rule was not followed.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Information Security Policy Owner**: approves and maintains the expectation-based logout wording and how it is communicated.
- **Application Owner / Engineering Lead**: implements and checks reliable logout integration and session-ending behaviour.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Team**: sets up IdP logout/sign-out behaviour and ensures audit events are available.
- **SOC/SIEM Analyst**: ensures logout events are collected, monitored, and used for evidence and exception handling.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The organisation can show a published rule stating: **after 15 minutes of inactivity, users must log out when they expect to be inactive longer than 15 minutes** (AC-2(5) parameter **ac-02.05_odp**).
- The application provides a **clear “Log out/Sign out”** option that, when used, results in **ended access** (no valid authenticated session remains for the user in the application/IdP context).
- User guidance containing the **expectation-based** instruction is included in onboarding and in-product help (or equivalent user-facing guidance).
- Logout/sign-out events are recorded in audit logs/the SIEM with enough information to show the behaviour for each user and time.
- There is a documented process for recording and handling suspected non-compliance (for example, user reports and audit review outcomes).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Update the access control and acceptable use policy to clearly state the exact time-based expectation for ac-02.05_odp (15 minutes), and link to it from the application security help.
- [ ] Implement identity provider (IdP) integrated sign-out (OpenID Connect (OIDC) and Security Assertion Markup Language (SAML) sign-out) so that the application’s “Log out/Sign out” button reliably ends user sessions and clears stored tokens.
- [ ] Add in-app user guidance next to the logout button (and in onboarding materials) telling users to log out when they expect inactivity to last longer than 15 minutes.
- [ ] Set up audit logging so that identity provider (IdP) and/or application sign-out events are sent to the security information and event management (SIEM) system, including the user, application, time of the event, and the type of event.
- [ ] Set up a security operations centre (SOC) and helpdesk process to log suspected cases where users are left signed in, and to keep records from audit logs so they can be reviewed later.


---

#### AC-2.6 — Dynamic Privilege Management (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure people’s access rights change immediately when their role or circumstances change, rather than staying the same until they log out and back in. Without this, someone who is reassigned, working outside permitted hours, or who should no longer have access could keep using privileges they no longer deserve. In urgent situations, the organisation could also be slow to reduce access when it matters most.

The organisation is expected to continuously check access rules using up-to-date information about the person and the situation. During use, it must re-check the rules and immediately block or adjust access whenever the rules say it should change. This includes when communications encryption keys are updated, so the new key state is reflected right away.

The organisation must document how this works, maintain clear procedures for updating the information that drives access, configure the systems to support these changes, and keep audit records showing when access was changed and why—including the impact of key updates.

##### Implement ABAC with real-time privilege revocation and key-epoch checks

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Implement dynamic privilege management capabilities that support **runtime, attribute-based access control (ABAC)** with **immediate privilege revocation** (no reliance on the user logging out or restarting), **continuous re-checking** of access decisions while the user is using the system, and **dynamic rule checks** based on time, job role/assignment, and emergency/duress conditions. Ensure that **changes to communications encryption keys** are taken into account in access decisions so the new key status is reflected immediately, without requiring a session restart.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-2(6)** enhancement requires implementing the organisation-defined parameter **ac-02.06_odp (dynamic privilege management capabilities)**. The guidance contrasts fixed (static) privileges with dynamic privilege management, including **immediate revocation**, **continuous re-checking**, **rules based on time and job function**, **emergency/duress triggers**, and **key-change propagation** (for example, encryption key updates affecting access decisions).

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define an authoritative attribute model** for the application’s authorisation decisions, including at minimum: job role/assignment, eligibility time windows, emergency/duress flag(s), and a **communications key epoch/version** (or equivalent key-status indicator) taken from the organisation’s key management/rotation process.
2. **Implement ABAC policy evaluation at runtime** using a central authorisation decision point (for example, an identity provider (IdP)/authorisation gateway/policy engine). Policies must be written so access is granted or denied based on the current attributes, not on a fixed list of privileges per user.
3. **Enforce continuous re-checking** during an active session:
   - Prefer **authorisation checks at the time of each request** for sensitive actions.
   - If checks at request time are not possible for every operation, issue **short-lived authorisation decisions/tokens** and re-check at a documented maximum “staleness” interval.
   - Ensure that when attributes change (role/job assignment, time eligibility, emergency/duress), the next enforcement point immediately reduces access or denies it.
4. **Implement real-time attribute updates** to the authorisation decision point using event-driven updates from authoritative systems (for example, human resources/assignment systems, identity and access management (IAM) group/role management, incident/duress workflows). Include ways to handle update delays (for example, **fail-closed** for high-risk actions).
5. **Integrate encryption key-change updates into access decisions**:
   - When communications keys rotate, update the **key epoch/version** attribute/context used by the authorisation decision.
   - Ensure that authorisation for communications-protected operations checks the current key epoch/version (or denies operations tied to an old epoch), without requiring a session restart.
6. **Produce audit evidence** for dynamic privilege changes and key-change impacts: record (a) the attribute inputs used for decisions, (b) the decision outcomes, (c) privilege revocation/denial events during active use, and (d) key epoch/version changes that affected access.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Owner / Security Architect**: owns the attribute model, policy design, and enforcement approach.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Engineer / Platform Engineer**: implements ABAC policies, runtime enforcement, and attribute propagation integrations.
- **Key Management / Security Operations**: provides key epoch/version signals and validates that key-change propagation works as intended.
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC)/Monitoring Engineer**: ensures audit logging, alerting, and security information and event management (SIEM) integration for evidence.

###### Acceptance criteria
- When a user’s job role/assignment attribute changes, **active sessions** are denied or adjusted at the next enforcement point **without requiring logout/login**.
- When a user becomes ineligible due to **time-based rules**, access is revoked or denied according to the policy within the documented maximum staleness interval (or immediately for request-time checks).
- When an **emergency/duress** flag is set, access is reduced or denied for affected users/actions within the documented enforcement behaviour.
- After **communications encryption key rotation**, authorisation decisions reflect the new **key epoch/version** and deny operations tied to the old key status (without session restart), or meet the documented expectation for immediate propagation.
- Audit logs show which attributes drove each decision and record the impact of privilege changes and key-epoch changes during active use.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the official attribute set for attribute-based access control (ABAC), including job or assignment, time eligibility, emergency or duress status, and the communications key epoch or version, and identify the systems that provide the source-of-truth for these details.
- [ ] Set up attribute-based access control (ABAC) rules in the central access decision point, using “policy as code” with version control and review.
- [ ] Set up runtime enforcement so access decisions are made for each request, or—during active sessions—no later than a documented maximum time interval for how long decisions can be reused.
- [ ] Set up event-based sharing of user attributes from human resources (HR), identity and access management (IAM), and duress processes to the point where access decisions are made, and ensure that high-risk actions are blocked if the process fails (fail-closed).
- [ ] Integrate key-rotation events so the communications key “epoch” or version used for access decisions is updated, and confirm that actions using the old epoch are correctly denied.
- [ ] Enable audit logging for changes that dynamically remove or block privileges, including the effects of key changes, and send the logs to security information and event management (SIEM) so they can be used as evidence.


---

#### AC-2.7 — Privileged User Accounts (Enhancement)

This requirement ensures that only the right people are given extra, high-impact access needed to run and protect key parts of the organisation, and that this access is closely monitored. Without it, someone could keep powerful permissions after their job changes, or the wrong permissions could be granted and not be noticed—making it easier for mistakes or misuse to cause serious harm.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must set up and manage privileged user accounts using its role-based access control (RBAC) scheme, so privileged access is granted based on defined roles. It must continuously monitor which privileged roles (or role features) each person has, track any changes to those roles or features, and regularly review whether the privileged access is still appropriate. If it is no longer appropriate, the organisation must promptly remove the privileged access. It must also keep clear records of what was changed and when, supported by audit and monitoring information that can be reviewed.

##### RBAC-governed privileged access with continuous role-change monitoring

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up administration for privileged user accounts so that privileged access is granted only through the organisation-approved **role-based access scheme**. Continuously check changes to who has privileged roles and associated attributes, and remove access quickly when it is no longer appropriate. Keep clear, auditable records of role membership changes and access removals.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-2(7) enhancement requires that privileged access is organised and granted using an approved scheme (parameter: **role-based access scheme**). It also requires continuous monitoring of privileged role/attribute assignments for changes, with timely removal when access is no longer appropriate.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define privileged roles in role-based access control (RBAC)** that match the organisation’s privileged access categories (for example: key management, account management, database/system/network/web administration). Link each role to the correct authoritative identity groups and entitlements in the enterprise identity provider (IdP).
2. **Make role-based assignment the only route to privilege**: configure the IdP/authorisation layer so privileged permissions come only from the approved RBAC roles and groups. Where possible, block or remove any direct/manual assignment of privileged permissions.
3. **Set up an automated privileged access lifecycle** using identity governance (entitlement management) with an approval process and time-limited access (where supported). Ensure role membership is granted only after approval and automatically expires.
4. **Continuously monitor privileged role membership changes** by sending IdP audit events and group/entitlement change events to the security information and event management (SIEM) and monitoring platform. Create alerts for:
   - new privileged role membership,
   - privileged role removals,
   - privileged role changes (add/remove/update),
   - privileged access granted outside expected approval time windows.
5. **Automatically revoke access when lifecycle events occur** (for example: HR joiner/mover/leaver, termination, role change). When a user’s role is no longer appropriate, remove privileged role membership promptly and record the revocation, including who/what triggered it, the time, and the reason.
6. **Keep an up-to-date system-generated list of privileged accounts** by checking the IdP’s authoritative privileged groups/roles and matching them to application/admin access identifiers. Use this list to produce evidence packs for periodic review.
7. **Produce audit-ready evidence**: keep records of privileged role assignment/removal and revocation actions, and provide scheduled reports for review (for example, monthly for high-risk roles) showing current privileged membership and the change history.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **IAM (identity and access management) / Identity Engineering Lead**: responsible for defining RBAC roles, configuring the IdP, and mapping entitlements.
- **Security Operations (SOC) / SIEM Engineer**: responsible for detections, alerting, and monitoring pipelines.
- **Application Owner / System Administrator**: confirms that application/admin functions are correctly mapped to privileged roles and that the revocation impact is acceptable.
- **HR/Workforce Systems Owner (or HRIS Integration Owner)**: ensures lifecycle triggers are integrated so revocation happens promptly.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Privileged access to application administrative functions is granted only through RBAC roles mapped to IdP groups/entitlements, with no remaining direct/manual privileged permission grants for supported pathways.
- A system-generated list of privileged accounts exists and matches the IdP’s authoritative privileged role membership.
- Privileged role membership changes (add/remove/update) are continuously monitored and produce auditable events in the SIEM.
- When a user’s role becomes inappropriate (for example, termination/role change), privileged role membership is removed promptly and the revocation is recorded with the timestamp, reason, and responsible actor/system.
- Audit evidence (assignment/removal/revocation logs and reports) is retained and can be retrieved for review.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define role-based access control (RBAC) roles with elevated permissions for the application’s security-critical administration functions, and link each role to identity provider (IdP) groups and entitlements.
- [ ] Set up the identity provider (IdP) and authorisation layer so that elevated permissions are granted only through approved role-based access control (RBAC) roles. Where possible, remove or disable any direct assignments of privileged permissions.
- [ ] Set up an identity governance process to approve requests for privileged role access, and grant that access only for a limited period of time.
- [ ] Send identity provider (IdP) privileged role or group change events to the security information and event management (SIEM) system, and put in place alerts to detect when privileged roles or groups are added, removed, or updated.
- [ ] Set up human resources (HR) lifecycle events (joiner, mover, leaver, and termination) so that privileged role access is automatically removed when it is no longer appropriate.
- [ ] Create and store a list of privileged accounts generated by the system, based on the identity provider’s (IdP) official roles, and schedule regular reviews with evidence packs.
- [ ] Check that audit logs are kept for the required period, and that revocation events record the person or system involved, the date and time, and the reason.


---

#### AC-2.8 — Dynamic Account Management (Enhancement)

This requirement is about automatically creating, turning on, updating, and turning off the special accounts that on-premises systems and integrations use to do their work—exactly when they are needed. Without this, accounts can be set up late, left active after they are no longer required, or given more access than they should have. This increases the risk of unauthorised access, misuse, and lingering access that is difficult to trace.

The organisation is expected to:
- define which service accounts and privileged system accounts are in scope
- document how this “account lifecycle” works (how accounts are created, changed, and removed over time)
- include it in its security planning and day-to-day procedures

It must use automated mechanisms to create, activate, manage, and deactivate these accounts while systems are running, including for integration entities that were not previously known—but only after checking that the request is properly authorised and that the approved level of access is granted or removed.

It should also keep an up-to-date list of these accounts and record what the automation did, so all actions can be audited.

##### JIT service account lifecycle automation with authorisation checks

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up **dynamic creation, activation, management (including privilege updates), and deactivation** of in-scope **system accounts** (service accounts and privileged system accounts used by on-premises applications and integrations) while the system is running. This is driven by integration and entity events.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-2(8)** (Enhancement) requires organisations to **create, activate, manage, and deactivate system accounts dynamically**. The guidance expects that, at runtime, the system can provision accounts for previously unknown entities. This should be supported by **trusted relationships, business rules, and checks** that confirm what access is allowed and what privileges are appropriate.

###### HOW (specific approach)
###### 1) Define the in-scope dynamic account model
- Create an account information structure (for example: integration entity ID, environment, owning system/team, requested operation, expiry/deactivation trigger, privilege template ID).
- Define privilege templates that translate entity details into **least-privilege** role/group/entitlement sets.

###### 2) Set up an authoritative entity registry and trust boundary
- Keep an approved **entity registry** listing integration partners/entities and the access attributes they are allowed to use.
- Set up a trust relationship so provisioning automation can check the registry and confirm the runtime entity is authorised to perform the requested operation.

###### 3) Build a runtime provisioning workflow using policy rules
- When an integration event happens (for example, when an entity is seen for the first time), the integration platform/workflow engine calls a **provisioning API** with: entity ID, requested lifecycle action (create/activate/update/deactivate), target system, and privilege template ID.
- The provisioning service applies policy rules to validate that:
  - the entity exists and is eligible in the registry,
  - the requested lifecycle action is allowed for the current state,
  - the privilege template is allowed for that entity type,
  - any required approval reference is present and valid (or a pre-authorised rule match applies).

###### 4) Apply least-privilege changes and update account status
- **Create**: create the account in the target on-premises system(s) (for example, directory/application/database/broker), but keep it disabled until activation conditions are met.
- **Activate**: enable the account only after authorisation checks pass.
- **Manage/Update**: when entity attributes change, update group/role/entitlement membership to match the approved privilege template.
- **Deactivate**: disable the account and remove privileges when the integration is disabled, the entity is removed, or a defined expiry/lifecycle signal occurs.

###### 5) Ensure lifecycle actions are auditable and ready as evidence
- For every lifecycle action (create/activate/manage/deactivate), generate structured audit records to the security information and event management (SIEM) and logging system, including:
  - the automation/workflow identifier,
  - entity ID and requested action,
  - the authorisation decision result (allowed/denied) and reason codes,
  - the privilege template ID and the privilege state before/after (or a hashed/structured representation),
  - the target system(s) and account identifier(s).

###### 6) Assign operational responsibilities
- Assign a responsible owner for the entity registry and privilege templates (for example, IAM/Application Security owner).
- Assign a responsible owner for the provisioning workflow and runtime triggers (for example, platform/integration engineering).
- Document the end-to-end lifecycle process and how exceptions are handled (for example, what happens if an entity is not found in the registry).

###### WHO
- **IAM/Application Security Owner**: defines the in-scope account model, governs the entity registry, and defines privilege templates.
- **Platform/Integration Engineering Lead**: builds the provisioning API/workflow integration and the runtime triggers.
- **Security Monitoring/SOC**: ensures audit events are collected, kept, and can be searched in the SIEM.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For an authorised, previously unknown integration entity, the system automatically **creates and activates** the in-scope system account at runtime, with privileges matching the approved template.
- For an unauthorised entity or a disallowed privilege template, the system **denies** the lifecycle action and records an auditable authorisation decision.
- When the integration/entity is removed or a deactivation trigger occurs, the system **deactivates** the account and removes privileges.
- Every lifecycle action produces structured, searchable audit evidence containing entity ID, action type, authorisation outcome, privilege template ID, and target system/account identifiers.
- During “manage”, privilege updates correctly move from the previous privileges to the approved template, maintaining least privilege.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the in-scope system account information structure (metadata schema) and the access permission templates for service accounts and privileged system accounts (ac-02.08_odp).
- [ ] Create and manage an official register of integration entities and the access permissions they are allowed. This register must be available for the automated provisioning process to check.
- [ ] Set up a provisioning programme and workflow that handles live requests to manage an entity’s lifecycle (create, activate, update, deactivate). Each request must include the entity ID and the privilege template that has been requested.
- [ ] Before any lifecycle action is carried out, check the authorisation rules defined in policy-as-code against the entity registry and the privilege templates.
- [ ] Set up the target on-premises systems so they can automatically change account status when needed: create as disabled, enable when activated, and revoke when deactivated.
- [ ] Set up structured audit and event records for every step in the lifecycle, and send them to the security information and event management (SIEM) system. This includes the result of each authorisation decision and the privilege level before and after the change.
- [ ] Define who is responsible for what, including how to handle exceptions when an organisation is not listed in the registry or when an authorisation request is refused.


---

#### AC-2.9 — Restrictions on Use of Shared and Group Accounts (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that shared or group logins are only used when there is a clear, responsible owner and when actions can still be traced back to a specific person. Without these safeguards, it becomes easier for the wrong person to use the account, for changes or mistakes to go unchallenged, and for investigations after an incident to be delayed because no one can be held accountable.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must only allow shared or group accounts when each one has a named custodian assigned, and that custodian’s responsibility is recorded. Access must be limited to only the approved tasks the organisation has documented for these accounts.

The organisation must also ensure individual accountability either by linking each user to the shared or group account in a way that clearly shows who did what, or, if that linking is not used, by putting in place alternative measures that provide the same level of traceability. Before allowing such accounts, the organisation should consider and document the additional risk created by reduced accountability.

##### Custodian-governed shared/group accounts with per-user attribution

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Only allow shared and group accounts when they are managed by a **named custodian**, limited to **approved functions**, and supported by **clear individual accountability** through either **a unique link between each user and the account** (preferred) or other compensating controls.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) requirement AC-2(9) enhancement says: “Only permit the use of shared and group accounts that meet {{ insert: param, ac-02.09_odp }}.” The guidance explains that these accounts carry **higher risk** when there is **no clear accountability**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
Set up a custodian-managed model across the enterprise identity and access management (IAM) / privileged access management (PAM) tools and application administration tools, so that every action taken using a shared/group account can be traced to a specific named person.

1. **Create an official shared/group account register** (an IAM governance database or configuration management database (CMDB)) that records, for each shared/group account: account identifier, role(s), **approved functions**, **named custodian**, owning team, and start/end dates.
2. **Make custodian assignment a required step before creation or activation**: no shared/group account can be created or enabled unless the register entry includes a **named custodian** and an approved-function set.
3. **Limit use to approved functions only** by setting least-privilege roles/permissions and (where applicable) using command/function allow-lists in the application’s administrative interfaces and any connected administration consoles.
4. **Use unique user-to-account linking (preferred)** with an enterprise PAM/access broker approach:
   - Users log in to the PAM using their own identity.
   - The PAM creates a session that records the link **(user → shared/group account → session → action carried out)**.
   - The shared/group account is used only as the back-end identity; the user’s actions are attributed to the individual through the PAM session records.
5. **Make sure actions are auditable**: configure logging so audit records include the user identity, shared/group account identifier, session ID, and the action performed; send logs to security information and event management (SIEM) for retention and investigation.
6. **Add a governance check for the increased risk**: document why the use of shared/group accounts is accepted (for example, why they are necessary), and require regular reviews of custodian assignment, approved functions, and whether the user-to-account linking is working effectively.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **IAM/PAM Engineer**: implement the register integration, session attribution, and access broker configuration.
- **Application Owner / Admin Console Owner**: define approved functions and implement least-privilege/allow-lists.
- **Security Governance / Governance, Risk and Compliance (GRC) Lead**: ensure risk consideration is addressed and that evidence of periodic review is maintained.
- **Custodian (named accountable role)**: owns the approved-function scope and confirms that access is used appropriately.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Every shared/group account used in production and test has a register entry with a **named custodian** and **approved functions**.
- Shared/group account use is only possible through the PAM/access broker (or an equivalent method), so audit records show **unique user-to-account/session mapping** for actions.
- Any attempt to use the shared/group account directly (bypassing the linking mechanism) is blocked, or results in no audit trail that could be used for attribution.
- SIEM/audit logs include at least: user identity, shared/group account identifier, session ID, timestamp, and the action performed.
- Evidence exists that risk acceptance was documented and that custodian assignment and approved-function scope are reviewed periodically.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Set up a shared or group account register that records the account custodian, the approved uses, and the account life-cycle information.
- [ ] Set up an identity and access management (IAM) and privileged access management (PAM) approval step so shared or group accounts cannot be created or turned on unless the custodian is involved and the request is within the approved-function scope.
- [ ] Limit access to applications and administration so that shared or group accounts can only be used for approved, documented purposes (least privilege and allow-lists).
- [ ] Route shared or group account access through an access broker so that each action is linked to the specific user, by recording a user → account → session → action trail.
- [ ] Set up and centralise audit logging in security information and event management (SIEM), including the user identity, the shared or group account identifier, the session ID, and the action that was carried out.
- [ ] Document and get approval for how risks are considered when using shared or group accounts, and carry out regular reviews of the account custodian and any approved functions.


---

#### AC-2.11 — Usage Conditions (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that special automated work accounts are used only in the correct way, at the correct time, and for the correct purpose. Without these limits, an account intended for routine system administration or automated batch jobs could be misused outside planned work periods, used as if a person were logging in directly, or left running for too long. This increases the risk of unauthorised changes, hidden misuse, and incidents that are difficult to trace.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must set clear rules for every in-scope service account so that it:
- can be used only during approved maintenance windows,
- cannot be used for interactive logons (no direct user-style sessions), and
- can only run in time-limited sessions that never exceed 8 hours.

The system must enforce these rules, record what happened, analyse any misuse, and raise alerts when an account is used outside the approved conditions. Alerts must include enough detail to identify the account and the specific rule that was broken.

##### Enforce service-account usage windows and block interactive logons

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Put technical controls in place to enforce the organisation’s approved usage rules for **system accounts**:
- **Only during approved maintenance windows**
- **No interactive sign-ins**
- **Sessions limited to a maximum of 8 hours**
- Create **audit records** and **alerts** if these rules are broken.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-2(11) (Enhancement) requires: **“Enforce {{ ac-02.11_odp.01 }} for {{ ac-02.11_odp.02 }}.”**
The organisation’s settings specify that the enforced circumstances/usage rules are: **approved maintenance windows only; no interactive sign-ins; sessions limited to a maximum of 8 hours** for the in-scope **service accounts used for on-premises infrastructure administration and automated batch processing**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
###### 1) Identify and define the in-scope system accounts
- Create and maintain an up-to-date list of the in-scope service accounts (on-premises infrastructure administration and automated batch processing).
