- For each account, record supporting details (for example, group membership or an account tag) that link the account to the AC-2(11) usage rules.

###### 2) Enforce “no interactive sign-ins”
- Route all administrative and batch execution activity for these service accounts through a controlled access method (for example, a privileged access gateway, a jump host, or a privileged access management broker) that can enforce policy.
- Configure the access method to **block interactive sign-in/session creation** for the in-scope service-account identities.
- Make sure any interactive attempts are logged with the account identity, the source, and the attempted action.

###### 3) Enforce “maintenance-window-only”
- Define the maintenance-window schedule in a machine-readable format (for example, exported from the change or ticketing system), including the time zone and any exceptions.
- Use policy-as-code (or an equivalent automated configuration) so the access method allows the in-scope service accounts only during the approved windows.
- Block access outside the window and record the reason (for example, “outside approved maintenance window”).

###### 4) Enforce “sessions limited to 8 hours or less”
- Set session lifetime controls for these service accounts so sessions are automatically ended or suspended when they reach **8 hours** (or sooner).
- Ensure the enforcement applies to the real session mechanism used in the access path (for example, the broker session duration, the jump-host session timeout, or the token/session lifetime).

###### 5) Audit and alert on rule violations
- Ensure audit logs include: the service account identifier, the result of the enforcement rule, start and end times, session length, and whether the attempt was interactive.
- Set up security information and event management (SIEM) and security orchestration, automation and response (SOAR) detection rules to alert when:
  - a service account is used outside the maintenance window,
  - an interactive sign-in is attempted,
  - a session exceeds 8 hours (or is ended at the 8-hour point).
- Include enough information to support monitoring and accountability (which account, when, and which rule was broken).

###### WHO
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) / Privileged Access Management (PAM) engineer**: implement and configure the enforcement and session controls.
- **Security Operations (SOC) / SIEM engineer**: implement alerting and check that audit log fields are correct.
- **Infrastructure/Operations owner**: provide the authoritative maintenance-window schedule source and confirm enforcement works as expected during change windows.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every in-scope service account, interactive sign-ins are blocked and audit events are generated.
- For every in-scope service account, access is allowed only within approved maintenance windows; attempts outside windows are blocked and audit events are generated.
- No enforced session for these service accounts can run longer than **8 hours**; sessions are ended or suspended at or before 8 hours and audit events are generated.
- SIEM generates alerts for each violation type (outside window, interactive attempt, session length breach) with traceable account identity and timestamps.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an official list of all in-scope service accounts, then label and group them so that AC-2(11) enforcement can be applied.
- [ ] Set up a controlled access route (privileged access management (PAM), jump host, or access broker) for these service accounts, and turn off any direct ability for them to log on interactively.
- [ ] Set the policy to block interactive logins for the in-scope service account identities, and record the blocked attempts in the logs.
- [ ] Build the maintenance window schedule into the access enforcement process, and block access outside the approved windows, recording the reason for each block.
- [ ] Set session time limits so that sessions for these service accounts are ended or paused within 8 hours, and record the session start time, end time, and duration in the logs.
- [ ] Set up security information and event management (SIEM) and security orchestration, automation and response (SOAR) alerts for rule violations (attempts outside the allowed time window, interactive attempts, and sessions lasting more than eight hours) and check that the alert message includes the correct data fields.


---

#### AC-2.12 — Account Monitoring for Atypical Usage (Enhancement)

This requirement is about closely monitoring system accounts so you can quickly spot suspicious login activity—especially logins that happen outside normal working hours or from locations or network areas that are not approved for that system. Without this, a rogue insider, stolen access, or an ongoing attack could go unnoticed while someone uses system accounts in ways that do not match normal behaviour.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must:
- define what counts as unusual use (for example, out-of-hours activity and use from an unapproved location or network segment);
- monitor all system accounts for these signs; and
- record enough information to identify which account was used, when it was used, and from where.

Whenever unusual use is detected, it must be reported to the Security Operations Centre incident response lead and the system owner.

Because this monitoring may reveal sensitive behaviour patterns, the organisation must complete and keep up to date a privacy risk assessment, and ensure the approach matches its privacy plan, including how the data is limited and protected.

##### SIEM/SOAR atypical login monitoring for system accounts

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up continuous monitoring for **unusual activity** involving **system accounts** by spotting sign-in events that happen **outside normal working hours** and/or from **locations or network areas that are not approved**. For every match, escalate the alert to both the **security operations centre (SOC) incident response lead** and the **system owner**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-2(12) enhancement requires the organisation to: (1) define what counts as **unusual activity** for system accounts, (2) monitor those accounts for that unusual activity, (3) record enough information to identify the **account** and the **login details** (time and location/network), (4) report/escalate unusual activity to the specified roles, and (5) assess and document **privacy risks** through a privacy impact assessment aligned to the privacy programme plan.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define unusual activity criteria** using the organisation’s agreed parameters:
   - Sign-ins outside normal working hours (normal duty hours are defined for each system/service).
   - Sign-ins from **unapproved locations** and/or **unapproved network areas** (approved lists/ranges are maintained for each group of system accounts).
2. **Make sure system accounts are included in the monitoring**:
   - For accounts that authenticate through the enterprise identity provider (IdP), enable sign-in logging with the fields needed for detection (timestamp, account identifier, source IP address, and any available location/network context).
   - For system accounts not covered by the IdP (for example, local sign-in or older authentication methods), send their authentication logs to the same security information and event management (SIEM) system using a standardised format.
3. **Create detection rules in the SIEM** (use “detection-as-code” where possible):
   - Alert when a system account sign-in matches the out-of-hours criteria.
   - Alert when a system account sign-in comes from an unapproved location or unapproved network area.
   - Ensure each alert includes: system account identifier, event time, source IP address, the location information recorded/derived (or a location indicator), and the network area/VPN exit identifier used to make the decision.
4. **Escalate using security orchestration, automation and response (SOAR)** to the required roles for every detection:
   - Send to the **SOC incident response lead** and the **system owner** (using a system-to-owner mapping).
   - Include the minimum useful details in the ticket/alert (what matched, when, where it came from, and which account).
5. **Carry out and keep up to date the privacy risk assessment** in line with the privacy programme plan:
   - Record the privacy impact of collecting/using login details (for example, source IP address/location).
   - Use privacy-aligned controls: keep retained data to the minimum needed where possible, limit access to logs to authorised roles, and set retention periods in line with the privacy plan.

###### WHO
- **SOC incident response lead**: receives and triages escalations; ensures incident handling and uses outcomes to improve detection.
- **System owner**: receives escalations for investigation and remediation specific to their systems.
- **Identity and access management (IAM)/SIEM engineering (with oversight from the privacy officer)**: builds the detection logic, standardises logs, sets up routing, and ensures privacy-aligned retention and access.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Unusual activity for system accounts is defined and documented as: **out-of-hours** and **unapproved locations/network areas**.
- SIEM detection rules create an alert for each matching sign-in event and include the required identifiers/details (account, time, and the location/network basis).
- Every alert is escalated to **both** the SOC incident response lead and the **system owner**, with actionable information.
- A current privacy impact assessment exists for this monitoring approach, and the logging/retention/access controls implemented match the privacy programme plan.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Document the criteria for when system accounts can be used outside normal hours, including the approved out-of-hours schedule and the approved locations and network segments, in line with ac-02.12_odp.01.
- [ ] For every inventory system account, record where the login comes from (identity provider (IdP) versus local or legacy login) to make sure security information and event management (SIEM) coverage is complete.
- [ ] Set up and standardise authentication logs in the security information and event management (SIEM) system so they record: the account identifier, the time of the event, the source internet protocol (IP) address, and location and network-segment indicators.
- [ ] Set up security monitoring rules in security information and event management (SIEM) to detect system account sign-ins that happen outside approved hours or from unapproved locations or network segments
- [ ] Set up security orchestration, automation and response (SOAR) playbooks so that every detection is escalated to the security operations centre (SOC) incident response lead and the system owner (routing map per ac-02.12_odp.02).
- [ ] Complete a privacy impact assessment for monitoring unusual usage, and put in place privacy-aligned rules for how long the collected login information is kept and who can access it.


---

#### AC-2.13 — Disable Accounts for High-risk Individuals (Enhancement)

This requirement is about acting quickly to stop dangerous access by turning off the accounts of people assessed as high risk. If a person is credibly linked to an intention to cause harm using their authorised access, or if there is credible evidence that an attacker could cause harm through them, keeping their account active could allow unauthorised actions, damage company systems and assets, harm other organisations, or harm individuals.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must define what counts as credible evidence of this kind, identify the affected individuals, and disable their system accounts within 24 hours of discovering the evidence. The organisation must also keep clear, verifiable records showing which accounts were disabled and when, maintain a list of risky user activities to help identify affected people, and document the approach in its account-management procedures and related system plans.

Finally, it must coordinate promptly with system administrators, information security staff, human resources, legal advisers, and the authorising officials to ensure the correct accounts are disabled properly, with appropriate oversight.

##### 24-hour high-risk account disablement via case-to-IdP workflow

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up an automated, traceable process that disables the system accounts of people identified as high-risk within **24 hours of discovery**. This must be based on **clearly defined criteria for a serious security and privacy risk**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The U.S. National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) **AC-2(13)** (enhancement) requires: **“Disable accounts of individuals within {{param ac-02.13_odp.01}} of discovery of {{param ac-02.13_odp.02}}.”**  
This means the organisation must (1) define what counts as a **serious risk**, and (2) ensure account disablement happens within the agreed **time limit (24 hours)** from the moment credible evidence is discovered, with coordinated oversight.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define “credible evidence / serious risks” in a structured way** in the case or ticket template used by the security operations centre (SOC), insider-risk, human resources (HR), and legal. Use the organisation’s parameter: **“credible evidence of intent to cause harm via authorised access or evidence that an adversary can cause harm through the individual.”** Require evidence tagging (for example, insider-risk indicator category, threat intelligence reference, HR incident reference) so decisions can be repeated consistently.
2. **Link each case to the correct person and accounts**: when a case is opened with the required evidence tags, automatically identify the person’s key identity details (for example, employee ID/UPN) and list all accounts connected to them (identity provider (IdP) user account, directory accounts, and any linked federated application identities).
3. **Apply the 24-hour target from the “discovery time”**: record the exact time the evidence was discovered (not the time the ticket was created). The workflow must calculate the deadline and set the case priority based on it.
4. **Automatically disable the account at the enterprise identity provider (IdP) and remove access**: once approval is granted (or an emergency override is used under your governance model), use IdP administration functions to **disable the account** and **end active sessions/tokens** where supported. Make sure the action creates an auditable record of the disabled account, and record when the workflow completes.
5. **Extend disablement to connected access routes**: trigger software-as-a-service (SaaS) offboarding (for example, SCIM deprovisioning) and remove federation group access, and block sign-in using conditional access rules so access is stopped while disablement spreads.
6. **Keep audit-ready records**: store (a) the discovery timestamp, (b) the evidence category and tags, (c) the approver identity (HR/legal/authorising official as applicable), (d) the disablement start and end timestamps, and (e) references to relevant IdP audit log entries.
7. **Make coordination part of the workflow**: ensure the workflow explicitly includes the system administrators, information security, HR, legal, and the authorising official in the approval and oversight steps for each case.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Information Security / identity and access management (IAM) team**: owns the workflow design, identity provider (IdP) integrations, and audit record creation.
- **Security operations centre (SOC) / insider-risk analysts**: open cases and provide evidence tagging and discovery timestamps.
- **HR and Legal**: provide required inputs and approvals for high-risk decisions.
- **Authorising official**: approves disablement (or authorises an emergency override) in line with organisational governance.
- **System administrators**: confirm that disablement covers any accounts not managed directly by the IdP.

###### Acceptance criteria
- In test cases where credible evidence is provided, the affected user’s IdP account is **disabled within 24 hours of the recorded discovery timestamp**.
- The workflow captures and retains **audit evidence** showing the discovery time, evidence tags (matching the defined serious risks), approver identity, and IdP disablement timestamps and log references.
- Disablement results in **blocked sign-in** (through IdP disablement and conditional access/session removal) for the affected user across connected applications.
- A measurable exception process exists for any delay, with documented reasons and authorising official approval.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and publish clear, structured criteria for what counts as a “significant risk”, aligned with the organisation’s requirements, to provide credible evidence that there is an intention to cause harm or that the individual poses a threat to cause harm.
- [ ] Create a standard case or ticket template that records the exact time the issue was discovered and includes the required evidence labels for AC-2(13) decisions.
- [ ] Integrate the case system with the organisation’s identity provider (IdP) to identify the affected users and list any linked accounts.
- [ ] Set up an automated process to disable an identity provider (IdP) account. This should disable the account and end any active sessions and tokens, with a strict 24-hour service-level agreement (SLA) from the time the issue is identified.
- [ ] Add automatic downstream removal for integrated applications, including SCIM deprovisioning and/or federation group removal, and block access using conditional access controls.
- [ ] Store audit-ready records that link, for each disablement event, the time it was discovered, the evidence tags, the identity of the person who approved it, and the identity provider (IdP) audit log references.
- [ ] Run tabletop and technical tests to confirm that accounts are disabled within 24 hours, and that any exceptions must be approved by an authorised official.


---

### AC-3 — Access Enforcement (Control)

This requirement is about ensuring that people and automated tasks can only access the correct information and systems, and only in the specific ways that the organisation has formally approved. Without this, unauthorised access could go undetected, sensitive data could be seen or changed by the wrong people, and mistakes or unauthorised changes could remain unnoticed—leading to real financial, legal, and reputational harm.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must set clear rules for who can access what, document how access is approved, granted, reviewed, and changed, and configure systems so these rules are enforced at the main system level and, where needed, within specific applications or services. The organisation must keep an up-to-date list of approved permissions, grant access using only those approved permissions, block anything outside the approved rules, and record access enforcement activity so it can be checked later.

Responsibilities must be assigned, and the approach must be reflected in security and privacy planning where applicable.

##### Enforce approved access via IdP-driven RBAC and app authorisation checks

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Put in place logical access enforcement so that users and automated processes can only access the application and its resources in line with **approved authorisations** (roles/permissions) set by the organisation, using **system/application-level controls** and keeping **audit evidence**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-3 requires the organisation to **enforce approved authorisations for logical access** to information and system resources in line with access control policies. The guidance also expects enforcement at the **system level**, and where needed at the **application/service level**, to block access that falls outside policy.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create and maintain an official authorisations catalogue** (roles/permissions → resources → allowed actions). Make sure every access request maps to an entry in this catalogue, including any exceptions, which must have an expiry date.
2. **Connect the application to the organisation’s enterprise identity provider (IdP)** (central identity system) and set up **server-side authorisation** using IdP-provided information (role-based access control (RBAC) / attribute-based access control (ABAC)). The application must **deny by default** when the required roles/claims are not present.
3. **Automate adding and removing access** (for example, system for cross-domain identity management (SCIM) or an equivalent method) so role/group assignments come only from the approved authorisations catalogue. Remove manual permission grants except for tightly controlled, time-limited exceptions.
4. **Add enforcement checks inside the application/service** for every sensitive function (user interface screens, application programming interface (API) endpoints, background tasks/service accounts, and administrative functions). Make sure authorisation checks happen **before** any data access or business action.
5. **Enable and keep audit logs** showing the results of access enforcement: successful and failed authorisation decisions, changes to role/group assignments, and access provisioning events. Send these logs to the organisation’s security information and event management (SIEM) system and keep them for the required period.
6. **Test and collect evidence in each environment** (test, staging, production): confirm that unauthorised users/processes are blocked (for example, HTTP 403/denied actions) and that authorised users can only carry out the actions they are permitted to do.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Owner / Engineering Lead**: implement application/service authorisation checks and configure them for each environment.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) / Access Management Team**: maintain the approved authorisations catalogue and IdP role/group mappings; manage provisioning processes.
- **Security Operations / SIEM Administrator**: ensure audit logging, log forwarding, and log retention.
- **Change Manager / Change Advisory Board (CAB) (where applicable)**: ensure changes to authorisation mappings follow the approved change process.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The application denies access by default when required roles/claims are missing (confirmed through unauthorised test cases).
- All role/group assignments used by the application are provisioned from the approved authorisations catalogue (confirmed through provisioning configuration and recent change records).
- Access enforcement outcomes (authorisation success/failure) and provisioning/role changes are logged and visible in SIEM for the application.
- Evidence exists linking: (a) approved authorisations catalogue entries, (b) IdP role/group mappings, and (c) the application enforcement configuration for production.
- Any exceptions (if present) are time-bound, owned, and recorded, and the application continues to block access outside the approved authorisations.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and get approval for an official authorisations catalogue that links job roles and permissions to the relevant application resources and the actions each role is allowed to take.
- [ ] Configure the organisation’s identity provider (IdP) to send the required role-based access control (RBAC) and attribute-based access control (ABAC) permissions for the application, and link the IdP groups and roles to the approved authorisations catalogue.
- [ ] Set up automated account creation and removal (for example, system for cross-domain identity management (SCIM)) so access to applications is granted only through approved role or group assignments
- [ ] Set up server-side authorisation checks for every sensitive user interface (UI), application programming interface (API), background job, and administrative function, using a “deny by default” approach.
- [ ] Turn on audit logs for authorisation decisions and for changes to user access and roles, and send those logs to security information and event management (SIEM) with the required retention period.
- [ ] Run tests in each environment to confirm that actions are allowed only when they are authorised and blocked when they are not, proving access is denied outside the agreed policy (for example, “HTTP 403” errors and blocked business actions).


---

#### AC-3.2 — Dual Authorization (Enhancement)

This expectation is about ensuring that important, high-risk changes are never made by a single person working alone. Any privileged action or other activity that changes who can access systems, changes security settings, or makes administrative changes to Secret or Confidential information must be approved first, and then carried out by two different authorised individuals.

Without this “two-person” approach, a single insider (or a compromised account) could quietly change permissions or security settings and lead to unauthorised access, data exposure, or other serious harm.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly define and document which specific commands and actions require two approvals, keep these lists up to date, and state which people are authorised to give those approvals. The system and its procedures must prevent the change from being carried out unless two separate authorised approvals are recorded before it happens, and must keep evidence showing who approved what and when.

The organisation should also consider how to reduce the risk of collusion and how to handle urgent situations where immediate action may be needed.

##### Two-person approval gate for privileged access/security changes

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Put in place a **two-person approval** rule for **privileged commands and/or other actions** that change access settings, security settings, or carry out administrative changes to **Secret/Confidential** data. The system must **stop the action from running** unless **two different authorised people** have approved that exact action **before** it starts.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication 800-53 **AC-3(2)** enhancement requires **two-person approval** for the organisation’s defined scope: **“privileged commands and/or other actions”** that change access settings, security settings, or administer Secret/Confidential data (parameter **ac-03.02_odp**). This lowers the risk of insider threats by preventing one person from making high-impact changes on their own.

###### HOW (specific approach)
###### 1) Define which actions are covered (authoritative list)
- Create and keep an **authoritative catalogue** of the actions that are in scope, mapped to these three categories:
  - access setting changes
  - security setting changes
  - Secret/Confidential administrative changes
- For each action, define:
  - the exact command/application programming interface (API)/script identifier (or “infrastructure as code” (IaC) module/job name)
  - which approval type(s) are required
  - which environments it applies to (production, testing, disaster recovery)
  - any emergency handling classification (see step 5)

###### 2) Set up a two-person approval step in the change/approval-to-run process
- Use the organisation’s normal change process (for example, an information technology service management (ITSM) change workflow) or an automated approval workflow linked to the execution method.
- Require:
  - **two approvals**
  - from **two different authorised individuals** (no “same person twice”)
  - both approvers must be members of the **authorised approver roles/groups** for that action category

###### 3) Block execution unless it has two approvals
- Link the approval workflow to the privileged execution method (for example, a continuous integration/continuous delivery (CI/CD) pipeline, an admin console application programming interface (API), runbook automation, or an infrastructure as code (IaC) deployment runner).
- The execution step must:
  - confirm the request is **“approved by two distinct authorised individuals”**
  - confirm the action identifier matches what was approved
  - refuse to run if the approval requirements are not met

###### 4) Keep evidence for audit (approval must happen before execution)
- Save evidence for each request, including:
  - who requested it
  - both approvers’ identities
  - the time each approval was given
  - the approved action identifier and details (parameters)
  - when it ran and the result
- Store logs in an **immutable/tamper-evident** logging system and send them to a security information and event management (SIEM) system for audit matching.

###### 5) Manage collusion risk and urgent response needs
- Apply rotation/separation rules for pairing approvers (for example, time-based rotation or pairing limits) to reduce collusion risk.
- Define an **emergency/safety exception** process only if the organisation decides it is necessary for immediate public/environmental safety, including:
  - explicit approval by two authorised individuals where possible
  - a mandatory after-incident review and a retrospective two-person approval evidence record when immediate action was taken

###### WHO
- **Security Governance Lead / Compliance Owner**: owns the in-scope action catalogue and the approval role definitions.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) / Privileged Access Administrator**: sets up authorised approver roles/groups and ensures separation of identities.
- **ITSM/Change Manager** (or workflow owner): implements the two-approval workflow and ensures it is mandatory for in-scope actions.
- **Platform/DevOps Engineering Lead**: connects the approval gate to the privileged execution method.
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC)/Security information and event management (SIEM) Analyst**: checks that audit evidence is received correctly and that alerts are raised for attempts to run with only one approval.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every in-scope privileged action (per **ac-03.02_odp**), execution is **blocked** unless two approvals are recorded from **two distinct authorised individuals**.
- Audit evidence must show approvals happened **before** execution, including both approver identities and the approval times.
- The execution method must reject requests with:
  - only one approval
  - the same person approving twice
  - mismatched action identifiers/details (parameters)
- Logs must be kept in a tamper-evident way and be searchable for audit purposes.
- Collusion-risk mitigations (for example, rotation/pairing constraints) must be implemented and documented.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and keep an up-to-date, authoritative list (catalogue) of the privileged commands and actions that are in scope, and link each one to the relevant access controls, security settings, and Secret or Confidential information administration.
- [ ] Set up approved approver roles and groups in the organisation’s identity system, and make sure the people who approve requests are different from the people who start them.
- [ ] Set up the change and approval process so that it needs two approvals from two different authorised people, with a rule that the same person cannot approve twice.
- [ ] Use the privileged execution mechanism (pipeline, administration application programming interface (API), or infrastructure as code (IaC) runner) to check the workflow status and confirm the approved action identifiers before running.
- [ ] Set up tamper-evident logs that record the request initiator, both approvers, the date and time, the approved parameters, and the result of the execution, and send these logs to security information and event management (SIEM).
- [ ] Add measures to reduce the risk of collusion (for example, limits on rotation or pairing) and document how emergency or safety exceptions are handled, including a review after any incident.


---

#### AC-3.3 — Mandatory Access Control (Enhancement)

This requirement is about preventing people and automated services from misusing information after they have been given access. It does this by setting strict rules for who may receive the information and what they may do with it. Without these controls, confidential material could be copied or shared into more sensitive areas, leaked to unauthorised colleagues, or used to quietly increase someone’s permissions—such as changing how information is labelled or altering the rules that decide who can access it. This creates a serious risk of data breaches and improper access.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must have a clear confidentiality policy stating that information cannot be passed to anyone or anything with a lower security level (no “write up” and no “read down”). The policy must also state that access privileges cannot be transferred, security labels cannot be changed, new or changed items cannot be given new labels, and the access rules cannot be altered.

The system must apply these rules consistently to all covered users, service accounts, and trusted processes, and to all covered resources labelled “Confidential” or “Secret”. Only explicitly approved trusted identities may be allowed the limited read, write, and execute exceptions defined by the policy.

##### Enforce Bell-LaPadula MAC with non-bypass reference monitor

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement a consistently enforced mandatory access control (MAC) system that applies the organisation’s Bell‑LaPadula policy (**no write up** and **no read down**) to all **covered subjects** (authorised users, service accounts, trusted system processes) and all **covered objects** (data/files/records managed by the system) that have **Confidential/Secret** security labels.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-3(3) requires the system to **enforce the organisation’s mandatory access control policy** across the full set of covered subjects and objects, and to ensure the policy is **consistently enforced** so it cannot be bypassed. The guidance also expects enforcement to work like a “reference monitor” (AC-25) and for mandatory rules to override weaker discretionary rules (AC-3(4)).

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the MAC policy in system-enforced terms**: implement Bell‑LaPadula confidentiality rules as clear enforcement decisions:  
   - **No read down**: block subject reads/open actions when the subject’s security level is higher than the target object’s level.  
   - **No write up**: block subject write/modify actions when the subject’s security level is lower than the target object’s level.
2. **Label everything the system controls**: make sure every covered object (files, database rows/records, message contents, and exports in transit where relevant) is assigned a security label (**Confidential** or **Secret**) by the system, not by end users.
3. **Make enforcement hard to bypass (reference monitor properties)**: put the access checks at the operating system/kernel level (or an equivalent trusted enforcement point) so application code cannot override them. Ensure every access route (user interface, application programming interface, batch jobs, background workers, admin tools) uses the same MAC decision point.
4. **Stop label/attribute and rule tampering**: limit who can change security labels, security attributes, and the MAC policy/rules. Only explicitly authorised trusted components may make controlled exceptions.
5. **Use trusted exceptions narrowly**: run trusted system processes (for example, backup, controlled data movement, indexing, controlled replication) under dedicated security settings that are explicitly allowed to perform only the required actions, and only for the defined types of objects—while still preventing the spread of privileges and unauthorised sharing of information.
6. **Apply controls at system boundaries**: ensure that when information leaves the system’s control (for example, exports, downloads, external integrations), additional controls are applied so the mandatory rules still work (for example, controlled export methods that handle labels correctly).

###### WHO
- **Security Architect / Identity and Access Management (IAM) & Access Control Lead**: defines how Bell‑LaPadula maps subject contexts to security levels and approves the design of trusted exceptions.
- **Platform/Infrastructure Engineers (Operating System/Cloud)**: implement the MAC enforcement boundary and deploy the policy as code.
- **Application Owners / Developers**: connect to the labelled storage/data access interfaces so all access is checked by the MAC layer.
- **Security Operations / Compliance**: checks the evidence, monitors for policy breaches, and manages exception governance.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The system enforces **no read down** and **no write up** for all covered subjects/objects labelled **Confidential/Secret**.
- Attempts to read higher-labelled data from lower-labelled subjects, and attempts to write lower-labelled data into higher-labelled objects, are denied and recorded.
- Users cannot change security labels/attributes for covered objects, and cannot change the MAC policy/rules.
- All access routes (web/application programming interface/background jobs/admin tools) are checked by the same MAC enforcement mechanism (no bypass through alternate code paths).
- Trusted exceptions are limited to explicitly authorised identities/contexts and only for the required actions/object types; any violations are auditable.
- Evidence is available showing consistent enforcement and correct handling at system boundaries for exports/integration outgoing data.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Choose and standardise a media access control (MAC)-capable enforcement boundary (for example, an operating system or kernel MAC framework, or an equivalent trusted enforcement layer) across all covered computing environments.
- [ ] Define how the Bell–LaPadula model links each user’s security status to a security level, and how it labels information as Confidential or Secret, then implement this as version-controlled policy-as-code.
- [ ] Use system-controlled labelling for all covered items (files, records, and messages) so users cannot set or change labels on newly created or modified items.
- [ ] Set enforcement rules to block “no-read-down” and “no-write-up” access across every way users or systems can access data, including the user interface (UI), application programming interface (API), batch jobs, and administrative tools.
- [ ] Create separate security permissions for only explicitly authorised trusted processes, and limit what they are allowed to do (and which types of objects they can access) to the minimum required.
- [ ] Strengthen the policy and label management controls so that only the trusted policy-management component can change mandatory access control (MAC) rules and labels, with audit logging enabled.
- [ ] Put label-aware export and integration controls in place at system boundaries so that any required rules still apply and remain effective when data leaves the system’s control.


---

#### AC-3.4 — Discretionary Access Control (Enhancement)

This requirement is about preventing people from quietly sharing sensitive information or changing access permissions in ways that put it at the wrong level of importance. Without it, someone who is allowed to access a document or part of a system could share it with others, hand over their access rights, or change security settings so that higher-importance material reaches the wrong audience, or so that access rules are weakened.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must set out and document a clear discretionary access policy. This policy must allow authorised users to pass information, grant their privileges, change security attributes, choose security settings for new or updated items, and adjust access rules only when the sender and the receiver (or the target system part) are all authorised at the same impact and classification level.

The organisation must apply this policy consistently to every relevant user and every relevant system resource, configure the systems to enforce it, and keep records showing when the system allowed or blocked these actions.

##### Enforce same-level discretionary sharing and privilege/attribute changes

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Implement the organisation-defined discretionary access control policy (AC-3(4)) so that discretionary actions—specifically sharing data with other people/systems, granting privileges, changing security settings, choosing security settings for newly created or updated items, and changing access-control rules—are allowed only when the person/system making the change and the target (person/item/system component) are authorised at the same impact level and classification level.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-3(4) enhancement requires enforcing the organisation-defined discretionary access control policy for the covered people and items. Where the policy allows discretionary actions, those actions must be limited to the same impact and classification level. This stops users who already have access from spreading information or changing privileges/security settings in ways that move data or authorisation outside the intended level boundaries.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the enforcement model and covered set**: Create an official mapping of covered subjects (users/service accounts/groups) and covered objects (documents/records, data items, application resources, and any system components that can be labelled or have access control lists applied) to structured attributes `impactLevel` and `classificationLevel`.
2. **Implement a decision point for discretionary actions**: Connect the application’s sharing, delegation, and administration functions to an authorisation decision engine (policy-as-code) that checks the acting subject’s level against the target subject/object/system-component level for each type of discretionary action.
3. **Enforce at every point where discretionary actions can start**: Add enforcement to every path that can perform the discretionary actions, including:
   - User interface (UI) and application programming interface (API) endpoints for “share”, “delegate”, “grant access/privileges”, “change labels/access control lists (ACLs)/security attributes”, “create/update with security attributes”, and “change access-control rules”.
   - Any indirect transfer methods provided by the application (for example, link creation, export, message attachments, workflow hand-offs) so the decision check happens before the transfer or security setting change is committed.
4. **Block cross-level actions by default**: If `actingSubject.impactLevel != target.impactLevel` OR `actingSubject.classificationLevel != target.classificationLevel`, deny the action and return a consistent error that does not reveal sensitive details.
5. **Require additional checks and explicit authorisation for privilege/security setting/rule changes**: For actions that grant privileges, change security attributes, set security attributes for new/updated items, or change access-control rules, require re-authentication (step-up) where supported by the enterprise identity provider (IdP) and re-run the same-level decision check.
6. **Record evidence for allow/deny decisions**: For every attempt at a discretionary action, log: acting subject identity, target identity/object, action type, the levels evaluated, the decision (allow/deny), and the policy rule identifier used.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application security architect / identity and access management (IAM) engineer**: define the level model, and integrate the decision engine with the application and the identity provider (IdP).
- **Application owner / backend engineers**: implement enforcement at all discretionary workflow entry points and ensure consistent denial behaviour.
- **Security operations / security information and event management (SIEM) engineer**: set up log collection, retention, and alerts for denied cross-level attempts.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every covered discretionary action type, the application denies the action when the acting subject and target are not authorised at the same impact level and classification level.
- For allowed actions, the decision can be traced to a specific policy rule evaluation and includes recorded acting/target levels.
- All discretionary transfer paths (direct and indirect) use the same-level decision check before committing changes or releasing data.
- Audit logs are available for both allowed and denied attempts with enough information to demonstrate compliance during an assessment.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and document the specific subjects and objects that are covered for AC-3(4) enforcement, using the `impactLevel` and `classificationLevel` attributes.
- [ ] Set up a decision point that checks whether the user has the same level of permission for each type of discretionary action, including: sharing, delegating, granting, changing attributes, choosing attributes, and changing access-control rules.
- [ ] Add the “PDP check” to every application endpoint or workflow that can send data or change permissions, labels, or access control lists (ACLs), including indirect transfer methods.
- [ ] Set a “deny by default” rule for any discretionary action when the person or role performing the action and the person or role being targeted do not match exactly.
- [ ] Add additional “step-up” authentication and re-check access rights for actions that change privileges, attributes, or rules, where this is supported by the enterprise identity provider (IdP).
- [ ] Enable audit logging of allow and deny decisions, including the person or system acting, the person or object being affected, the type of action, the levels that were checked, and the identifier of the policy rule used.


---

#### AC-3.5 — Security-relevant Information (Enhancement)

This requirement is about keeping the most sensitive “security settings” locked away from everyone except when the system is intentionally not operating normally. These security settings include permission lists, the rules that decide what network traffic is allowed or blocked, the configuration details for security services, and the information used to manage encryption keys. If unauthorised people could view or change these items during normal day-to-day operation, they could weaken protection, expose private information, or cause security safeguards to fail.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly define which information is security-relevant, identify where it is stored, and ensure access is blocked by default using appropriate access permissions and safeguards. Access must be granted only during secure, non-operational periods such as maintenance, start-up, troubleshooting, or shutdown, and only to authorised roles with a legitimate need.

The organisation must also keep records showing who attempted to access these items, when the attempts occurred, what was targeted, and whether access was allowed or denied. This approach must be included in the organisation’s system security planning and supporting documentation.

##### State-gated access to security-relevant config and key info

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Put in place technical controls so that access to **security-relevant information**—meaning **access control lists (ACLs), firewall/router filtering rules, security service configuration settings, and cryptographic key management information**—is **blocked during normal day-to-day operation** and **only allowed when the system is in a secure “not in service” state** (for example: offline maintenance, start-up, troubleshooting, or shutdown).

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Access Control (AC)-3(5) enhancement requires: **“Prevent access to {{ insert: param, ac-03.05_odp }} except during secure, non-operable system states.”** The guidance explains that security-relevant information includes **access control lists, router/firewall filtering rules, configuration settings for security services, and cryptographic key management information**. It also explains that secure, non-operable states include **periods when systems are not carrying out mission/business processing** (offline maintenance, start-up, troubleshooting, shutdown).

###### HOW (specific approach)
###### 1) Identify the security-relevant information and where it is stored
- Create an official inventory that links each security-relevant item to where it is stored or managed (for example: firewall rule sets, router ACL exports, security service configuration files/settings, secret/key-management entries, and any administrator screens or application programming interfaces (APIs) that expose them).
- Label each item with a **privileged access management (PAM) / authorisation “resource” identifier** and the **system/server** it belongs to.

###### 2) Block access by default during normal operation
- Remove direct read access to these resources from standard privileged user groups.
- Make sure all access goes through an access gateway (for example, PAM), which checks and applies policy before access is granted.

###### 3) Allow access only when the system is in an approved “not in service” state
- Define the approved non-operable states in the access policy (for example: `MAINTENANCE_APPROVED`, `HOST_OFFLINE`, `BOOT_TROUBLESHOOT`, `SHUTDOWN_MODE`).
- Link the access gateway to the system’s operational status “source of truth” (for example: information from the IT service management (ITSM) maintenance ticket plus whether the host is reachable/marked for maintenance, or signals from orchestration/automation). **Access must be released only after the system is confirmed to be in an approved non-operable state**.
- Set the workflow so that if the state is not confirmed, access is denied even if the user is otherwise authorised.

###### 4) Limit access to approved roles and time-limited sessions
- Allow access only to explicitly authorised privileged roles (for example: security engineers/approved operators), using the minimum permissions needed (for example, read-only where possible).
- Use just-in-time, time-limited authorisation, and require the non-operable state to be checked again when the session starts (and, where supported, periodically).

###### 5) Keep audit records
- Ensure the gateway records: user identity, role, resource identifier (which ACL/rule/config/key item), target system, requested action (read/view/export), approval/ticket reference, evidence of the non-operable state, the outcome (allow/deny), and timestamps.
- Send the logs to the organisation’s security information and event management (SIEM) system for retention and review.

###### WHO
- **Security Architect / Identity and Access Management (IAM) / PAM Owner:** defines the inventory of security-relevant information, the resource mapping, and the state-based access rules.
- **Platform/Infrastructure Engineering Lead:** implements the integration with ITSM and host status signals, and ensures normal-operation default blocking is in place.
- **Privileged Access Management (PAM) Administrator:** sets up PAM policies, session controls, and audit logging.
- **Change Manager / Operations Lead:** ensures maintenance tickets and approvals for non-operable states are created and accurately reflect whether the system is processing business/operational work.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each defined security-relevant resource (ACLs, firewall/router filtering rules, security service configuration settings, cryptographic key management information), attempts to access it during normal operation are **denied**.
- Access is **only** granted when the target system is in an approved secure, non-operable state (maintenance/offline/start-up/troubleshooting/shutdown) and the request is linked to an approved workflow.
- Every allowed or denied attempt produces audit records that include the required details (who/what/when/where/decision and evidence of the state).
- Any direct access routes that bypass the gateway are removed or blocked so the control works in practice.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an inventory that lists each security-relevant information item (access control lists (ACLs), firewall and router filtering rules, security service configuration settings, and key management information) and shows where it is stored and managed, including the system host where it resides.
- [ ] Set the default to “deny” so that normal-operation privileged groups cannot directly access resources that contain security-relevant information.
- [ ] Set up “state-gated” authorisation in the access gateway and privileged access management (PAM) so access is granted only after the target system is confirmed to be in an approved secure state that is not operational.
- [ ] Integrate privileged access management (PAM) policy with the information technology service management (ITSM) maintenance workflow, using signals for host status (online, offline, booting, troubleshooting, and shutdown) to provide clear, enforceable proof of the current state.
- [ ] Limit access to only explicitly authorised roles, using the minimum permissions needed (least privilege) and time-limited sessions. At the start of each session, require re-checking that the access conditions are still valid.
- [ ] Turn on and send audit logs for every access attempt (both allowed and denied), including the user, the resource, the target system, the decision made, the date and time, and evidence showing when the system was not able to operate.


---

#### AC-3.7 — Role-based Access Control (Enhancement)

This expectation is about making sure people can only do what their job requires in the systems they use. It does this by linking access to clearly defined job roles, rather than granting permissions to individuals one by one. Without this, the wrong person could view or change sensitive information, or someone could take on a more powerful role without proper approval. This increases the risk of data misuse, mistakes, or unauthorised changes.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must:
- Define which users are covered and which parts of the system are protected.
- Create and document roles such as Information System Security Officer, Information System Owner, identity and access management (IAM) Administrator, Data Steward, System Administrator, and Helpdesk Analyst.
- Record what each role is allowed to do.

Users must be assigned to these roles. Any role change (role assumption) must be allowed only after the person’s approved role assignment is verified using authorised human resources (HR) or contractor records.

The organisation must also:
- Keep role and permission records up to date.
- Ensure the system enforces these rules for all covered users and functions.
- Keep audit records that show role changes and access decisions.
- Include this approach in security and privacy planning where relevant.

##### Enforce RBAC roles and role assumption via approved HR/contractor records

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement an organisation-controlled role-based access control (RBAC) policy that limits access to specific application objects/functions based on a user’s role, and only allows users to take on a role when their role assignment has been verified against approved human resources (HR) or contractor records.

###### WHY (control requirement)
National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 Access Control (AC)-3(7) (Enhancement) requires: “Enforce a role-based access control policy over defined subjects and objects and control access based upon {{ insert: param, ac-3.7_prm_1 }}.” The enhancement guidance also expects RBAC roles and permissions to be defined and passed on to users assigned to those roles, with careful control to prevent excessive access.

This solution directly uses the organisation-defined parameter **ac-3.7_prm_1**: “RBAC roles for Department of Defense (DoD) system users (e.g., information system security officer (ISSO), identity and access management (IAM) administrator, data steward) with authorised role assumption via approved HR/contractor records”.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define RBAC subjects and objects/functions in the application**: create a clear authorisation model that links (a) covered subjects (users allowed to take on roles) to (b) covered objects/functions (e.g., admin consoles, application programming interface (API) endpoints, data areas, workflow actions).
2. **Create the required roles and role-to-permission mapping** in the central authorisation layer (identity provider (IdP)/authorisation service) for the organisation-defined roles: **ISSO, ISO, IAM Administrator, Data Steward, System Administrator, Helpdesk Analyst**.
3. **Link role assumption to approved HR/contractor records**: connect to the HR/contractor system (or an approved HR/contractor export/workflow) as the single source of truth for whether a user is eligible for a role. Add users to a role only when an approved record exists for that user and role.
4. **Make access decisions based on role membership (not one-off permissions)**: configure the application (and any admin interfaces/application programming interfaces (APIs)) so that authorisation is granted only according to the user’s RBAC role(s) and the role-to-permission mapping.
5. **Implement controls for role assumption**: if the application supports “assume role” or higher-privilege (step-up) access, allow the assume action only when the user’s HR/contractor-approved role assignment is present and up to date; otherwise deny and record the attempt.
6. **Create audit evidence**: enable logging for role assignment/provisioning, role assumption events, and access denials; keep logs centrally for audit and link them to the provisioning/approval workflow.
7. **Run periodic reviews**: schedule regular checks that role membership in the authorisation layer matches the current approved HR/contractor records for the covered roles.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **IAM Administrator**: sets up the IdP/authorisation configuration, role-to-permission mappings, and provisioning enforcement.
- **Information System Owner (ISO)**: approves the RBAC object/function scope and role permission definitions.
- **Information System Security Officer (ISSO)**: confirms that role assumption and access enforcement meet security requirements and reviews audit evidence.
- **Data Steward / System Administrator / Helpdesk Analyst (as applicable)**: confirm role permission definitions for data and operational functions.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The application enforces access to defined objects/functions based on RBAC roles (ISSO, ISO, IAM Administrator, Data Steward, System Administrator, Helpdesk Analyst), with no effective one-off, per-user authorisation for covered functions.
- A user cannot assume or be added to a covered role unless an approved HR/contractor record exists for that user and role (role assumption is denied when the record is missing or expired).
- All role assignment/provisioning, role assumption, and access denial events are logged and kept for audit.
- Periodic review confirms that current role membership matches the approved HR/contractor records for the covered roles.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define which people (users) are allowed to take on specific roles, and which system parts and actions those roles can access for this system.
- [ ] Create role-based access control (RBAC) roles for each of the following: information system security officer (ISSO), information security officer (ISO), identity and access management (IAM) administrator, data steward, system administrator, and helpdesk analyst. Then set up a mapping that links each role to the specific permissions (privileges) it is allowed to use for every covered object or function.
- [ ] Use the approved human resources (HR) and contractor records as the official source for whether someone is eligible for a role, and only add them to that role when an approved record exists.
- [ ] Configure the application, administration consoles, and application programming interfaces (APIs) so they only allow requests based on role-based access control (RBAC) role membership and the privileges that are mapped to those roles (deny by default).
- [ ] Set up role-assumption and step-up controls so that “assume role” is allowed only when the user’s approved job role (from Human Resources or contractor approval) is up to date.
- [ ] Enable and centralise audit logging for role provisioning, role assumption, and access denials. Keep the audit logs for the required audit period.
- [ ] Carry out an initial and regular check to confirm that the people assigned to each access role in the authorisation system match the latest approved Human Resources (HR) and contractor records.


---

#### AC-3.8 — Revocation of Access Authorizations (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that when someone’s permissions should change, the system stops them from accessing anything they should no longer be able to reach. Without this, a person or an automated task could keep access for too long after their status changes, or after a file, folder, or record is reclassified. This creates a real risk of unauthorised viewing, changing, or misuse.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must define and document clear rules for removing access whenever either a person or a resource has its security settings changed. If the change is a security-label change, access must be removed immediately. Otherwise, access must be removed at the earlier of: the next time the resource is opened, or the next time the person tries to access it.

The organisation must configure its systems to enforce these timings, make sure the relevant staff understand their responsibilities, and keep records showing that access was removed at the correct time, with enough detail to confirm whether it was immediate or delayed.

##### Immediate revocation on security-label change; event-triggered otherwise

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Update access permissions so they are withdrawn when either a **person (subject)** or **resource (object)** changes its security details, following timing rules defined by the organisation:
- **If the change is a security-label change**: withdraw access **immediately**.
- **Otherwise**: withdraw access at the **earliest** of the **next time the object is opened** or the **next time the subject tries to access** it.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The control requirement **AC-3(8)** enhancement says we must withdraw access permissions that result from changes to the security details of people (subjects) and resources (objects), using the organisation’s defined rules (parameter **ac-03.08_odp = rules**). It also explains that some withdrawals may only take effect at the next “access boundary” unless the system supports immediate withdrawal; if immediate withdrawal is needed, the system must use an alternative approach.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Classify changes to security details**: Use a predictable method (for example, security-detail metadata in the authorisation data model) to mark each update as either a **security-label change** or a **non-label attribute change**.
2. **Immediate withdrawal path (security-label changes)**: If a security label changes, apply enforcement right away by invalidating the authorisation status for the affected people. Concretely, implement one or more of:
   - **Session and token invalidation** through the enterprise identity provider (for example, revoke or rotate refresh tokens, force re-authentication, clear cached entitlements), and/or
   - **Clear the authorisation cache** and perform a **fresh authorisation check** on the next request, with immediate effect.
   The implementation must ensure access is refused without waiting for the next “object-open” or “access attempt” boundary.
3. **Event-triggered withdrawal path (non-label attribute changes)**: For non-label changes, ensure the application enforces authorisation at the correct boundary:
   - At **object-open** (for example, when a file or document is retrieved, when a record view is first created, or when an API “open/read” operation runs), and
   - At **subject access attempt** (for example, when a protected action starts and the object-open boundary does not apply).
   Achieve this by making sure the application performs the authorisation decision (or policy check) at these points, rather than relying on long-lived cached entitlements.
4. **Auditability and evidence**: Keep audit records with timestamps detailed enough to show the difference between **immediate** withdrawal and withdrawal at the **earliest next boundary**:
   - Attribute-change event (subject/object identifiers, attribute name, whether it was label vs non-label, timestamp)
   - Enforcement decision event (allow/deny, reason code showing which timing rule was applied, timestamp)
   - For delayed cases, record the first boundary that triggered the withdrawal (object-open vs subject access attempt) and the related access attempt identifier.
5. **Operational controls**: Document how withdrawal timing works and who is responsible for maintaining the classification logic and enforcement triggers (for example, the identity and access management/authorisation team for policy and identity provider integration; the application owner for boundary enforcement).

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application owner / engineering lead**: implement enforcement at the boundaries (object-open and access-attempt checks) and ensure authorisation is checked at those points.
- **Identity and access management (IAM) / authorisation architect**: implement immediate withdrawal integration with the enterprise identity provider, including the session/token approach and authorisation cache invalidation.
- **Security and compliance**: confirm that audit evidence supports “immediate” versus “earliest boundary” timing, and that the procedures are kept up to date.

###### Acceptance criteria
- When a **security-label** detail changes for a person or resource, subsequent access is refused **immediately** (no waiting for the next object-open/access-attempt boundary). This must be shown in audit logs with the attribute-change timestamp and the timestamp of the first refused enforcement decision.
- When a **non-label** detail changes, access is refused at the **earliest** of the next object-open or next subject access attempt. Audit logs must show which boundary triggered the withdrawal and confirm that no earlier access was allowed.
- Audit records must include: subject/object identifiers, whether the change was label vs non-label, timestamps, and the enforcement result with a reason code linked to the **AC-3(8)** timing rule.
- Automated tests must exist that change attributes and verify the refusal timing without requiring the user to log out for security-label-change scenarios (or using the agreed immediate alternative approach).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and implement a way to classify changes to each subject or object, so every update is tagged as either a security-label change or a non-label change.
- [ ] Work with the organisation’s identity provider (IdP) and session management system to immediately cancel any affected user sessions and access tokens when security labels are changed.
- [ ] Ensure the application checks permissions (authorisation) both when a user tries to open an object and again at the start of any protected operation, for changes to non-label attributes.
- [ ] Clear or skip any stored permission or entitlement checks so that label-change cancellations take effect immediately, without waiting for the next access checkpoint.
- [ ] Set up audit logs for changes to user attributes and for the enforcement decisions that follow. Include the date and time of each event, and record a reason code showing whether the action was taken immediately or at the earliest boundary revocation time.
- [ ] Create automated tests that update security settings and check how long access is blocked in both cases: when a label is changed (immediate) and when it is not changed (at the earliest boundary).


---

#### AC-3.9 — Controlled Release (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure information leaves your organisation only when the outside recipient can be trusted to protect it properly. Without this, sensitive information could be sent to the wrong place, handled in a weaker way than you expect, or accessed by unauthorised people—leading to legal, financial, and reputational harm.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must first decide which external systems or contractor-controlled devices are allowed to receive information. It must then share information only when the recipient provides security and privacy protections that are equivalent and suitable for their level of authorisation and for how they will handle the data.

Before sending, the organisation must check that the recipient is appropriate using:
- contractual commitments,
- periodic independent evidence (for example, inspection or test results), and
- a documented match between the information’s protection needs and the recipient’s safeguards.

The organisation must keep records of the recipient’s safeguards, the checks it carried out, and the supporting agreements and assessment results.

##### Attribute-mapped controlled release gate for external recipients

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a controlled “go/no-go” decision point so the application can only send information outside the organisation to an **authorised external receiving system/contractor-controlled endpoint** when the recipient’s **security and privacy protections** are **equivalent and suitable** for the recipient’s **authorisation status and how they handle data**. The organisation must also be able to **prove** this decision using **contractual assurances**, **results from regular reviews**, and **attribute mapping**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-3(9) (Controlled Release) requires that information is only released outside the system when the organisation can confirm that the **security and privacy characteristics** linked to the exported information are **suitable for the receiving system**. The guidance also requires process and assurance methods when external protections cannot be directly verified, including **regular reviews**, **agreements**, and/or other processes. It must also confirm that protections are sufficient to apply security and privacy rules consistently.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define eligible external recipients**: create and keep an authoritative register of external receiving systems and contractor-controlled endpoints (AC-03.09_odp.01), including the endpoint identifiers the application uses (for example: integration IDs, API base URLs, partner codes).
2. **Define required recipient controls for each recipient**: for each eligible recipient, record the controls they provide (AC-03.09_odp.02), plus the scope and expiry date of the evidence.
3. **Create an attribute mapping model**: link each type of outbound data release (for example: “restricted personal data”, “confidential business data”, “regulated data”) to the security and privacy characteristics required (for example: encryption requirements, access logging, how long data is kept, expectations for breach notifications, and any limits on lawful basis where applicable). This mapping is used to confirm suitability (AC-03.09_odp.03).
4. **Implement a release gate in the outbound process** (policy-as-code or workflow enforcement at the application programming interface (API)/export layer): before any outbound transmission, the gate must:
   - check the **recipient is eligible**;
   - check **contractual assurance** exists for that recipient and data category;
   - check the **regular review evidence** is up to date (for example: inspection/test results, audit reports) and recorded;
   - check that the recipient’s recorded controls are **equivalent and suitable** for the mapped security and privacy characteristics of the specific release request.
5. **Enforce the decision outcomes**:
   - allow release only when all checks pass;
   - otherwise block release and send it to an exception process that requires documented risk acceptance/approval (with evidence recorded).
6. **Produce audit-ready evidence for each release decision**: store the recipient safeguards record, the contractual reference, the periodic assessment evidence reference/expiry, the attribute mapping version used, and the gate decision outcome.

###### WHO (roles responsible)
- **Application owner / Integration lead**: implements the release gate in the outbound data/export pathway.
- **Information security and privacy (governance, risk and compliance (GRC)) lead**: maintains the recipient register, equivalence criteria, contractual assurance records, and regular review evidence.
- **Data governance / data protection officer (DPO) (where personal data is involved)**: owns the attribute mapping model and the required security and privacy characteristics.
- **Security architect**: defines the equivalence/suitability rules and how exceptions are handled.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The application must not send outbound data to an external recipient unless the release gate confirms: (a) recipient eligibility, (b) contractual assurance, (c) current regular review evidence, and (d) equivalence/suitability of the attribute mapping for the specific data release.
- For every allowed release, an auditor can retrieve: the recipient safeguards/controls record, contractual reference, regular review evidence reference (with expiry), attribute mapping version, and the gate decision log.
- For every blocked release, the system records the reason for refusal and routes it to an exception process with required approvals and evidence capture.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and maintain an official list of approved recipients for external receiving systems and contractor-controlled devices.
- [ ] Define the attribute mapping model that converts each type of outgoing data release into the security and privacy attributes that are required.
- [ ] Record the contractual assurance reference and the expiry date for each eligible recipient and each type of data.
- [ ] Set up an outbound release checkpoint in the application, application programming interface (API), and export process to confirm that releases are eligible, supported by contractual commitments, up to date with required periodic reviews, and that the data attributes match correctly.
- [ ] Set up an exception process for releases that are blocked, so they can only proceed with documented approval and recorded evidence.
- [ ] Enable audit logging and keep supporting evidence for every release decision, including the version mapping, references to the evidence, and the results of each approval gate.


---

#### AC-3.10 — Audited Override of Access Control Mechanisms (Enhancement)

This requirement ensures that emergency access changes are allowed only when they are genuinely necessary, and that they are properly authorised and recorded. Without this, staff could bypass the normal access rules during a crisis by mistake or for convenience, which could expose sensitive information or allow the wrong people to access systems. This could seriously harm people or prevent the organisation from carrying out critical work.

The organisation must clearly define the only situations where an override is allowed: where there is a threat to human life, or where it is needed to prevent the loss of mission-critical capability during an active incident or a declared emergency.

Any override must be approved before it happens by both the Information System Security Officer and the designated duty officer. Only they (or people explicitly authorised to act on their behalf) may carry it out.

Every override must be recorded in the system’s audit records with enough detail to show what was changed, who was involved, and when, so it can be reviewed afterwards.

##### Dual-approved break-glass access with immutable audit trail

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up an emergency “audited override” option for automated access control systems. It can only be used when the organisation-defined **conditions** apply (a threat to human life, or to prevent loss of mission-critical capability during an active incident or declared emergency) and only by the organisation-defined **roles** (the **Information System Security Officer (ISSO)** and the **designated duty officer**). Every override must create audit records that can be reviewed later.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST Special Publication 800-53 **AC-3(10)** requires that an automated access control system can be overridden **only under the defined conditions** and **by the defined roles**, and that the override is **audited**. The guidance limits override conditions to specific situations (for example, threat to human life or critical mission capability during an active incident or declared emergency). It also requires that audit events/records are defined in **AU-2** and are generated and kept in line with **AU-12**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
###### 1) Define and enforce who is eligible (conditions)
- Create an internal “Emergency Override Eligibility” policy that covers the two allowed condition categories:
  - **Threat to human life**, or
  - **Prevent loss of mission-critical capability** during an **active incident** or **declared emergency**.
- Require each override request to include:
  - An **incident/emergency reference** (ticket/incident ID) and its **current status** (must be Active/Declared Emergency), and
  - A **justification** that matches one of the two condition categories.
- Add a technical check so the override cannot proceed unless the incident status and justification meet the policy.

###### 2) Require two approvals (roles)
- Create a “break-glass” process in the enterprise identity and access management (IAM) and privileged access system that requires **two separate approvals**:
  - Approval by the **Information System Security Officer (ISSO)**, and
  - Approval by the **designated duty officer**.
- Ensure the process supports separation of duties (two different approvers) and prevents one person from completing the override alone.

###### 3) Limit who can carry out the override
- Configure the system so only the **ISSO/duty officer** (or explicitly approved delegates acting under their control) can perform the final “execute override” step.
- Other users may submit requests, but they cannot execute the override.
