###### 4) Make the override limited in scope and time
- Implement the override as a **limited, time-limited** increase in access (for example, temporary permission to specific resources/actions), not a general bypass.
- Automatically end the override when the time limit is reached, and require re-approval for any extension.

###### 5) Create audit records for every override (cannot be altered)
- For each override, automatically generate audit records that include at least:
  - Who requested, who approved (ISSO and duty officer), and who executed
  - Which access control mechanism was overridden (for example, the policy/rule/permission set identifier)
  - The affected resource(s)/application(s)
  - When it started and when it ended
  - The incident/emergency reference and the condition category
- Send audit events to the central logging and security information and event management (SIEM) pipeline using **append-only/tamper-evident** protections (for example, write-once-read-many (WORM) or other immutable log storage, or equivalent integrity controls).

###### 6) Update documentation and governance
- Update the application/system access control procedures and system security documentation to reference:
  - The defined override conditions and roles
  - The break-glass process steps
  - The required audit details and retention expectations (aligned to AU-2/AU-12)

###### WHO
- **Information System Security Officer (ISSO):** approves emergency override requests and is an authorised executor.
- **Designated duty officer:** approves emergency override requests and is an authorised executor.
- **Identity and access management (IAM)/Privileged Access Administrator (with security engineering support):** builds the workflow, technical checks, scoping/time limits, and audit integration.
- **Security Operations / Security Operations Centre (SOC):** monitors override activity and supports linking to incidents and review.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The override process cannot execute unless:
  - The incident/emergency reference exists and is in **Active** or **Declared Emergency** status, and
  - The justification matches one of the two allowed condition categories, and
  - Both the **ISSO** and **designated duty officer** approve.
- Only the ISSO/duty officer (or explicitly configured delegates acting under their control) can execute the override.
- Every override execution produces an audit record containing: approvals, executor, affected resource/policy identifier, start/end times, and incident reference.
- Override permissions are limited in scope and automatically end without manual extension.
- Audit events are stored in an append-only, tamper-evident way and can be retrieved later for review.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and publish the emergency override eligibility rules for the two permitted situations, including the required evidence of incident status.
- [ ] Set up a “break-glass” process in the enterprise identity and access management (IAM) and privileged access layer. This process must require separate, independent approvals from the information system security officer (ISSO) and the designated duty officer.
- [ ] Set up the system so that only the ISSO (information system security officer) or the duty officer can carry out the override, or only explicitly approved delegates acting under their control.
- [ ] Set up override permissions that are limited to a specific scope and time period, automatically expire, and require re-approval if an extension is needed.
- [ ] Integrate override actions with central logging to create tamper-evident audit records. These records must include the approval details, the person who carried out the override, the affected resources or policy identifiers, the date and time, and the related incident reference.
- [ ] Update the system access control procedures and the system security documentation to reference the override process, the conditions for using it, the roles responsible, and the required audit evidence.


---

#### AC-3.11 — Restrict Access to Specific Information Types (Enhancement)

This expectation is about protecting the most sensitive parts of your systems so only the right people can access them. This specifically includes data stores that contain cryptographic keys, authentication information, and selected system information. If these are not protected, unauthorised staff or attackers could steal the keys that protect data, gain access to accounts by using login-related information, or view sensitive system details. This could lead to fraud, data theft, or a wider compromise of the system.

To meet this requirement, the organisation must:
- define which types of information are protected
- publish an access rule that clearly states only authorised personnel may access those data stores
- create clear written steps for how access is granted, checked, and blocked

The system must be designed and configured to enforce these restrictions through how permissions are set. The approach must be recorded in the system design and security plan. Finally, the organisation must keep audit records as evidence that access attempts are logged and that only authorised access is permitted.

##### Repository access rules for protected information types

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Put in place and enforce access restrictions for data repositories that hold the organisation-defined **information types** (parameter **ac-03.11_odp**). Specifically, these are: **cryptographic keys, authentication information, and selected system information**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-3(11) (enhancement) requires that access is restricted to **data repositories containing {{ insert: param, ac-03.11_odp }}**. The guidance explains the goal is to allow access to specific information types (for example, keys and authentication data), rather than allowing broad access to the repository itself.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define what is protected**: In the data or asset catalogue, create an official list that marks each repository (for example, database schema, table/view, object storage bucket/prefix, secret store, configuration store) as containing one or more protected information types from **ac-03.11_odp**.
2. **Apply permissions at the repository/type level**: Configure the relevant platforms so only authorised identities can access the protected repositories at the point where access is granted (for example, database roles limited to specific schemas/tables/views; object storage rules that allow only specific groups to protected prefixes; secret-management rules that restrict reading to specific secret paths/labels).
3. **Use “deny by default” and least privilege**: Make sure all protected repositories use **deny-by-default** settings (no broad “read everything” permissions) and that only explicitly approved roles/groups can access the protected items.
4. **Enforce using central identity and privileged access controls**: Link to the organisation’s identity provider so access to protected repositories requires the correct group membership and, where needed, extra approval (step-up authentication) for privileged roles.
5. **Keep records and evidence**: Turn on audit logging for attempts to access protected repositories (both allowed and denied) and keep those logs for audit. Export proof of the configuration (for example, policy-as-code or configuration exports) showing the repository/type-level rules.
6. **Operational governance**: Set up an approval process and regular review for the roles/groups authorised for each protected repository/type, including how exceptions are handled and documented.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application/Platform Owner**: owns the repository inventory mapping and ensures protected repositories are correctly labelled.
- **IAM/Identity Team**: implements identity integration, sets up group/role membership, and applies privileged access requirements.
- **Database/Storage Administrators**: implement repository/type-level permissions and configure **deny-by-default**.
- **Information Security (or Security Governance)**: checks the access rules, approves exceptions, and reviews audit evidence.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Every repository that contains cryptographic keys, authentication information, and/or selected system information is **labelled** and mapped to the protected information types from **ac-03.11_odp**.
- For each protected repository/type, access is enforced at the **repository/type level** (for example, schema/table/view, object prefix, secret path) using **deny-by-default**, with **no broad repository access** for non-authorised roles.
- Audit logs show access attempts to protected repositories are recorded with the result (allowed/denied) and are retained for audit.
- Configuration evidence (for example, policy-as-code/configuration exports) exists showing the enforced access rules.
- Access to protected repositories is reviewed on a defined schedule, and any exceptions are approved and documented.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and keep up to date an official inventory that maps each repository to the protected information types defined in **ac-03.11_odp**.
- [ ] Set up repository and type-level permissions using database roles, schema, table, and view permissions, object name prefix rules, and secret path rules, with “deny by default” (everything is blocked unless explicitly allowed).
- [ ] Limit access to protected repositories to identity groups or roles that have been explicitly authorised and are connected to the organisation’s identity provider.
- [ ] Turn on audit logging for every attempt to access protected repositories, and make sure the results (allowed or denied) are recorded and kept.
- [ ] Store proof of configuration (either policy-as-code or platform configuration exports) showing that the access rules are enforced.
- [ ] Set up an access approval process and a periodic review process for authorised roles and groups for each protected repository and type, including how to handle exceptions.


---

#### AC-3.12 — Assert and Enforce Application Access (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure every app only gets the access it genuinely needs, and that it cannot quietly request or use more access after it is installed. Without this, an app that looks harmless could still access sensitive information such as your contacts, location, camera, microphone, keyboard input, network details, phone functions, or other files. This could lead to privacy breaches, spying, or misuse of company information.

To meet this requirement, the organisation must ensure that, at the time of installation, each application clearly states what access it needs for user contacts, global positioning systems, cameras, keyboards, microphones, networks, phones, and other files. The organisation must also be able to enforce these stated permissions so that any unauthorised access is blocked immediately after installation. Finally, any later changes to an installed app’s access must be paused until they are reviewed and approved before the updated access is allowed.

##### Enforce install-time app permission profiles with update approval gates

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up an organisation-controlled process that (1) records each application’s required access to **system applications and functions** at **install/deployment time** (for example: user contacts, GPS/location, camera, keyboard input, microphone, networks, phones, and other files), (2) **allows only the approved declared access** immediately after installation, and (3) requires **approval before any permission/access changes after installation** take effect.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The AC-3(12) enhancement is intended to make sure applications cannot access more than they declare (and what has been approved), with enforcement starting immediately after installation and continuing for any later access changes. This helps prevent unauthorised access to sensitive device/data/functions such as contacts, location, camera, microphone, keyboard input, network details, phone functions, and files.

###### HOW (specific approach)
###### 1) Define an approved permission profile for each application
- Create an authoritative application record (from your enterprise application catalogue and/or managed store information) that lists the application’s **declared permission categories** from the set in the parameter **ac-03.12_odp** (user contacts, GPS, camera, keyboard, microphone, networks, phones, other files).
- For each application version/platform, define an **approved permission profile** (the specific subset of categories that are allowed for that application).

###### 2) Enforce permissions through the endpoint/operating system management layer (not by trusting the app)
- Limit application installation to managed channels (for example, mobile device management (MDM) or enterprise application deployment) so the organisation can apply policy at install time.
- Use operating system/endpoint management controls so that **any category not in the approved profile is blocked**.
- Apply enforcement during deployment so there is **no “first run” bypass** (meaning the app cannot gain access to blocked categories after installation).

###### 3) Add a gate to stop permission changes after updates
- For every application update, automatically compare the newly declared permission categories with the previously approved permission profile.
- If there is any increase or new category (permission drift), stop the update from being deployed/allowed to run with the expanded access until an approval process is completed.

###### 4) Require approval for any permission/access changes after installation
- Put in place a workflow where any requested change to the application’s permission categories (including changes caused by updates) must be reviewed and approved by the application owner/security approver.
- Only after approval should the approved permission profile be updated and the endpoint policy applied again.

###### 5) Produce evidence suitable for audit
- Record and keep: the declared permission categories at install time, the approved profile applied, the enforcement result (granted/blocked per category), and any update/approval decisions.
- Send permission-drift and enforcement-block events to security information and event management (SIEM) for monitoring and alerts.

###### WHO
- **Endpoint/mobile device management (MDM) administrator**: sets up managed deployment and endpoint/operating system enforcement policies.
- **Application owner**: checks that declared permissions are complete and justified for the business.
- **Information security / identity and access management (IAM) governance**: approves permission profile changes and update gates.
- **Security monitoring/security operations centre (SOC)**: monitors permission drift and alerts for enforcement blocks.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each managed application, the organisation can show that at installation time the application’s declared access categories (from the ac-03.12_odp set) were captured and matched to an approved permission profile.
- After installation, the endpoint policy blocks any permission category not included in the approved permission profile (confirmed by test cases showing blocked access attempts).
- If an application update introduces new permission categories, the update is blocked or prevented from taking effect until approval is granted.
- Audit evidence is available: declared permissions, approved permission profile, enforcement actions, and approval records for post-install changes.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an official catalogue entry for each application, version, and platform. Each entry must record the permission categories that are declared in the ac-03.12_odp set.
- [ ] Define and keep an approved set of permissions for each application, listing the categories it is allowed to access.
- [ ] Configure managed deployment (MDM) and the enterprise app store to apply operating system and device permission controls when the app is installed, and block any app categories that are not included in the approved profile.
- [ ] Set up an automated “update gate” that checks for changes in permissions and stops the update from being deployed or used effectively until approval is completed.
- [ ] Set up an approval process for permission and access changes after installation, and make sure policy updates are only made after the approval is granted.
- [ ] Enable audit logging for declared permissions, approved profiles, enforcement results, and approval decisions. Send alerts about permission changes (“permission drift”) and enforcement denials to security information and event management (SIEM).


---

#### AC-3.13 — Attribute-based Access Control (Enhancement)

This expectation is about making sure people can only access the correct systems and files when their job role and access rights genuinely match what they are allowed to access, and only from the right location and during approved times. Without this, someone could view or change sensitive information they are not entitled to, or access it from an unauthorised place or outside approved working hours, increasing the risk of data leaks, fraud, or disruption.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly define which users and which resources are covered. It must create and maintain a set of access rules that check the person’s job function, how confidently their identity has been verified, their security clearance level (Secret or Confidential), their system role, the resource’s classification, and the on-premises location and approved time window. The organisation must then configure its systems so access is granted only when all these details match the rules, and denied when they do not.

The organisation must also document how access is enforced, record access decisions for later review, assign responsibility to the right staff, and keep evidence that the settings are in place and working as intended.

##### Policy-driven ABAC enforcement using IdP attributes and resource metadata

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement attribute-based access control (ABAC) so the application/application programming interface (API) authorises each request using a defined set of **subject attributes** (for example: job function, identity assurance level, clearance, system role), **object attributes** (for example: resource classification), and **environment attributes** (for example: on-premises location and an approved time window). Access must be **granted only when all required attributes match** and **blocked otherwise**, with decision records that can be audited.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-3(13) enhancement requires enforcing an **attribute-based access control policy** for **defined users and resources**, and controlling access based on the organisation-defined parameter **ac-03.13_odp (attributes)**. The guidance also expects ABAC rules based on organisational attributes, action/environment/resource details, and an implementation that is enforceable and auditable.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define ABAC scope (users/resources):** Create an inventory that maps (a) user types/accounts (employees, contractors, service accounts, privileged operators) and (b) protected resources (APIs/endpoints, user interface functions, data entities/records, and administrative actions). For each resource, define the **resource classification** attribute and how it is determined (for example: from an authoritative data classification register).
2. **Set authoritative sources for each attribute:**
   - **Job function** from the human resources (HR) system.
   - **Identity assurance level** from the authentication method used (identity provider (IdP) session/claim).
   - **Clearance** from the clearance process (for example: Secret/Confidential) and ensure it is recorded as a claim/attribute.
   - **System role** from the role mapping used by the application (from role-based access control (RBAC)).
   - **Environment attributes**: determine **on-premises location** using server-side network information (for example: approved outbound internet protocol (IP) ranges / zero trust network access (ZTNA) client posture) and enforce the **time window** on the server side (not on the client side).
3. **Create the ABAC policy in code:** Implement ABAC rules in a policy-as-code format (or a vendor policy language) that checks, for each request/action:
   - subject attributes (job function, assurance level, clearance, system role)
   - object attribute (resource classification)
   - environment attributes (on-premises + time window)
   and produces a clear **allow/deny** decision.
4. **Enforce at a central authorisation point:** Connect the application/API to a central authorisation component (for example: an API gateway/authorisation service) that evaluates ABAC on every attempt to access the defined resources/actions. Make sure enforcement is consistent across all layers (user interface and API) and includes both standard and administrative functions.
5. **Produce audit records showing the decision basis:** For every allow/deny decision, write audit logs to the organisation’s logging system/security information and event management (SIEM) system including: user identifier, resource identifier, action, allow/deny outcome, and the attribute values (or attribute identifiers) used to reach the decision (redacting sensitive values where needed). Keep logs for the required period set by the organisation.
6. **Run the programme and prove compliance:** Store ABAC policy definitions and configuration in version-controlled repositories; use infrastructure/application configuration management to deploy policy changes. Document the enforcement design and responsibilities in the system security documentation so auditors can trace policy to enforcement.

###### WHO
- **Application Owner / identity and access management (IAM) Architect:** defines ABAC scope, attribute model, and policy logic.
- **Security Engineering (Authorisation/Policy team):** implements policy-as-code and the integration for enforcement.
- **Identity and Access Management (IdP) team:** ensures the correct claims/attributes for assurance level and clearance.
- **Platform/DevOps team:** deploys and manages the enforcement component and policy files.
- **Security Operations:** checks that audit logging to SIEM works correctly and supports review and monitoring.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The application enforces ABAC for all defined user/resource combinations and actions, using the organisation-defined **attributes** parameter (job function, identity assurance level, clearance, system role, resource classification, on-premises location/time window).
- Requests are **blocked** when any required attribute condition is not met (including environment/time window).
- Audit logs are produced for **every** access decision (allow/deny) and can be traced back to the ABAC decision basis.
- ABAC policy definitions and enforcement configuration are stored with version control and deployed through controlled change management.
- Evidence is available showing the authoritative source for each attribute and how environment attributes are derived on the server side.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an attribute-based access control (ABAC) scope inventory that maps defined users or accounts to defined resources and actions, including each resource’s classification attribute.
- [ ] Set up the identity provider (IdP) and the connected upstream systems so that the job role, identity assurance level, and security clearance are sent as official, trusted information (claims/attributes) to the point where access decisions are enforced.
- [ ] Set up server-side checks to automatically determine the on-premises location and whether access is within approved time windows (for example, only allow approved egress/zero trust network access posture and enforce the time limits on the server).
- [ ] Create access control rules written as code that check a person’s job function, assurance level, security clearance, system role, resource classification, and environment details, and then produce a clear decision to either allow or deny access.
- [ ] Set up a single central authorisation check point (an application programming interface (API) gateway or authorisation service) so that every request to the defined objects and actions is assessed against the attribute-based access control (ABAC) policy.
- [ ] Enable audit logging for every attribute-based access control (ABAC) decision to allow or deny access. Each log must include the user (subject), the resource (object), the action taken, and the reason for the decision. Send these logs to the security information and event management (SIEM) system and keep them for the required retention period.


---

#### AC-3.14 — Individual Access (Enhancement)

This requirement is about helping people safely see what personal information an organisation holds about them, and why it is used. Without this, someone might not realise their records are inaccurate, their information could be shown to the wrong person, or their information could be used and shared without the required transparency.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must provide:
- a secure online (web-based) way to request access, and  
- a signed-in area where individuals can view their own records held in the organisation’s official systems.

This viewing must include the specific information held, the reasons the organisation processes it, and (where relevant) any history of sharing or transfers.

The organisation must also:
- set clear rules and written procedures for how access decisions are checked and approved,
- involve the correct staff who are responsible for enforcing access, and
- seek legal advice where any limits may apply.

Finally, the organisation must keep the necessary records and documentation of access activity, so that requests and viewing can be traced back to the correct person.

##### Identity-first SAR portal for individual PII access

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Create a web-based subject access request (SAR) portal and a secure, authenticated viewing page. This will let individuals see specific parts of their personally identifiable information (PII) that are held in system-of-record databases. Where relevant, the viewing page must show:
- the PII data fields
- the reasons the data is processed
- the history of disclosures and transfers

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Access Control (AC)-3(14) (enhancement) requires: “Provide {{ insert: param, ac-03.14_odp.01 }} to enable individuals to have access to the following elements of their personally identifiable information: {{ insert: param, ac-03.14_odp.02 }}.” The organisation-defined parameters specify:
- **mechanisms**: “a web-based subject access request portal and authenticated application interface to privacy records”
- **elements**: “the individual’s PII in system-of-record databases, including data fields, processing purposes, and disclosure/transfer history”

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Build and run the SAR portal** as the single way for individuals to submit requests, including a request reference number and status tracking.
2. **Connect to the organisation’s identity system** so individuals authenticate to the portal and viewing page. Enforce access rules on the server for every request. Use stronger (“step-up”) authentication for viewing and export actions when risk indicates.
3. **Create a privacy “view model”** (a standardised structure) that shows only the records that the individual is authorised to access, including:
   - PII **data fields**
   - **processing purposes** in plain language (from privacy metadata)
   - **disclosure/transfer history** where applicable (from disclosure and transfer logs)
4. **Apply access checks when data is retrieved**: the system must look up information from system-of-record sources using least-privilege service accounts and filter strictly by the authenticated individual’s subject identifier. Do not rely on filtering done in the user’s browser or device.
5. **Ensure auditability**: record SAR submission, identity verification outcome, the access decision, record retrieval, and any view/export/download actions. Include timestamps and the authenticated subject/request reference.
6. **Build in access limits**: where some record types are exempt or require higher assurance, use policy-based decision points that require privacy and legal approval before the request is fulfilled (the control guidance notes consultation with privacy/legal counsel).

###### WHO
- **Product/Service Owner (Privacy/Information Governance)**: owns the SAR scope, the data elements included, and any access limitations.
- **Application Security/Engineering Lead**: builds the portal and authenticated viewing interface, implements access enforcement, and sets up audit logging.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Team**: implements authentication and step-up authentication policies.
- **Privacy Officer / Legal Counsel**: defines exemptions and limitations and approves the policy decision points.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A user can submit a SAR through the web portal and then, after successful authentication and authorisation, view their own PII records.
- The viewing interface shows **data fields**, **processing purposes**, and **disclosure/transfer history** (where applicable) for the authenticated individual.
- The system returns no records when identities do not match (confirmed through negative tests).
- All SAR and viewing/export actions produce audit records that can be linked to the request reference and the authenticated identity.
- Access limitations/exemptions are enforced through documented, policy-driven decision points, with privacy/legal approval where required.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the exact personal data (PII) items that will be shown in the SAR view model, including the data fields, the reasons for using them, and any disclosure or transfer history where this applies.
- [ ] Set up the web-based SAR (subject access request) portal process—submission, identity checks, approval decision, fulfilment, and completion—using a unique request reference.
- [ ] Connect the portal and viewing interface to the organisation’s identity provider (IdP) and require users to be signed in before they can view or export anything.
- [ ] Set up server-side authorisation and data retrieval so that system-of-record queries return only data linked to the authenticated user’s identifier.
- [ ] Create a privacy view that puts records into a consistent format, so information can be shown clearly, including data fields, the purposes for using the data, and the history of data transfers or disclosures.
- [ ] Enable audit logs for suspicious activity report (SAR) submissions, identity checks, decisions about whether someone is allowed to access information, record lookups, and any actions to view, export, or download records.
- [ ] Document and apply access limits or exemptions based on policy, with privacy and legal approval checkpoints.


---

#### AC-3.15 — Discretionary and Mandatory Access Control (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that every person and every system resource can only be accessed in the correct way, for the correct reasons, every time. It helps prevent serious security incidents where someone with a lower clearance level, or a user account that has been misused, could read or change more sensitive information than they are allowed to. It also helps prevent one compromised account from being used to spread access across the whole system.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must apply two layers of access checks to the specific in-scope users and resources it has identified. First, it must use fixed security levels (such as Confidential and Secret) so that a user can only access information at their clearance level or lower. Second, it must apply permission rules based on job roles and on file, service, and network permissions, following the principle of least privilege.

The organisation must make sure these rules are applied consistently, and that all access decisions are recorded so they can be reviewed.

##### Enforce MAC + DAC together via PDP/PEP with labelled data and RBAC/ACL

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement **simultaneous mandatory access control (MAC)** and **discretionary access control (DAC)** for all in-scope subjects and objects in the application/data access path.
- **MAC**: apply security labels to objects and clearance levels to users/services.
- **DAC**: apply least-privilege permissions for the requested action using **role-based access control (RBAC)** (roles/groups) and **access control lists (ACLs)**.
- **Combined enforcement**: ensure the final access decision reflects both rules (for example, **MAC denial overrides DAC approval**; **DAC denial overrides MAC approval**).

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-3(15) enhancement guidance states that **simultaneously implementing a mandatory access control policy and a discretionary access control policy** provides extra protection against unauthorised execution of code by users or processes acting on behalf of users. This helps prevent a single compromised user/process from compromising the entire system.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the MAC model**: create the authoritative set of **security labels** for objects (for example, Confidential/Secret) and **clearance levels** for subjects, including clear mapping rules for how identity attributes (for example, a clearance claim) are derived.
2. **Define the DAC model**: create least-privilege **RBAC roles/groups** and action-specific **ACLs** for objects/endpoints (files, services, application programming interface (API) operations, message topics/queues, and similar). Keep roles minimal and avoid broad “admin” permissions in normal workflows.
3. **Label objects and link subject attributes**:
   - Label every in-scope data object at creation/update time with its classification label.
   - Ensure every in-scope subject (user and service account/workload identity) has a clearance attribute used for MAC checks.
4. **Use a central authorisation decision pattern** at the mediation point(s) where access decisions are made (for example, API gateway, authorisation service, service mesh policy enforcement, or application authorisation middleware).
   - The **policy decision point (PDP)** checks **MAC first** (clearance vs object label), then checks **DAC** (RBAC + ACL) for the requested action.
   - The **policy enforcement point (PEP)** enforces the PDP decision and blocks the request when either policy denies.
5. **Make the combined decision rules explicit** in policy logic and tests (for example, “**deny if MAC denies OR DAC denies**”). Remove any ways to bypass checks (for example, direct database access, unauthorised internal service calls, or code paths that skip authorisation).
6. **Create auditable records for every access attempt**: record subject identity, subject clearance, object label, requested action, MAC result, DAC result, final decision, and policy version.
7. **Use change control**: store MAC+DAC policies as code in a version-controlled repository, require peer review, and deploy through controlled pipelines so policy changes are traceable.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Security Architect / IAM Architect**: define MAC/DAC models and combined decision rules.
- **Platform/Integration Engineer**: implement PDP/PEP enforcement and integrate with identity and data platforms.
- **Security Operations / Compliance**: verify audit evidence, retention, and review processes.
- **Change Manager / Engineering Lead**: ensure policy-as-code changes follow the organisation’s change management process.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For all in-scope access requests, the system produces an auditable record showing **MAC evaluation** (clearance vs object label) and **DAC evaluation** (RBAC/ACL), and the **final allow/deny** decision.
- Requests are **blocked** when **either** MAC or DAC denies, with **no bypass paths** for in-scope objects/endpoints.
- All in-scope objects are labelled, and all in-scope subjects have clearance attributes used by the PDP.
- Policy changes are traceable (policy version recorded in logs; controlled deployment with review).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the mandatory access control (MAC) label set and the subject clearance levels, including how identity attributes are mapped to clearance levels.
- [ ] Define role-based access control (RBAC) roles and groups, and set action-specific access control lists (ACLs) for each in-scope object type and endpoint.
- [ ] Set up object classification so that every in-scope object is created or updated with a security label.
- [ ] Set up a single, central authorisation process that checks both mandatory access control (MAC) and discretionary access control (DAC), and applies “deny takes priority” rules.
- [ ] Remove or disable any ways to bypass authorisation, such as direct access to data or internal calls that do not carry the user’s identity.
- [ ] Enable structured audit logs for every access attempt, including the mandatory access control (MAC) result, the discretionary access control (DAC) result, the final access decision, and the policy version.
- [ ] Store mandatory access control (MAC) and discretionary access control (DAC) rules as code in version control, and release them through a reviewed, controlled deployment process.


---

### AC-4 — Information Flow Enforcement (Control)

This requirement is about making sure information only goes to the places it is meant to go, both within your systems and between connected systems, following the organisation’s approved rules. Without this, sensitive or restricted information could be sent to the wrong place, appear to come from inside the organisation when it does not, or be leaked to the public internet in an unprotected form, including export-controlled data. It could also break the rules of another business area or partner system, or allow unauthorised changes to how information is handled.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must enforce its approved cross-domain information-flow rules. This includes checking that the sender is allowed to send (write), routing information based on its assigned handling labels, and applying special handling for export-controlled material. The organisation must apply these rules to both normal information and system “control” traffic, such as routing and name lookups. It must block traffic that tries to pretend it is internal, require web requests to go through the organisation’s internal web proxy, limit transfers between organisations to agreed structures and content, and only allow such transfers when an agreement specifies that these controls are enforced.

The organisation must also use trusted enforcement mechanisms at boundary points, verify permissions before accepting incoming information, support one-way flows and any required label changes, and keep audit records showing what was allowed or blocked.

##### Label-aware flow enforcement at cross-domain boundaries

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Put in place technical controls that allow or block the movement of information **inside the system and between connected systems** only in line with the organisation’s **approved information flow control policies** (ac-04_odp). These controls must apply to both **data traffic** and **control traffic** (for example, DNS and services closely related to routing). They must include **checks that writing is allowed**, **routing based on information labels**, **handling for export-controlled information**, and **trusted regrading/label changes** only at designated points where the policy is enforced.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-4 requires the organisation to **enforce approved permissions for controlling how information flows** inside the system and between connected systems, based on **information flow control policies** (param: ac-04_odp). The guidance also requires enforcement at boundary points, including:
- blocking traffic that incorrectly claims it is internal,
- preventing export-controlled information from being sent to the Internet in an unprotected form,
- restricting web requests so they can only go through the internal web proxy,
- limiting transfers between organisations based on agreed structures and content,
- checking **write permissions** before accepting information coming from another security or privacy domain,
- applying flow controls to **control traffic**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create an authoritative flow policy model (policy-as-code)** that covers: which source can send to which destination, which protocols and ports are allowed, which information-handling labels are required, what actions must be taken (permit/deny/route to proxy/route to guard), and clear rules for export-controlled handling and “web-proxy-only” exit. Keep it under version control with formal change control.
2. **Place enforcement points at every cross-domain boundary** (for example, network boundary protection devices, application programming interface (API) gateways, service mesh entry/exit points, zero trust network access (ZTNA) access brokers, and DNS enforcement resolvers) so that **all** traffic crossing domains must pass through a policy decision point.
3. **Enforce label-aware routing and write-permission checks**:
   - For incoming traffic from another domain/system: confirm the sender is allowed to **write** to the destination, and confirm the session’s handling labels are permitted for that destination.
   - Allow, route, or deny based on labels (for example, higher-sensitivity labels are sent to stronger inspection and exit controls; disallowed label/destination combinations are denied).
4. **Implement export-controlled and web-proxy restrictions**:
   - Deny or tightly restrict exit for sessions carrying export-controlled labels so that sending to the Internet in an unprotected form is blocked by default; only allow approved encrypted paths and approved destinations.
   - Block direct outbound HTTP and HTTPS traffic except to the **internal web proxy**, and ensure the proxy routing rules follow the same label model.
5. **Apply the same flow enforcement to control traffic**:
   - Restrict DNS to approved resolvers and enforce the allowed domain-to-resolver paths.
   - Ensure routing-adjacent and management-adjacent services (where applicable in the environment) are included in the same policy model and cannot bypass enforcement.
6. **Allow trusted regrading/label reassignment only at designated enforcement points**:
   - Where regrading is needed (for example, after sanitisation or a verified transformation), perform it only at the boundary enforcement point using a trusted method, and record the label decision before and after for audit purposes.
7. **Produce audit evidence**: record the results of policy decisions (permit/deny/route), the matched rule identifiers, source and destination, labels, and the results of write-permission checks for both data traffic and control traffic; send logs to the security information and event management (SIEM) system.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Security Architecture Lead**: owns the flow policy model and the boundary enforcement design.
- **Network and Security Engineering**: implements boundary enforcement (firewall, proxy, API gateway, DNS enforcement) and ensures traffic cannot bypass the controls.
- **Application and Platform Engineering**: integrates label propagation (where applicable) and ensures application endpoints use the enforced paths.
- **Information Owner or Steward**: approves the ac-04_odp information flow control policies and the label handling rules.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every defined cross-domain boundary, traffic is enforced through the designated policy enforcement points; attempts to bypass enforcement are blocked.
- For incoming inter-domain traffic, write-permission checks are completed before information is accepted; unauthorised writes are denied.
- Label-based routing decisions are applied consistently for both data traffic and control traffic (including DNS and routing-adjacent services as applicable).
- Export-controlled sessions cannot exit to the Internet in an unprotected form; web traffic is restricted to the internal web proxy.
- Trusted regrading/label reassignment (where required) happens only at designated enforcement points and is auditable.
- SIEM evidence exists showing rule matches and permit/deny outcomes for representative test cases (including blocked spoofed-internal traffic and blocked disallowed inter-domain transfers).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and get approval for the “policy-as-code” information flow model (where data comes from and goes to, which communication methods are used, how items are labelled, and the actions to take: allow, block, or route), aligned to **ac-04_odp**.
- [ ] Identify all points where systems cross between different domains, and ensure that all traffic between those domains—including Domain Name System (DNS) and control-plane traffic—passes through the designated enforcement points.
- [ ] Set up boundary enforcement to check write permissions and use label-based routing for incoming and outgoing network traffic.
- [ ] Apply export-controlled handling when data leaves the system: block unencrypted transmission to the public internet by default, and allow only pre-approved encrypted routes and destinations.
- [ ] Limit web traffic so that outbound web connections using Hypertext Transfer Protocol Secure (HTTPS) or Hypertext Transfer Protocol (HTTP) are only allowed through the internal web proxy, and ensure the proxy rules apply based on the label model.
- [ ] Only carry out trusted regrading or label reassignment at the agreed enforcement points, and record the label decision before and after the change.
- [ ] Set up security information and event management (SIEM) logging to record the results of policy decisions, including the rule ID, labels, source and destination, and whether access was permitted or denied. Confirm it works correctly by running test cases.


---

#### AC-4.1 — Object Security and Privacy Attributes (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure sensitive information is only sent to the right places, and that personal data is shared only under the rules that apply. Without it, confidential information could be sent by mistake or on purpose to the wrong team, system, or country, and personal information could be combined or released in ways that break privacy obligations. This could lead to financial loss, legal risk, and harm to people.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must define the labels it will use for both sensitivity and privacy. This includes whether data is **Secret** or **Top Secret**, whether it is limited to certain countries, and what type of personal data it contains, how it is used, and how sharing is restricted. It must then ensure that every piece of information, every sending device or service, and every receiving device or service is marked with these labels. The organisation must keep an up-to-date record of the labels and where they apply, and apply a clear rule: transfers are allowed only when both the sensitivity and privacy sharing rules permit them. If not, the transfer must be blocked and an alert raised. The organisation must also record the decision in audit records and document the approach in its system security and privacy plans.

##### Enforce security+privacy attributes for allow/deny flow decisions

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up attribute-based controls for how information moves through the organisation. Use **organisation-defined security attributes** and **organisation-defined privacy attributes** attached to **information objects**, **source objects**, and **destination objects** to make an **allow/deny** decision for every attempted information flow.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(1)** enhancement requires: **“Use {{ac-4.1_prm_1}} associated with {{ac-4.1_prm_2}} to enforce {{ac-04.01_odp.09}} as a basis for flow control decisions.”**
This means the control must compare security and privacy attributes and **deny and alert** when a flow is not explicitly permitted by the organisation’s information flow control policies.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
###### 1) Define the attribute model and decision rule (authoritative inputs)
- Use the organisation-defined **security attributes** (for example: `classification: Secret|Top Secret|Unclassified`, `releasability: ITAR/EAR/US-only`, `system: owning enclave`).
- Use the organisation-defined **privacy attributes** (for example: `PII type: none|direct|indirect`, `processing: HIPAA covered|GDPR personal data`, `sharing: restricted`).
- Ensure the decision rule matches the organisation’s policy basis: **allow only when both** (a) the security classification/releasability permits the flow **and** (b) the privacy sharing rules permit the flow; otherwise **deny and alert**.

###### 2) Ensure objects are tagged with the required attributes
- Tag **information objects** (dataset/record/message/file) with both security and privacy attributes.
- Tag **source objects** (host/sensor/controller/application) with both security and privacy attributes.
- Tag **destination objects** (host/service/data store/OT historian) with both security and privacy attributes.
- Where possible, use label propagation (for example: when a dataset is loaded into a platform, the platform attaches the dataset’s security/privacy attributes to any derived records/messages).

###### 3) Implement an attribute-aware Policy Decision Point (PDP)
- Create a PDP that, for each attempted flow, checks the attributes of:
  - source object + destination object
  - information object (or its associated label/metadata)
- The PDP must apply the organisation-defined policy basis (**ac-04.01_odp.09**) to return an **allow/deny** decision.

###### 4) Enforce the PDP decision at the flow enforcement point
- Connect the PDP to the system’s flow enforcement mechanism (for example: a policy-driven firewall/secure gateway/zero trust network access (ZTNA)/segmentation control, or an application/API gateway that controls data movement).
- Configure enforcement so that:
  - **Allow** happens only when **both** the security and privacy checks pass.
  - **Deny** happens when either check fails or when the flow is not explicitly permitted.

###### 5) Deny-and-alert with audit evidence
- For denied flows, create security events that include at minimum:
  - source object label(s)
  - destination object label(s)
  - information object label(s)
  - which part of the policy basis caused the denial (security versus privacy)
- Send events to the security information and event management (SIEM) system and keep them according to existing logging and retention requirements.

###### 6) Operationalise and test
- Add automated tests (policy unit tests) to check representative flows, including:
  - Secret → Secret allowed; Top Secret → Secret denied
  - A restricted personal data (PII) dataset denied to a restricted destination
- Provide a runbook for handling label mismatches (for example: if labels are missing or unknown, default to deny in line with the policy).

###### WHO
- **Security architect / identity and access management (IAM) and data governance lead**: defines the attribute model and maps it to the policy basis.
- **Platform/network security engineer** (or **application security engineer** if enforcement is done at the gateway): implements PDP integration and flow enforcement.
- **Data steward / privacy officer**: confirms the privacy attribute definitions and sharing rules.
- **Security operations centre (SOC) / SIEM engineer**: ensures denied-flow alerts and audit event mapping are correct.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For at least the defined test cases, the system produces **allow** only when **both** security and privacy attributes permit the flow; otherwise it **denies**.
- Denied flows generate audit/alert events that include the relevant source/destination/information attributes and the denial reason category (security versus privacy).
- All required object types (information/source/destination) can carry the organisation-defined attributes, and enforcement uses those attributes from the authoritative sources.
- Changes to policies (attribute or rule updates) are deployed in a controlled way and verified using automated tests.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Set the official security and privacy data rules (schemas) and the rule for deciding access: access is allowed only when both security allows it **and** privacy sharing is permitted.
- [ ] Add labels (tags) to information objects, source objects, and destination objects so each one includes the required security and privacy information.
- [ ] Set up or configure a **policy decision point (PDP)** that checks the source, destination, and information details against the organisation’s agreed rules for controlling how information flows.
- [ ] Connect the policy decision point (PDP) to the system’s flow enforcement point, so that the enforcement point uses the PDP’s allow or deny decision for every attempted data flow.
- [ ] Set up denied-flow handling so that blocked traffic is stopped and alerts and audit records are generated, including the relevant attribute values and the category of the reason for the denial.
- [ ] Create automated tests covering representative allowed and blocked scenarios (for example, “Secret to Secret” allowed, “Top Secret to Secret” blocked, and restricted personal data sharing blocked) and run them automatically in continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD).


---

#### AC-4.2 — Processing Domains (Enhancement)

This requirement is about keeping information in the right “separate areas” within your systems, and only allowing it to move where the rules permit. Without this, sensitive information could be copied, leaked, or used in the wrong place, or one part of a system could quietly pass information to another part that should not receive it. This could lead to fraud, privacy breaches, or regulatory issues.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must set up protected, controlled processing areas and define clear rules for which types of information are allowed to move between those areas, and to or from specific data items. This includes defining which areas may communicate with each other and which processes may switch from one area to another. The system must then make allow-or-block decisions based on these rules, with processes placed in the correct areas and information clearly labelled by type.

The organisation must document how this works, configure the system to enforce it, keep evidence through audit records, and assign responsibility to the relevant administrators and security staff.

##### Protected domain + type enforcement for flow control decisions

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up protected processing areas and enforce **domain and type enforcement** so that runtime “allow or block” decisions are based on the organisation’s **information flow control policies** (ac-04.02_odp).

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-4(2) requires that the system uses **protected processing domains** to enforce **{{ insert: param, ac-04.02_odp }}** as the basis for **flow control decisions**. This is done by controlling how different processing areas interact, and by allowing or blocking information movement based on **domain plus information type**—including permitted **signalling** and **process transitions**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define protected processing domains** in the application/service layer (for example: `HR-Apps`, `Finance-Apps`, `Public-API`, `Partner-Integration`, `Admin-Functions`) and document which interactions are allowed between them.
2. **Define information types** that match the organisation’s data classification and the policy set (for example: `PII`, `Confidential-Finance`, `Operational-Metrics`). Make sure every information item is assigned a type when it is created or ingested.
3. **Implement domain and type enforcement** using a central policy decision mechanism (policy-as-code) that checks at runtime:
   - source domain (where the process/data comes from)
   - destination domain/object (where the data is going)
   - information type (the label on the data/object)
   - action (read/write/transfer, inter-service call, event publish, workflow transition)
   and returns **allow/deny**.
4. **Assign processes to domains** by linking each workload/process identity to a domain context (for example: Kubernetes namespace/workload identity, service account-to-domain mapping, or platform workload claims). Ensure the enforcement point can reliably identify the calling process’s domain.
5. **Enforce allowed signalling and process transitions** by sending inter-domain events/callbacks and workflow/job handoffs through the same policy decision mechanism; block any transitions/signalling that are not explicitly permitted by the domain/type policy.
6. **Create audit evidence for every decision** (allowed or denied), including: time, source/destination domains, information type, action, policy rule identifier, and correlation IDs. Send this to the enterprise security information and event management (SIEM)/logging platform.
7. **Run policy governance in practice**: keep policies in version control, require peer review, run automated policy tests (allow/deny test cases), and ensure changes follow the organisation’s change management process.

###### WHO
- **Application Security Architect / Security Engineering Lead**: defines the domain structure, information types, and enforcement design.
- **Platform/Cloud Engineer**: implements workload-to-domain linking and the enforcement integration.
- **Application Owner / Engineering Manager**: ensures inter-service/event/workflow interactions are routed through the enforcement point.
- **Security Operations / SIEM Analyst**: checks that audit events are ingested correctly and that alerts are raised for denied flows.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The system enforces flow control decisions using **protected processing domains** and **domain plus information type** evaluation, with decisions traceable to the organisation’s **ac-04.02_odp** policy rules.
- Every inter-domain information flow (including signalling/events and process/workflow transitions) is either **allowed by policy** or **blocked**.
- Processes are consistently mapped to domains, and the enforcement point can determine the source domain for runtime decisions.
- Information objects carry an information-type label, and the label is used in policy evaluation.
- Audit logs exist for allow/deny decisions and are kept and sent to the enterprise monitoring/SIEM tooling.
- Policy changes are version-controlled, reviewed, tested, and deployed through the organisation’s change management process.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a clear classification system for domains and for types of information, aligned to **ac-04.02_odp**, and document which interactions between different domains are allowed.
- [ ] Set up workload and process-to-domain binding so the enforcement point can identify the source domain during runtime.
- [ ] Apply information-type labelling when data objects are created or received, and ensure those labels carry through any processing or transformations.
- [ ] Create a “policy as code” ruleset that allows or blocks network flows using (source domain, destination domain or object, information type, action) as defined in ac-04.02_odp.
- [ ] Route all messages between different domains (events and callbacks) and all process or workflow changes through the policy decision point, and block any actions that are not permitted.
- [ ] Create structured audit records for every decision to allow or deny access (including the rule reference and correlation identifiers) and send them to the security information and event management (SIEM) and logging system.
- [ ] Add automated test cases for representative “allow” and “deny” policy scenarios, and require peer review and a controlled, change-managed deployment process for any policy updates.


---

#### AC-4.3 — Dynamic Information Flow Control (Enhancement)

This requirement is about allowing information to be shared only in specific, time-critical situations, and stopping it immediately if the situation changes. Without this, staff could accidentally or automatically share sensitive information at the wrong time, or while the organisation is under heightened threat. That increases the risk of data leaks, fraud, or disruption to essential services.

The organisation must set clear rules for when “dynamic” information sharing is permitted. This must be based on two live checks:

1. The specific mission-critical conditions must be met.
2. The latest threat and risk assessment must confirm that sharing is allowed.

If either check fails, the system must block dynamic sharing and immediately alert the Security Operations Centre so they can respond.

The organisation must also:
- document how these decisions are made,
- configure the system to enforce the rules,
- keep records of every decision to allow or block sharing, including the reason,
- include how this is implemented in system plans and design documentation.

##### Dynamic info-flow gate with mission and threat permit checks

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a “live information flow gate” that only allows information to move dynamically when **mission-critical conditions are met** and the **latest threat and risk assessment allows it**. If either requirement is not met, the system must **block the information flow** and **alert the security operations centre (SOC) immediately**, including decision details that can be audited.

###### WHY (control requirement)
This requirement (AC-4(3) enhancement) calls for enforcing **AC-04.03_odp (information flow control policies)**. Dynamic information flows are permitted only when mission-critical conditions are met and the threat and risk assessment allows the flow. Otherwise, the flow must be denied and the SOC alerted. The guidance links changes in conditions to **changes in risk tolerance driven by mission or business urgency**, **changes in the threat environment**, and **the detection of potentially harmful or adverse events**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
###### 1) Define the rule for allowing or blocking dynamic flows
- Create a policy that can be enforced automatically and that checks each dynamic flow request against:
  - **Mission-critical condition** (for example: an emergency/incident mode flag, an approved change window, or a mission status token).
  - **Latest threat and risk assessment permission** (for example: the output from the risk engine, or an assessment version/ID that explicitly permits dynamic flows for the relevant scope).
- Use **block-by-default** if either check fails.
- Add clear **reason categories** for SOC triage, based on the two checks (for example: `MISSION_CRITICAL_NOT_MET`, `RISK_NOT_PERMITTED`), plus a third category for adverse events (for example: `ADVERSE_EVENT_DETECTED`).

###### 2) Enforce this rule during live operation (decision and enforcement)
- Set up a central **Policy Decision Point (PDP)** that receives dynamic flow requests and returns **allow** or **block**.
- Set up an enforcement point (**PEP**) at the network or application gateway layer (for example: a zero trust network access (ZTNA) gateway, a micro-segmentation controller, an application programming interface (API) gateway, or a next-generation firewall with dynamic policy hooks) so the decision is applied when a connection or session is created.
- Re-check the decision at session revalidation intervals (or only at connection time if your design cannot support revalidation) so that changes during an active session can still cause the flow to be blocked.

###### 3) Use signals that can change over time
- Provide the PDP with trusted mission status signals (signed events or authenticated calls from incident/change systems).
- Provide the PDP with the **latest** threat and risk assessment result, including an **assessment version/ID** so the PDP can show it used the current assessment.
- Provide the PDP with detection of potentially harmful or adverse events (from security information and event management (SIEM) and security orchestration, automation and response (SOAR) detections or security telemetry). If adverse events are detected, the PDP must block the dynamic flow and record the reason as `ADVERSE_EVENT_DETECTED`.

###### 4) SOC alerts and auditable decision records
- When a dynamic flow is blocked, send an immediate structured alert to the SOC (via SIEM/SOAR integration) containing:
  - source and destination identifiers (where available),
  - dynamic flow identifier (request/session ID),
  - reason category,
  - mission-critical state identifier,
  - threat and risk assessment version/ID used,
  - timestamp and policy version.
- Record every allow/block decision with the inputs and outputs above. Store logs in an append-only/immutable format where possible.

###### 5) Documentation and governance evidence
- Document the policy logic, the input sources, the decision rule, and where enforcement happens in security architecture/design documentation and the system security plan.
- Keep evidence of change control for policy updates and integration changes.

###### WHO
- **Security Architect / Identity and Access Management (IAM) & Network Security Lead**: defines the decision rule, reason categories, and where enforcement is placed.
- **SOC Engineering / SIEM/SOAR Engineer**: implements alert routing, alert format/schema, and adverse-event integration.
- **Platform/Infrastructure Engineer**: implements PDP/PEP integration and live enforcement.
- **Application Owner / Engineering Lead**: ensures dynamic flow requests are routed through the enforcement points (where applicable) and provides the required identifiers.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For any dynamic flow request, the system must return **block-by-default** unless **both** mission-critical conditions are met **and** the **latest** threat and risk assessment explicitly permits the flow.
- If either condition fails (or adverse events are detected), the flow is blocked and the SOC receives an immediate alert within the organisation’s defined alerting time target.
- SOC alerts and audit logs must include: decision (allow/block), reason category, mission-critical state identifier, threat and risk assessment version/ID used, policy version, and timestamps.
- Live enforcement must happen when a connection or session is established (and revalidation must occur where supported) so that changes in mission or threat conditions can lead to blocking.
- Security architecture/design documentation and the system security plan must reflect the implemented decision logic, enforcement points, and integration sources.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the mission-critical condition states and map each one to an official, machine-readable mission state token.
- [ ] Set the “allow or block” rule for dynamic network flows using the latest threat and risk assessment results, and use clear, predefined reason categories.
- [ ] Set up a policy enforcement point (PEP) and policy decision point (PDP) integration so that dynamic traffic requests are checked while they are happening, and are blocked by default if the checks do not pass.
- [ ] Add the latest mission status and threat or risk assessment information into the programme decision process (PDP), and track the assessment version or identification number to show that the “latest” information was used.
- [ ] Set up security operations centre (SOC) alerts for denied dynamic network flows, using a structured message that includes the reason category, relevant identifiers, the policy version, and timestamps.
- [ ] Set up tamper-resistant, append-only audit logs for every allow or deny decision, including the information used to make the decision and the decision result.
- [ ] Update the system security plan and the architecture and design documents to describe where security controls are applied (enforcement points), how decisions are made (decision logic), and what records are used as proof (evidence sources).


---

#### AC-4.4 — Flow Control of Encrypted Information (Enhancement)

This requirement is about preventing secret-looking messages from passing your checks. If encrypted or encoded information could move around your inspection process, harmful or sensitive material might be sent without being identified, classified, or blocked according to your rules, creating real risk to the organisation.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must ensure that encrypted (and encoded) data is still inspected and matched to the correct data-type rules, rather than being able to bypass them. It must achieve this by using approved encrypted-traffic inspection gateways, configured so they only allow traffic to explicitly approved destinations. It must also block any encrypted traffic that would otherwise get around the checks, and end any communication attempts that try to do so.

When blocks or session terminations happen, the organisation must alert the Security Operations Centre, maintain clear procedures and system design records, document the configuration settings, record events for later review, and include this approach in its security plan.

##### TLS inspection gateway to block encrypted bypass and terminate sessions

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Deploy and run approved Transport Layer Security (TLS) inspection gateways at the key network points (“choke points”) where encrypted traffic could otherwise avoid content checks. Configure the gateways to decrypt and inspect TLS traffic, apply the organisation’s content-check security rules (including content type identifiers), handle encoded data that the rules do not recognise, and enforce a default “deny” approach for any encrypted or encoded traffic that does not meet the rules.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 AC-4(4) (enhancement) requires: **“Prevent encrypted information from bypassing {{ insert: param, ac-04.04_odp.01 }} by {{ insert: param, ac-04.04_odp.02 }}.”**
- **{{ ac-04.04_odp.01 }}**: information flow control mechanisms (implemented as content-check security rule filters and content type identifiers)
- **{{ ac-04.04_odp.02 }}**: blocking the flow of the encrypted information and ending communications sessions that try to pass encrypted information
The guidance also states that encryption applies to **encoded data that the filtering rules do not recognise**, so enforcement must treat this traffic as something that can be inspected, not as a way to bypass checks.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Select inspection choke points**: place TLS inspection gateways at exit points for incoming and outgoing internet access, and for any internal-to-external paths where encrypted traffic could avoid inspection (for example, hub-and-spoke internet exits, Zero Trust Network Access (ZTNA)/Secure Access Service Edge (SASE) edges, or controlled access routes to services).
2. **Define what gets inspected**: set which TLS versions and cipher suites, and which destinations/services, are subject to inspection. Make sure the rules explicitly cover “encoded but not recognised” payloads by using cautious handling (for example, treat unknown or unsupported encodings as inspectable, and deny/end the session when they cannot be matched to an allowed content type).
3. **Allow only approved destinations**: configure the gateway so that only explicitly approved destinations (Fully Qualified Domain Name (FQDN)/Internet Protocol (IP)/service categories) can be forwarded after inspection. Everything else must be blocked, or only allowed if the policy explicitly authorises it.
4. **Block and end the session**: if inspection shows that encrypted or encoded content would bypass the content-check rules/content type identifiers, the gateway must **block the traffic** and **end the communications session** (not just record it).
5. **Send alerts and keep audit evidence**: send structured events to the security operations centre (SOC) through the security information and event management (SIEM) system for every block/end action. Include reason codes (policy/rule/content-type mismatch), destination, session details, and the applicable policy identifier. Keep logs to support audits and incident investigations.
6. **Run through change control**: manage gateway inspection rules as version-controlled configuration (policy-as-code where possible), with peer review, testing in non-production environments, and controlled promotion to production.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Network/Security Engineering**: design where the TLS inspection gateways are placed and implement the gateway configuration (rule scope, allow-lists, block-and-end actions).
- **SOC/SIEM Engineering**: implement alerting, event structure, and detections for repeated bypass attempts.
- **Information Security Governance / Change Management**: approve inspection rules and allow-listed destinations; ensure evidence is retained and review happens on a regular schedule.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Encrypted TLS sessions that would otherwise bypass content inspection are **blocked and actively ended** by the gateway.
- The gateway applies **content-check security rule filters and content type identifiers** to decrypted traffic, including cases where payloads are **encoded but not recognised** by the filters (no bypass/allow-by-default behaviour).
- Only **allow-listed destinations** are forwarded after inspection; destinations not on the allow-list are blocked/ended.
- For every enforcement action, the system produces **auditable SIEM events** that include the policy ID, destination, and the reason for the block/end.
- Gateway inspection policies are managed through **version-controlled change control**, with tested promotion to production.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Install approved Transport Layer Security (TLS) inspection gateways at agreed “choke points” in the network for routes that carry encrypted traffic.
- [ ] Set up gateway inspection rules to apply content-check security filters and data type labels to decrypted Transport Layer Security (TLS) traffic.
- [ ] Create explicit approved (allow-list) destinations for the inspection gateway, and block all other destinations by default (default-deny).
- [ ] Put controls in place to block attempts to bypass security using encrypted or encoded data, and end the related communication sessions.
- [ ] Adjust how the system handles encoded data that it cannot recognise, so it cannot get around filters (use a conservative approach: deny and stop the process if the data cannot be mapped).
- [ ] Integrate gateway start/stop events with the security information and event management (SIEM) system and security operations centre (SOC) using clear reason codes and policy identifiers.
- [ ] Manage inspection gateway configuration using version-controlled change control, with testing in non-production environments and approval before changes go live in production.


---

#### AC-4.5 — Embedded Data Types (Enhancement)

This requirement is about preventing files from hiding other files inside them too deeply, or in ways your organisation cannot properly examine. If someone sends a document or message that contains nested content (for example, a file inside a file, or compressed material that contains further hidden items), it can be harder to see what is actually inside. This increases the risk that harmful or unauthorised content passes through checks unnoticed.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must set out and record clear rules that limit embedded content to a maximum of two levels of nesting. It must also reject any embedded content that its inspection tools cannot analyse. Staff must follow procedures that enforce these limits, system settings must be configured so the rules apply to incoming and outgoing content, and the organisation must keep records of enforcement decisions—including when items are blocked because they are too deeply nested or cannot be analysed.

##### Enforce max-2 embedded depth and block uninspectable types

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Put in place a control that checks for embedded data types inside files going in and out, and **limits embedding to a maximum of 2 levels**. The control must also **block embedded data types that are beyond what the inspection tools can handle** (meaning they cannot be reliably unpacked and analysed).

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Access Control (AC)-4(5) enhancement requires enforcing the organisation’s defined limits on how data types can be embedded within other data types. The guidance explains that embedding (for example, a file inside a file, or nested archive files) can weaken effective control of data movement. The limits must take account of **embedding levels** and **what the tools can inspect**. The organisation’s requirements are: **maximum 2 levels**, and **block any embedded data type that the inspection tools cannot handle**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the rules that will be enforced** in a version-controlled configuration (policy-as-code), including:
   - Maximum embedding depth: **2 levels**.
   - A documented **inspection capability and limitations list** showing which container or object types the inspection service can unpack and analyse.
2. **Build and enable a content inspection checkpoint** in the application’s file handling process (for example, upload/download, application programming interface (API) file transfer, message attachment handling, or integration ingestion) that:
   - Repeatedly unpacks and analyses embedded objects.
   - Tracks embedding depth and **stops immediately at level 2**.
   - If level 3 (or deeper) is found, returns a **blocked** decision.
   - If an embedded object type is not on the capability list (or cannot be unpacked and analysed), returns a **blocked** decision with the reason “uninspectable embedded type”.
3. **Apply the enforcement to the real data path** so non-compliant content cannot be stored, forwarded, or released:
   - For inbound: block before saving it and before any downstream processing.
   - For outbound: block before delivery and before it leaves the organisation.
4. **Keep audit evidence for every decision**:
   - Record the rule version, detected embedding depth, embedded type(s), decision (allow/block), and a clear reason (for example, “depth>2” or “uninspectable type”).
