5. **Put operational procedures in place**:
   - Runbooks for updating the capability/limitations list (with change control).
   - A workflow for handling blocked items (for example, notify the sender, quarantine the item, and request re-submission in an allowed format).
6. **Test and validate** using representative examples (including nested archive files and file-in-file cases) to confirm the checkpoint blocks anything beyond depth 2 and blocks uninspectable types.

###### WHO
- **Application Security Lead / Security Engineering**: owns the rule set, the design of the inspection checkpoint, and the audit logging requirements.
- **Platform/Integration Engineers**: implement the checkpoint integration into upload/download and API flows.
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC) / Security Information and Event Management (SIEM) Analyst (with Security Engineering)**: checks that alerts and monitoring work for repeated attempts to bypass the control.
- **Change Manager / Governance**: approves updates to the capability/limitations list and rule versions.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The system **blocks** any content with embedded data types beyond **2 levels**.
- The system **blocks** embedded objects whose types are **not analysable by the inspection tools**, according to the organisation-defined capability/limitations list.
- Every allow/block decision creates an **audit log entry** that includes the rule version, embedding depth, embedded type(s), and reason.
- The enforcement is applied **before storage, forwarding, or release** (no bypass through alternate upload/download routes).
- Tests demonstrate correct behaviour for both (a) depth violations and (b) uninspectable embedded types.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a version-controlled set of automated policy rules that limits embedded content to a maximum depth of 2 levels and blocks embedded types that cannot be inspected.
- [ ] Maintain an organisation-owned list of the embedded container and object types that the inspection tools can unpack and analyse, including any limitations.
- [ ] Add a repeated (recursive) content check at every point where files enter or leave the system, including uploads, application programming interface (API) transfers, attachments, and download of data leaving the system.
- [ ] Set the gate to stop permanently at embedding level 2, and to block access if embedding level 3 or higher is detected.
- [ ] Set up the gate to block embedded objects that have types that cannot be analysed, based on the limitations list.
- [ ] Record an audit log for every decision to allow or block traffic, including the rule version, the depth that was detected, the embedded file types, and the explicit reason for the block.
- [ ] Run test cases using nested archives and files inside files to confirm that blocking works correctly and to produce evidence for audit purposes.


---

#### AC-4.6 — Metadata (Enhancement)

This expectation is about making sure the labels and descriptions attached to each piece of information are trustworthy, move with the information, and are used to decide where that information is allowed to go. Without this, someone could change the labels so sensitive information is shared by mistake, or the information could be sent based on incorrect details—leading to unauthorised access, data leaks, or breaches of compliance requirements.

To meet this, the organisation must set clear rules for which information transfers are allowed or blocked based on specific metadata tags, and document the types of tags used. The system must securely link those tags to the actual information item, so any unauthorised change is detected. It must also check that the tags have not been altered before allowing or blocking sharing.

The tags must be stored in a metadata store that only authorised people and systems can access or update. Finally, the organisation must keep audit records showing who made the decisions, which tags were used, what happened, and must handle any tag integrity failures safely.

##### Cryptographically bound metadata tags for flow decisions

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Put in place metadata-driven information flow control where the metadata tags used to make allow/block decisions are **trusted** and **cryptographically linked** to each piece of data. Only enforce the decision after checking that the metadata has not been altered.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-4(6) (Enhancement) requires: **"Enforce information flow control based on {{ insert: param, ac-04.06_odp }}"**. The guidance also expects organisations to consider:
- the **trustworthiness of metadata**
- protection against **unauthorised changes** to metadata tags
- linking metadata to the data payload using **strong enough linking methods**, with appropriate assurance

###### HOW (specific approach)
###### 1) Define authoritative metadata tags for flow decisions
- Create a tag structure (for example: classification, destination/zone, regulatory handling, tenant, retention category) and clearly state which tags are **authoritative** for flow decisions.
- Record whether tags are **structural** and/or **descriptive** metadata, and which ones control routing/release decisions.

###### 2) Link metadata to the payload using a cryptographic integrity check
- For each data item, create a **metadata envelope** that includes:
  - the authoritative tag values (from the defined structure)
  - a payload hash (or payload digest)
  - envelope version and tag schema identifier
  - an integrity protection value (for example, a digital signature or message authentication code (MAC)) calculated over **both** the payload digest and the tag values
- Make sure the integrity check detects any attempt to swap or tamper with tag values.

###### 3) Verify metadata integrity at every enforcement point
- Build verification into the data path at the points where information flows are authorised (for example: API gateway, secure transfer service, message broker interceptor, and the application layer before data is stored or exported).
- If verification fails, **deny or restrict** the flow and do not fall back to using untrusted metadata.

###### 4) Use an access-controlled metadata store for policy mappings
- Store the authoritative mapping of tag values to allow/block rules in an access-controlled metadata/policy store.
- Limit write access to a small set of authorised services/roles. Use least privilege and separate responsibilities for managing policies/tags.

###### 5) Audit decisions with tag-level traceability
- For each enforcement decision, record:
  - data identifier (or payload hash)
  - the authoritative tag values used
  - destination/target context
  - decision result (allow/deny/restrict)
  - integrity check result (pass/fail) and a category for the reason if it fails
  - the identity of the person/service that initiated the flow
- Send the logs to the enterprise security information and event management (SIEM) system for retention and correlation.

###### WHO (roles responsible)
- **Application Security Architect / Security Engineering Lead**: define the tag structure, envelope design, and where enforcement checks are added.
- **Platform/Cloud Security Engineer or OT/Integration Engineer**: implement envelope creation and verification in the relevant parts of the data path.
- **IAM/Policy Owner (for example, Data Governance Lead)**: approve the authoritative tag definitions and policy mappings.
- **SIEM/Monitoring Engineer**: ensure audit events are correctly formatted, retained, and monitored.

###### Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
- For all flows governed by AC-4(6), enforcement uses **only** metadata tags that have passed cryptographic integrity verification.
- Tests that tamper with tag values during transfer or storage must result in **deny/restrict** outcomes and an audit record showing integrity verification failure.
- The integrity protection covers **both** the payload digest and the tag values (so tags cannot be changed without detection).
- The policy/tag mappings used for decisions are stored in an access-controlled store with restricted write access.
- Audit logs include the tag values, data identifier/payload hash, decision outcome, and integrity verification result for every enforcement decision.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and document the official metadata tag categories used to decide which information flows are allowed or blocked, including whether the tags are structural and/or descriptive.
- [ ] Design and implement a metadata “envelope” format that uses cryptography to protect both the payload digest and the authoritative tag values.
- [ ] Create the envelope at the moment the data is generated or labelled, and make sure the tags come from the organisation’s official metadata store.
- [ ] Carry out envelope verification at every point where enforcement decisions are made, before any decision about routing, exporting, saving, or releasing is taken.
- [ ] Set up the policy-to-tag mapping store with least-privilege access controls and limit write access to only authorised roles and services.
- [ ] Put in place “deny” or “restrict” handling when integrity checks fail, and make sure the system never falls back to metadata that has not been verified.
- [ ] Record an audit log for every enforcement decision, including the data identifier (or payload hash), the tag values, the destination, the result (outcome), and whether integrity checks passed. Then send the log to security information and event management (SIEM).


---

#### AC-4.8 — Security and Privacy Policy Filters (Enhancement)

This requirement is about preventing sensitive or private information from leaving the correct systems without being checked first. It matters because, without these checks, staff could accidentally send the wrong kind of file, send a file that is too large, include hidden or prohibited content, or share personal details that should not be shared—especially when data moves from internal systems to external services or between different on-premises security areas.

To meet this requirement, the organisation must set up approved security and privacy checking rules. These rules must cover:
- allowed file types and file size limits  
- limits on field values for structured data  
- detection of prohibited words and patterns that indicate personal data  

These checks must be the deciding step for whether a transfer is allowed or blocked.

For the specific routes covered, transfers are allowed only when **both** the security and privacy checks pass. This includes checks of the content, the data about the data (metadata), and the file type.

If the checking process fails, or if the data does not meet the rules, the data must be quarantined by blocking the transfer or preventing release as appropriate. The system owner and security operations must be notified within **1 hour**. If the issue is privacy-related, the privacy officer must also be notified within **1 hour**.

##### Inline security+privacy policy filters at egress and cross-domain gateways

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement organisation-defined **security policy filters** and **privacy policy filters** as an **inline decision** at the points where covered data leaves the enclave or moves between security zones. The system must allow transfers **only when both the security and privacy filters pass**; otherwise it must apply the organisation’s approved quarantine or block actions.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-4(8) enhancement requires that **organisation-defined security or privacy policy filters** be used to address **data structure and content** (for example: limits on file type and size, checks on structured field ranges, prohibited words or hidden content, and personal data (PII) detection and minimisation) as the **basis for enforcing information flow control**. The organisation-defined settings also require **time-bound notifications** and **quarantine behaviour** when filter processing fails.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define and version filter sets (policy-as-code)** for the covered data flows:
   - **Security policy filter**: check file type and size, validate structured schema and field ranges, and detect prohibited keywords or patterns (including hidden or prohibited content patterns).
   - **Privacy policy filter**: detect and minimise personal data (PII) (for example: identifiers and patterns for sensitive personal data such as HIPAA/GDPR-related identifiers) and apply privacy actions when checks fail.
2. **Define covered information flows** and ensure they are routed through the inspection enforcement points:
   - Network traffic leaving internal enclaves to cloud-adjacent services or external endpoints.
   - Cross-domain transfers between **Secret** and **Confidential** processing zones.
3. **Apply “both pass” logic** in the gateway/inspection component:
   - For each transfer, calculate the **security filter result** and the **privacy filter result**.
   - Allow the transfer only if **security=pass AND privacy=pass**.
4. **Use fail-closed behaviour for filter processing failures**:
   - If the security filter cannot be evaluated or fails: **quarantine the data, deny the transfer, and notify** the system owner and security operations **within 1 hour**.
   - If the privacy filter cannot be evaluated or fails: **quarantine the data, block release, and notify** the privacy officer and security operations **within 1 hour**.
5. **Create audit evidence for each decision**:
   - Record the flow identifier, filter set version, security result, privacy result, action taken (allow/quarantine/deny/block release), and the timestamps for notification triggers.

###### WHO
- **CISO / Security Policy Owner**: approves the definitions of the security policy filter and privacy policy filter (including DoD mixed-environment policy filters).
- **Security Operations (SOC)**: receives time-bound notifications and carries out incident handling.
- **Privacy Officer**: receives time-bound privacy failure notifications.
- **Application/Platform Owner**: ensures the application/gateway routing enforces that covered flows pass through the inspection points.
- **Platform/Network Engineering**: implements the inline enforcement configuration and fail-closed behaviour.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each covered flow (egress and Secret↔Confidential transfers), transfers are **allowed only when both the security and privacy filters pass**.
- For security filter processing failures: the system **denies the transfer**, **quarantines** the data, and **notifies the system owner and security operations within 1 hour**.
- For privacy filter processing failures: the system **blocks release**, **quarantines** the data, and **notifies the privacy officer and security operations within 1 hour**.
- Audit logs show, for each transfer, the **filter set versions**, the **security/privacy outcomes**, and the **enforcement action** taken.
- All covered flows are clearly routed through the inspection enforcement points (there are no bypass routes for the defined flows).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Map and document the exact information routes for outgoing (egress) traffic and for transfers between Secret and Confidential data, including every network and application path, to prevent any bypass.
- [ ] Create security policy filter rules that have been approved by the Chief Information Security Officer (CISO) to enforce file type and size limits, check that structured fields fall within the required ranges, and block or hide prohibited content patterns.
- [ ] Create privacy policy filter rules that have been approved by the Chief Information Security Officer (CISO) to detect and minimise personal information, including Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act (HIPAA) and General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR) identifiers and patterns for sensitive personal data.
- [ ] Set up the inline inspection and gateway checks to review both filter sets, and allow transfers only when **security** is **pass** and **privacy** is **pass**.
- [ ] Set filter-processing to a “fail-closed” approach when it encounters errors: deny any transfer for security-related failures, and stop release for privacy-related failures. Also, send alerts to the security operations centre (SOC), the system owner, and the privacy officer.
- [ ] Enable audit logging that records the version of each filter set, the security and privacy results, the enforcement actions taken, and the date and time needed to prove the 1-hour notification requirement.


---

#### AC-4.9 — Human Reviews (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that important information is not automatically shared or accessed when the system is not confident. It matters because, without a human check, confidential or highly sensitive data could be sent to the wrong place or people, or moved between different environments, simply because automated rules cannot reliably tell whether the request is allowed. It also matters because specific legal and contractual obligations (such as those relating to export controls, certain compliance frameworks, or health and privacy laws) require extra oversight.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly identify which data transfers and access to confidential or secret datasets involve cross-domain transfers, sharing outside the organisation, or other highly sensitive access. It must document the exact situations that trigger a human review, including when automated decisions are not reliable and when regulations require it. It must route or pause those actions for review, record what was decided and the reason for each case, keep those records for later checks, assign named staff to carry out the reviews, and ensure that the system, its processes, plans, and audit logs show that the review happens whenever required.

##### Human review gate for Confidential/Secret cross-domain and external flows

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a control that **stops or blocks** information-sharing actions and requires a **human check** whenever the action involves the organisation’s defined **information flows** and the organisation’s defined **conditions**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(9)** (Enhancement) requires: **“Enforce the use of human reviews for {{ac-04.09_odp.01}} under the following conditions: {{ac-04.09_odp.02}}.”**
This means the system must not rely only on automatic decisions about whether a flow is allowed when the defined triggers show that automation cannot reliably approve it, or when required by relevant regulations.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the in-scope flows (ac-04.09_odp.01)** in an official policy or configuration document. At minimum, include flows that represent **cross-domain transfers**, **sharing with external parties**, and **access to Confidential or Secret datasets**.
2. **Define the enforcement conditions (ac-04.09_odp.02)** as triggers that the system can check automatically, including:
   - cases where **automated enforcement cannot reliably decide whether access is allowed** for Confidential/Secret data flows (for example: missing or unknown destination, not enough policy information, low-confidence decision, or uncertainty during policy checking), and
   - cases required by **ITAR/EAR/CMMC Level 2 or Level 3 (CMMC-L2/L3)** or **HIPAA/General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR)** (implemented as explicit policy rules that map to your compliance requirements).
3. **Set up a decision and enforcement mechanism (policy decision point plus enforcement point)** in the application’s data path (for example: an application programming interface (API) gateway, service mesh, security access broker for cloud (CASB) / data loss prevention (DLP) integration, or authorisation checks inside the application). When the triggers occur, the system must **send the request to a human approval process** instead of allowing it.
4. **Create a human review process** (for example, a ticket or approval system) that records auditable details for every paused action: flow identifier, source system, destination/external partner, dataset classification (Confidential/Secret), the specific trigger condition(s), requester identity, proposed action, reviewer identity, decision (approve/deny), and reviewer justification.
5. **Make the workflow outcome control the action**: only an approval can allow the flow/access to proceed; denials must stop it. Confirm that the enforcement point is the component that actually permits the transfer/access.
6. **Produce tamper-resistant audit evidence** by sending workflow events and enforcement outcomes to the organisation’s **security information and event management (SIEM)** or log storage. Use correlation identifiers to link: request → trigger → pause/block → reviewer decision → release/deny.

###### WHO
- **Application Owner / Data Protection Officer (DPO)/Privacy Lead**: define and approve the in-scope **information flows** and the regulatory mappings for the **conditions**.
- **Security Engineering / Identity and Access Management (IAM) Team**: implement the decision/enforcement integration and controls for who can review.
- **Compliance/Legal (as needed)**: confirm the ITAR/EAR/CMMC-L2/L3 and HIPAA/GDPR trigger mappings.
- **Named Human Reviewers**: carry out approvals/denials and provide justification.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For any request that matches **ac-04.09_odp.01** and where **ac-04.09_odp.02** triggers, the system **must not complete** the transfer/access until a **human review decision** is recorded.
- Every reviewed event must have complete, searchable audit records showing the required link between the flow, the trigger condition, the reviewer decision, and the enforcement outcome.
- Approvals must be enforced by the actual enforcement point (there must be no “manual-only” bypass route for these triggers).
- Regulatory trigger mappings (ITAR/EAR/CMMC-L2/L3 and HIPAA/GDPR) must be explicitly shown in the policy/trigger configuration and must demonstrably cause human review when applicable.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Catalogue and label the in-scope information flows, including cross-domain transfers, external sharing, and access to Confidential and Secret datasets.
- [ ] Turn the enforcement conditions (automation uncertainty and the mapping to International Traffic in Arms Regulations (ITAR), Export Administration Regulations (EAR), Cybersecurity Maturity Model Certification Level 2 (CMMC Level 2), Cybersecurity Maturity Model Certification Level 3 (CMMC Level 3), or Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act (HIPAA) / General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR)) into policy triggers that can be checked automatically by a system.
- [ ] Add a policy decision step and an enforcement step into the application’s data flow so that requests that match the trigger can be paused or blocked.
- [ ] Set up a human approval process that records the flow ID, the trigger condition, the reviewer’s identity, the decision made, and the reason for that decision.
- [ ] Set up enforcement so the transfer or access can be released only when an approved workflow outcome is reached, with no bypass routes.
- [ ] Export and keep tamper-proof audit records that link each request to the sequence of: pause or block, the reviewer’s decision, and then release or denial, in the security information and event management (SIEM) system.


---

#### AC-4.10 — Enable and Disable Security or Privacy Policy Filters (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that only trusted, highly authorised people can switch key privacy and security checks on or off for specific transfers of sensitive information. Without this control, someone could accidentally or intentionally disable protections such as blocking harmful software or suspicious indicators, restricting which websites can be accessed, or applying rules that stop sensitive data from leaving approved areas. This could also allow personal data to be sent without the required consent or without safeguards for removing sensitive details.  

To meet this requirement, the organisation must clearly define which checks can be switched on or off, limit this ability to privileged administrators, and allow changes only when the transfer has been approved for Confidential or Secret information within the authorised security areas, and when the change is within the system’s permitted scope.  

For privacy consent or redaction, the destination must require privacy controls, and the administrator must have an approved privacy impact assessment. The organisation must also keep records that can be audited showing what was changed, for which data transfer, and maintain clear procedures and plans that explain how these decisions are governed.

##### Privileged, approved enable/disable of security & privacy filters per data flow

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Provide a capability for authorised privileged administrators to turn **organisation-defined security or privacy policy filters** on or off (for example: **data loss prevention (DLP)**, **web content filtering**, **malware and indicator of compromise (IOC) blocking**, **privacy consent and redaction**) **only when the request meets the defined conditions**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
This requirement (NIST AC-4(10) enhancement) means the system must allow privileged administrators to turn the specified filters on or off **only under the organisation-defined conditions**:
- **Security filters**: only for **approved data movements involving Confidential or Secret data** and **authorised security areas**, and **within system authorisation**.
- **Privacy filters**: only when the **destination requires privacy controls for personal data**, and the administrator has an **approved privacy impact assessment**.

###### HOW (specific approach)
###### 1) Define an authoritative list of toggles and where they are allowed
- Create a **toggle catalogue** that lists the organisation-defined filters that can be switched (security and privacy) and the **allowed data-movement contexts** for each filter.
- Maintain an authoritative **security-area matrix** that lists which **source and destination security areas** are allowed, and which data classifications (Confidential/Secret) are permitted for the relevant data movements.

###### 2) Allow only privileged users, using an approval-gated workflow
- Provide an admin user interface and/or application programming interface (API) endpoint that accepts a request containing:
  - data movement identifier (or source/destination plus data type)
  - the filter(s) the administrator wants to enable or disable
  - the target destination
  - references to **system authorisation** (scope), and for privacy filters, the **approved privacy impact assessment ID**
- Before any configuration change is applied, automatically check that the request meets policy rules. The change is allowed only if the checks pass:
  - **Security filters**: allowed only when the data movement is approved in the security-area matrix, the data classification is **Confidential or Secret**, the destination security area is authorised, and the change is **within system authorisation**.
  - **Privacy filters**: allowed only when the destination requires privacy controls for personal data and an **approved privacy impact assessment** is provided and validated.

###### 3) Apply changes only to the specified data movement
- Configure the system so the enabled/disabled setting applies to the **specific data movement** (based on data type, source/destination security areas, and other security/privacy-relevant features), not across the whole system.
- Use least-privilege service accounts to send configuration to the enforcement points (for example: **DLP**, **cloud access security broker (CASB)**, email security, web gateway, and privacy redaction services).

###### 4) Produce audit-ready evidence of enable/disable decisions
- Send tamper-evident audit events to the **security information and event management (SIEM)** system and audit storage, including:
  - admin identity (who)
  - data movement identifier and source/destination security areas (what/where)
  - filter(s) enabled or disabled (which)
  - before and after state (result)
  - the validated system authorisation reference (why it was allowed)
  - for privacy filters: the privacy impact assessment ID and the destination privacy-control requirement reference

###### 5) Put it into practice with documented procedures
- Document the admin workflow, validation rules, and evidence requirements in the system security documentation and privacy procedures so auditors can repeat and verify the decision logic.

###### WHO
- **System Owner / Security Architect**: defines the toggle catalogue, security-area matrix, and validation rules.
- **Privileged Administrator (authorised role)**: submits enable/disable requests and provides the required system authorisation and privacy impact assessment references.
- **Security Engineering / Platform Engineering**: builds the gated workflow, performs policy validation, configures enforcement points, and records audit logs.
- **Privacy Officer / Privacy Governance**: approves the privacy impact assessments used for privacy filter toggling.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A privileged administrator can enable or disable only the **authorised organisation-defined** security/privacy filters.
- Any attempt to toggle security filters outside **approved Confidential/Secret data movements**, outside authorised security areas, or outside **system authorisation** is rejected and logged.
- Any attempt to toggle privacy filters without a **validated approved privacy impact assessment** (and where the destination does not require privacy controls for personal data) is rejected and logged.
- When approved, the change affects only the **specified data movement**, and the system produces audit records that include admin identity, data-movement context, filter(s), before/after state, and approval/privacy impact assessment references.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and keep up to date a list of on/off settings (a “toggle catalogue”) for the organisation’s security and privacy filters, including data loss prevention (DLP), website content filtering, malware and indicator of compromise (IOC) blocking, and privacy consent and redaction.
- [ ] Create an official security “domain matrix” that lists which source and destination domains are allowed to exchange data, which data sensitivity levels are permitted (Confidential or Secret), and which data movements are approved.
- [ ] Set up an admin “enable/disable” process that only allows users with an approved privileged role to make changes. Record the data-flow identifier, the filters that were requested, and a reference to the system authorisation scope.
- [ ] Set up automated checks to confirm that both security and privacy requirements are met. This includes verifying that the data flow is approved in the matrix, the data is classified as Confidential or Secret, the data is sent only to authorised domains, and the system is authorised for the relevant use. It also includes verifying that when personal data is sent to the destination, the destination has the required privacy controls and that an approved privacy impact assessment is in place.
- [ ] Set up enforcement points so the filtering rules apply only to the chosen data flow (not across the whole system), and ensure they are based on the validated request context.
- [ ] Set up tamper-evident audit logs and send them to a security information and event management (SIEM) system for every on/off change, including the administrator’s identity, what changed before and after, where the data came from and how it was used, and the relevant approval and privacy impact assessment (PIA) references.


---

#### AC-4.11 — Configuration of Security or Privacy Policy Filters (Enhancement)

This requirement ensures that only trusted, senior administrators can set up and adjust the rules that scan information for harmful or sensitive content. These rules must be configured separately for each system and each type of data.

Without this control, the organisation could:
- miss dangerous material such as malware, exploit attempts, or other prohibited content, or
- accidentally expose personal or health information by not blocking the right terms, even while still allowing approved exceptions.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must provide a way for privileged administrators to configure security and privacy filter sets using:
- word lists,
- pattern-matching rules, and
- allow/deny lists.

Security filters must cover malware, exploit, and prohibited-content terms in line with the organisation’s Department of Defence (DoD) and Cybersecurity Maturity Model Certification (CMMC) expectations. Privacy filters must block personally identifiable information (PII) and protected health information (PHI) terms in line with the General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR) and the Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act (HIPAA), including clearly defined exceptions.

The organisation must also:
- keep clear configuration documentation,
- maintain an up-to-date list of the configured filters,
- restrict editing to privileged roles, and
- record in audit records who changed what and when.

##### Privileged configuration of versioned security/privacy filter sets

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Create a capability for **authorised privileged administrators** to set up **organisation-approved security and privacy policy filters** (word lists, rule patterns, and allow/deny lists). These filter sets can be **selected and applied separately for each system and each type of data**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The NIST AC-4(11) enhancement requires: “Provide the capability for privileged administrators to configure {{ insert: param, ac-4.11_prm_1 }} to support different security or privacy policies.” Guidance also expects administrators to be able to configure filters such as **inappropriate words** checked by policy controls. The organisation-defined parameters specify:
- **ac-04.11_odp.01**: security policy filters (for example, malware/exploit/prohibited-content keyword and rule-pattern filters aligned to DoD and CMMC)
- **ac-04.11_odp.02**: privacy policy filters (for example, PII/PHI keyword and rule-pattern blocklists aligned to GDPR/HIPAA, with defined exceptions)
- **ac-4.11_prm_1**: configurable security and privacy policy filter sets (word lists, rule patterns, allow/deny lists) per system and data classification

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
###### 1) Create a filter-set catalogue and configuration structure
- Build a central **filter catalogue** (or a module within the application administration area) to store **security** and **privacy** filter sets as structured items.
- Each filter set must be identified by **system identifier** and **data classification**, and must support:
  - word lists
  - rule patterns
  - allow/deny lists (including explicit exceptions)
- Include metadata fields: filter-set name, category (security/privacy), target system(s), data classification(s), version, owner, and effective dates.

###### 2) Provide a controlled configuration process for privileged administrators
- Limit the ability to make changes to **authorised privileged administrators only** (enforced through enterprise identity and access management roles).
- Provide an administration interface and/or application programming interface (API) to create and edit filter sets, but require changes to follow a controlled process:
  - peer review
  - approval by an authorised security/privacy role
  - promotion to an “approved” state

###### 3) Check and test filter accuracy before deployment
- Add automated checks before a filter set can be activated:
  - validate rule-pattern syntax and safety (for example, prevent patterns that could cause excessive processing)
  - enforce limits on size and complexity
  - test against a curated set of test cases (including both expected matches and expected non-matches)
- Ensure the catalogue supports the organisation’s required content types:
  - security filters for malware/exploit/prohibited-content keywords and rule patterns
  - privacy filters for PII/PHI with GDPR/HIPAA-specific terms and defined exceptions

###### 4) Deploy filter sets with traceable, version-controlled change management
- When an approved filter set is activated, create a versioned deployment package (for example, a signed configuration bundle) and apply it to the relevant enforcement points for the selected system and data classification.
- Record whether deployment succeeded or failed, and the exact filter-set version used.

###### 5) Provide audit evidence and keep documentation and inventories
- Maintain an inventory/list of configured **security policy filters** and **privacy policy filters** (including version identifiers and the systems/data classifications they apply to).
- Store documentation for how configurations are set up, including examples of how to structure word lists/rule patterns and how exceptions/allow rules are written.
- Ensure audit records capture at minimum:
  - who changed the filter set (identity)
  - what changed (filter-set version and change summary)
  - when it changed (timestamp)
  - where it was applied (system and data classification)
  - deployment result

###### WHO
- **Privileged administrators**: create and approve filter-set changes.
- **Security/privacy governance approvers** (role-based): review and approve before activation.
- **Platform/DevOps engineers**: build the catalogue, implement validation and deployment automation, and produce audit logging.
- **Application owner**: ensures filter sets are correctly mapped to the right systems and data classifications.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Privileged administrators can create/edit and activate **security** and **privacy** filter sets (word lists/rule patterns/allow-deny) for each **system** and **data classification**.
- Security filter sets include configurable malware/exploit/prohibited-content keyword and rule-pattern rules aligned to DoD/CMMC expectations.
- Privacy filter sets include configurable PII/PHI keyword and rule-pattern blocklists aligned to GDPR/HIPAA expectations, including explicit exceptions.
- All changes are version-controlled, require approval through the privileged workflow, and are deployed with recorded outcomes.
- Audit logs and an inventory of configured filter sets are available, showing who changed what, when, and where it was applied.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define a filter-set structure that supports:

- word lists  
- regular expression (regex) rules  
- allow and deny lists, organised by system and data classification
- [ ] Set up an admin user interface and application programming interface (API) that only allows privileged users to create and edit security and privacy filter sets, using role-based access control (RBAC).
- [ ] Add automated checks to confirm the regular expressions are safe, and run a set of approved match and non-match test cases before putting them into use.
- [ ] Create a deployment method that is version-controlled and digitally signed, and that applies the chosen filter set to the correct enforcement points.
- [ ] Set up audit logs that record: the user’s identity, the filter set version and a summary of what changed, the date and time, the target system and classification, and the outcome of the deployment.
- [ ] Publish and keep an up-to-date inventory (list) of the security and privacy filter sets that have been configured, and store the configuration documentation with examples.


---

#### AC-4.12 — Data Type Identifiers (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that when information is shared between different parts of the organisation, the receiving system can confirm what the data really is. It should do this by checking the data’s identifying markers and then verifying that the content matches an approved format. Without these checks, a file could be made to look legitimate (for example, by using the right name or file type) while actually having the wrong structure or meaning. This could lead to incorrect decisions, fraud, or sensitive information being shared when it should not be.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must keep a list of approved data formats and the matching allowed identifiers (such as file signatures and any required tokens). Systems must use these identifiers rather than relying only on filenames or file counts. The system must then check both the structure and the meaning of the data against the approved specification, allow only compliant transfers, block non-compliant ones, and record the result in audit records so it is clear what was checked and why a transfer was accepted or rejected.

##### Cross-domain data type gateway using signature/token + semantic validation

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a “data type gateway” (also called a policy enforcement point) at every point where data moves between different security domains. This gateway must identify the data type using **approved data type identifiers** (for example, file signatures/magic bytes and/or transfer tokens). The transfer must be allowed **only after** the data has passed both **syntactic validation** (structure) and **semantic validation** (meaning) against the **approved data format specifications**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-4(12) requires that when information is transferred between different security domains, the system uses **{{ insert: param, ac-04.12_odp }} (data type identifiers)** to check data needed to make information-flow decisions. Guidance says identification and validation must be based on **defined specifications linked to each allowed data format**. It also states that **filename and number alone are not used**; the content must be validated **syntactically and semantically**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create an approved data format specification library** for every allowed cross-domain data type (for example, schema/structure rules for syntactic validation and business/content rules for semantic validation). Each specification must clearly state required fields, allowed values, and semantic constraints.
2. **Create and maintain an identifier-to-spec registry** that maps each approved data type identifier (file signature patterns and/or required tokens) to the matching approved data format specification. Where the specification allows it, support **multiple internal file signatures/tokens**.
3. **Implement the gateway enforcement logic** at the boundary (for example, an API gateway, message broker policy, ETL ingestion service, or a wrapper around the file transfer service):
   - Extract and validate **file signature(s)** from the data itself (not the extension/filename) and/or verify **transfer tokens** (including issuer/signature checks where tokens are protected using cryptography).
   - Match the validated identifier(s) to **exactly one** approved data format specification (otherwise fail).
   - Run **syntactic validation** (structure/schema) and **semantic validation** (meaning/content consistency) against the matched specification.
   - **Fail closed**: reject the transfer if identifier validation fails, if mapping fails, or if syntactic/semantic validation fails.
4. **Create audit evidence** for every transfer attempt, recording: source/destination domain, identifier match result, mapped specification version, syntactic pass/fail, semantic pass/fail, and the reason codes linked to each validation step.
5. **Operationalise governance** using versioned, reviewable configuration/policy-as-code for the specification library and identifier registry, integrated into the organisation’s change management and release process.

###### WHO
- **Application/Data Platform Owner**: responsible for defining the allowed data types and maintaining the specification library.
- **Security Engineering / Identity and Access Management (IAM) & Policy Engineering**: builds the gateway enforcement logic and performs token/signature verification.
- **Compliance/Assurance**: checks the audit evidence and confirms enforcement is fail-closed and version-controlled.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every cross-domain transfer, the system identifies the data type using **file signature(s) and/or token-based identifiers** (not filename/extension alone).
- Transfers are **rejected** when identifier validation fails or when syntactic or semantic validation fails.
- The gateway supports **multiple internal signatures/tokens** for a given allowed data format where the specification requires it.
- Audit logs show, for each attempt, the identifier validation outcome, the mapped specification (including version), and the syntactic/semantic validation results.
- The specification library and identifier registry are **versioned and controlled for change** (reviewed and deployed through the normal release process).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and get approval for the list of permitted data formats for each type of cross-domain data, including the rules for checking that the data is correctly structured (syntax) and meaningfully correct (semantics).
- [ ] Set up an approved registry that links file signature patterns and/or token identifiers to the correct specification versions.
- [ ] Set up a data type gateway at every point where data moves between different domains to extract and verify the identifiers inside the data and access tokens (not the file names or extensions).
- [ ] Set up the gateway to check that incoming data matches the mapped specification, both in structure and meaning, and to stop processing (fail closed) if any part does not match.
- [ ] Turn on audit logging for every transfer attempt, including the identifier match result, the mapped specification version, and the reason codes for whether it passed or failed the syntax and meaning checks.
- [ ] Add updates to the specification and identifier registry to the organisation’s change management process, using version control and review before any deployment.


---

#### AC-4.13 — Decomposition into Policy-relevant Subcomponents (Enhancement)

This expectation is about making sure that, before any information is moved from one protected area of your organisation to another, it is first broken down into clear, decision-ready details. These details must cover where the information is coming from, where it is going, how sensitive it is, what type of information it is, what special handling it needs, what attachments it includes, and what security label or certificate it carries.

Without this, the wrong rules may be applied. That could allow sensitive material to be sent to the wrong place, handled incorrectly, or released without the right checks, which can lead to data leaks, regulatory breaches, and reputational damage.

To meet this expectation, your organisation must determine and record the source and destination areas, the classification (Secret or Confidential), the data type, handling requirements, attachments information, and certificate or label information. You must package these as a single set of transfer details and submit them to your organisation’s information-movement decision process before the transfer is allowed to complete.

You must also document your approach, configure the decision process to use these details for filtering, checks, and any possible sanitisation, assign named responsibilities, and keep records showing the decisions made for each transfer.

##### Cross-domain transfer context decomposition via policy decision API

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement a cross-domain transfer process that requires a structured **transfer context** (policy-relevant details) to be submitted **before** the content is released to a different security domain.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Access Control (AC)-4(13) enhancement requires that, **when transferring information between different security domains**, the organisation **breaks the information into policy-relevant details** ({{ insert: param, ac-04.13_odp }}) for **submission to policy enforcement mechanisms**. The policy enforcement mechanisms then use these details for **filtering, checking, and/or cleaning** to control the flow before the transfer is completed.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define enforceable security-domain identifiers** used by the application or gateway (for example, `sourceDomain`, `destinationDomain`) and map them to the organisation’s real security domains (such as network, tenant, recipient groups, or portal targets).
2. **Create a standard “TransferContext” format** that breaks the transfer into the organisation-defined **policy-relevant details** (ac-04.13_odp), at minimum:
   - `sourceDomain`
   - `destinationDomain`
   - `classification` (Secret/Confidential)
   - `dataType`
   - `handlingRequirements`
   - `attachmentsMetadata` (for example, filename, file extension, MIME type, and size/count where available)
   - `certificateOrLabelInfo` (for example, label or certificate identifiers, or references available to the transfer channel)
3. **Add a policy decision point (PDP) / policy engine** that takes `TransferContext` and returns an enforcement decision (for example, `ALLOW`, `BLOCK`, `INSPECT`, `SANITISE_THEN_ALLOW`) plus any required actions (for example, a sanitisation profile or inspection rule set).
4. **Add pre-transfer “gating” in the transfer path** (email/file/portal/application programming interface (API)): the gateway or service must **pause** the transfer until the policy engine decision is received, and must **log** the submitted `TransferContext` and the decision result for audit purposes.
5. **Apply the decision to the transfer**: if the decision requires checking or cleaning, route the content through the matching checking/cleaning workflow before releasing it to the destination domain.
6. **Stop bypass routes** by ensuring all supported cross-domain transfer methods use the same gateway/service (for example, disable or limit direct cross-domain sending paths, require the portal/gateway for external recipients, and block unmanaged transfer routes where feasible).

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Security Architect / Security Engineering Lead**: owns the TransferContext format, policy engine integration design, and enforcement logic.
- **Platform/Integration Engineer**: implements the gateway/service integration and logging.
- **Data Governance / Information Security Officer**: validates the classification and handling mapping rules and the domain definitions.
- **Operations / Security Operations Centre (SOC)**: monitors decision logs and enforcement outcomes.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every cross-domain transfer, the system submits a completed `TransferContext` containing the required details (source/destination domains, Secret/Confidential classification, data type, handling requirements, attachments metadata, and certificate/label information) to the policy enforcement mechanism **before** the transfer is completed.
- The policy enforcement mechanism’s decision is enforced by the gateway (allow/block/inspect/sanitise) and is recorded with audit evidence (submitted context + decision + timestamp + identifiers).
- Cross-domain transfers carried out through supported channels cannot bypass the gating mechanism (documented and technically enforced for the application’s transfer interfaces).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and publish security-domain identifiers that can be enforced, and document how each identifier maps to the real source and destination environments.
- [ ] Create a standard (canonical) **TransferContext** data structure that covers: **source domain**, **destination domain**, **classification**, **data type**, **handling requirements**, **attachment metadata**, and **certificate or label information**.
- [ ] Connect the transfer gateway/service to a policy decision system (policy decision application programming interface (API) or rules engine) that takes the transfer context information (TransferContext) and returns a decision to either allow, block, inspect, or sanitise the transfer.
- [ ] Block the release of the payload until the policy decision has been received and recorded, by enforcing a pre-transfer check (gating).
- [ ] Set up inspection and sanitisation steps so they run when the policy engine asks for them, before the content is released to the destination domain.
- [ ] Enable audit logging of the submitted transfer details (TransferContext) and the result of the enforcement decision for every transfer event.
- [ ] Close or limit any bypass routes so that all supported cross-domain transfer interfaces use the same “break down and decide” process.


---

#### AC-4.14 — Security or Privacy Policy Filter Constraints (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that any information shared between different parts of your organisation uses a strict, pre-approved message format, rather than an open-ended one. If messages are allowed to be flexible or loosely structured, attackers or careless systems could send unexpected data, hidden instructions, or incorrectly formed content that avoids safeguards. This could result in unauthorised actions, privacy breaches, or damaged records.

To meet this requirement, the organisation must:
- Define and document the approved cross-domain message rules.
- Ensure receiving systems only accept messages that exactly match the fixed structure (with no extra or unknown fields).
- Require messages to be written using UTF-8.
- Limit messages to no more than 10KB per message.
- Allow only letters and numbers, with no special characters.

Any message that fails any of these checks must be rejected. The organisation must also:
- Keep a documented list of the checks (filters) used.
- Record whether each message was accepted or rejected in audit logs.
- Include these constraints in both the system security plan and the privacy plan.

##### Enforce enumerated UTF-8/10KB/alpha-numeric filters at cross-domain boundary

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up one single, centrally managed “gatekeeper” at every point where information moves between different security areas (for example, an application programming interface (API) gateway and/or a message broker). This gatekeeper must only allow messages that match **fully listed, fixed-format** security and privacy rules.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **Access Control (AC)-4(14)** enhancement requires that, when information is shared between different security domains, the organisation implements **{{ insert: param, ac-4.14_prm_1 }}**. This means using **fully listed formats** that **limit both the structure and the content** of the data. The guidance specifically requires restricting **data structure and content**, such as fixed schemas/tags/field sets, file or message size, field lengths, character set and encoding, and preventing special characters.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the approved filters** using the organisation-defined parameter **ac-4.4.14_prm_1**:
   - fixed schema (no free-form fields)
   - UTF-8
   - maximum **10KB** payload
   - letters and numbers only (alpha-numeric only)
   - reject special characters
2. **Create two listed schema rules** in a version-controlled policy repository:
   - **Security policy filter**: enforce **XML/JSON schema with fixed tags** (no free-form fields) (align to **ac-04.14_odp.01**).
   - **Privacy policy filter**: enforce **JSON schema with a fixed set of fields** (no free-form fields) (align to **ac-04.14_odp.02**).
3. **Apply these rules at every boundary** for each cross-domain transfer:
   - Check that **UTF-8 decoding** is valid; reject if it is not.
   - Enforce **payload size ≤ 10KB** using the canonical payload bytes; reject if it is not.
   - Check the **message structure** matches exactly; reject unknown or missing fields and reject any free-form fields.
   - Enforce **letters and numbers only** across the character data fields and **block special characters** (define “special characters” precisely in the policy, for example punctuation/symbols/whitespace according to your standard).
4. **Default to rejection**: if any requirement fails (structure, encoding, size, or character rules), the boundary component must **reject** the transfer in a consistent, predictable way.
5. **Audit evidence**: record an accept/reject decision for every message, including:
   - rule set/filter ID and schema version
   - which requirement failed (for example structure mismatch versus UTF-8 versus size versus character rules)
   - date and time, source and destination domain identifiers
   - **do not** include sensitive message content in logs
6. **Update governance documents**: reference the boundary enforcement component and the specific schema/filter versions in the **system security plan** and the **privacy plan**, so auditors can trace the implemented requirements back to AC-4(14).

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Solution owner / application security architect**: defines the listed security and privacy filters and the acceptance criteria.
- **Platform/API gateway or message broker engineer**: implements the boundary enforcement and the logging.
- **Security compliance lead**: ensures the system security plan and privacy plan reference the implemented filter artefacts.
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC) / security information and event management (SIEM) engineer**: ensures audit events are sent to the SIEM with appropriate retention.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every cross-domain transfer path that goes through the boundary component, messages that do not match the **fixed listed schema** are rejected.
- Messages larger than **10KB** are rejected.
- Messages that are not valid **UTF-8** are rejected.
- Messages containing **special characters** (as defined in the policy) or non-alpha-numeric characters in the restricted fields are rejected.
- Audit logs show accept/reject outcomes with the filter ID/schema version and the failure reason, without exposing sensitive message content.
- The system security plan and privacy plan explicitly reference the boundary enforcement mechanism and the filter policy versions.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Identify and route all cross-domain data transfer paths through a single boundary control component (an application programming interface (API) gateway and/or a message broker).
- [ ] Set up the listed security and privacy filter rules using the agreed fixed structure and field set, using UTF-8 encoding, with a maximum size of 10 kilobytes, allowing only letters and numbers, and with no restrictions on special characters.
- [ ] Set up checks that validate incoming data and reject any requests that include unexpected fields or omit required fields. Also prevent free-form input by using fixed data formats for security (XML and JSON with fixed tags) and privacy (JSON with a fixed set of fields).
- [ ] Check incoming request data at the entry point to ensure it is valid UTF-8 text and that its size is 10 kilobytes (KB) or less, using the standard (canonical) form of the payload bytes.
- [ ] Apply character-set rules to all limited input fields so they accept only letters and numbers, and block any special characters that the policy does not allow.
- [ ] Set up consistent “reject” responses and structured audit records for every accept or reject decision. Each record must include the filter ID, the schema version, and the reason for the failure, but must not include any sensitive payload content.
- [ ] Update the system security plan and privacy plan to mention the boundary enforcement component and the exact schema and filter policy versions.


---

#### AC-4.15 — Detection of Unsanctioned Information (Enhancement)

This expectation is about preventing dangerous or private information from being sent from one part of the organisation to another. When information is shared between different security areas, the organisation must check what is being sent for three risks: malicious software, information that is not allowed to leave the original security area, and files that can run and could disrupt or damage the systems in the receiving area. If any of these organisation-defined unauthorised items are found, the transfer must be blocked so the receiving area never receives the item.

This matters because, without these checks, harmful software could be introduced, confidential or restricted information could be exposed to the wrong place, or the receiving systems could be damaged or disrupted by files that can run.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must follow its Cross-Domain Security Transfer Policy and Data Transfer Privacy Policy, using the rules and procedures defined in those documents to detect these risks during cross-area transfers and stop the transfer when they are found.

##### Policy-driven inspection gateway for cross-domain transfer blocking

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a central, policy-led inspection point (gateway/proxy/secure transfer service) that checks the information being sent during **cross-domain transfers** for:
1) **malicious code**,
2) **unauthorised release data** defined by the organisation, and
3) **harmful executable content**.
If any unauthorised information defined by the organisation is found, the transfer is **prohibited** (blocked/quarantined/rejected) in line with the organisation-defined cross-domain security transfer policy and data transfer privacy policy.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-4(15) requires that, when transferring information between different security domains, the organisation **checks for the presence of the organisation-defined “unsanctioned information”** and **prohibits the transfer** in line with the organisation-defined security and privacy policy.
The guidance explains that unsanctioned information includes **malicious code**, **information that is not suitable for release**, and **executable code that could disrupt or harm destination services/systems**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Make all cross-domain transfer routes go through the inspection point**: identify every way the application moves data between domains (for example, web uploads/downloads, application programming interface (API) payloads, file transfer, messaging/software-as-a-service (SaaS) connectors) and configure routing so the payloads cannot bypass inspection.
2. **Turn policy into rules that systems can enforce**: implement policy-as-code that maps the organisation-defined unsanctioned categories to specific detection checks (for example, malware signatures and behavioural detection; content classification and permission checks for unauthorised release data; detection of file types that can run, embedded macros/scripts, and other indicators of executable content).
3. **Check payloads in multiple layers before delivery**:
   - Malware scanning, including extracting archives and scanning recursively, and (where the risk justifies it) running suspicious content in a safe test environment (sandbox).
   - Data and privacy checks using the organisation’s classification labels and release permissions to detect unauthorised release data.
   - Executable-content checks to block payloads that can run or contain scripts and could disrupt or harm destination systems.
4. **Apply “deny by policy” with clear block reasons**: if any rule matches, the inspection point must **prohibit the transfer** (for example, reject the request, quarantine the payload, and return a controlled error) and record the specific policy rule(s) that triggered the block.
5. **Create audit evidence for the policy reasons**: record the transfer identifier, source and destination domains, payload hash, detected unsanctioned category, action taken (blocked/quarantined/rejected), and the relevant organisation-defined policy references so auditors can verify enforcement against the security transfer policy and data transfer privacy policy.
6. **Operational hardening and change control**: where feasible, use fail-closed behaviour when inspection fails, protect the inspection service itself, and use controlled change management for policy updates (versioned rules, tested before rollout).

###### WHO
- **Security Architect / Compliance Lead**: owns how AC-4(15) unsanctioned categories map to the organisation-defined policy rules.
- **Platform/Network Engineering**: ensures cross-domain transfer routing goes through the inspection point.
- **Application Security / Security Engineering**: builds the detection integrations (malware scanning, content classification and permissions checks, executable-content detection).
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC) / security information and event management (SIEM) Operations**: ensures logs are sent to the right place, kept for the required period, and can be searched for audit purposes.

###### Acceptance criteria
- All identified cross-domain transfer methods for the application are routed through the inspection point (no bypass routes).
- For test payloads containing (a) malicious code, (b) unauthorised release data, and (c) harmful executable content, the transfer is **prohibited**, and the block reason references the organisation-defined policy.
- Audit logs show, for each blocked transfer: transfer ID, source and destination domains, payload hash, detected category, action taken, and the relevant policy rule reference(s).
- Inspection failures do not result in unsanctioned content being delivered to the destination (fail-closed or controlled quarantine in line with policy, with documented risk acceptance).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Take an inventory of, and document, every method the application and its integrations use to move data between different systems (cross-domain transfers).
- [ ] Set up routing so that all cross-domain data passes through the inspection gateway or proxy, with no bypass.
- [ ] Set up “policy-as-code” rules that translate **AC-04.15 ODP.01** unsanctioned categories into specific, practical detection controls.
- [ ] Add malware scanning, checks that confirm data is correctly classified and authorised, and detection of executable content to the inspection process.
- [ ] Apply a “deny-by-policy” rule so transfers are blocked when unauthorised information is detected, and send controlled error messages back to the callers.
- [ ] Set up audit logging to record: transfer identifiers, a hash (fingerprint) of the data payload, the categories detected, the actions taken, and the references to the policy rules used.
- [ ] Test with realistic harmful files, including unauthorised release attempts, and confirm that file transfers are blocked.


---

#### AC-4.17 — Domain Authentication (Enhancement)

This requirement ensures that, for every piece of information your systems send and receive, the organisation can clearly and reliably identify both the sender and the recipient. Without this, wrongdoing, mistakes, or breaches of policy can be concealed by missing or unclear identities, making investigations harder and preventing personal information journeys from being checked or corrected.

To meet this expectation, your organisation must put in place a clear method to label and verify the sending and receiving points for information transfers. This includes using distinct identifiers for the organisation, a system, an application, a service, and an individual, and making sure these identifiers are different and not open to misinterpretation.

During the transfer, the system must apply this verification, record the authenticated sender and receiver details in audit records so they can be traced later, and keep the required procedures and documentation up to date, including what staff should do if identity cannot be confirmed.

##### Domain authentication for source/destination with audit-ready labels

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Set up domain authentication for information transfers by **uniquely identifying and cryptographically verifying** both the **source (sending)** and **destination (receiving)** domain points. Use the organisation-defined domain types in **{{ insert: param, ac-04.17_odp }}** (for example: organisation, system, application, service, individual). Make sure transfers **cannot happen** unless both ends have **valid and unambiguous** domain labels, and that the **verified source and destination** are recorded so the transfer can be reconstructed for investigations.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-4(17) enhancement requires the organisation to **“uniquely identify and authenticate source and destination points”** for information transfer using **{{ insert: param, ac-04.17_odp }}**. The guidance highlights that accurate attribution depends on system labels that clearly distinguish **systems, organisations, and individuals**. This supports forensic reconstruction, correct policy compliance attribution, and the ability to trace personally identifiable information (PII) through its lifecycle.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create a domain label structure and registry** aligned to **{{ insert: param, ac-04.17_odp }}**, including the required label formats and the allowed values for each domain type (organisation/system/application/service/individual). Assign responsible owners and set a review schedule.
2. **Authenticate the source domain at the transfer boundary:**
   - **Individual**: link to the authenticated user identity from the enterprise identity provider (for example, the single sign-on (SSO) session) and require strong authentication in line with enterprise policy.
   - **System/Application/Service**: link to the authenticated caller identity using workload or service identity (for example, mutual Transport Layer Security (mTLS) identity, signed service assertions, or short-lived tokens issued by the enterprise identity/workload system).
3. **Authenticate the destination domain at the transfer boundary:**
   - Determine destination labels from the authenticated receiving endpoint identity (for example, the receiving application programming interface (API) service identity, message consumer identity, or resource owner), rather than from user-supplied fields.
4. **Apply domain authentication to every transfer:**
   - Add a validation step in the application, API, or messaging gateway to check that **both** source and destination domain labels are present, valid, and match the authenticated identities.
   - Block transfers (fail closed) when labels are missing, unknown, incorrectly formatted, or cannot be cryptographically verified.
5. **Record authenticated endpoints in audit logs:**
   - For every transfer event, record audit details that include the authenticated **source** and **destination** domain labels (including all required domain types as per **{{ insert: param, ac-04.17_odp }}**) and a correlation identifier to support end-to-end reconstruction.
   - Send logs to the enterprise security information and event management (SIEM) or monitoring platform with integrity protections in line with existing logging standards.
6. **Document operational procedures and failure handling:**
   - Provide runbook guidance for troubleshooting and responding when domain authentication fails (for example, invalid tokens or destination identity mismatch), including what evidence to capture for investigations.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Security Architect / Security Engineering Lead**: owns the domain label structure, enforcement design, and audit log format.
- **Platform/Identity Engineering**: implements and maintains service identity and token/mTLS validation mechanisms.
- **Application/API/Messaging Engineers**: implement boundary enforcement and ensure authenticated domain labels are carried through correctly.
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC)/Monitoring Lead**: checks audit fields, correlation, and security information and event management (SIEM) ingestion.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For **100%** of information transfer events, the system records **authenticated** source and destination domain labels for **all** domain types specified by **{{ insert: param, ac-04.17_odp }}**.
- Transfers with missing, invalid, or unverifiable domain labels are **rejected** (fail closed) and produce an auditable failure event.
- Domain labels are derived from authenticated identities (identity provider (IdP) session principal and/or workload/service identity), not from unauthenticated client-provided metadata.
- Audit records support forensic reconstruction: a single correlation identifier links source to destination across tiers and services.
- Evidence is available: the label registry, enforcement code/configuration review records, and sample audit log entries showing correct attribution.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and get approval for a domain label taxonomy and registry that covers the domain types listed in **ac-04.17_odp**, using unique and unambiguous label formats.
- [ ] Set up source authentication checks at the point where data is transferred, for both: (1) individual users (the identity provider session principal) and (2) systems, applications, and services (workload or service identities, such as mutual Transport Layer Security (mTLS) or signed assertions).
- [ ] Set up destination authentication binding by getting the destination domain from the authenticated receiving endpoint identity (not from any data supplied by the client).
- [ ] Add “fail-closed” enforcement in the application, application programming interface (API), and messaging gateway so that transfers are rejected when the authenticated source and destination domain labels are missing, invalid, or cannot be verified.
- [ ] Extend audit logging to record the authenticated source and destination domain labels (all required domain types), plus a correlation identifier so that events can be linked together for end-to-end reconstruction.
- [ ] Set up security information and event management (SIEM) and monitoring to bring in the new audit fields, and add protections to keep the data accurate and unchanged. Then test the transfers across environments (production, test, and disaster recovery).


---

#### AC-4.19 — Validation of Metadata (Enhancement)

This expectation is about making sure that when information moves between different parts of the organisation that follow different rules, any extra details that travel with it are checked and corrected before it is delivered. Without this, sensitive information could be labelled incorrectly, sent to the wrong place, or include personal information that should have been limited or removed—creating legal and reputational risk.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must define and apply checks to these extra details (metadata) for every transfer between different security domains, so that the metadata follows the required United States Department of Defense handling rules. This includes the correct security classification, what can be shared, the system of record, and the required security markings. The organisation must also ensure the metadata matches the classification of the main information it describes.

The organisation must also protect privacy in the metadata by limiting personal data to what is strictly necessary, ensuring the required lawful basis information is included, and removing (redacting) personal-data fields.

The organisation must document how this works, configure its systems to enforce it, and keep audit records showing what was checked, filtered, corrected, or blocked for each transfer.

##### Metadata validation and filtering on cross-domain transfers

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up automatic checks for metadata whenever information is moved between different security areas. The system must check, fix, or stop (block) the metadata based on the organisation’s agreed security and privacy rules (including Department of Defense (DoD) rules for moving data between domains). It must also ensure the metadata matches the information’s required security level (classification).

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Access Control (AC)-4(19) enhancement requires that, when information is moved between different security domains, the system applies **ac-4.19_prm_1** to metadata. Guidance explains that organisations can treat metadata and the data it relates to as separate items, or as part of the main information (payload). The solution must therefore validate metadata in the way the organisation has chosen, and ensure the required filtering and inspection covers both the metadata and the linked data as needed.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the metadata checking rules (policy-as-code)** aligned to **ac-04.19_odp.01** (DoD metadata domain-transfer rules: classification, what can be shared, system of record, and required security markings) and **ac-04.19_odp.02** (privacy filters: Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act (HIPAA) “minimum necessary”, General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR) lawful-basis tags, and redaction of personal-data fields). Encode these rules so they apply to each source-to-destination domain transfer.
2. **Set up a single enforcement point for cross-domain transfers** (for example, an application programming interface (API) gateway, a managed file transfer service, a secure message/event export component, or transfer middleware) so that all approved cross-domain transfers go through one metadata-aware validation step.
3. **Check metadata during transfer**:
   - Determine the **effective classification of the payload** from the payload label/trusted classification header (or from the payload itself, if that is the organisation’s approach).
   - Check that the metadata claims (classification, what can be shared, system of record, security markings) are **consistent** with the payload classification and the destination domain’s rules.
   - Apply **privacy protections to metadata**: enforce HIPAA minimum-necessary metadata fields, require and validate GDPR lawful-basis tags, and **redact or mask personal-data fields** in metadata according to the organisation’s redaction rules.
4. **Decide the outcome**:
   - **Correct-and-forward** when the metadata can be reliably fixed to meet the destination rules.
   - **Block** when required markings/tags cannot be validated, when fixing would be unclear, or when redaction cannot be applied safely.
5. **Keep audit evidence for every transfer attempt**: record the source and destination domains, the rule set version, which metadata fields were checked, the result (allowed/corrected/blocked), and any redaction or correction actions taken.

###### WHO (role responsible)
