- **Application/Platform Security Engineer**: build the metadata-aware cross-domain transfer enforcement component and the policy-as-code rules.
- **Data Protection Officer / Privacy Lead**: confirm the HIPAA and GDPR metadata requirements and the redaction rules.
- **Information Assurance / Security Policy Owner**: approve the DoD domain-transfer rule mappings and the metadata field meanings (schema).
- **Security Operations / Compliance**: review the audit evidence and ensure appropriate retention and monitoring.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every cross-domain transfer, the system validates metadata against **ac-4.19_prm_1** and the encoded DoD/privacy filters.
- Transfers with non-compliant metadata are either corrected to meet destination rules or blocked (no silent acceptance).
- Metadata handling matches the organisation’s chosen interpretation of metadata versus the linked payload data (either treated as separate items or treated as part of the payload), and results in the correct filtering and inspection.
- Audit records exist for each transfer attempt showing which validation checks were performed and the final outcome (allowed/corrected/blocked), including any redaction or correction actions.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the official metadata structure and the field-by-field rules for: classification, whether information can be released, the system of record, required security markings, and how to identify personal data.
- [ ] Set up Department of Defense (DoD) domain-transfer rules and privacy filters as enforceable “policy as code” for every source-to-destination domain pair.
- [ ] Set up a metadata-aware cross-domain transfer control point (a gateway or middleware) so that every cross-domain data flow is checked and validated before it is allowed to pass.
- [ ] Add checks during data transfer to work out the correct payload classification and confirm that the metadata matches the destination requirements.
- [ ] Put privacy protections in place for metadata, including: allowing only the minimum information needed for health data under the Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act (HIPAA), checking that data processing follows the correct legal basis under the General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR), and removing or masking personal data.
- [ ] Set up predictable results (either correct-and-forward or block), and make sure any metadata that does not meet requirements cannot be released unless it passes validation.
- [ ] Enable audit logging that records the rule set version, which fields were checked and validated, what redaction or correction actions were applied, and the outcome (allowed, corrected, or blocked) for every transfer attempt.


---

#### AC-4.20 — Approved Solutions (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that very sensitive information is only allowed to move between different security areas using approved methods. If this is not done, confidential or secret information could be sent through the wrong systems, blocked incorrectly, or routed using unapproved tools or settings. This increases the risk of unauthorised access, data leaks, or the information being handled incorrectly.

To meet this requirement, the organisation must use only National Security Agency (NSA)-approved cross-domain solutions and settings to control how Confidential and Secret information moves between security domains. It must also clearly document, in its cross-domain information flow rules, the step-by-step procedures staff must follow, the system design, and the actual configuration settings.

The organisation must keep an up-to-date list and baseline of the approved configurations, maintain audit records that show the approved controls are being applied, and include the approach and named responsibilities in the system security plan so that it can be independently checked.

##### Enforce NSA-approved cross-domain solutions for Confidential/Secret flows

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up and run cross-domain information-flow controls using **only National Security Agency (NSA)-approved cross-domain solutions and configurations** to manage **Confidential and Secret** data flows between security domains.

###### WHY (control requirement)
National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Access Control (AC)-4(20) requires: **“Employ {{ ac-04.20_odp.01 }} to control the flow of {{ ac-04.20_odp.02 }} across security domains.”** The organisation must define the **approved solutions/configurations** and the **information flows** they govern. It must also ensure the controls are auditable and match the approved configuration baseline.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Create an authoritative inventory of cross-domain solutions** covering every enforcement point (for example, gateway/relay instances). Include direction (Domain A→B and B→A), and clearly map each enforcement point to the **NSA-approved solution/configuration** used for **Confidential/Secret** flows.
2. **Update the cross-domain information-flow enforcement policy** by adding an appendix that lists the **exact approved solution/configuration identifiers** (names/versions/profile IDs as provided in the NSA-approved listing) and the **information-flow rules** for Confidential and Secret data across each domain boundary. This includes allow/deny decisions, protocol/application limits, and any required handling constraints.
3. **Implement configuration from a controlled baseline**: keep the approved configuration profiles/templates in version control, and deploy them to each enforcement point using automation (for example, infrastructure-as-code or configuration management). Treat the approved baseline as the single source of truth and stop manual changes from drifting away from it (for example, pipeline checks that confirm the live configuration matches the approved profile).
4. **Create and keep evidence**:
   - Record the **configuration settings** actually applied on each enforcement point (for example, exported device configuration snapshots or configuration compliance reports).
   - Ensure **audit logs** record enforcement decisions (allowed/denied) with enough identifiers to link each decision to the specific enforcement component and the approved profile.
5. **Run the controls as an operational process**: publish runbooks/procedures for operations teams explaining how to confirm the controls are active, how to handle exceptions (only through approved change routes), and how to confirm Confidential/Secret flows are governed by the approved solutions/configurations.
6. **Include this in the System Security Plan (SSP)**, including the enforcement approach and **named responsibilities** (who owns the inventory, who approves changes, who checks configuration compliance, and who reviews audit evidence) so independent verification is possible.

###### WHO
- **Cross-domain security architect / compliance lead**: owns policy updates, the solution inventory, and the SSP content.
- **Platform/network engineering (or cloud security engineering)**: implements the approved configurations and automation.
- **Operations/Security Operations Centre (SOC)**: ensures audit log ingestion works, monitors enforcement decisions, and follows the runbooks.
- **Configuration management/change control authority**: ensures changes are reviewed against the approved baseline.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every security-domain boundary where **Confidential/Secret** data may flow, there is a documented mapping to an **NSA-approved cross-domain solution/configuration** (inventory plus policy appendix).
- The cross-domain enforcement policy clearly defines the **information-flow rules** for Confidential/Secret data across domains.
- Deployed enforcement points are demonstrably configured to match the **approved configuration baseline** (with configuration compliance evidence available for audit).
- Audit logs show enforcement decisions (allowed/denied) that can be traced to the specific enforcement component and approved profile.
- The SSP includes the enforcement approach and named responsibilities, and operational runbooks exist for verification and exception handling.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an authoritative inventory that lists every security-domain boundary involved in Confidential and Secret information flows, and links each boundary to the specific National Security Agency (NSA)-approved solution and configuration profile used.
- [ ] Update the cross-domain information flow enforcement policy to:

- list the approved solutions and configurations, and  
- set clear allow/deny rules for how **Confidential** and **Secret** information may move between domains.
- [ ] Create an approved, version-controlled set of configuration templates and profiles for each enforcement point (templates/profiles), and deploy it using automated configuration management.
- [ ] Put in place controls to detect configuration drift, so that live system settings are checked continuously or at set intervals against the approved baseline.
- [ ] Export and keep configuration evidence for each enforcement point, showing the approved settings that were applied.
- [ ] Enable and keep audit logs that record enforcement decisions (allowed or denied), including identifiers for the enforcement component and the approved profile.
- [ ] Update the System Security Plan to describe how enforcement will work and who is responsible for each task, and publish operational guides for how to verify compliance and handle exceptions.


---

#### AC-4.21 — Physical or Logical Separation of Information Flows (Enhancement)

This requirement is about keeping different types of information traffic on separate routes through your network, so they cannot be mixed up while they are moving. Without this, a mistake or misuse could allow sensitive information to go to the wrong environment, enable unauthorised access between systems, or make operational technology (the systems that run equipment) reachable from the wrong place—raising the risk of disruption or data exposure.

To meet this requirement, the organisation must first decide and document which types of traffic must be separated. This includes, for example: incoming versus outgoing communications, requests versus replies, Secret versus Confidential information, and IT versus operational technology (OT) control traffic. The organisation must then set up and document how those separations will be enforced, both logically and physically. Examples include dedicated network segments and firewall areas, separate encrypted connection routes for each traffic type and sensitivity level, and separate routing paths for each category. It also includes dedicated physical network and switching routes for Secret information and OT control traffic.

The organisation must keep the separation rules, designs, configuration details, and proof that enforcement is in place up to date. It must assign named responsibilities and keep records so that the separation can be checked and verified.

##### Implement VRFs/VLANs + firewall zones + per-classification VPN tunnels

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up clear, separate network paths for the application using **virtual local area networks (VLANs)** and/or **virtual routing and forwarding (VRFs)**, **separate firewall security areas** with **connection-aware inspection**, and **separate virtual private network (VPN) tunnels** for each type of traffic and each data category. Make sure that **incoming versus outgoing**, **service requests versus replies**, **Secret versus Confidential** data, and **information technology (IT) versus operational technology (OT) control traffic** all use **separate (non-overlapping) routes**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) access control requirement **AC-4(21)** says information flows must be separated **logically or physically** using the organisation’s chosen methods (for example, VLANs/VRFs, firewall security areas, separate VPN tunnels) to achieve the organisation’s required separations (for example, incoming versus outgoing, requests versus replies, Secret versus Confidential, and IT versus OT control traffic). This stops different types of traffic being mixed while travelling through the network and allows control to be applied based on the intended path.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the required separations** in a traffic “map” (taxonomy and mapping table):
   - Incoming versus outgoing communications
   - Service requests versus replies (for each externally available service/application programming interface (API))
   - Secret versus Confidential (based on the organisation’s data classification)
   - IT versus OT control traffic (where applicable)  
   Then assign each traffic type to the intended building blocks: **VLAN/VRF → firewall security area → routing domain → VPN tunnel(s)**.
2. **Create logical network separation**:
   - Use **VLANs and/or VRFs** to separate at least **Confidential** and **Secret** traffic.
   - Use **separate routing domains** (VRFs/routing instances) for each classification and traffic category.
   - Do not allow “route sharing” between categories. If any connection is needed, document it and restrict it explicitly.
3. **Enforce separation through firewall rules**:
   - Create **dedicated firewall security areas** for each traffic category (for example, incoming entry, service request processing, outgoing return; plus separate areas for each classification).
   - Turn on **stateful (connection-aware) inspection** and apply **default-deny** between areas, allowing only traffic that has been explicitly approved.
   - Ensure the direction is correct (for example, rules for the incoming entry area must differ from rules for the outgoing return area).
4. **Separate remote access and between-site paths**:
   - Set up **separate VPN tunnels** for each **traffic type and classification** (for example, one tunnel for Confidential incoming/outgoing, another for Secret; separate tunnels for IT versus OT control if applicable).
   - Link each tunnel to its own address pool and allow only the destinations that belong to the corresponding **VRF/security area**.
5. **Check that requests and replies are separated**:
   - For each service/API, confirm that requests enter through the correct incoming security area and replies return through the correct outgoing/return security area.
   - Use firewall logs/telemetry to verify traffic is passing through the expected pairs of security areas.
6. **Produce audit-ready evidence**:
   - Keep diagrams showing the logical network and the zone/security area mapping.
   - Keep the firewall rule sets (or exported rules), VRF/routing instance configuration evidence, and VPN tunnel binding details.
   - Retain logs showing enforcement (for example, allowed traffic between security areas and blocked attempts under default-deny).

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Network Security Architect**: owns the segmentation design and the traffic taxonomy mapping.
- **Network Engineers / Platform Engineers**: implement VLANs/VRFs, firewall security areas, and VPN tunnel configurations.
- **Security Operations (SOC) / Security Engineering**: checks enforcement using logs and supports evidence retention.
- **Application Owner** (for each service/API): confirms which endpoints are requests versus replies and aligns service routing to the agreed security areas.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A documented mapping exists for all required separations (incoming versus outgoing, requests versus replies, Secret versus Confidential, IT versus OT control where applicable) using the selected methods (VLANs/VRFs, firewall security areas, VPN tunnels).
- VRFs/routing domains and firewall security areas are implemented so traffic categories do not mix (default-deny between areas that are not approved to communicate).
- VPN tunnels are separated by traffic type and data classification, with tunnel-to-VRF/security area restrictions enforced.
- For each externally available service/API, request and reply paths use the designated incoming/request and outgoing/return security areas, confirmed through firewall logs.
- An evidence package is available, including: network/security area diagrams, firewall configuration exports, VRF/routing instance configuration evidence, VPN tunnel binding configuration, and sample log extracts showing both allowed and blocked traffic.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Document how network traffic is classified and mapped, including inbound versus outbound traffic, requests versus responses, Secret versus Confidential data, and IT versus operational technology (OT) controls. Record how these map to virtual local area networks (VLANs) and virtual routing and forwarding instances (VRFs), firewall zones, routing domains, and virtual private network (VPN) tunnels.
- [ ] Set up virtual routing and forwarding (VRF) instances—one for Secret and one for Confidential—and assign each application tier and endpoint to the correct VRF.
- [ ] Set up separate firewall security zones for each type of network traffic, and configure rules that remember active connections (stateful rules) to block traffic by default between zones that are not approved.
- [ ] Set up separate virtual private network (VPN) connections for each type and category of traffic. Assign each connection its own dedicated pool of IP addresses, and restrict those addresses to the matching virtual routing and forwarding (VRF) instance or network zone.
- [ ] Update the service and application programming interface (API) network routes so that requests enter through the designated inbound (request) zone and responses return through the designated outbound (return) zone.
- [ ] Collect and keep evidence (network diagrams, exported firewall, virtual private network (VPN) and virtual routing and forwarding (VRF) settings, and copies of relevant logs) showing that separation was enforced and that attempts to mix or share traffic were blocked.


---

#### AC-4.22 — Access Only (Enhancement)

This requirement is about allowing people to use one single computer to view information from different security areas, while ensuring that information from one area cannot be seen or taken into another. Without this, a user could accidentally or intentionally move sensitive information from a higher-protection area into a lower-protection area, which could lead to unauthorised disclosure, fraud, or breaches of legal and regulatory requirements.

To meet this, the organisation must set up the single device so it behaves as “view only” across security areas. Users must be able to open and read content in each area, but the system must actively stop any ways of transferring information between areas, such as copying and pasting, saving or exporting, printing, using shared storage, or moving data using removable media.

The organisation must document:
- the rules for how the separation is enforced,
- the procedures for running and maintaining it,
- the system design and configuration settings that make it work.

It must also keep audit records showing that access occurred and that attempts to transfer information were blocked. Finally, it must clearly assign named responsibilities to the appropriate staff, so that system administrators and security staff oversee the separation properly.

##### Access-only multi-domain terminal with enforced no-transfer controls

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a dedicated “view-only” access device (a terminal or virtual desktop infrastructure (VDI) session host) that lets users view information from multiple security areas (security domains) on one device, while **technically stopping any movement of information between those areas** (no copying/exporting/printing/saving/moving, and no transfer to removable media).

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Access Control (AC)-4(22) enhancement requires: “Provide access from a single device to computing platforms, applications, or data residing in multiple different security domains, while preventing information flow between the different security domains.” The guidance also expects an “access-only solution” (for example, a terminal) that keeps information separate and gives users no way to transfer information between security domains.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create a standardised view-only endpoint build** (gold image) with the minimum software needed, no saving of user data on the device, and limited administrative access.
2. **Assign each security domain to its own isolated working space on the same device** (for example, separate VDI sessions or separate browser/viewer containers for each domain) with **no shared clipboard, no shared files, and no communication between those spaces**.
3. **Turn off or block information-transfer features** at the endpoint or session level for every domain working space:
   - clipboard sharing (copy/paste between spaces) disabled;
   - downloads/export/save-as to local storage blocked;
   - printing blocked or limited to **domain-specific** print queues, with no output shared across domains;
   - drag-and-drop disabled;
   - removable media access disabled;
   - any “save to shared drive” or network share access restricted to the correct locations for that domain only.
4. **Maintain separation using centrally managed configuration controls** (for example, endpoint management and VDI “policy as code”) so the “no-transfer” settings are applied consistently and stay enabled after updates.
5. **Add audit logging to security information and event management (SIEM)** to record:
   - successful access events for each domain working space;
   - denied attempts to copy/export/print/download/use removable media.
6. **Document day-to-day operating procedures and how exceptions are handled** (for example, how controlled printing is approved, how exceptions are time-limited, and how the view-only build is kept up to date).

###### WHO
- **Security Engineering / Endpoint/VDI Administrator**: builds and configures the view-only endpoint and the isolation settings.
- **Information Security (AC/Policy owner)**: defines how each security domain maps to a working space, approves exception procedures, and checks that audit evidence is sufficient.
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC) / Monitoring team**: ensures SIEM receives the logs and that alerts are raised for denied transfer attempts.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A user can view content from multiple security domains using the single access device.
- There is **no working way to transfer information between domains** (clipboard, copy/paste, export/download, printing to general printers, drag-and-drop, shared storage, and removable media are blocked or restricted to the correct domain only).
- Audit records show domain access and clearly record denied transfer attempts with enough detail to support investigation.
- The view-only endpoint configuration is repeatable (gold image) and centrally managed, so controls remain enforced after updates.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Set up a mapping that shows which user roles are allowed to view each domain context on the access-only terminal.
- [ ] Create and deploy a secure “access-only” endpoint or virtual desktop infrastructure (VDI) session host “gold” image with the minimum required software and no persistent local storage.
- [ ] Set up separate, isolated working environments for each security domain, with clipboard sharing turned off and no ability to access files across those environments.
- [ ] Disable or strongly block ways to move data out of the system, including copy-and-paste between different contexts, downloading or exporting files, drag-and-drop, using removable storage devices, and general printing.
- [ ] Centralise and version-control the endpoint and virtual desktop infrastructure (VDI) policy settings to ensure the no-transfer controls stay enabled after any changes.
- [ ] Turn on security information and event management (SIEM) logging for access to the domain and for denied transfer attempts; check that the logs are complete using test cases.
- [ ] Publish clear operating procedures for maintenance and for time-limited exceptions (for example, controlled printing for specific business needs), and assign named owners to each one.


---

#### AC-4.23 — Modify Non-releasable Information (Enhancement)

This requirement is about protecting information when it is shared between different parts of the business that do not have the same level of trust. Any information that is not authorised to be shared must be deliberately hidden or changed before it leaves. Without this, sensitive information—such as secret identifiers, confidential customer information, or internal-only data—could be accidentally exposed during transfers, previews, or record keeping. This could lead to data leaks, misuse, or attacks.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must:
- Clearly define which cross-area information transfers require this protection.
- Document that any information not approved for sharing will be handled using redaction and masking.
- Build these rules into its official guidance, day-to-day procedures, system design, and security plans.
- Configure systems so masking is applied automatically for the relevant transfer routes.
- Ensure the right named staff are responsible, including system and network administrators and information security staff.
- Keep audit records showing that masking was applied.

##### Cross-domain transfer redaction/masking via secure exchange service

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a control that enforces cross-domain information transfers by **changing information that must not be released** using the organisation-defined **modification action** (parameter **ac-04.23_odp**), specifically **redaction and masking**, before the information is sent into the receiving security domain.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(23)** requires that when information is transferred between different security domains, the organisation **changes information that must not be released** by applying the organisation-defined **modification action** (**ac-04.23_odp**). This helps prevent data leakage and reduces the chance that an attacker can use sensitive content during or after the transfer.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Define the transfer paths** for the application (for example: file export, email attachments, API responses, web downloads, report generation, preview/thumbnail generation) and link each path to a **sending domain** and **receiving domain**.
2. **Create a redaction/masking specification** based on the organisation’s data classification and release rules, covering:
   - Which data items are **not allowed to be released** for each receiving domain.
   - The **masking/redaction formats** (for example: `****1234`, `[REDACTED]`) and whether the masking must be consistent for matching purposes.
   - Whether the change is **masking, redaction, or removal** (the organisation parameter specifies redaction and masking).
3. **Apply the change rules in a central secure transfer layer** (gateway/secure transfer service) that every cross-domain transfer must use. The service must:
   - Carry out **content-aware checks** (where possible) and/or **transform based on metadata/tags** using the application’s data classification labels.
   - Apply the redaction/masking rules to the content **before** it is released to the receiving domain.
   - Ensure the original sensitive values are never sent out in any form (including logs, error messages, previews, or other secondary views).
4. **Use the authorisation context** so that release decisions are based on who/what is requesting the transfer (identity/service) and the destination domain’s policy (for example: workload identity, role, and data classification).
5. **Produce audit evidence** for each transfer to show that the change was applied, using non-sensitive indicators (for example: rule identifiers, transformation type, counts, and hashes of the changed content), without recording the original sensitive values.
6. **Test and verify** across all transfer methods (including previews/downloads and any secondary representations) to confirm that fields that must not be released are always redacted/masked.

###### WHO
- **Application Owner / Security Architect**: defines transfer paths and the redaction/masking specification.
- **Platform/Integration Engineer**: implements the secure transfer enforcement and transformation logic.
- **Information Security (Data Protection/Data Loss Prevention owner)**: checks the rules against data classification and release policy.
- **Operations/Monitoring Lead**: ensures audit logging and integration with security information and event management (SIEM).

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every defined cross-domain transfer path, information that must not be released is **changed** using **redaction and masking** before it reaches the receiving domain.
- No original sensitive values appear in the receiving domain content, previews/secondary views, or in the transformation/audit records.
- Audit records exist for representative transfers showing that transformation occurred (for example: rule identifiers/transformation types) and can be reviewed in SIEM.
- Cross-domain transfer tests (including at least one example for each transfer method) show consistent enforcement and no ways to bypass the defined paths.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Identify and record all ways data can move between different security areas for the application, including preview or secondary screens, and document the security areas that send and receive the data.
- [ ] Define which data elements must not be released for each receiving area, and specify the redaction or masking format for each type of data element.
- [ ] Set up a secure exchange or gateway enforcement point so that all cross-domain data transfers must pass through the transformation service.
- [ ] Set up content-aware or label-based transformation so the organisation’s required change (redaction and masking) is applied before the content is released.
- [ ] Link transformation events to audit logging and security information and event management (SIEM) using non-sensitive evidence (rule identifiers, transformation types, and hashes), and confirm that the records are kept for the required retention period.
- [ ] Run complete end-to-end tests for each transfer method to confirm that any fields that cannot be released are always hidden or masked, and that the original values never appear in the data sent or in any logs.


---

#### AC-4.24 — Internal Normalized Format (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure information is understood correctly when it moves between different parts of the organisation. It does this by first converting the information into a standard internal format, and then recreating it in the exact format the receiving area expects. Without this, attackers or careless systems could introduce data with unexpected formatting, which could be misread and lead to incorrect decisions, damaged or corrupted records, or harmful data leaving the organisation in the wrong form.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must:
- clearly identify which information transfers cross between different security areas;
- ensure the system reads incoming data and converts it into a consistent internal representation; and
- then recreate it so it matches the destination’s requirements for fields, data types, structure, and formats.

Any invalid or unreadable data must be rejected or handled safely. The organisation must also keep clear records showing what happened, who is responsible, and how this approach is documented in design, procedures, configuration, and the organisation’s security planning.

##### Canonical parse-and-regenerate at cross-domain data boundaries

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a defensive process at every point where information moves between security areas: **parse → convert to a standard internal format → recreate the output**. This ensures incoming data is understood safely, then re-sent exactly in the format required by the receiving area.

###### WHY (control requirement)
Control AC-4(24) requires that when transferring information between different security domains, the system **parses incoming data into a standard internal format** and **recreates the data** so it matches its intended specification. This helps prevent malformed or maliciously crafted data from being interpreted incorrectly, and reduces the risk of data being leaked or integrity failures occurring.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Identify cross-domain transfers** (for example: application programming interface (API) calls, message consumption, extract, transform, load (ETL) jobs, event ingestion) and define a boundary for each transfer path (API gateway, message consumer, integration service, ETL step).
2. For each data type transferred, define a **standard internal model** with strict rules for data types and standardisation (for example: ISO 8601 timestamps, standard decimal format, UTF-8 normalisation, controlled lists for allowed values, and clear handling of null or optional fields).
3. At the boundary, implement **strict parsing and validation** into the standard model:
   - Reject unknown fields and any data that breaks the expected structure (schema).
   - Enforce correct encoding, field types, length limits, and allowed value sets.
   - Convert to standard formats (for example: time zone normalisation, whitespace trimming rules) only after validation.
4. Use the standard model as the **only internal format** for all later processing in the receiving domain.
5. **Recreate the output** from the standard model to match the receiving contract:
   - Produce the destination schema/structure, required and optional fields, field ordering rules (where applicable), and encoding exactly as specified.
6. Implement **safe failure**:
   - If parsing, standardisation, or recreation fails, do not send the payload to the destination.
   - Quarantine the payload (restricted access) and raise an alert/event for investigation.
7. Produce **evidence suitable for audit**:
   - Record whether parsing succeeded or failed, the standard model version, the destination contract version, and a correlation or transaction identifier that links parse → standardise → recreate → delivery.
   - Keep configuration and version records for the standard models and recreation templates.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Integration Engineer / Software Engineer**: implement boundary parsing, standard models, recreation templates, and safe-failure/quarantine logic.
- **Security Architect / Compliance Lead**: approve standardisation rules, define expectations for an inventory of cross-domain transfers, and review audit evidence requirements.
- **Operations / Security Operations (SecOps)**: manage quarantine handling, coordinate alert investigation, and ensure logs are kept according to organisational policy.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every identified cross-domain transfer, the boundary performs strict parsing into a standard internal model and recreates output to match the destination specification.
- Malformed or invalid payloads are rejected or quarantined and are not delivered to the destination domain.
- Standardisation and recreation are repeatable and controlled by version (record the standard model version and destination contract version for each transaction).
- Audit logs exist for each transaction, including correlation identifiers linking parse → standardise → recreate → delivery, and quarantine events are recorded when failures occur.
- Security testing (unit and integration testing) shows correct handling of invalid encodings, schema violations, type mismatches, and attempts to bypass the boundary (for example, payloads that would otherwise be interpreted differently).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a list of information moved between different systems, and clearly define where each integration starts and ends (for example, an application programming interface gateway, a message receiver, or an extract, transform, and load (ETL) step).
- [ ] Create standard “canonical” internal models for each type of data being transferred, using strict data typing and clear rules for how data is converted into its standard form.
- [ ] Apply strict checks at every point where data enters or leaves the system: convert incoming information into the standard internal format, and stop immediately if there are problems with the data’s structure, encoding, or data type.
- [ ] Set up regeneration templates that produce destination-compliant outputs strictly from the approved source model only, with no direct copying of fields from the original input.
- [ ] Quarantine any invalid data payloads and use safe-failure handling to stop them being sent to the destination domain.
- [ ] Set up audit logging to include correlation identifiers, the canonical model version, the destination contract version, and the results of parsing and regeneration.
- [ ] Develop and run automated tests to check for invalid encodings, schema rule violations, incorrect data types, and that regenerated outputs are correct.


---

#### AC-4.25 — Data Sanitization (Enhancement)

This requirement ensures that when information is moved between different security areas, any information that should not be transferred is permanently wiped or destroyed so it cannot be recovered. Without this, sensitive information could end up in the wrong place, where it could be read, misused, or used to support harmful activity—such as delivering malicious content, taking control of malicious code, adding malicious capabilities, or hiding information in plain sight.

To meet this requirement, the organisation must first identify every instance where information is transferred between security areas, and then apply its own data sanitisation policy for each transfer. The organisation must also have clear written procedures, make sure the right staff can access and follow them, and assign named responsibilities to accountable roles, including system and network administrators.

In addition, the organisation must build sanitisation into system design and settings, keep records showing that sanitisation has taken place, retain those records for verification, include the approach in the system security plan, and maintain any supporting documentation needed to demonstrate compliance.

##### Policy-driven sanitise-on-transfer for cross-domain data flows

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up an automated, policy-based “sanitise-on-transfer” capability that detects when information is moved between different security zones and permanently sanitises the transferred data in line with the organisation’s data sanitisation policy.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Access Control (AC)-4(25) requires that, **when information is transferred between different security zones**, the organisation **sanitises data to reduce the risk of sensitive information being exposed** (AC-04.25_odp.01) **in line with the organisation’s data sanitisation policy** (AC-04.25_odp.02).

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create and maintain a cross-zone transfer catalogue (“transfer map”)** for the application and every integration point (for example: application programming interfaces (APIs), message buses, file/object storage replication, backup and restore, email and software-as-a-service (SaaS) connectors, removable media processes, and any demilitarised zone (DMZ) or broker components). For each transfer route, record the source zone, destination zone, data category, and transfer method.
2. **Turn the organisation’s data sanitisation policy into enforceable rules** (policy-as-code) that choose the sanitisation approach based on *data type + where it is stored + how it is transferred* (for example: cryptographic erasure for encrypted storage, secure erase for solid-state drives (SSDs) and hard disk drives (HDDs) where supported, and physical destruction for media types that require physical destruction). Make sure the rules explicitly aim to **reduce the exposure of sensitive information**.
3. **Apply sanitisation at the moment of transfer** by integrating with the transfer tools and processes so that a transfer is **stopped or altered** until sanitisation is finished (or the policy explicitly says sanitisation does not apply). Examples of where to enforce include:
   - API or microservice exit point (sanitise data and attachments before publishing to another zone)
   - Messaging or streaming exit point (sanitise before publishing to the destination zone)
   - Storage replication and backup jobs (ensure cryptographic erasure and retention settings match the policy)
   - Gateway or broker components (sanitise before forwarding)
4. **Create audit evidence for every sanitisation action**, including: date and time, source and destination zone identifiers, data category, sanitisation method used, target identifiers (for example, object IDs or volume IDs), workflow identity and operator identity, and the result of verification (for example, confirmation that cryptographic keys were handled as required, or confirmation that secure erase completed).
5. **Keep audit records for review** in line with the organisation’s logging and retention requirements. Also ensure the sanitisation approach is documented in the system security plan and supporting operational guides (runbooks) so operations can repeat and verify the expected behaviour.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Security and Architecture Lead**: owns the transfer map and the policy-as-code rule design.
- **Platform and Infrastructure Engineer**: implements the sanitisation enforcement integrations for storage, messaging, and gateway components.
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC) / Security Information and Event Management (SIEM) Engineer**: ensures sanitisation audit events are correctly formatted, collected, and retained.
- **System Owner**: approves the sanitisation ruleset and ensures it matches the organisation’s data sanitisation policy.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every cross-zone transfer route listed in the transfer map, the system either (a) sanitises the data before or as part of the transfer in line with the organisation’s sanitisation policy, or (b) records a policy-documented reason why sanitisation does not apply.
- Transfers cannot complete successfully without the required sanitisation step (unless the policy explicitly allows otherwise).
- For each sanitisation action, audit logs exist showing the method, target identifiers, source and destination zones, and the verification result.
- Audit evidence is retained and can be retrieved for assessment.
- The system security plan and operational runbook describe the sanitisation enforcement mechanism and responsibilities.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and get approval for a cross-domain transfer map that covers all ways data can leave or enter applications, including all integration points.
- [ ] Turn the organisation’s data sanitisation policy into enforceable “policy-as-code” rules, matched to the type of data, the storage or transmission medium, and the way data is transferred.
- [ ] Set up “sanitise on transfer” checks in the outgoing (egress), gateway, and transfer process, so transfers are blocked until sanitisation is complete.
- [ ] Integrate checks that confirm sanitisation is complete and that key erasure has finished, so the system can record an auditable result.
- [ ] Set up audit logging for every sanitisation event (domain, method, target, user identity, and verification result) and send it to the security information and event management (SIEM) system.
- [ ] Update the system security plan and day-to-day operating guides to document the sanitisation approach, who is responsible, and where the supporting evidence is stored.


---

#### AC-4.26 — Audit Filtering Actions (Enhancement)

This requirement is about keeping a clear record of what happens to each message as it moves between different parts of the organisation’s systems that have different rules. It matters because, without detailed records, you cannot prove that the correct filtering rules were applied. You also may not be able to explain why a message was changed, blocked, or quarantined, which increases the risk of inappropriate information crossing boundaries and makes mistakes harder to fix.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must:
- define which message transfers across boundaries are included
- ensure that every message that is checked has its specific filtering action recorded and linked to that message
- record the final result of the filtering decision

These records must be kept for the required period, be searchable for later review, and include enough detail to support troubleshooting—for example, the reason a message was modified or blocked.

##### Per-message content filtering audit records at cross-domain boundaries

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up audit logging for **content filtering actions and results** for **every individual message** that passes through a **cross-domain security boundary** (for example: email, web, API payloads, file transfers). The audit record must show both the **filtering action** taken and the **filtering result** achieved.

###### WHY (control requirement)
Control **AC-4(26)** requires that when information is moved between different security domains, the organisation **records and audits the content filtering actions and results** for the information being filtered. It also states that these actions/results must be recorded **for individual messages** to prove the correct filtering was applied and to help with troubleshooting (for example: why content was changed or why it did not pass filtering). Audit event requirements are defined in **AU-2**, and audit records are generated in **AU-12**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Identify where filtering is enforced and where boundaries exist** for content moving between security domains, and make the boundary mapping clear (for example: Internal to DMZ, Corporate to Internet, Partner to Internal).
2. **Turn on content filtering** at each boundary enforcement point using the organisation’s approved policy set (for example: data loss prevention/anti-virus/url checks/attachment sanitisation or quarantine rules, as applicable to the boundary technology).
3. **Set up per-message audit details** produced by the filtering component so that each filtered item creates an audit event that includes:
   - **Message identifier** (a stable reference used to link events, such as Message-ID, transaction ID, session ID, or file reference)
   - **Filtering action** (for example: allowed, modified/sanitised, blocked, quarantined, replaced)
   - **Filtering outcome** (for example: passed policy, failed policy, delivered, quarantined, rejected)
   - **Troubleshooting details** (for example: rule name, reason code, policy version, and time)
4. **Standardise and link (correlate) the events** in the logging system (security information and event management (SIEM) / log management) so the **action** and **outcome** can be searched together for the same **message identifier**. This also lets downstream systems (for example: quarantine portal, delivery logs, ticketing) use the same correlation reference.
5. **Check coverage** by sending controlled test messages through each boundary path, and confirm that for every message an audit record exists and contains the correct action/result pair.
6. **Apply audit retention and protection** by sending events to an immutable (write-once capable) log store (or an equivalent enterprise logging capability) with retention set to match organisational audit requirements, and by limiting access to audit data.

###### WHO
- **Security Engineering / Platform Engineering**: implement and configure boundary filtering and audit event generation.
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC) / SIEM Engineering**: standardise fields, ensure events are linked correctly, and build dashboards/queries.
- **Application Owner / Network/Email/Web Gateway Owner**: confirm boundary mapping and that the right policies apply.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each configured cross-domain boundary where content filtering is applied, **100% of test messages** generate an audit event containing the **message identifier**, **filtering action**, and **filtering outcome**.
- Audit events include **troubleshooting-relevant information** (rule name/reason code and policy version) sufficient to explain any modifications or blocks.
- Audit events are **linked (correlated)** in the SIEM/log store so the action and outcome can be retrieved for a single message identifier.
- Audit events are kept and access-controlled according to organisational audit requirements, and protections prevent unauthorised changes to audit data.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Match each cross-domain security boundary to the specific content-filtering enforcement point(s) used in the environment.
- [ ] Set up the filtering components to record an audit event for each message. Each audit event must include a consistent message identifier, the filtering action taken, and the result of the filtering.
- [ ] Add troubleshooting details to audit records (rule name, reason code, policy version, and timestamps) and make sure these details are filled in for every outcome.
- [ ] In the security information and event management (SIEM) and log system, combine and match audit events using the message identifier, so the action taken and the result can be searched together.
- [ ] Set up rules to keep audit records for the required period and protect their accuracy in the organisation’s central log system, and limit who can access the audit data.
- [ ] Run boundary-path test cases to confirm that every test message results in the correct action and outcome audit record, and that end-to-end correlation works correctly.


---

#### AC-4.27 — Redundant/Independent Filtering Mechanisms (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that when information moves between different protected areas of the business, it is checked against the agreed rules more than once, using different systems. Without this, if one checking tool fails, has an outage, or contains a bug, unsafe or unwanted content could pass through, or enforcement could stop completely—creating real exposure such as malware, sensitive data leaks, or breaches of policy.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must:
- Define which types of information (each data type) must be checked during transfers between areas.
- Set out clear procedures for how the checks are carried out and how the results are applied.
- Document how the filtering is built, configured, and monitored.
- Keep records showing that the checks were completed and what decision was made.
- Include this approach in the security plan.
- Assign clear responsibility to the relevant staff.

For each data type, the system must use redundant and independent filtering mechanisms—for example, different implementations and separate running components—so that if one mechanism fails, the other still enforces the rules.

##### Active-active dual content filters per data type at cross-domain transfer

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up redundant and independent content checks at every point where information moves between different security domains. For **each defined data type**, the content must be checked by **two separate filtering engines** that are **independent** (different code base/libraries and separate processes/services). Enforcement must still work properly if one engine fails.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-4(27) enhancement requires that when information is transferred between different security domains, the organisation uses **content filtering solutions** with **redundant and independent filtering for each data type**. Redundancy ensures that if one filtering mechanism fails, enforcement does not stop. Independence ensures that a single defect, shared dependency, or update problem does not affect both mechanisms.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Identify cross-domain transfer points and data types** (for example: secure email gateway attachments, web uploads/downloads, API payloads, file transfer payloads, document/image types). For each data type, define the **policy action** (for example: allow/quarantine/block) and the **decision rule** (for example: block/quarantine if either engine flags it).
2. **Deploy two independent filtering engines per data type** at the same enforcement point (active-active):
   - Engine A and Engine B must use **different code bases and supporting libraries** (for example: different vendor products, or separately developed components with different dependencies).
   - Run each engine as **separate system processes/services** (for example: separate containers/virtual machines, separate service accounts, separate update pipelines), with clear operational separation.
3. **Use fail-closed enforcement**: if one engine is unavailable, the other must still enforce the per-data-type policy (no “soft fail” that allows on error). If both engines are down, apply the most restrictive policy defined for that data type.
4. **Enable auditability**: record filtering events with enough information to prove enforcement happened (timestamp, source and destination domains, data type, each engine’s decision, the final decision, reason codes, and indicators of errors or unavailability).
5. **Operate independence and redundancy**: keep separate health checks, alerts, and runbooks for each engine. Control configuration changes and make them repeatable (for example: versioned configuration with change management).

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Security Architect / Solution Engineer**: defines data types, decision rules, and the target design for independent engines.
- **Network/Security Engineering Lead**: implements the gateway/enforcement deployment and fail-closed behaviour.
- **SOC (Security Operations Centre)**: confirms alerts work, monitors engine health, and reviews audit logs.
- **Change Manager / Configuration Manager**: ensures controlled configuration and update processes for both engines.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every defined cross-domain **data type**, two filtering engines are deployed, and both are actively used during normal operation.
- The engines are demonstrably **independent** (different vendor/code base/libraries and separate processes/services).
- If Engine A fails/unloads/crashes, Engine B continues to enforce the policy for the affected data types (**fail-closed**), and vice versa.
- Audit logs show filtering occurred for representative transfers, including each engine’s decision and the final enforcement outcome.
- Evidence is available for configuration/version control and operational runbooks for both engines.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Identify all points where information moves between different domains, and list the types of data that need content filtering.
- [ ] Set policy actions for each type of data and define the enforcement decision rule (for example, block or quarantine if either system flags it).
- [ ] For each type of data, set up and run two separate filtering engines that are independent of each other—using different code bases or libraries—and run them in separate processes or services.
- [ ] Set the system to “fail closed” so that if the engine is unavailable, it will not allow access by default for any type of data.
- [ ] Set up and check audit logs that record the engine’s decisions, the final decision, and any error or unavailability messages.
- [ ] Set up separate health checks, alerts, and step-by-step recovery instructions (runbooks) for each engine, and test failover by deliberately simulating an engine failure.


---

#### AC-4.28 — Linear Filter Pipelines (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that whenever information is sent between different parts of the organisation that have different levels of protection, it is checked in the same clear, step-by-step way every time, with no way to skip the check. Without this, sensitive or unsuitable information could get through by taking an alternative route, due to a misconfiguration, or by being handled in a way that avoids the check—leading to unauthorised access, breaches of policy, or data leaks.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must:
- clearly define which information transfers are covered,
- publish a rule that all covered transfers must pass through one single, sequential filtering process,
- design the system so that this filtering process cannot be bypassed and is always used.

It must also enforce the filtering result using:
- permission rules (who is allowed to send or receive information), and
- policy rules (what types of content are allowed).

Finally, it must keep clear documentation and audit records showing that the filtering process ran, what decision was made, and whether the transfer was allowed or blocked.

##### Non-bypassable linear content filtering for cross-domain transfers

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement a **single, step-by-step (linear) content checking process** that is **always used** for **moving information between security areas**, and make sure the checking result is **enforced** using both:
- **discretionary access controls (who is allowed to send/receive)**, and
- **mandatory access controls (policy-based allow/deny/quarantine)**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-4(28) requires that when information is transferred between different security domains, the organisation uses a **linear content checking process** that is **not possible to bypass** and is **always used**, and that the decision is enforced using both **discretionary and mandatory access controls**. The guidance also notes that **parallel checking designs** for the same type of data can create **bypass opportunities** and can lead to cases where checking is **not triggered**.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Define and publish the transfer scope**: document which protocols, interfaces, and data routes count as “cross-domain information transfer” for this application (for example: web/API outbound traffic, file transfer, messaging, and software-as-a-service (SaaS) connectors), and map each one to a **single inspection route**.
2. **Create one “choke point” per transfer type**: send all cross-domain traffic through a dedicated inspection gateway/proxy (or an active/standby pair) that is the **only allowed** path for traffic between domains. Enforce this using network controls (firewalls/security groups/software-defined wide area network (SD-WAN)/route policies) so there is **no alternate route** that could bypass inspection.
3. **Set up a strictly linear checking process inside the gateway**: implement inspection as one ordered sequence (for example: normalise → decode → classify → scan → policy check → enforce action). Make the process **fail-closed** (if inspection fails, default to **deny/quarantine**).
4. **Link discretionary and mandatory enforcement**:
   - **Discretionary**: enforce permission checks for the sender/receiver identity/role (for example, using enterprise identity provider (IdP) claims and role-based access control (RBAC)/attribute-based access control (ABAC)).
   - **Mandatory**: apply content/classification policy decisions (for example, allow/deny/quarantine based on data classification and the destination domain).
   - Ensure **mandatory policy cannot be overridden** by discretionary “allow” (so a mandatory deny/quarantine always results in deny/quarantine).
5. **Ensure checking cannot be skipped**: remove or disable any parallel inspection routes for the same data type (or ensure they are not reachable). Confirm that all relevant traffic flows go through the **same linear checking process**.
6. **Produce audit-ready evidence**: for every transfer attempt, record the sender/receiver identity authorisation result (discretionary), the content checking decision (mandatory), the final outcome (deliver/quarantine/deny), and gateway invocation details (timestamp, correlation identifier, source/destination domains). Keep logs according to the organisation’s retention requirements.

###### WHO (roles responsible)
- **Application Owner / Security Architect**: define transfer scope, design the choke point, and define the enforcement logic.
- **Network/Platform Engineer**: implement routing and firewall rules so the gateway is the only path.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Engineer**: connect identity/authorisation information into the gateway’s policy checks.
- **Security Operations / Security Operations Centre (SOC)**: confirm logging, retention, and monitoring; support incident handling for blocked/quarantined transfers.

###### Acceptance criteria
- All configured cross-domain transfer paths for the application go through the inspection gateway/proxy, and there is **no alternate route** that can deliver data without inspection.
- The gateway inspection process is implemented as a **single sequential chain** (with no parallel, bypassable branches) and is **fail-closed**.
- For each transfer attempt, the system records: (a) the discretionary authorisation result, (b) the mandatory content policy decision, and (c) the final enforcement action.
- Mandatory deny/quarantine outcomes are enforced regardless of discretionary authorisation (no override).
- Evidence is available (architecture diagram, configuration/routing rules, gateway pipeline configuration, and sample audit logs) showing non-bypassable checking for representative traffic types.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and document the application’s cross-domain data transfer scope (the protocols, interfaces, and data routes it uses), and map each one to a single inspection route.
- [ ] Set up a dedicated inspection gateway (or proxy) as the only allowed way for traffic to enter or leave between security zones, using firewall, routing, and software-defined wide area network (SD-WAN) rules.
- [ ] Set the gateway inspection to run in a strict, step-by-step order, and ensure that if inspection errors occur, access is blocked (fail-closed).
- [ ] Integrate enterprise identity information into the gateway so discretionary access decisions are assessed together with mandatory content and classification rules.
- [ ] Enforce required policy outcomes (block or quarantine) so they cannot be overridden by optional “allow” decisions.
- [ ] Turn off or remove any duplicate or alternative inspection routes for the same type of data, and check that all traffic goes through the single, straight processing pipeline.
- [ ] Enable and keep audit logs that record, for every transfer attempt: the gateway being called, the discretionary decision, the mandatory content decision, and the final enforcement action.


---

#### AC-4.29 — Filter Orchestration Engines (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that when information is shared between different parts of the organisation that have different protection rules, it is checked in a coordinated, step-by-step way, and that the checking process finishes properly. Without this, checks could fail without anyone noticing, stop part way through, or run in the wrong order—allowing unsafe or incorrect information to pass through, or leaving the organisation unable to prove what happened.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must use a coordinating “orchestration” system for these cross-domain transfers. This system must ensure that content checks run to completion without errors (including unexpected stopping or unusual behaviour), that each check is carried out in the approved order, and that the actions follow the organisation’s approved cross-domain data handling policy. The system must also produce clear completion reports showing that the checks succeeded in the required sequence, so that anyone reviewing the records can confirm the process worked as intended.

##### Orchestrate cross-domain content filtering with ordered completion reports

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement a content filtering coordination engine for moving information between different security areas that (1) runs filtering activities in the approved order, (2) treats unusual actions and unexpected stopping of the filtering process as coordination errors, and (3) produces completion updates in the required order.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-4(29) enhancement requires that, when transferring information between different security domains, the organisation uses content filter orchestration engines to ensure filtering actions are coordinated, complete successfully, and provide completion proof in the correct sequence. The guidance also states that errors include unusual actions or unexpected stopping of the content filter process (which is different from the content being rejected because it does not meet policy requirements).

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create a workflow definition for each transfer type** that turns the approved cross-domain data handling policy into a clear, fixed sequence of steps (for example: intake → classify → filter/scan → transform/quarantine as required → deliver → finalise).
2. **Implement one cross-domain transfer entry point** in the application/integration layer so all cross-domain transfers go through the orchestration engine (no separate ad-hoc or manual filtering routes).
3. **Add coordination controls and tracking** so steps cannot run out of order; enforce dependencies between stages and record each stage transition.
4. **Implement error handling**: if the orchestration detects unusual actions or unexpected stopping/timeouts of the content filtering process, mark the run as failed and stop any further “success” reporting (do not produce a successful completion report).
5. **Produce audit-ready completion evidence**: for each transfer, create stage reports and a final completion report that include transfer ID, workflow version, stage statuses in order, timestamps, and the final result. Ensure the report order matches the workflow definition and what actually happened.
6. **Control and version workflow/policy changes**: store workflow definitions and policy mappings in version control; require change management approval and automated checks to prevent unauthorised changes to the step sequence.
7. **Send evidence to central logging/security information and event management (SIEM)**: forward orchestration run logs and completion reports to the organisation’s monitoring/logging platform using immutable retention or tamper-evident storage.

###### WHO
- **Application/Integration Architect**: defines workflow steps and maps them to the approved cross-domain data handling policy.
- **Security Engineering Lead**: confirms the error handling approach and evidence requirements.
- **Platform/Integration Engineers**: build the orchestration engine, implement entry point routing, and generate reports.
- **Change Manager / Governance**: ensures workflow/policy updates follow the organisation’s change management process.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each cross-domain transfer, the orchestration engine runs filtering steps in the approved order defined by the cross-domain data handling policy.
- If the content filtering process stops unexpectedly or shows unusual actions, the orchestration run is marked as failed and no successful completion report is produced.
- Each transfer produces a successful completion report (only when the run is successful) and stage reports that reflect the required sequence, including transfer ID, workflow version, ordered stage statuses, and timestamps.
- All cross-domain transfers use the orchestration entry point (no bypass routes).
- Orchestration logs and completion reports are available for audit in the central logging/security information and event management (SIEM) system with tamper-evident/immutable retention.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the order of steps for each workflow, based on the approved cross-domain data handling policy for each type of cross-domain data transfer.
- [ ] Set up one single transfer starting point that sends all transfers through the content filtering coordination engine.
- [ ] Set up orchestration controls to ensure stages run in the correct order and to stop tasks from running out of sequence.
- [ ] Set up failure handling so that unusual actions or unexpected stopping/timeouts cause the run to be marked as failed, and prevent any “success” completion reports from being sent.
- [ ] For each transfer, produce a stage-by-stage report and a final completion report. Each report must include the transfer ID, the workflow version, the status of each ordered stage, and the relevant timestamps.
- [ ] Add orchestration run logs and completion reports to a central logging and security information and event management (SIEM) system, and keep them for a set period using immutable or tamper-evident storage.
- [ ] Store workflow and policy definitions in version control and require change-management approval before updating the step sequence.


---

#### AC-4.30 — Filter Mechanisms Using Multiple Processes (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that when information is moved between separate security areas, it is checked for unsafe or unwanted content using more than one independent checking step. This way, if one check fails, it should not allow harmful material to pass through.

Without this, a single broken or bypassed check could create a weak point where harmful files, messages, or data could reach the wrong area. This could lead to fraud, disruption, or the exposure of sensitive information.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly state in its information-sharing rules that information moving between security areas is subject to multiple independent content checks. It must also write and follow step-by-step procedures that explain how the checks are carried out and what happens if one check fails.

The organisation must design and configure its systems so that these multiple checks are actually applied, keep records showing that the checks were carried out during transfers, and include this approach and the responsible roles in the organisation’s security plan.

##### Dual-engine content filtering for cross-domain transfers

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up content filtering for information moving between different security areas (domains) using **multiple independent checks** (for example, two separate scanning engines or stages). A transfer is only allowed when the required filtering results are achieved.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-4(30) enhancement requires that, when information is transferred between different security domains, organisations use **multiple processes** for content filtering. This guidance is intended to reduce the risk of a **single point of failure**.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Identify cross-domain transfer routes** for the application (for example, web/application programming interface (API) gateway, email relay, file transfer, message bus outbound) and define which source and destination zones/domains count as **different security domains**.
2. **Set up a multi-process filtering approach** at the point where transfers are enforced for those routes:
   - Use at least **two independent filtering processes** (separate services or processes with different configuration and decision logic), such as:
     - Engine A: malware and content signature scanning
     - Engine B: data loss prevention (DLP) policy classification and/or sandbox or dynamic analysis
   - Keep them independent by running each engine as its own separate process/service, with its own health checks, configuration objects, and log identifiers (for example, `engineName`, `stage`, `verdict`).
3. **Apply a “no bypass” decision rule**:
   - Configure the transfer decision so the final outcome is based on a rule such as **AND** (both engines must complete successfully and agree) or a defined **quorum**.
   - If one engine fails to complete, times out, or is unavailable, the system must **block or quarantine** the transfer (there must be no silent fallback to only one engine).
4. **Keep audit evidence for every transfer attempt**:
   - For each transfer, record one correlation identifier (for example, message ID, transaction ID, or file hash) and capture the result from each engine (verdict, reason codes, timestamps, engine version/configuration identifiers, and failure/timeout indicators).
5. **Document the design and how it operates**:
   - Update the system security plan and create or maintain procedures that describe the multi-process filtering workflow, how failures are handled (including what happens if an engine is down), and how incidents are escalated and managed.

###### WHO (roles responsible)
- **Application/Platform Security Architect**: designs the multi-process filtering approach, defines the decision rule (AND/quorum), and sets audit requirements.
- **Gateway/Integration Engineers**: implement the filtering pipeline and configure engine independence and failure behaviour.
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC) / security information and event management (SIEM) Operations**: checks that logs are collected correctly, correlated properly, and that alerts are raised for blocked/quarantined transfers and engine failures.
- **System Owner / Authorising Official**: ensures the approach is included in the system security plan and approved for operation.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every configured cross-domain transfer route, the final “allow” or “quarantine” decision is produced using **two independent filtering processes**.
- If either filtering process fails, times out, or is unavailable, the transfer is **not allowed** (it is blocked or quarantined) and the event can be audited.
- Audit logs show, for a sample of transfer attempts, the correlation ID plus **each engine’s verdicts and failure indicators**, sufficient to demonstrate that multi-process filtering occurred.
- The system security plan and procedures clearly describe the multi-process filtering workflow and how failures are handled for cross-domain transfers.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Identify every data-transfer route between different security domains, and define the security-domain boundaries each route passes through.
- [ ] Set up a filtering pipeline with multiple processes at the point where enforcement happens, using at least two independent engines or stages.
- [ ] Set the final decision rule to **AND** with a quorum, and place items in **block/quarantine** if any detection engine fails or does not respond in time.
- [ ] Set up logging for each transfer that records, for every engine used, the engine’s decision (verdict), the reason for that decision, the time it happened, and which engine made the decision.
- [ ] Update the system security plan and document procedures for the multi-step filtering process and what to do when it fails.
- [ ] Use test cases to confirm that bypass is not possible when one engine is unavailable, and that audit evidence is produced.


---

#### AC-4.31 — Failed Content Transfer Prevention (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that when information is shared between different parts of your organisation, anything that does not meet the required checks is stopped at the boundary and never reaches the other side. Without this, a corrupted or unsafe item could get through and then be stored, processed, or used in the receiving area, which could lead to damage, errors, or further security problems.

To meet this expectation, your organisation must:
- clearly identify which information transfers move between separate security areas
- ensure the system carries out the required checks before any transfer is completed
- block any item that does not pass the checks so it is not delivered in any form

You also need to document the rule and how it is enforced. This includes describing how the system is designed to stop failed items, configuring the relevant settings, keeping evidence of those settings, and keeping records showing that failed transfers were blocked. Named staff must be responsible for overseeing and maintaining this enforcement.

##### Reject-and-quarantine at cross-domain transfer boundaries

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Set up a control point at every transfer of information between different security areas, so that any content that fails the required checks is **rejected and never delivered** to the receiving security area. This includes ensuring it is not written to receiving inboxes, shared folders, message queues, storage, or used to trigger any later processing.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-4(31) enhancement requires that when information is moved between different security areas, the organisation **stops failed content from reaching the receiving area**. The guidance notes that content that fails filtering checks can **damage or disrupt systems** if it is allowed to transfer.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Identify and document all in-scope cross-area transfers** for the application (for example: application programming interface (API) calls, file transfers, message relays, event or record replication, and partner or supplier integrations). Define the approved transfer routes and interfaces.
2. **Send all in-scope transfers through one enforcement point per interface** (for example: a secure gateway, proxy, relay, or service) that checks the content **before** the receiving side accepts or delivers it.
3. **Apply pre-transfer validation** using the organisation’s required checks (for example: malware scanning, rules for content type and size, API schema checks, allow and deny lists, signature or integrity verification, and any data classification and data loss prevention (DLP) rules that apply to the transfer).
4. **Use “reject-and-quarantine”**:
   - If validation fails, the enforcement point must cause a hard failure in the sender’s workflow.
   - The content must not be delivered to the receiving area in any form.
   - Optionally, store the failed item in a **quarantine area** that is isolated from the receiving area and can only be accessed by authorised security operations staff.
5. **Ensure delivery is all-or-nothing** so the receiving area only receives and commits the content after validation succeeds (avoid approaches such as “send to a queue then scan”, where failed content could be picked up before the scan completes).
