6. **Create audit evidence for every attempted transfer**, including: transfer ID, sender identity, destination security area, interface, which validation rule(s) were triggered, and the final result (“blocked/rejected/not delivered”).
7. **Stop people from bypassing the control** by blocking any direct routes that avoid the enforcement point (for example: network controls, service-to-service allowlisting, and routing controls), and alert on any bypass attempts.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Owner / Solution Architect**: owns the list of cross-area transfers and the design of the boundary controls.
- **Security Engineering / Platform Team**: implements the gateway or relay enforcement, quarantine isolation, and audit logging.
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC) / Security Operations**: reviews blocked-transfer events and manages quarantine handling.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every documented cross-area transfer interface, traffic is enforced through the boundary enforcement point.
- For test transfers that fail the configured validation checks, the receiving area shows **no delivery** (no inbox, share, queue, or storage record, and no downstream processing or action).
- Audit logs show the details of attempted transfers and a clear blocked or rejected outcome linked to the validation rule(s).
- Bypass attempts (direct-to-destination or incorrectly routed traffic) are prevented or detected and do not result in delivery to the receiving area.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Take an inventory of all cross-domain data transfer interfaces used by the application, map where they connect, and define the approved routes for transferring data between domains.
- [ ] Set up a boundary enforcement point (gateway, proxy, relay, or service) for each interface, and make sure every data transfer goes through it.
- [ ] Set up checks before data is transferred for each transfer type, such as malware/content/type checks, API format checks, approved (allow) and blocked (deny) lists, and integrity or signature verification.
- [ ] Set up a “reject and quarantine” process so any failed content is rejected and never sent to the receiving domain, with the quarantine kept isolated from the receiver.
- [ ] Ensure delivery is all-or-nothing, so the receiver only accepts and commits the content after validation is successful.
- [ ] Turn on audit logging for every attempted transfer, recording the transfer ID, sender, destination domain, the validation rule(s) applied, and whether the transfer was blocked or rejected.
- [ ] Block or detect any bypass routes that get around the enforcement point, and raise an alert when bypass attempts are detected.


---

#### AC-4.32 — Process Requirements for Information Transfer (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure information moves between different parts of your organisation only after it has been properly checked, and that it is sent on unchanged. Without this, sensitive or restricted information could be sent to the wrong place, or handled incorrectly because the instructions for how it should be treated were wrong or incomplete.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must use a transfer process that does not alter or filter the message content during handover. It must, however, check that the “filtering instructions” or labels that describe what should happen are valid. The organisation should only forward the content after the associated filtering step has finished successfully, and then send the approved content to the next stage.

The transfer method should be kept as simple and limited as possible, with built-in assurance that it is working correctly. This should be supported by clear procedures, design and configuration records, and audit logs.

##### Immutable payload transfer with metadata validation and success gating

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a transfer method between security “filter” processing pipelines that treats the information being moved as unchanged (no alteration of the content). Use filter instructions/labels only as validated control information (metadata). The method must send the information onward only after the related filtering step confirms it has completed successfully.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-4(32) requires that, when moving information between different security domains, the process that transfers information between filter pipelines has **the minimum necessary complexity and functions** to provide assurance that the processes work correctly. The guidance also requires that the transferring processes have **the minimum necessary complexity and functions** to provide assurance they operate correctly.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define an information-flow agreement**: treat the message as an unchangeable payload (exactly the same bytes) plus a separate metadata/envelope that includes, at minimum, a correlation identifier, a filter policy identifier/version, and the destination pipeline identifier.
2. **Check filtering metadata before using it**: in the transfer component, strictly validate the metadata structure (data types, lengths, allowed values) and validate its trust (for example, metadata is signed by an authorised component or comes from a trusted policy registry). Do not transfer any payload whose metadata fails validation.
3. **Link metadata to the payload context**: require that the correlation identifier in the metadata matches the processing context for that payload. Do not determine the destination mapping from untrusted input.
4. **Use explicit success “gating”**: connect the transfer component to the filtering step so it forwards only when it receives a clear success result (for example, a specific event/message) from the filtering step for the same correlation identifier. If the filtering step fails, times out, or returns a non-success result, do not forward.
5. **Ensure exact pass-through**: configure the transfer component to forward the payload exactly as received, without normalisation, removal/redaction, re-encoding, or rewriting the content. If transport framing is needed, keep that framing outside the payload.
6. **Record assurance evidence**: for every transfer attempt, record auditable events showing the metadata validation result, whether the correlation matched, the filtering success/failure outcome, and the time the destination hand-off occurred.
7. **Keep the transfer logic minimal**: implement the transfer component as a small, single-purpose service/workflow that does only: (a) metadata validation, (b) correlation linking, (c) success gating, and (d) exact forwarding of the unchangeable payload.

###### WHO
- **Application/Platform Engineer**: implement the transfer component, metadata schema validation, correlation linking, and success-gating integration.
- **Security Architect**: define the information-flow agreement, the metadata trust model, and the policy registry/signing approach.
- **Security Operations (SOC) / Monitoring Engineer**: set up alerts and review the audit evidence.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every transfer between filter pipelines, the payload is forwarded **byte-for-byte unchanged** (confirmed by a test harness comparing payload hashes before and after the transfer).
- The transfer component **never forwards** if metadata validation fails, correlation linking fails, or the filtering step does not return an explicit success for the same correlation identifier.
- Audit logs exist for each transfer attempt showing the metadata validation outcome, filtering completion status, and the destination hand-off time.
- The transfer component code/configuration is demonstrably minimal, covering only the required responsibilities for validation, gating, and exact pass-through forwarding.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define an unchangeable message body, and a separate agreement for how the supporting information (the “envelope”) is sent, including a correlation identifier, a filter policy identifier and version, and a destination pipeline identifier.
- [ ] Implement strict checks that the metadata follows the required format, and verify that the metadata is trustworthy (for example, by using signed metadata or by looking it up in a trusted policy register) in the transfer component.
- [ ] Add checks that link related metadata together, so the metadata correlation identifier must match the current payload processing context before any decision is made to forward the data.
- [ ] Integrate with the filtering stage by using a clear success or failure result linked to the same correlation identifier, and forward the block unless success is received.
- [ ] Set up the transfer component to forward data exactly as received, without changing the content (for testing, compare the data byte-for-byte).
- [ ] For every transfer attempt, record the audit evidence (the metadata validation result, whether the records matched, the filtering outcome, and the time the hand-off to the destination was made).
- [ ] Set up automated tests and monitoring alerts for these failure scenarios: forwarding without success, metadata validation failure, and a correlation mismatch.


---

### AC-5 — Separation of Duties (Control)

This requirement is designed to ensure that the same person does not both (1) have control of powerful systems and (2) approve or check the evidence that those controls were used correctly. If this separation is not in place, one person could quietly change access rights, alter the rules that control access, and then review the records in a way that conceals wrongdoing—whether accidental or deliberate—across all systems covered by the organisation’s policy.

To meet this requirement, the organisation must clearly identify and document who is responsible for three separate duties: administering privileged access, making security policy or role changes, and reviewing and approving audit log information. The organisation must then set up access permissions so that these responsibilities are separated in practice. This includes ensuring that no single person is authorised to both administer privileged access and handle audit log review or approval. Where required, the same separation rules must also apply to security policy or role changes across all relevant systems and components.

##### Enforce separation of duties for privileged admin, policy change, audit approval

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Set up and document three separate types of duties—(1) managing privileged access, (2) making security policy or role changes, and (3) reviewing and approving audit logs. Make sure no single person (or job role) can carry out more than one of these duties where separation is required.

###### WHY (control requirement)
Control AC-5 requires separation of duties to reduce the risk of misuse of authorised privileges and harmful activity, including where people might work together. It also expects separation across the organisation’s systems and components—specifically ensuring that security staff who manage access control do not also manage audit log activities.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create an organisation-defined separation-of-duties (SoD) matrix** covering the application’s full scope (production, test, disaster recovery as applicable). Use the duty types listed in the organisation-defined parameter **“duties of individuals requiring separation”**, and map them to named roles or teams (for example: privileged account administrators; security policy/role change implementers and approvers; audit log reviewers and approvers).
2. **Set clear “no-overlap” rules** in the SoD matrix for duty pairs that must not be held by the same person, including:
   - Managing privileged access vs reviewing and approving audit logs.
   - Managing privileged access vs making security policy or role changes (where your organisation’s SoD policy requires separation for these duties).
   - Security staff who manage access control must not manage audit activities.
   Include exception handling: what counts as an exception, what risk acceptance is required, what compensating controls are needed, and any time limits.
3. **Enforce separation using identity and access controls** across all in-scope systems and components:
   - Set up identity groups or roles so that privileged access administration actions (for example, managing privileged accounts, approving privileged access requests, changing privileged entitlements) are only allowed for the “Privileged Admin” role.
   - Set up audit log review and approval access so it is only allowed for the “Audit Reviewer/Approver” role, with permissions limited to viewing audit evidence and approving actions in the workflow.
   - Set up security policy or role change permissions so they are only allowed for the “Policy/Role Change” role(s), and ensure changes follow the defined approval route.
4. **Use separate workflows for privileged actions and policy/role changes** (for example, ticketing or governance, risk and compliance workflow). Make sure the person approving or reviewing is different from the person carrying out the privileged action or implementing the change. Ensure workflow records capture the approver’s identity, the time of approval, and links to supporting evidence.
5. **Carry out periodic SoD checks and collect evidence**:
   - Produce an auditable report showing current role-to-person assignments and confirming there are no conflicting duty combinations (or listing approved exceptions with the required compensating controls).
   - Store evidence in the organisation’s governance, risk and compliance repository (the SoD matrix, exports of access authorisation settings, workflow approval logs, and exception approvals).

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Information Security / Identity and Access Management (IAM) Governance Lead**: owns the SoD matrix, exception criteria, and the schedule for verification.
- **Privileged Access Management (PAM) / IAM Administrator**: sets up identity role mappings and enforces access authorisation.
- **Security Operations / Security Operations Centre (SOC) Audit Team Lead**: owns the audit log review and approval workflow and assigns reviewer roles.
- **Application Owner / System Owner**: ensures the SoD matrix is applied to the application’s in-scope environments and that any exceptions are properly justified.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A documented SoD matrix exists for the application’s in-scope systems and components, clearly defining the three duty types and the required no-overlap rules.
- For all in-scope environments, access authorisations are configured so no user can hold conflicting duty roles at the same time (at minimum: managing privileged access and reviewing and approving audit logs).
- Privileged access administration actions and security policy or role changes (where applicable under your SoD policy) are carried out through workflows that require approvals from different authorised roles.
- Audit log review and approval are carried out only by authorised “Audit Reviewer/Approver” personnel who are different from “Privileged Admin” personnel.
- Audit evidence is available, including current role assignments, workflow approval records, and any approved exceptions with compensating controls and time limits.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a separation-of-duties matrix that lists the application’s in-scope environments and shows how the three types of responsibilities map to specific named roles or teams.
- [ ] Define and get approval for clear “no overlap” rules between privileged access administration and audit log review and approval (and any required policy or role changes). Include the criteria for exceptions and the compensating controls to use when an exception is granted.
- [ ] Set up identity permissions so that **Privileged Administrator** roles cannot access audit log review or approval functions, and **Audit Reviewer/Approver** roles cannot manage privileged access.
- [ ] Route requests for privileged actions and changes to security policy or roles through separate ticket and workflow approvals, with the approving people different from the people who implement the changes.
- [ ] Carry out an initial and ongoing segregation of duties (SoD) conflict check across all in-scope systems and components, and record the results (including any approved exceptions) in the governance, risk and compliance (GRC) repository.
- [ ] Store audit-ready evidence: segregation of duties (SoD) matrix, exports of access authorisation settings, workflow approval records, and records of approved exceptions


---

### AC-6 — Least Privilege (Control)

This requirement is about ensuring people and automated tasks only have the access they genuinely need to do their jobs, and no more. Without this, a mistake or misuse could allow someone to view, change, or damage information or systems they were never meant to touch, and automated tasks could cause wider harm if they are compromised.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must have a clear written rule for least privilege (only the minimum access needed) and keep it up to date. It must also have step-by-step procedures for requesting, approving, changing, and removing access. The organisation must keep a current, documented list of what each person is allowed to do, and ensure system settings match that list.

The organisation must also document and keep up to date the system settings, keep relevant records of access activity for review, and maintain a plan showing how least privilege is applied—including ensuring background processes run with no higher privileges than they need.

##### Least-privilege RBAC with JIT elevation and access matrix evidence

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement least privilege for the enterprise business application by (1) defining an authoritative role and permission catalogue, (2) keeping a current list of who is allowed to access what (access matrix) with reasons, (3) enforcing those permissions through the integrated identity provider and the application’s own access controls, and (4) ensuring privileged access is time-limited and approved (just-in-time (JIT)) rather than permanent.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-6 requires using least privilege so users (and processes acting on behalf of users) are given only the access they are authorised to perform their assigned organisational tasks. This includes applying least privilege to system processes and ensuring access is both implemented and evidenced.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Create and maintain a least-privilege access model** for the application: define roles and detailed permissions based on job functions and application tasks (for example, read-only, create, approve, and admin-console access). Require a **task justification** and link each permission to the minimum data and functionality it covers.
2. **Maintain a current access authorisation list** (access matrix) that records: user, group, and role mapping; the permission set; the environment (production, test, development, disaster recovery); and the justification and approver. This list must be the single source of truth for what the application should allow.
3. **Enforce authorisations technically using the enterprise identity provider (IdP)**: configure role and group information and access conditions so only the mapped roles can access the application’s endpoints, user interface functions, and application programming interfaces (APIs). Ensure the application performs server-side access checks based on those role and group details (not only user interface controls).
4. **Implement time-limited privileged access (just-in-time (JIT))** for administrative functions (for example, configuration changes, user and role management, and production data administration). Any privileged access must be approved, recorded in logs, and automatically removed when the time limit ends.
5. **Apply least privilege to processes acting on behalf of users**: for application programming interface (API) calls and background jobs that use a user’s context, ensure they inherit only the caller’s permitted access (for example, OAuth scopes and claims) and do not gain extra privileges. For background services, use dedicated service identities with narrowly defined permissions for each job.
6. **Produce audit-ready evidence**: centralise logs for authentication and authorisation decisions (where possible), role and group changes, privileged access events, and administrative actions. Limit access to logs to authorised reviewers and keep logs for the required period under organisational policy.

###### WHO
- **Application Owner / Product Owner**: approves the role and permission catalogue and the mapping from tasks to permissions.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Engineer / Security Engineer**: sets up identity provider role and group mappings, access conditions, and just-in-time (JIT) privileged access workflows.
- **Application Security Lead / Backend Engineer**: implements server-side access controls and ensures APIs and background jobs enforce least privilege.
- **Operations Lead**: ensures service identities and environment-specific permissions are correctly set up and kept up to date.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The application has an approved role and permission catalogue and an access authorisation list that is current for each environment.
- For each user role, the permissions the application actually grants match the access authorisation list (no permanent admin or data access for non-admin roles).
- Privileged access to administrative functions is granted only through time-limited, approval-based just-in-time (JIT) elevation and is automatically removed.
- Application programming interface (API) and background processes acting on behalf of users do not increase privileges; they operate within the caller’s permitted access or using a dedicated least-privilege service identity.
- Audit evidence exists showing: role and group changes, privileged access events (who, what, when, and expiry time), and administrative actions. Logs must be retained and available to authorised reviewers.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and get approval for an application role and permission list, including the reasons for each permission and the minimum data and functions each role can access.
- [ ] Create and keep an up-to-date access authorisation list (access matrix) for each environment, and link it to identity provider (IdP) groups and roles.
- [ ] Set up the organisation’s identity provider (IdP) to include role and permission information in login responses, and apply conditional access rules for the application.
- [ ] Carry out authorisation checks on the server for every user interface action and every application programming interface (API) endpoint, based on the assigned roles and permissions.
- [ ] Set up just-in-time (JIT) privileged access for administrative tasks, where access is granted only after approval, lasts for a limited time, is automatically removed afterwards, and every use is recorded in logs.
- [ ] Provide dedicated service accounts for background jobs, and make sure actions that run in the user’s context only use the permissions the caller is allowed to use.
- [ ] Set up centralised audit logs for sign-ins, changes to roles and groups, requests to gain elevated (privileged) access, and administrative actions. Limit access to these logs to authorised reviewers.


---

#### AC-6.1 — Authorize Access to Security Functions (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that only a small, named group of trusted roles can access and change the tools that protect your systems and decide who is allowed in. Without this, the wrong people could quietly weaken key defences, change the rules that block unwanted network traffic, alter how suspicious activity is detected, switch off or tamper with what is recorded for investigations, or change the information used to manage encryption keys—leaving the organisation exposed to fraud, data theft, and attacks that go undetected.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must explicitly authorise only the following roles to manage the security functions used for access decisions: System Security Officer, Security Administrator, System Administrator, System Programmer, and Privileged Access Manager. This includes managing hardware and software protections and the related firmware settings, as well as security-relevant configuration data such as firewall and router access rules, detection settings, audit recording settings, and encryption key management information.

The organisation must document who is allowed to do this, keep clear procedures that follow the principle of giving the minimum access needed, set up system accounts and permissions accordingly, and keep records showing the authorisations and the system audit trail for changes.

##### PAM-gated RBAC for security functions and security-relevant configs

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Put in place a clear authorisation process so that only the organisation-defined privileged roles—**System Security Officer, Security Administrator, System Administrator, System Programmer, and Privileged Access Manager**—can access and change **security functions** and **security-relevant information** that are used to make access decisions.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST **AC-6(1)** (enhancement) requires that access is authorised for the specified set of people and roles (**ac-06.01_odp.01: individuals and roles**) to the security functions and security-relevant information types set out in the control guidance. This includes, for example: accounts, access permissions, audit event settings, and intrusion detection parameters. It also includes security-relevant information such as firewall/router access control lists, intrusion detection system/intrusion prevention system parameters, security information and event management (SIEM) audit event settings, and cryptographic key management metadata.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create an authorisation matrix** that lists what each of the five privileged roles is allowed to do (for example, view versus change) for every category of security function and every category of security-relevant information.
2. **Control access through a Privileged Access Management (PAM) broker** for all administrative sessions that change security functions, including:
   - **Hardware**: hardware security module (HSM) management interfaces, firewall/router management controls, dedicated intrusion detection system (IDS) appliances.
   - **Software**: host-based IDS, endpoint security agent management, SIEM administrative interfaces, operating system (OS) security configuration services.
   - **Firmware**: where the organisation can access it through vendor management tools, restrict actions related to secure boot, unified extensible firmware interface (UEFI), and trusted platform module (TPM) configuration to the authorised roles.
3. **Apply role-based access control (RBAC) on the target systems** for each security function, so that only identities managed through PAM can make configuration changes (for example, firewall/router access control list edits, IDS/IPS detection parameter changes, SIEM audit-event setting changes, and cryptographic key metadata changes).
4. **Restrict changes to cryptographic key metadata** so that only the **Privileged Access Manager** and **System Security Officer** (or an equivalent role under your role mapping) can update key-management metadata and related HSM configuration.
5. **Enable and protect auditability**: require session recording and command logging for all PAM-controlled administrative actions, and ensure the resulting logs are sent to the SIEM. Also restrict who can change SIEM audit-event settings to the authorised roles.
6. **Keep evidence and manage changes**: store the authorisation matrix and PAM policy configuration in version control with an approval workflow, and keep evidence of authorisations and configuration changes for audit purposes.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Privileged Access Manager (PAM owner)**: sets up PAM broker policies, session recording, and evidence retention.
- **System Security Officer**: checks and approves the authorisation matrix and the mapping of roles to actions for security functions and security-relevant information.
- **Security Administrator / System Administrator**: implements target-side RBAC and ensures administrative interfaces are connected to PAM.
- **System Programmer**: helps integrate security configuration services/application programming interfaces (APIs) with PAM and RBAC.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Only the five named privileged roles can successfully make **configuration changes** to: firewall/router access control lists and filtering rules, IDS/IPS detection parameters, SIEM audit-event settings, and cryptographic key management metadata.
- All privileged administrative actions to security functions are carried out through PAM (or an equivalent broker) with **session recording/command logging** enabled.
- Attempts by non-authorised roles to access security-function management interfaces are blocked and recorded.
- The organisation can provide an **authorisation matrix** and PAM policy evidence showing which roles are authorised to perform which actions for the defined security functions and security-relevant information categories.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a role-to-action authorisation matrix covering the five privileged roles for each security function and each category of security-relevant information.
- [ ] Connect all security administration systems—hardware security modules (HSMs), firewalls and routers, intrusion detection and prevention systems (IDS/IPS), security information and event management (SIEM), endpoint security, and operating system security configuration services—to a privileged access management (PAM) broker.
- [ ] Set up privileged access management (PAM) rules so only authorised roles can make configuration changes. Deny this permission by default for all other roles.
- [ ] Set up role-based access control (RBAC) on the target systems so that only identities managed by privileged access management (PAM) can change firewall and router access control lists (ACLs), intrusion detection system/intrusion prevention system (IDS/IPS) settings, security information and event management (SIEM) audit-event settings, and cryptographic key information.
- [ ] Enable session recording and command logging for all privileged access management (PAM)-mediated administration of security functions, and send the logs to the security information and event management (SIEM) system.
- [ ] Limit changes to cryptographic key information to the Privileged Access Manager and the System Security Officer, and document that these roles are kept separate.
- [ ] Store the privileged access management (PAM) policy and the authorisation matrix in version control, using an approval workflow, and keep audit records showing who approved access and any changes made.


---

#### AC-6.2 — Non-privileged Access for Nonsecurity Functions (Enhancement)

This expectation is about making sure that people who can view sensitive security information or use security tools do not use their highest level of access for routine, non-security work. The real-world risk is that if someone uses a highly privileged account for everyday tasks, then a mistake, misuse, or compromise could expose security records or settings. This could make it easier for an attacker to hide their actions or change protections.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly document:
- which security-related information and security tools are covered (including audit logs, security monitoring screens, and security configuration settings), and
- which user accounts or roles have access to them.

It must also define:
- what counts as non-privileged access, and
- what counts as non-security tasks.

The system must then be configured so that access to non-security functions is only allowed when the person is using a non-privileged account, or (where roles are used) an equivalent non-privileged role.

The organisation must document the approach, assign responsibilities to the right staff, configure the system to enforce it, and keep records showing that the rule is being followed.

##### Enforce non-privileged role for nonsecurity functions

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up technical controls so that users who can access **security functions or security-relevant information** (for example: audit logs, security monitoring consoles, security configuration management) can use **nonsecurity functions** only when they are logged in with **non-privileged accounts or roles**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
This requirement (NIST AC-6(2) enhancement) means that when users use system accounts or roles that can access the organisation-defined **security functions or security-relevant information**, they must use **non-privileged accounts or roles** when they access **nonsecurity functions**. This reduces risk by limiting what can be accessed while operating from within privileged accounts or roles.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define what is in scope and how roles are grouped**: Create an official list that maps application features and endpoints into two groups: (a) covered **security functions or security-relevant information** and (b) **nonsecurity functions**. Also define which identity provider (IdP) or application roles are **privileged** versus **non-privileged** for this scope.
2. **Separate access rules for security and nonsecurity actions**: Configure the application (and any application programming interface (API) gateways) so that every **nonsecurity function** endpoint or workflow requires a **non-privileged role** whenever the user is in any covered privileged security role.
3. **Require role switching with the same level of protection**: If a user needs to carry out security-related tasks, allow them to switch to the covered privileged role. But when they access **nonsecurity functions**, require switching to the equivalent **non-privileged** role. Make sure the protection level is the same (for example: the same multi-factor authentication (MFA) strength, device or session checks, and the same session management approach), changing only what the user is authorised to do.
4. **Stop reusing privileged access**: Ensure the application does not treat a privileged login as enough to access **nonsecurity functions**. Use separate permission checks for each role, and do not allow privileged-role tokens or sessions to access nonsecurity endpoints.
5. **Create audit evidence**: Record, in both the IdP and the application, the user identity, the role or entitlement used, whether the target is security or nonsecurity, and whether access was allowed or denied. Collect these logs in a security information and event management (SIEM) system so auditors can confirm that nonsecurity access from users in covered security roles happened only under **non-privileged** roles.
6. **Run governance and change control**: Keep a controlled record of the configuration (for example, identity and access management (IAM) policy-as-code and application authorisation settings) and require change management for updates to role mappings and endpoint category assignments.

###### WHO
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) / Identity Engineer**: builds the IdP role structure, conditional access rules, and role switching/session controls.
- **Application Security / Backend Engineer**: implements endpoint-level permission rules and prevents privileged-session reuse.
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC) / Compliance**: checks the audit evidence and supports periodic reviews.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For all users assigned roles that grant access to the defined **security functions or security-relevant information**, attempts to access **nonsecurity functions** are **blocked** unless the user is operating under the defined **non-privileged** role.
- Role switching is enforced so that nonsecurity access is carried out only under the equivalent **non-privileged** role, with no way to reuse a privileged session for nonsecurity access.
- Audit logs show, for each nonsecurity access attempt, which role was used and whether access was allowed or denied, and evidence can be provided for compliance review.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an authoritative inventory that maps application endpoints and workflows to the security functions and information that are covered, versus those that are not security-related.
- [ ] Define which users have privileged access and which users have non-privileged access in the identity provider (IdP) for the security functions and information covered by this scope.
- [ ] Set up authorisation for the application programming interface (API) gateway so that users with covered security roles can only access non-security functions using non-privileged roles.
- [ ] Set up role switching rules so that non-security access is allowed only when it uses the same level of non-privileged role, with the same session assurance controls.
- [ ] For non-security endpoints, do not reuse privileged roles or sessions. Instead, check that the user is authorised for the specific role on every request.
- [ ] Enable and centralise audit records of each user’s identity, the role they used, the function category, and whether actions were allowed or denied, so this information can be used as evidence.
- [ ] Add policy-as-code and change-management controls for updates to role mappings and endpoint category assignments.


---

#### AC-6.3 — Network Access to Privileged Commands (Enhancement)

This requirement is about letting trusted staff make important system changes remotely, but only when there is a real and urgent need. If remote access is not tightly controlled, someone could use it to run powerful commands when they should not, which could lead to service outages, data loss, or unauthorised changes that are difficult to trace.

The organisation must approve which remote methods are allowed for running system administration commands (for example, approved tools such as `sudo`, PowerShell remoting, or vendor administration command tools). It must also allow network-based access to those commands for 24 hours a day, 7 days a week (24x7) only for on-call support used for time-critical incident response and maintenance when it is not practical to attend the device locally. The organisation must record the reason for granting this remote access in the organisation’s security plan, including the specific operational need that justifies it.

##### Policy-gated PAM/remote admin for approved privileged commands

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a controlled and auditable way to run **network-based privileged commands** only through **approved privileged access tools**, and only for **authorised staff with a strong operational reason** (specifically **24/7 on-call**, for **time-critical incident response and maintenance** when **local access is not practical**). Make sure the **reason for each authorisation** is recorded and referenced in the **system security plan**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-6(3) (enhancement) requires that network access to **{{ insert: param, ac-06.03_odp.01 }} (privileged commands)** is authorised **only for {{ insert: param, ac-06.03_odp.02 }} (strong operational needs)**, and that the **reason for this access** is **documented in the system security plan**.

###### HOW (specific approach)
###### 1) Define and enforce the approved privileged command list (tool-based)
- Create an inventory for each system of the **exact privileged command actions** that can be run remotely (for example: Linux **sudo** command allowlists, Windows PowerShell remote access endpoints, and vendor administration command profiles).
- Configure the privileged access platform (privileged access management (PAM) / zero trust network access (ZTNA)) so remote sessions can use **only** the **approved tool workflows** and **allowed command profiles** (no ad-hoc shells or unrestricted command execution).

###### 2) Allow network access to privileged commands only when operationally necessary
- Put in place a policy check that requires the requester to be:
  - **On-call** (included in the **24/7 roster**), and
  - Carrying out **time-critical incident response and maintenance**, and
  - Confirming/attesting that **local access is not practical**.
- Require a **ticket/incident reference** (or equivalent operational record) and record the **strong operational need reason** at the start of the session.

###### 3) Ensure auditability and evidence for the security plan rationale
- Turn on command-level logging and capture session details (who, when, which system, which approved command profile, justification fields, ticket reference, and approvals if applicable).
- Keep logs for the required period and ensure they can be exported for audit.

###### 4) Update the system security plan with the authorised reason
For each affected system, update the security plan to include:
- The **approved privileged command tools** (linked to the command profiles),
- The **operational need definition** used for authorisation (24/7 on-call, time-critical incident response/maintenance, and local access not practical), and
- How the PAM/ZTNA workflow records and retains the **reason** for each network-based privileged command session.

###### WHO
- **Privileged Access / PAM Administrator**: sets up PAM/ZTNA, command allowlists, and session logging.
- **System Owner / Security Owner**: ensures the system security plan is updated with the authorised reason and operational need definition.
- **On-call Duty Manager / Incident Manager**: ensures the on-call roster is correct and that incident/ticket references are used.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Network-based privileged command execution is possible **only** through the approved privileged command tools/workflows and **only** for sessions that pass the policy check (on-call + time-critical incident/maintenance + local access not practical + justification/ticket reference).
- The privileged access platform generates **audit records** that include the justification reason fields and the approved command profile used for each session.
- The **system security plan** includes the documented reason for network access to privileged commands, including the operational need and why local access is not practical, and references the implemented workflow/logging mechanism.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] For each system, list and approve the privileged command profiles, and identify the approved remote tools that can run them.
- [ ] Set up privileged access management (PAM) and zero trust network access (ZTNA) to handle all privileged command sessions that use the network, and allow execution only for the pre-approved command profiles.
- [ ] Put in place a rule at the start of a session that only allows access when the person is on the on-call rota, the request is linked to a time-critical incident or maintenance activity, and there is a documented reason why local access is not practical, with a reference to the relevant ticket or incident.
- [ ] Enable and keep detailed audit records of commands, including the user’s identity, the system targeted, the type of command run, and the reason recorded for why it was executed.
- [ ] Update each affected system’s security plan to record the authorised operational reasons for the approach (24/7 on-call support, responses and maintenance that must happen quickly, and the fact that local access is not practical). Also, link to the implemented working process and the sources of supporting evidence.
- [ ] Test and confirm that direct remote administration routes (for example, unrestricted shells or admin network ports) are blocked, or that they cannot be used to run privileged commands outside the approved privileged access management (PAM) and zero trust network access (ZTNA) process.


---

#### AC-6.4 — Separate Processing Domains (Enhancement)

This requirement is about keeping different parts of a system clearly separated, so people only have the permissions they truly need for the specific area they are working on. Without this separation, a user who has extra rights in one area could potentially use them to access other areas, or even the underlying platform that runs everything. This increases the risk of unauthorised changes, data exposure, or wider impact if something goes wrong.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must design and set up the system using separate, isolated processing areas (for example, separate virtual machines, separate physical environments, or other built-in isolation methods). It must also ensure that permissions do not carry over between these areas, or to the underlying platform.

The organisation must document the access rules and the principle of least privilege, keep clear records of the system design and configuration that show the separation is in place, and maintain audit records of domain access and any changes to privileges. It must also include the approach and responsibilities in the system security plan, with named individuals responsible for defining least-privilege access, supported by information security and system developers.

##### Isolated VM domains with enforced privilege boundaries

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up separate processing areas using virtualisation (for example, dedicated virtual machines (VMs) or VM clusters). This allows user permissions to be granted within one area, while preventing access to other areas and blocking access to the underlying physical systems and management functions.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-6(4) (Enhancement) requires separate processing areas so user permissions can be assigned more precisely. The guidance specifically includes using virtualisation to allow additional user permissions inside a virtual machine, while limiting those permissions from applying to other virtual machines or to the underlying physical machine.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the boundaries** by mapping business functions and administrative tasks to separate VMs (for example, “App Domain”, “Data Domain”, “Admin/Operations Domain”). Avoid “shared admin” approaches where one administrator identity can manage multiple domains.
2. **Secure the management functions** so only a dedicated privileged administrator identity can access the hypervisor management interfaces (console and application programming interface (API)). Block normal users from any route to hypervisor credentials, management sockets, or host log-in.
3. **Control network traffic between domains** using virtual network segmentation and default-deny rules. Allow only the required application and data traffic. Explicitly block cross-domain administrative connections and protocols.
4. **Apply least privilege within each domain** by using domain-specific role-based access control (RBAC) and operating system (OS) groups, with separate administrative roles for each domain. Ensure that an “Admin for Domain A” cannot carry out administrative actions in “Domain B”, including through shared credentials, shared admin groups, or shared service accounts.
5. **Automate and regularly check separation** using infrastructure-as-code (and policy-as-code where available) to prevent changes from drifting over time (for example, VM security baselines, network access control lists (ACLs) and security groups, and administrative role assignments).
6. **Create audit evidence** by enabling and centralising logs for: (a) attempts to access each domain, (b) changes to permissions and roles within each domain, and (c) management-function actions (hypervisor console and API use). Keep logs according to organisational policy.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Infrastructure/Cloud platform engineer**: design the VM domain layout, secure the hypervisor management functions, and implement network segmentation.
- **Application security lead / identity and access management (IAM) engineer**: define and implement domain-specific RBAC/role mappings and controls for privileged identities.
- **Security operations (SOC) / security information and event management (SIEM) engineer**: set up central logging, alerts, and evidence retention.
- **System owner / change manager**: approve domain boundary changes through the change management process.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Each defined processing domain runs in its own VM (or VM cluster) with documented boundaries.
- Normal users from one domain cannot access administrative functions in other domains and cannot access the underlying host or management functions.
- Network controls enforce default-deny between domains, except for explicitly required traffic.
- Permission and role assignments are limited to each domain (no shared administrator identities or groups across domains).
- Centralised logs show domain access attempts and permission changes, and record hypervisor management-function actions so they can be reviewed.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a domain map that assigns business functions and administrative tasks to separate virtual machines (VMs) (or groups of VMs), with clearly documented boundaries.
- [ ] Limit access to the hypervisor management “control” functions to a dedicated privileged user group, and block host access routes for all non-management users.
- [ ] Set up virtual network segmentation so that traffic between different network areas is blocked by default, and allow only the specific application and data connections that are explicitly required.
- [ ] Set up domain-local role-based access control (RBAC) and operating system (OS) groups so that administrative roles are limited to each domain and cannot be used to administer other domains.
- [ ] Use infrastructure-as-code and policy-as-code to enforce virtual machine (VM) security hardening, network access control lists (ACLs), and domain-scoped administrator role assignments, and to enable configuration drift detection.
- [ ] Set up and centralise audit logs for attempts to access the domain, changes to privileges or roles, and actions made through the hypervisor management interface; check that these logs are reviewed in security information and event management (SIEM).


---

#### AC-6.5 — Privileged Accounts (Enhancement)

This expectation is about keeping control of the system’s “keys” tightly restricted to the right people. If ordinary staff could use powerful administrator or super-user accounts, they could accidentally or on purpose change security settings, view sensitive information, or leave the system in a weak and unsafe state.

To prevent this, the organisation must ensure that every privileged account on the system—including any built-in super-user accounts—can be used only by the authorised system administrator role and the authorised information system security officer (ISSO) role.

The organisation must also be able to show this in practice. It must keep clear written rules for who is allowed privileged access, step-by-step procedures for granting and removing the minimum access needed, an up-to-date list of all system-generated privileged accounts, a current list of authorised administrators, and documentation of the system settings that enforce these limits. It must also retain records that show privileged activity.

Finally, the organisation must include this approach in the system’s security plan and keep the supporting records, so it can demonstrate that privileged access is ongoing and limited.

##### Restrict privileged accounts to System Admin and ISSO roles

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Limit access to all privileged accounts on the system (including any built-in or “super-user” accounts and any operating system or application administrator accounts) so they can only be used by the authorised **System Administrator** role and the authorised **Information System Security Officer (ISSO)** role.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **AC-6(5)** (enhancement) requires that privileged accounts on the system are restricted to **{{ insert: param, ac-06.05_odp }}**. The organisation-defined parameter sets the authorised personnel/roles as the **system administrator role** and the **ISSO role**. This stops everyday users from accessing privileged information or privileged functions.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Set the official privileged roles** in the enterprise identity provider (IdP) and ensure only those roles can be linked to privileged capabilities (System Administrator and ISSO).
2. **List all privileged accounts** on the system (including built-in/super-user accounts, local administrator accounts, and any application or operating system administrator accounts) and classify them as either:
   - **Linked to identities** (mapped to IdP roles), or
   - **Created by the system/built-in** (must be disabled, renamed, or tightly restricted so they cannot be used except through the authorised roles).
3. **Apply the technical restriction** so privileged capabilities cannot be used by unauthorised identities:
   - Remove permanent privileged access from users who are not authorised.
   - For identity-linked administrator access, configure checks so access is granted only to members of the authorised roles.
   - For built-in/super-user accounts, disable them where possible; if not, use strong authentication and ensure they can only be used by the authorised roles (for example, through controlled password or credential management and “break-glass” procedures governed by the same roles).
4. **Use time-limited privilege elevation** (just-in-time (JIT) or just-enough-access (JEA), where supported) for privileged actions, so access is granted for a limited time and can be traced to an authorised role holder.
5. **Create and keep audit evidence** that the restriction is up to date and enforceable:
   - Keep an up-to-date list of privileged accounts and the authorised privileged administrators/personnel (aligned to the two roles).
   - Ensure privileged session records and administrative action records are sent to security information and event management (SIEM) and kept according to the organisation’s logging retention requirements.
6. **Document the approach in governance documents** (for example, the system security plan and access control procedures) describing the privileged-account restriction rule and the operational process for granting and removing privileged access.

###### WHO
- **System Owner / Application Owner**: responsible for ensuring privileged access is restricted for the application and its administrative functions.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Engineer**: sets up IdP role definitions, role mappings, and enforcement.
- **ISSO**: confirms that only the authorised ISSO role can obtain privileged capability and approves the privileged access procedures.
- **Infrastructure/Platform Engineer**: disables or locks down built-in/super-user accounts and ensures operating system/platform administrator access follows the same role restriction.

###### Acceptance criteria
- All privileged accounts (including built-in/super-user accounts) are either disabled/locked down or can only be used by identities mapped to the **System Administrator** role or the **ISSO** role.
- No unauthorised users can obtain privileged capability (confirmed through access reviews and a technical test of administrative functions).
- An auditable, current inventory exists showing privileged accounts and their authorised role mapping.
- Privileged actions are logged with enough detail to identify the authorised role holder, and logs are retained and available for audit.
- The system security plan/access control procedures document the restriction rule and the operational process for granting and removing privileged access.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create or confirm the official identity provider (IdP) roles for “System Administrator” and “ISSO”, and make sure that all high-privilege permissions are assigned only to these two roles.
- [ ] Create a complete list of all privileged accounts on the system, including built-in super-user accounts. Record their current status (enabled, disabled, or locked down) and how they can be used to access the system (their effective access route).
- [ ] Where possible, disable or restrict built-in “super-user” accounts. If this is not feasible, use controlled sign-in and ensure these accounts are only used for the authorised roles.
- [ ] Remove permanent high-level access rights from all users who are not authorised, and where supported, require temporary, time-limited permission increases based on role for any privileged actions.
- [ ] Set up administrative endpoints and functions so they allow only users in the authorised roles, and block all other users.
- [ ] Set up and confirm logging of privileged sessions and administrative actions in the security information and event management (SIEM) system, so that every log entry can be traced back to the authorised person holding the relevant role.
- [ ] Publish and keep up to date an auditable list of privileged accounts and the authorised privileged administrators or staff, aligned to the two roles.


---

#### AC-6.6 — Privileged Access by Non-organizational Users (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that only trusted people can perform high-level, sensitive system tasks, and that no one outside the organisation is ever given those powers. Without this, a contractor, visitor, or other person who is not an employee-equivalent could accidentally or deliberately change security settings, view confidential information, or take control of the system. This could cause serious financial, legal, and reputational harm.

To meet this requirement, the organisation must clearly define what “privileged access” means and what counts as a non-organisational user. It must then create and follow rules that explicitly prevent non-organisational users from being given any privileged role or account.

The organisation must also:
- keep up-to-date lists of non-organisational users and any system-created privileged accounts
- configure the system so these privileges cannot be assigned to non-organisational users
- include this restriction in its system plans
- keep audit records showing that attempts are blocked

It should regularly check that privileged access assignments match the lists, and review audit records to confirm that no successful privileged access occurred.

##### Block non-organisational users from privileged roles via IdP + PAM

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Ensure that **non-organisational users** (that is, people who are not employees or not treated as employee-equivalent) are **not allowed to get privileged access** to the application and its administrative functions.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-6(6)** enhancement requires: **“Prohibit privileged access to the system by non-organizational users.”** The guidance explains that organisational users include employees and people treated as equivalent (for example, contractors or guests) under agreed organisational criteria. Anyone who does not meet those criteria must be blocked from privileged access.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
Put in place an enforcement approach that combines (1) blocking at the identity level and (2) controlled handling of privileged sessions:
1. **Define what “privileged access” means in the application’s access rules**. This should be the set of roles/permissions that allow administrative or security-sensitive actions (for example, administrator roles, the ability to change security settings, access to administrative consoles/application programming interfaces (APIs), or any permission that gives elevated control of the system).
2. **Define non-organisational status using official identity information** (for example, employment type from human resources (HR) or an “employee-equivalent” flag). If the required employee-equivalent status is not present, treat the user as non-organisational.
3. **Set up the enterprise identity provider (IdP) to block privileged role assignment for non-organisational users** using conditional access/authorisation rules, so privileged groups/roles can only be assigned to organisational users. The default must be “deny” for privileged authorisation.
4. **Send all privileged actions through privileged access management (PAM)**. This ensures that even if a user can sign in, they cannot obtain privileged access tokens/sessions for administration without PAM approval and an extra “step-up” check.
5. **Connect the IdP and PAM** so PAM only approves elevation requests from organisational users (based on the same employee-equivalent attribute) and blocks elevation for non-organisational users.
6. **Keep evidence that can be checked later** by turning on logging for: attempts to assign privileged roles, PAM elevation requests, start/end of privileged sessions, and any blocked attempts. Review privileged assignments regularly against the organisational vs non-organisational determination.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Lead**: identity provider (IdP) attribute model; conditional access/authorisation rules.
- **Privileged Access Management (PAM) Administrator**: PAM integration; enforcing the elevation process.
- **Application Security/Platform Owner**: defining privileged roles and how they map to the application’s permissions.
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC) / Compliance**: reviewing audit logs and keeping evidence.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A non-organisational user (as defined by the employee-equivalent attribute) **cannot** be given privileged roles/groups in the IdP for the application.
- A non-organisational user **cannot** obtain a privileged PAM session or privileged tokens for administrative functions.
- Audit logs show **blocked** attempts and **no successful privileged access** for non-organisational users during a defined test period.
- Privileged role mappings and PAM elevation eligibility are clearly based on the organisational vs non-organisational determination (not manual exceptions).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and document which application roles and permissions count as privileged access for enforcing AC-6(6)
- [ ] Create or confirm an official identity provider (IdP) attribute that shows the person’s employee-equivalent status, using data from human resources (HR) and workforce systems, and managed through approved processes.
- [ ] Set up identity provider (IdP) access rules so that, by default, non-organisational users are blocked from being assigned privileged roles or groups.
- [ ] Connect the application’s administrative functions to privileged access management (PAM) so that any privileged session is handled through PAM and requires an additional identity check (step-up authentication).
- [ ] Set up privileged access management (PAM) eligibility so that permission to request elevated access is allowed only when the identity provider (IdP) employee-equivalent attribute shows that the user is an organisational user.
- [ ] Enable and keep audit logs for attempts to assign privileged roles, requests to elevate privileges, activity during privileged sessions, and any denials.
- [ ] Carry out a controlled test to prove that a user who is not part of the organisation cannot gain privileged access, and that any access denials are recorded in logs.


---

#### AC-6.7 — Review of User Privileges (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that only the right people and automated services have the highest levels of access, and that this access is still justified. Over time, job roles change, systems are updated or retired, and business needs shift. If higher access is left in place without regular checks, it increases the risk that someone could do more than they should, either by mistake or misuse, which could result in unauthorised changes or damage.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must review, at least every 90 days, the access privileges held by privileged users, system administrators, and service accounts with elevated access. For each one, it must confirm whether the access is still needed for the organisation’s mission and business needs, and promptly remove or reassign privileges that are no longer required.

The organisation must also keep clear records of what was reviewed, the reasons for each decision, and any privilege removals or reassignments. These records must be supported by the organisation’s documented access rules and its least-privilege approach.

##### 90-day privileged access recertification with automated entitlement changes

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Carry out a periodic review (at least every 90 days) of access rights given to **privileged users, system administrators, and elevated service accounts**, and **confirm that each access right is still needed**. If the need cannot be confirmed, **remove or reassign the access right(s) without delay** and keep records showing what was reviewed and what corrective actions were taken.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The NIST AC-6(7) enhancement requires the organisation to review user access rights regularly because the need for those rights can change over time. The review must check whether the reason for granting the access rights is still valid. If it cannot be confirmed, corrective actions must be taken.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Identify which access rights are in scope** within the enterprise identity and access management (IAM) system: privileged user roles/groups, system administrator roles, and **elevated service accounts** (including any system-generated roles/classes that are linked to identity records).
2. **Run a 90-day recertification campaign** that automatically lists all in-scope access entitlements (roles, groups, and direct access rights) for each environment (production, testing, disaster recovery) and for each application where relevant.
3. **Require reviewers to confirm each entitlement with evidence**, including:
   - the system/application and the access right/role name,
   - a required explanation linked to the current mission or business need,
   - a clear decision: **“still required”** or **“remove/reduce”**.
4. **Apply corrective actions through a controlled process**:
   - If “remove/reduce” is selected (or if the explanation is not sufficient or not valid), start an automated access change where it is safe and approved; otherwise, raise a change request that requires approval.
   - Record what changed (the access entitlement difference), the decision, the reviewer, dates and times, and the corrective action that was applied.
5. **Create audit-ready evidence for each cycle**, including: the campaign list, reviewer decisions and explanations, any exceptions (if there are any) with justification, and proof that access rights were removed or reassigned.
6. **Set clear ownership and escalation**: assign accountable roles (for example, an IAM/Access Governance owner; an application/system owner; and a security reviewer) and escalate if recertification is overdue or corrective actions are still pending.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Access Governance/IAM Owner**: sets up recertification campaigns, defines which entitlements are in scope, and ensures evidence is produced.
- **Application/System Owners (or delegated administrators)**: provide the business or operational explanation during attestation.
- **Information Security / Security Governance**: checks that explanations are high quality and that corrective actions meet least-privilege expectations, and reviews any exceptions.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A recertification campaign runs **at least every 90 days** and covers all in-scope categories: privileged users, system administrators, and elevated service accounts.
- For each entitlement reviewed, reviewers provide a **required explanation** and a clear decision.
- Where the need is not confirmed, access rights are **removed or reduced promptly** through an automated access change or an approved change process.
- Audit evidence exists for each cycle showing: the entitlements reviewed, reviewer decisions and explanations, access changes (or change tickets), and dates and times.
- System-generated roles/classes are included through identity-to-entitlement mapping, with **no manual gaps**.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Identify and label all in-scope privileged users, system administrators, and elevated service accounts (including system-generated roles and classes) in identity and access management (IAM).
- [ ] Set up an identity and access management (IAM) access review campaign to re-check permissions every 90 days for all in-scope access rights.
- [ ] Set mandatory sign-off fields for reviewers, including: the system or application, the privilege or role, an evidence-based reason for the change, and a decision on whether it is still required or should be removed or reduced.
- [ ] Automate the removal or reduction of access rights for “remove/reduce” outcomes, or send the request through an approved change process with required time limits.
- [ ] Enable audit logging and export of evidence packs for each cycle (roster, decisions and reasons, entitlement changes, timestamps, and corrective actions).
- [ ] Assign named responsible owners and set escalation rules for overdue reviews and outstanding corrective actions.


---

#### AC-6.8 — Privilege Levels for Code Execution (Enhancement)

This requirement is about preventing risky or unknown code from quietly gaining more access than the person who started it. If an unapproved or unsigned script, programme, or installer (including PowerShell, batch files, and Microsoft Installer (MSI) packages) is started using an elevated prompt, it must not end up running with higher privileges than the user who clicked to start it. Otherwise, a standard user could indirectly obtain access they are not authorised to have, which could lead to unauthorised changes, data theft, or a full system takeover.

To achieve this, the organisation must keep a documented list of software that is not allowed to run with higher privileges, including anything unsigned or not approved. It must also set clear rules that enforce this “no privilege gain” behaviour even when elevated prompts are used. Named roles must be responsible for keeping the list and rules accurate. Information security must review and approve them. System designs and settings must be documented and configured to enforce the restriction, and audit records must be kept to show attempts are blocked and the results are recorded.

##### Block unsigned/unapproved elevated execution via endpoint allowlisting

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Stop the organisation-defined **software** (parameter **ac-06.08_odp**) from running with higher permissions than the user who starts it. This specifically applies to **all un-signed or not-approved scripts, programmes, and installers (PowerShell, batch, MSI)** when they are started from an elevated prompt.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-6(8) (enhancement) requires: **“Prevent the following software from executing at higher privilege levels than users executing the software: {{ insert: param, ac-06.08_odp }}.”** The guidance notes the risk that elevated prompts can unintentionally give more permissions than intended.

###### HOW (specific approach)
Put a technical enforcement mechanism in place on computers (and any managed administrator workstations) that:
1) **Blocks by default** the prohibited categories (un-signed or not-approved PowerShell scripts, batch files, programmes, and MSI installers).
2) Ensures the enforcement applies **no matter how the user starts it** (for example, starting from an elevated prompt must not allow the prohibited software to run with higher permissions).
3) Uses an **approved catalogue** (hash/signature/publisher/version) so only items that are explicitly approved can run.
4) Sends legitimate privileged administration through **controlled administrator processes** (for example, approved management tools/packages) rather than allowing any arbitrary script/installer to run with elevated permissions.

###### Implementation steps (practical)
- Create and maintain an **Approved/Prohibited Execution Catalogue** for the covered software types, including the information needed for enforcement (publisher/signing certificate, hash, version, and intended use).
- Configure **endpoint application control** (or an equivalent enterprise execution control) in **block-by-default** mode for:
  - un-signed PowerShell scripts
  - un-signed or not-approved batch files
  - un-signed or not-approved programmes
  - un-signed or not-approved MSI installers
- Set rules so enforcement is based on **file identity (signature/hash/publisher)** and is checked **even when the process is started from elevated prompts**.
- Centralise rule distribution and change control using configuration management (policy-as-code), ensuring updates to the approved catalogue are reviewed for security impact.
- Send enforcement reporting to the **security information and event management (SIEM)** system (blocked execution events, signature/hash mismatch reasons, and process history) and raise alerts for repeated attempts.
- Test by trying to run each prohibited category from both standard and elevated prompts, and confirm the prohibited software cannot run (or cannot carry out privileged actions).

###### WHO (roles responsible)
- **Information Security (lead):** owns the approved/prohibited execution catalogue and approves rule changes.
- **Endpoint/Platform Engineering:** implements and maintains the endpoint application control configuration and its distribution.
- **System Owners / Administrator Workflow Owners:** define legitimate privileged processes that do not depend on arbitrary elevated scripts/installers.
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC)/SIEM Team:** adjusts detections and reporting for blocked elevated execution attempts.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every software item in the prohibited scope (ac-06.08_odp: un-signed/not-approved scripts/programmes/installers including PowerShell, batch, MSI), execution from an elevated prompt is **blocked** (or fails in a way that prevents it from running with higher permissions than the user who started it).
- Only items listed in the approved catalogue can run; un-signed/not-approved items are consistently blocked.
- Audit evidence is available: the configuration baseline, catalogue approval records, and SIEM logs showing blocked attempts and enforcement results.
- Test results confirm the “no privilege gain” requirement: an elevated prompt does not allow prohibited software to run with higher permissions than the user who started it.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and publish the restricted scope for AC-06.08_odp, covering unsigned or unapproved PowerShell scripts, batch files, executable files, and Microsoft Installer (MSI) packages that are run using elevated prompts.
- [ ] Create an approved execution catalogue that includes the publisher and signing certificate, a file hash, and a version number. Then set up a security review and approval process for any changes to the catalogue.
- [ ] Set up endpoint application control to block the prohibited categories by default, and make sure the system still checks and enforces this rule even when users have elevated prompts.
- [ ] Set up controlled processes for administrators so that legitimate high-privilege tasks use approved management tools or software packages, rather than ad hoc elevated scripts or installers.
- [ ] Centralise and version the distribution of application control policies (policy-as-code), with change control evidence.
- [ ] Send application control enforcement information into security information and event management (SIEM), and set up alerts for repeated blocked attempts to run elevated (higher-privilege) commands.
- [ ] For each prohibited category, run validation tests using both standard and higher-privilege prompts, and record the results as audit evidence.


---

#### AC-6.9 — Log Use of Privileged Functions (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the organisation keeps a clear record of every time a high-privilege action is carried out on its systems. If high-privilege access is misused—by someone who should not have done it, by an insider making a mistake, or by an attacker who has taken over an account—the damage can be difficult to notice and even harder to investigate later.  

To meet this expectation, the organisation must first decide and document exactly which high-privilege actions must be tracked, and which types of events count as the official records to capture. The system then needs to automatically record an entry in its audit records every time one of those listed actions is carried out, and keep those records available for later review.  

Finally, the organisation must assign responsibility for reviewing these logs to the right people, and document in its security plan how the logging and review will work.

##### Audit and centralise privileged-function execution events

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Put in place reliable auditing for the execution of **defined privileged functions**, and make sure these events are **recorded in system audit logs** and **sent to a central logging platform/security information and event management (SIEM)** for later review.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-6(9) (enhancement) requires: **“Log the execution of privileged functions.”** The guidance notes that misuse of privileged functions by authorised users, or by attackers who compromise accounts, is an ongoing concern. Logging helps with detection and investigation.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create and maintain a privileged-functions register** for the application and supporting infrastructure (for example: actions in an admin console, changes to roles/permissions, starting or stopping services, configuration changes, key/certificate operations, database administration actions, and any other actions your organisation defines as privileged). For each privileged function, record the **authoritative event source(s)** that can produce an audit record.
2. **Turn on built-in auditing** at the relevant layers (operating system/host, application, and database where applicable) so that each privileged function execution produces an auditable event (event identification/type). Make sure the audit policy includes the specific event sources listed in the register.
