3. **Protect audit logging from being bypassed**: limit who can change audit settings, prevent unauthorised clearing or rotation of audit logs, and ensure systems keep the correct time (for example, using network time protocol (NTP)) so events can be linked together during investigations.
4. **Send audit events to a central SIEM/log platform** using authenticated connections and granting only the minimum access needed (least-privilege). Set up the process so privileged-function events are **kept for the required retention period** and, where supported, stored in an **append-only / tamper-evident** way.
5. **Set up and check the mapping of audited events**: keep a documented **“privileged function → event type(s)”** mapping, and carry out periodic checks (for example, controlled test executions) to confirm that each privileged function produces at least one expected audit event.
6. **Assign ownership for review and run triage operations**: send privileged-function events to the right review teams (for example, the security operations centre (SOC) for initial triage, and information and access management (IAM) or platform owners for confirmed misuse). Define an evidence trail (ticket/incident links) for later audits.
7. **Record this in the system security plan**: include the privileged-functions register, audit sources, event mappings, central logging destinations, retention approach, and review responsibilities.

###### WHO
- **Application Security/Platform Engineering Lead**: owns the privileged-functions register and event mapping.
- **Infrastructure/Operations (System Administrator/Cloud Operations)**: enables and strengthens built-in auditing and log forwarding.
- **SIEM/SOC Lead**: ensures central collection, alerting/triage workflows, and correct routing for review.
- **Information Security/Compliance**: ensures the approach is documented and ready for audit in the system security plan.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A current **privileged-functions register** exists and is approved by the relevant system owner(s).
- For each privileged function, there is a documented **mapping to one or more audit event types** that are generated when it is executed.
- In a controlled test, running each privileged function results in **the expected audit events** appearing in the central logging platform within the expected data-collection time window.
- Changes to audit logging settings and access to audit logs are limited to authorised roles, and audit logs cannot be cleared/disabled without being detected.
- Privileged-function events are kept in line with the organisation’s retention requirements and are available for investigation.
- The system security plan documents audit sources, mappings, retention, and **who reviews** the logs.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and get approval for a register of privileged functions, using the official event sources for each privileged action.
- [ ] Turn on built-in auditing for the operating system (host), the application, and the database, so that every mapped privileged function produces audit records.
- [ ] Strengthen audit logging by limiting changes to the audit settings and preventing unauthorised users from clearing or turning off the logs.
- [ ] Set up authenticated forwarding of privileged-function audit records to the central security information and event management (SIEM) or log system, using the minimum necessary permissions (least-privilege access).
- [ ] Create and keep up to date a list that links privileged actions to event types, then confirm it works by running controlled test executions.
- [ ] Update the system security plan to include the audit sources, how events are mapped, how long records are kept, and who is responsible for reviewing logs.


---

#### AC-6.10 — Prohibit Non-privileged Users from Executing Privileged Functions (Enhancement)

This expectation is about preventing ordinary users from carrying out powerful, high-risk actions that could weaken the protections around your systems. Without it, someone without the right permission could disable or get around key safeguards, create hidden user accounts, run checks that expose tampering, or take control of the secret keys used to protect sensitive data—making fraud, theft, or misuse that goes unnoticed more likely.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly document which actions are considered “privileged” and exactly which authorised people are allowed to perform each one. It must also define who is classed as “non-privileged”. The system then needs to be configured so that non-privileged users are blocked from trying any privileged actions, including attempts to bypass security or malicious-code protections.

The organisation must also keep evidence that these controls are in place, record any attempts in system logs, include this in its security planning, and test that non-privileged attempts are consistently denied while authorised privileged users can carry out only the actions they are permitted to do.

##### Enforce privileged-function allowlisting with JIT elevation

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Put in place a technical control that stops non-privileged users from carrying out **privileged actions** (for example: disabling, bypassing, or changing security or privacy protections; creating system accounts; running system integrity checks; and managing cryptographic key management activities, including bypassing intrusion detection/prevention or malicious-code protection).

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-6(10) enhancement requires that **non-privileged users are prevented from carrying out privileged actions**. The guidance explains that privileged actions include activities that disable, bypass, or change security/privacy protections, create system accounts, run system integrity checks, and manage cryptographic key management activities. It also states that enforcement is supported by AC-3.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create a list of privileged actions for each application/system** and link each item to the exact operational action in your environment (for example: “disable web application firewall (WAF)/intrusion detection system (IDS)/intrusion prevention system (IPS)”, “change security group/firewall policy”, “run integrity check”, “create privileged/service account”, “rotate/manage key management service (KMS)/cryptographic keys”, “disable anti-malware/endpoint detection and response (EDR) tamper protection”).
2. **Allow each privileged action only through an authorised privileged role** in the enterprise identity provider (IdP), and make sure non-privileged users cannot be assigned those roles.
3. **Use step-up and just-in-time (JIT) elevation for privileged roles** (a privileged access management (PAM)-style approach): privileged actions must require explicit elevation at the time they are needed (using strong authentication), rather than being allowed by default.
4. **Limit how privileged actions can be started** so they can only be triggered through controlled administrative interfaces or workflows (for example: admin consoles, continuous integration/continuous delivery (CI/CD) approval gates, PAM broker sessions, controlled scripts). Remove or block direct access from normal user settings (including local administrator rights and direct access to security tool consoles where feasible).
5. **Apply technical “deny by default” controls** at the points where access is enforced (role-based access control (RBAC) and attribute-based access control (ABAC) in the IdP and the application, plus authorisation checks in admin endpoints/automation tools) so attempts by non-privileged users are rejected.
6. **Keep evidence that can be audited**: record both successful and blocked attempts, including the user identity, the privileged action attempted, the target system/component, the time, and the result. Send this to security information and event management (SIEM) for correlation and alerts on repeated blocked attempts.
7. **Test and provide proof**: run scripted attempts from a non-privileged test account for each privileged action to confirm they are consistently blocked, and confirm that authorised privileged users can carry out only the actions they are permitted to do.

###### WHO
- **Application Owner / System Owner**: maintains the list of privileged actions and maps them to operational actions.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Lead**: sets up role mappings, conditional access, and step-up/JIT elevation.
- **Security Engineering / Platform Engineering**: implements enforcement in admin interfaces, automation tools, and logging integrations.
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC) / SIEM Analyst**: checks that alerts are working and reviews blocked privileged-action attempts.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every privileged action in the list, **non-privileged users are technically blocked** (requests are denied) and the denial is recorded in logs.
- Privileged actions can only be carried out by users with the matching privileged role **and** after successful step-up/JIT elevation.
- Logs for blocked attempts must include: user identity, privileged action attempted, target component/system, time, and outcome, and must be visible in SIEM.
- A test plan shows: (a) non-privileged attempts fail for 100% of privileged actions; (b) authorised privileged users succeed only for permitted actions; (c) evidence is kept according to the organisation’s logging and retention requirements.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and get approval for a list of privileged functions, mapped to the specific administrative actions they perform across the application and supporting infrastructure.
- [ ] Assign each privileged task to its own dedicated privileged role in the enterprise identity provider (IdP), and make sure non-privileged users cannot be given those roles.
- [ ] Set up “step-up” authentication and just-in-time (JIT) permission increases for privileged roles using a privileged access management (PAM)-style process.
- [ ] Set up “deny by default” access checks for all privileged administrator endpoints, application programming interfaces (APIs), and automated workflows.
- [ ] Where possible, limit privileged actions to approved administration interfaces or brokers, and prevent direct access from normal user accounts.
- [ ] Enable and centralise audit logs for both successful and denied attempts to use privileged functions, and send the logs to security information and event management (SIEM).
- [ ] Run test cases using non-privileged accounts to confirm that every privileged function is consistently blocked, and collect audit evidence.


---

### AC-7 — Unsuccessful Logon Attempts (Control)

This requirement is about preventing repeated attempts to guess login details by limiting how many times a person can enter an incorrect username or password within a short time. Without this, an attacker could keep trying different username and password combinations until they eventually succeed, which could lead to unauthorised access to accounts or systems.

The organisation must configure the system so that each user is allowed no more than **5 consecutive invalid login attempts** within any rolling **15-minute** period. This limit must include both local logins and logins made over the network. If the limit is exceeded, the system must automatically **lock the user account or the affected system component** until an administrator explicitly unlocks it. After the lock, the system must not allow the account or component to be used again until the administrator has released it.

The organisation must also:
- document the policy and the administrator unlock procedures;
- keep evidence in audit logs showing when accounts are locked and when administrators unlock them;
- include this in the system security plan; and
- ensure the appropriate teams are responsible for implementing and maintaining the settings and enforcing the control.

##### Enforce 5 invalid logons/15 mins with admin-controlled unlock

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up the application’s login (authentication) integration so that **no user can make more than 5 consecutive invalid login attempts within a rolling 15-minute period**. If the limit is exceeded, the system **automatically locks the account (or affected node/session) until an administrator explicitly unlocks it**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
Control AC-7 requires limiting unsuccessful login attempts for both **local and network** logins, using the organisation’s defined limits (number and time period), and taking the organisation’s defined action when the limit is exceeded. It also requires that **automatic unlocking is generally not allowed**, except where operational needs require it.

###### HOW (specific approach)
Apply the enforcement in the **central identity system** used by the application (identity provider (IdP)/single sign-on (SSO)). This ensures the lockout covers all network sign-in routes (web, application programming interface (API) gateways with interactive login, and any VPN/portal that applies) and that the application cannot override or bypass the IdP lockout.

1. **Set the organisation’s thresholds in the IdP**: configure the sign-in policy to allow **5 consecutive invalid attempts** within a **rolling 15-minute** window.
2. **Set the action when the limit is exceeded**: configure the IdP to **lock the account until administrator release** (align to ac-07_odp.03), rather than allowing temporary self-unlock.
3. **Make sure the rolling limit is counted across all relevant components**: confirm the application uses the IdP for authentication (SSO/identity and access management (OIDC)/security assertion markup language (SAML)) so invalid attempts are counted consistently for the same user across all network sign-in routes.
4. **Send audit evidence to monitoring**: forward IdP sign-in logs to the security information and event management (SIEM) system and keep records showing (a) invalid attempts reaching the limit, (b) lock initiation, and (c) administrator unlock/release.
5. **Set up an administrator unlock process**: create an auditable, role-restricted process for unlock/release (for example, using the IdP administration console or a privileged access workflow) with separation of duties and explicit approval.
6. **Check how the application behaves for locked accounts**: ensure the application shows a generic login failure message and does not provide clues that could help someone guess usernames; confirm that locked users cannot authenticate even if they try again.

###### WHO
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) engineer**: configure IdP lockout/throttling and the administrator unlock workflow.
- **Application security engineer**: confirm the application uses the IdP for authentication and does not bypass lockout.
- **SIEM/monitoring engineer**: ensure logs/events are collected, linked together, and kept.
- **Privileged access administrator**: perform explicit unlock/release actions when authorised.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For a test user, after **5 consecutive invalid attempts within 15 minutes**, the account is **automatically locked**.
- The user **cannot log in again** until an **administrator explicitly unlocks/releases** the account.
- Lockout applies to **network logins** to the application (and any other interactive sign-in routes that use the same IdP).
- SIEM includes auditable events for **limit reached**, **lock initiated**, and **administrator release/unlock**.
- The application does not bypass the IdP lockout (no other login method allows access while the account is locked).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Set the identity provider (IdP) lockout rule defined by the organisation to block access after 5 consecutive invalid login attempts within any rolling 15-minute period.
- [ ] Set the “exceeded attempts” response to lock the account until an administrator unlocks it (no automatic self-unlock).
- [ ] Check that the application signs in only through the identity provider (single sign-on (SSO) using OpenID Connect (OIDC) or Security Assertion Markup Language (SAML)), so network sign-in attempts are recorded consistently.
- [ ] Enable and send identity provider (IdP) sign-in, lock, and unlock audit records to the security information and event management (SIEM) system, and keep them long enough to support investigations.
- [ ] Set up an auditable administrator “unlock” process that only certain authorised roles can use, and require an explicit approval and written reason for each unlock.
- [ ] Test and record that users whose accounts are locked cannot sign in to the application until an administrator unlocks the account.


---

#### AC-7.2 — Purge or Wipe Mobile Device (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that if a staff phone or tablet is lost, stolen, or accessed by mistake, it cannot keep organisation information on it after repeated failed attempts to unlock it. Without this, someone could keep trying codes until they eventually guess, or a device could be left in a state where sensitive information is still accessible even after it should have been removed.

The organisation must:
- Define which mobile devices used by staff are covered.
- Set them up so that after **10 consecutive unsuccessful attempts to unlock the device** (this does not mean failed attempts to log into a specific app or user account), the organisation information on the device is automatically **removed** (purged or wiped).
- If the device uses **strong full-device encryption**, use **cryptographic erase**; otherwise use an **approved wipe method**.
- Ensure that a successful unlock resets the failure count to **zero**.

The organisation must also keep documented procedures, lists of covered devices, and rules for the wipe technique, configure the devices accordingly, and keep audit records showing that the wipe happened and which method was used.

##### MDM-enforced wipe after 10 failed device unlocks (AC-7(2))

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Set up the organisation’s mobile device management (MDM) so that **in-scope mobile devices** automatically **erase or wipe organisation information** after **10 consecutive unsuccessful attempts to unlock the device**, using a wipe method chosen according to the device’s **full-device (full-disk) encryption strength**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The AC-7(2) enhancement requires purging/wiping information from **{{ insert: param, ac-07.02_odp.01 }}** based on **{{ insert: param, ac-07.02_odp.02 }}** after **{{ insert: param, ac-07.02_odp.03 }} consecutive unsuccessful device unlock attempts**. It also requires that **a successful device unlock resets the unsuccessful-attempt count to zero**, and that **cryptographic erase** is used when **sufficiently strong full-device encryption** is present; otherwise use an **NIST Special Publication (SP) 800-88 Revision 1 purge/wipe**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define and enforce the in-scope device group**: Use MDM enrolment scope (and/or conditional access) to ensure only **organisation-approved mobile devices used by staff to access organisation information** are covered.
2. **Enforce device-level lock and counting**: Configure the operating system (OS) and MDM integration to trigger wiping based on **consecutive unsuccessful device unlock attempts** (device logon), not failed application or account sign-ins.
3. **Set the threshold**: Configure the wipe trigger to activate after **10 consecutive unsuccessful device unlock attempts**.
4. **Reset the counter after success**: Confirm that a **successful device unlock resets the unsuccessful-attempt counter to zero** (test by failing unlocks up to the threshold minus one, then unlocking successfully, then confirming the counter resets).
5. **Choose the sanitisation method based on encryption strength**:
   - If the device has **strong full-device encryption**, configure the MDM/OS wipe action to perform **cryptographic erase**.
   - Otherwise configure an **approved purge/wipe** aligned to **NIST SP 800-88 Revision 1**.
   - Implement the encryption-strength decision using reliable information available to the MDM (for example, OS encryption status/attestation). If the signals are unclear, require a minimum OS/encryption posture for enrolment.
6. **Create evidence that can be audited**: Ensure the MDM produces audit events for wipe start and completion, including **device identifier**, **user (if applicable)**, **timestamp**, **wipe method chosen** (cryptographic erase versus purge/wipe), and **result/status**, and send this to **security information and event management (SIEM)** and log retention.
7. **Document the decision logic and configuration**: Keep configuration documentation covering the in-scope device criteria, the 10-failure trigger, how encryption strength is determined, and how this maps to sanitisation methods.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **MDM/Endpoint Security Engineer**: implement the MDM configuration (wipe trigger, encryption-based method selection, logging integration).
- **Security Compliance/Assurance Lead**: approve the documented decision logic and check that audit evidence is sufficient.
- **Service Desk / IT Operations**: support enrolment controls and manage exceptions under change control.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For an in-scope device, after **10 consecutive unsuccessful device unlock attempts**, the device automatically performs the configured sanitisation action.
- A **successful device unlock** resets the failure counter (verified by testing).
- Devices identified as having **strong full-device encryption** use **cryptographic erase**; others use an **NIST SP 800-88 Revision 1-aligned purge/wipe** (verified via MDM configuration and test evidence).
- SIEM audit logs show wipe events for test cases with **device identifier, timestamp, and wipe method**.
- Only **MDM-enrolled/in-scope devices** can access organisation information (enrolment scope/conditional access evidence).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a mobile device management (MDM) enrolment scope for mobile devices that are in scope and are used to access organisation information
- [ ] Set up the operating system (OS) and mobile device management (MDM) rules for locking devices, and turn on counting of unlock failures at the device level.
- [ ] Set an automatic wipe to activate after 10 consecutive unsuccessful attempts to unlock the device.
- [ ] Implement detection of strong encryption in mobile device management (MDM) and link strong full-disk encryption to cryptographic erase.
- [ ] Set up devices that do not use strong encryption to use an approved wipe method from National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication 800-88, Revision 1 (NIST SP 800-88 Rev. 1).
- [ ] Integrate mobile device management (MDM) wipe events into security information and event management (SIEM), keep them for the required retention period, and ensure the device identifier, the time of the wipe, and the wipe method are recorded.
- [ ] Run and record test cases to prove the counter resets after a successful unlock, and to confirm the correct wipe method is selected.


---

#### AC-7.3 — Biometric Attempt Limiting (Enhancement)

This requirement is about stopping repeated failed biometric sign-ins from being tried again and again. It sets a hard limit of 5 unsuccessful biometric attempts. Without this limit, someone could keep trying different fingers, faces, or other biometric options until they eventually succeed, or they could overload the system and make genuine access harder to manage.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must configure its biometric sign-in process so that after 5 failed attempts, the system blocks any further biometric sign-in attempts. The organisation must follow its documented plan for what happens next (for example, blocking biometric use or requiring an alternative way to sign in).

The organisation must also:
- keep clear written procedures
- make sure the system is actually configured to 5 attempts
- record the results in audit records, including when the limit is reached
- include this approach in its security planning and system design documentation, so it can be demonstrated and maintained over time.

##### Enforce 5 biometric failure attempts with audit and post-limit fallback

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up the organisation’s authentication process so that **no more than 5 unsuccessful biometric sign-in attempts** are allowed. After the 5th failure, **any further biometric attempts are blocked/denied**, and the system follows a **documented behaviour after the limit is reached**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **AC-7(3)** enhancement requires: **“Limit the number of unsuccessful biometric logon attempts to {{ insert: param, ac-07.03_odp }}.”** The guidance notes that biometric checks are probabilistic, so a fixed limit is needed to prevent repeated attempts and to support detection of presentation attacks and matching variations.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Identify where biometric sign-in is used** in the application’s sign-in journey (for example, Windows Hello for Business, biometric sign-in on a mobile device, biometric-capable client sign-in, or any biometric factor that ultimately authenticates through the organisation’s central identity provider (IdP)).
2. **Apply the attempt limit in the central identity system** (preferred, because the application uses enterprise identity): configure the IdP/authentication service to enforce **maximum unsuccessful biometric attempts = 5** (using the organisation-defined parameter **ac-07.03_odp = 5**).
3. **Define and implement what happens after the limit is reached** in the sign-in flow. Once the threshold is met, biometric sign-in must no longer be accepted, and the user must be directed to the documented alternative (for example, require a password plus multi-factor authentication (MFA), require a helpdesk-assisted reset, or apply account lockout in line with the existing access control policy). Make sure this behaviour is the same across environments (production and testing).
4. **Turn on audit logging** for every unsuccessful biometric attempt and for the **limit-reached/blocked** event. Ensure logs include at least: user identifier, date and time, authentication method/factor, failure result, and a clear indicator that the biometric attempt limit was reached.
5. **Ensure the configuration is auditable and can be repeated**: make changes through configuration management (for example, policy-as-code and infrastructure as code (IaC) where supported), and keep configuration exports/snapshots showing the limit is set to **5** and when it was last changed.
6. **Update documentation and security planning records**: record the 5-attempt limit and the user experience/operational response after the limit is reached in the biometric sign-in procedures, system design documentation, and the system security plan.

###### WHO
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) engineer / Security architect**: configure IdP/authentication policies and ensure the limit is set to 5.
- **Application owner / Engineering lead**: confirm the application’s sign-in journey uses the central enforcement point and that the user experience matches the documented post-limit behaviour.
- **Security operations / Security information and event management (SIEM) engineer**: check the audit events and ensure SIEM correlation and retention meet organisational requirements.
- **Compliance / Governance, Risk and Compliance (GRC)**: confirm the evidence is complete against AC-7(3).

###### Acceptance criteria
- The authentication system enforces **exactly 5 unsuccessful biometric attempts** (organisation parameter **ac-07.03_odp**) before biometric sign-in is blocked.
- After the 5th failure, **any further biometric attempts are denied**, and the user is routed to the **documented** alternative behaviour.
- Audit logs show **each failed biometric attempt** and a **separate event** when the attempt limit is reached and biometric is blocked.
- Evidence exists (configuration exports/snapshots and change records) showing the limit is set to **5** in the relevant environments.
- Documentation (biometric procedures, system design, and security plan) clearly states the 5-attempt limit and the post-limit behaviour.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Identify every place where the application uses biometric sign-in as part of its sign-in process.
- [ ] Set the organisation’s identity provider (IdP) and authentication rules to limit unsuccessful biometric attempts to a maximum of 5 (ac-07.03_odp).
- [ ] Implement documented behaviour after the limit is reached to block any further biometric attempts and route users to the approved alternative authentication method.
- [ ] Enable and check audit logs for every failed biometric attempt, and record a separate event when a threshold is reached and the attempt is blocked.
- [ ] Apply the configuration using configuration management (policy-as-code and infrastructure as code where available) and keep the exported evidence showing that the limit is set to 5.
- [ ] Update the biometric sign-in procedures, the system design documentation, and the system security plan to refer to the limit of 5 attempts and what happens after that limit is reached.


---

#### AC-7.4 — Use of Alternate Authentication Factor (Enhancement)

This requirement ensures people have a safe backup way to prove who they are when they mistype their usual login too many times, without allowing attackers to keep trying indefinitely. Without it, a person could lock themselves out, and at the same time a wrongdoer could repeatedly guess passwords or codes until they succeed—particularly if the system allows unlimited retry attempts using backup methods.

The organisation must allow users to use only **two approved alternate ways** to sign in when these are different from the user’s usual method:  
- an approved **hardware security key**, or  
- a **one-time code** generated by an approved **authenticator app**.

These alternate methods may be used only after the organisation’s set number of consecutive failed attempts for the usual method has been exceeded.

When users are signing in using an alternate method, the system must:  
- block further attempts after **5 consecutive failed attempts** within any rolling **15-minute** period, and  
- count failures during the alternate sign-in process.

The organisation must document how this works, configure the system accordingly, and keep audit records showing the failed attempts and their timing so the approach can be checked.

##### IdP alternate-factor recovery with 5-in-15 rolling throttle

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up the organisation’s central identity provider (IdP) so that, once a user exceeds the organisation’s allowed number of consecutive failed logins for their **primary** login method, they can regain access using **only** the approved **alternate** login methods:
- **Hardware security key (FIDO2/WebAuthn)**
- **One-time passcode (OTP)** from an **approved authenticator app**

When alternate methods are used, apply a strict limit of **5 consecutive failed alternate-method attempts within any rolling 15-minute period**. This includes failures that occur during the alternate-method recovery process.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The AC-7(4) enhancement guidance requires that alternate methods:
- help users recover from lockout and remain available, and
- also prevent misuse by limiting consecutive failed alternate-method attempts.

The organisation’s settings require:
- which alternate methods are allowed (ac-07.04_odp.01),
- the number of consecutive failed alternate-method attempts (ac-07.04_odp.02 = 5),
- the time period for the rolling window (ac-07.04_odp.03 = 15 minutes).

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define primary and alternate roles in the IdP**: For each application and login journey, clearly label the user’s current login method as **primary**. Make sure the alternate-method policy only applies when the alternate method is **different from the primary**.
2. **Set when alternate methods are allowed**: Configure the IdP so alternate methods are permitted **only after** the user exceeds the organisation-defined limit of consecutive failed attempts for the **primary** method.
3. **Allow only the approved alternate methods**: Permit only **FIDO2/WebAuthn security keys** and **authenticator-app OTP** as alternate methods. Ensure other multi-factor authentication (MFA) methods cannot be used as alternates for this recovery route.
4. **Apply rolling limits to alternate attempts**: Configure the IdP to enforce **5 consecutive failed alternate-method attempts within any rolling 15-minute window**. Block further alternate-method attempts until the rolling window condition has cleared. Make sure the failure count increases after **each** failed alternate-method attempt.
5. **Use separate, auditable counters**: Confirm the IdP records:
   - when alternate-method recovery becomes available,
   - each failed alternate attempt with timestamps, and
   - the block and unblock events linked to the rolling 15-minute window.
6. **Send audit evidence to security information and event management (SIEM)**: Send authentication failure events and throttling/block events to the organisation’s SIEM so they can be checked for audit purposes and used for alerts.
7. **Document operational recovery steps**: Create helpdesk and user guides explaining what to do when locked out, and how helpdesk should check whether alternate-method recovery is currently allowed (including how the rolling 15-minute window affects retry timing).

###### WHO
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) engineer**: implements the IdP authentication policy, primary/alternate method classification, and throttling configuration.
- **Security operations / security operations centre (SOC)**: checks that SIEM receives the data correctly, that alerts work, and that audit evidence is complete.
- **Information security governance**: reviews and approves the documented recovery procedures and evidence retention.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Alternate-method recovery is available **only after** the user exceeds the organisation-defined limit of consecutive failed attempts for the **primary** method.
- Alternate-method recovery allows **only** FIDO2/WebAuthn security keys and authenticator-app OTP.
- If the user’s primary method is one of the approved alternates, the alternate method offered must be **different from the primary** (no way to bypass by choosing the same method).
- The system blocks alternate-method attempts after **5 consecutive failed attempts** within any **rolling 15-minute** period, and the block is lifted only when the rolling window clears.
- Audit logs and SIEM events show timestamps for: primary failures that trigger eligibility, each failed alternate attempt, and the resulting block/unblock actions.
- Helpdesk and user procedures accurately describe the throttling behaviour and the recovery steps.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] For each authentication journey, identify the main factor used for sign-in, and specify which approved alternative methods can be used if they are different from the main factor.
- [ ] Set the identity provider (IdP) policy so that alternate-factor recovery is allowed only after the number of consecutive invalid primary login attempts set by the organisation has been exceeded.
- [ ] Limit the backup sign-in method to only FIDO2/Web Authentication (FIDO2/WebAuthn) security keys and one-time passwords (OTPs) from an authenticator app.
- [ ] Set up a rolling limit that blocks alternating-factor attempts after 5 consecutive invalid attempts within any rolling 15-minute period.
- [ ] Check that the “alternate attempt” failure counter increases every time an alternate authentication attempt fails, and that it only resets after the rolling time window has fully passed.
- [ ] Enable and export audit logs for primary failures, alternate eligibility checks, every failed alternate attempt, and block and unblock events to security information and event management (SIEM).
- [ ] Publish and get approval for helpdesk and user runbooks that describe the recovery steps and the effect of the 15-minute rolling time window.


---

### AC-8 — System Use Notification (Control)

This requirement ensures people receive a clear warning before they enter a government system, so they understand what they are agreeing to. Without this, users could access the system without realising that their activity may be monitored and recorded, or that unauthorised use is not allowed and may result in criminal and civil penalties—raising the risk of misuse and privacy harm.

The organisation must show the warning before any user can log on through a human sign-in screen. The warning must state that it is a United States (U.S.) Government system, that use may be monitored, recorded, and reviewed, that unauthorised use is prohibited and subject to penalties, and that using the system means the user consents to monitoring and recording. The message must remain on screen until the user acknowledges the conditions and takes the explicit step to log on or continue.

If the system is publicly accessible, it must also display a public notice before any further access. This notice must explain that monitoring and recording are limited to security and fraud prevention, that no content is used for unauthorised profiling, and that only authorised uses are permitted. It must also include any privacy-consistent limits and a description of authorised uses.

The organisation must have the warning text approved, configure the system so the notice appears and cannot be skipped, and keep records showing that users acknowledged the warning and proceeded.

##### IdP pre-auth warning with explicit acknowledgement gate

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up an approved system use warning (and, where applicable, a public access notice) on the identity provider (identity provider (IdP)) login screen for all people using the application through a sign-in (logon) page. The warning must be shown before access is allowed, and it must stay on screen until the user clearly confirms they have read it and then takes a clear action to continue.

###### WHY (control requirement)
Control AC-8 requires that system use warnings are shown by the system before granting access through sign-in pages for people. It also requires organisations to consider additional, risk-based warnings after the initial network sign-in. The guidance also requires legal and privacy review and approval of the warning text, and that public access notices include privacy-appropriate limits and authorised-use information.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Get approval for the warning text**: Send the United States Government system warning and any public access notice text to the privacy team and legal review (Office of the General Counsel or equivalent). Once approved, lock the final wording as a controlled configuration item.
2. **Create warning templates**: Prepare two versions of the notice:
   - **System use notification** (United States Government system warning) for all people signing in.
   - **Public access notice** for publicly available entry points, including the required privacy-appropriate limits (for example, monitoring and recording limited to security and fraud prevention; no unauthorised profiling) and an authorised-use statement.
3. **Set up an IdP confirmation step**: In the IdP sign-in process, add a **required step before authentication** that shows the approved banner and stops the user from continuing until they take an explicit confirmation action (for example, a checkbox plus a button such as “Acknowledge and continue”). Make sure the banner cannot be closed or skipped automatically without confirmation.
4. **Ensure the timing is correct**: Show the banner **before** the IdP issues the session or access token that allows entry to the application (or, at minimum, before any application session is created). This meets the requirement to show the warning **before granting access**.
5. **Support multiple languages (if needed)**: If organisational needs or user demographics require it, show the banner in multiple languages while keeping the approved meaning and content the same (use approved translations under the same approval process).
6. **Keep audit evidence**: Make sure the IdP records that the user confirmed the warning, including at least: user identifier, date and time, banner version (or version hash), and whether confirmation was completed. Send the IdP authentication and audit logs to the security information and event management (SIEM) and log retention system so the activity can be audited.

###### WHO
- **Information Security / Compliance Lead**: owns the AC-8 implementation plan and evidence requirements.
- **Privacy Officer**: reviews privacy wording for the public access notice.
- **Legal (General Counsel or equivalent)**: approves the warning banner content.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Engineer**: implements the IdP confirmation step and logging.
- **SIEM/Logging Engineer**: ensures logs are sent, kept, and can be searched.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every person using the application through the IdP sign-in interface, the approved system use notification is shown **before access is granted**.
- The banner remains until the user explicitly confirms and clicks/takes action to continue; users cannot skip the step.
- For publicly accessible entry points, the approved public access notice is shown and includes the privacy-appropriate limits and authorised-use statement.
- IdP audit logs show confirmation completion and include the banner version (or version hash) and timestamp for each successful confirmation.
- Banner content is demonstrably approved (with documented approval records) and changes are controlled (versioned configuration).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Obtain documented approval for the system use notification and any public access notice from the Privacy team and Legal team.
- [ ] Create controlled, versioned banner templates for the United States Government warning and the public access notice.
- [ ] Set up the identity provider (IdP) sign-in process to show the banner before the user signs in, and prevent the user from continuing until they explicitly confirm they have read and understood it.
- [ ] Make sure the banner is displayed before the identity provider issues the session or token that allows the application to access the service.
- [ ] Where needed, enable rendering in multiple languages using approved translations, under the same approval process.
- [ ] Check that the identity provider (IdP) logs show when the acknowledgement is fully completed, including the banner version or hash, the user, and the time it happened, and then send these logs to security information and event management (SIEM) for secure record-keeping.


---

### AC-9 — Previous Logon Notification (Control)

This requirement is about showing users the date and time of their last successful login immediately after they sign in. This is important because, if the system never shows this information, a user may not notice something suspicious—such as unexpected access that could indicate misuse of their account or unauthorised activity.

To meet this requirement, the organisation must ensure that whenever a person signs in through a normal login screen (for example, a website or a desktop sign-in), the system immediately displays a message that includes the date and time of that person’s most recent successful login. The message must use the organisation’s stored records of successful logins, not failed sign-in attempts.

The organisation should also document how this works, configure the system so the message appears on all supported login screens, and be able to provide evidence that the correct date and time are shown after successful sign-in.

##### Show last successful logon time in IdP sign-in experience

- Category: Software
- Priority: High

###### WHAT
Set up the organisation’s central identity provider (identity provider (IdP)) so that, after a **successful sign-in by a person** using a **person-facing sign-in screen**, the sign-in page shows the **date and time of the user’s most recent successful sign-in**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 **AC-9** requires: “Notify the user, upon successful logon to the system, of the date and time of the last logon.” This is intended to help users spot inconsistencies with their last access.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Send all person sign-ins through the IdP** for every supported way people can access this application using a person-facing interface (for example, a web portal or single sign-on (SSO)-enabled client apps). Make sure the application does not do its own separate sign-in check in cases where the IdP cannot provide the required notification.
2. **Turn on or build the “previous sign-in / last successful sign-in” message** in the IdP’s person sign-in process (or sign-in customisation feature), and ensure the message is shown **only after the sign-in is successful**.
3. **Use the IdP’s official “last successful sign-in” information** for the displayed date and time (not failed attempts, and not account lock events). Confirm the date and time change only after successful sign-ins.
4. **Standardise how date and time are shown**: agree the organisation’s required time zone and format (for example, UK local time versus Coordinated Universal Time (UTC)), and configure the IdP so the message is consistent and easy to understand.
5. **Keep audit evidence**: ensure the IdP records logs/audit events that include the user identifier and the last successful sign-in date and time, and keep evidence for audit (for example, a screenshot of the sign-in message and the matching IdP audit record).
6. **Test negative cases**: confirm the message does **not** appear after failed sign-ins, and that attempts to sign in with a locked or disabled account do not change the “last successful sign-in” value.

###### WHO
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) engineer**: implement the IdP configuration and the sign-in message.
- **Application owner / security engineer**: confirm all person-facing access routes for the application use the IdP.
- **Quality assurance (QA) / security testing**: carry out acceptance testing and collect evidence.

###### Acceptance criteria
- After a successful person sign-in, the user sees a message showing **both date and time** of their **most recent successful** sign-in.
- The shown date and time matches the IdP’s official “last successful sign-in” record for that user.
- The message appears only after successful sign-ins and does not appear, and does not change, after failed authentication attempts.
- Evidence is available for at least one representative user and environment (for example, a screenshot of the sign-in message and the corresponding IdP audit log entry).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Confirm that every interactive human logon route for the application is authenticated through the organisation’s identity provider (IdP).
- [ ] Enable the identity provider (IdP) to show “previous sign-in” and “last successful sign-in” messages during the interactive sign-in process.
- [ ] Set the sign-in template to show the identity provider’s (IdP) last successful sign-in date and time only after the user has been successfully authenticated.
- [ ] Standardise and set the time zone and time format used in the notification.
- [ ] Set up automated collection of proof (for example, a screenshot of the sign-in page, linked to the relevant entry in the identity provider’s audit log).
- [ ] Run acceptance tests for these login scenarios—successful login, failed login, and locked or disabled accounts—and record the results.


---

#### AC-9.1 — Unsuccessful Logons (Enhancement)

This expectation helps a person notice suspicious or unexpected sign-in behaviour. Right after they sign in successfully, the system should show them how many times their sign-in attempts failed since their last successful sign-in. Without this, a user may not realise that someone else is trying to access their account, or they may not be able to tell whether repeated failures are due to their own actions. This can delay reporting and increase the risk of unauthorised access.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must:
- keep a running count for each user of rejected sign-in attempts that occur after their last successful sign-in and before the next one;
- increase the count each time the system rejects incorrect sign-in details; and
- reset the count to zero immediately after a successful sign-in.

Each time the user signs in successfully, the system must show that user the correct number for the most recent period.

##### Per-user failed-logon counter with post-success user notification

- Category: Software
- Priority: High

###### WHAT
Set up a per-user counter that records how many times that user has tried to log in unsuccessfully since their last successful logon. The counter must reset to zero the next time the user logs in successfully, and the user must be told immediately after that successful logon.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-9(1) (Enhancement) requires: **“Notify the user, upon successful logon, of the number of unsuccessful logon attempts since the last successful logon.”** This helps users tell whether the pattern of failures matches their own activity.

###### HOW (specific approach)
###### 1) Counter logic (per user, per authentication context)
- Keep a **server-side counter** for each authenticated user, keyed by the user identifier (for example, username or subject claim). This counter represents **unsuccessful logon attempts since the last successful logon**.
- Each time the user has an **unsuccessful authentication**, **increase the counter by 1**.
- Each time the user has a **successful authentication**, **use the current counter value** and then **reset the counter to 0 immediately** as part of the same processing step (for example, using an atomic transaction or a compare-and-set approach) to prevent timing issues.

###### 2) Notification delivery (immediately after success)
- After successful authentication, show a **message to the user** on the application’s post-login landing page (or in the identity provider’s post-login experience) stating: **“Unsuccessful logon attempts since your last successful logon: N”**.
- Show the message **only to the user who just authenticated**, and ensure it reflects the **most recent period** (failures since the immediately preceding successful logon).

###### 3) Auditable evidence
- Record (in the security information and event management (SIEM) system or audit log store) the following at authentication time:
  - user identifier
  - success or failure outcome
  - the counter value used for the notification after success
  - the timestamp when the counter was reset
- Keep these logs according to the organisation’s logging and retention requirements so the behaviour can be demonstrated during assessment.

###### 4) Scope and exclusions
- Apply this to the **centralised enterprise authentication flow** used by the application (and any integrated login entry points). Do not change unrelated legacy/operational technology (OT) authentication methods unless they are part of the same enterprise identity flow.

###### WHO
- **Application Security Engineer / Identity and Access Management (IAM) Engineer**: implement the counter storage, event handling, and notification display.
- **Identity and Access Management (IdP) Administrator**: configure authentication event hooks/webhooks and ensure correct linking to the right user identifiers.
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC) / SIEM Administrator**: check that audit logging, dashboards, and retention are correct.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For a test user, after a series of failed logons followed by a successful logon, the user sees the exact number of failures since their last successful logon.
- After a successful logon, the counter resets so the next successful logon shows only failures that occurred after that reset.
- The notification is shown **only** to the authenticated user and is not shown to other users.
- Audit logs exist for both the counter increases and the success-time counter value/reset used for the notification, and they can be matched to the user’s authentication events.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Design the per-user counter data model and the way keys are assigned, using the application’s or identity provider’s (IdP) user identifier (for example, the subject or username).
- [ ] For each unsuccessful sign-in attempt, increase that user’s failure count by one in a single, indivisible step within the sign-in event handler.
- [ ] After each successful authentication, perform an atomic “read and reset” so the notification value always reflects the most recent failure period.
- [ ] Add a post-login user notification that shows the counter value immediately after a successful logon.
- [ ] Only show notification content for the authenticated session of the user who has just logged in.
- [ ] Set up and check audit logs for counter increases, the selection of success-time values, and reset actions, and send them to the security information and event management (SIEM) system with reference identifiers so related events can be linked together.
- [ ] Create automated tests that mimic failure situations, and check that the shown count and reset behaviour work correctly even when multiple attempts happen at the same time.


---

#### AC-9.2 — Successful and Unsuccessful Logons (Enhancement)

This requirement helps people notice unusual sign-in activity. Right after a user signs in successfully, the system must show how many sign-in attempts happened in the last 24 hours—both successful sign-ins and unsuccessful attempts.  

Without this information, a user may not realise their account is being targeted, or that the pattern of sign-ins does not match what they actually did. This could mean unauthorised access goes unnoticed for longer.  

To meet this expectation, the organisation must configure the sign-in system to count both successful and failed attempts during the 24 hours leading up to the time of the user’s successful sign-in, and then display both numbers together in the message the user sees immediately after they log on.

##### Post-login notification of last-24h successful and failed logons

- Category: Software
- Priority: High

###### WHAT
Implement a user notification that shows immediately after a successful logon and displays:
- the number of **successful logons** during the organisation-defined time period, and
- the number of **unsuccessful logon attempts** during the same time period.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) AC-9(2) enhancement requires: **“Notify the user, upon successful logon, of the number of {{param ac-09.02_odp.01}} during {{param ac-09.02_odp.02}}.”**
The organisation-defined parameters set **both** outcomes (ac-09.02_odp.01 = both) and a **time period** (ac-09.02_odp.02 = 24 hours). This helps users understand whether recent sign-in activity matches their own logon behaviour.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
###### 1) Define where events come from and how to count them
- Use the main, trusted authentication event records already connected to the application and identity provider (for example, identity provider sign-in audit logs or authentication service logs).
- Define “unsuccessful logon attempts” as the authentication failures that relate to the sign-in process (for example, invalid credentials and multi-factor authentication (MFA) failures), based on what the authentication system records.
- Use the **event date and time** to calculate counts within a **rolling 24-hour window** ending at the time of the user’s successful logon.

###### 2) Calculate counts at the time of successful logon
- Start a server-side process immediately after a **successful logon**.
- Look up events for the same user in the previous 24 hours and calculate:
  - `success_count_last_24h`
  - `unsuccess_count_last_24h`
- To handle multiple sessions at the same time, calculate counts separately for each successful logon event using its specific date and time.

###### 3) Show the notification right after login
- Insert the calculated counts into the user’s post-login experience (for example, the web page shown after sign-in, the mobile app screen after sign-in, or application session details).
- Show both counts together in the same notification displayed immediately after successful authentication.

###### 4) Evidence and auditability
- Record (on the server) that the notification was created for a specific successful logon event, including:
  - user identifier
  - successful logon event reference and time
  - calculated counts
  - the start and end times used for the event lookup window
- Keep this evidence to support audit checks.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Security Engineer / Identity and Access Management (IAM) Engineer**: build the post-authentication handler, the event lookup and counting logic, and the user interface/session update.
- **Identity Provider Administrator**: ensure sign-in audit/event logging is enabled and available for the required outcomes.
- **Security Operations / Compliance**: confirm evidence is retained correctly and that the notification appears after successful logon.

###### Acceptance criteria
- When a user successfully logs on, the application immediately displays a notification containing **both**:
  - the number of successful logons in the previous **24 hours**, and
  - the number of unsuccessful logon attempts in the previous **24 hours**.
- Counts are calculated using the authentication system’s event date and time, and reflect a rolling 24-hour window ending at the time of the successful logon.
- A notification is generated for each successful logon event (including concurrent sessions) without mixing time windows.
- Server-side evidence exists showing the calculated counts and the successful logon event reference used to generate the notification.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Set up and confirm reliable records of authentication activity in the integrated identity service, for both successful and unsuccessful sign-in attempts.
- [ ] Set up a post-login process that runs after a successful logon and calculates the number of events in the most recent 24 hours, based on the event times.
- [ ] Define and document what counts as an “unsuccessful sign-in attempt” for the organisation’s sign-in process (for example, incorrect credentials and multi-factor authentication (MFA) failure), using the event types that are available.
- [ ] Add the calculated figures to the user’s immediate post-login experience (user interface or session details) and show one notification that includes both numbers.
- [ ] Add server-side recording of evidence for each successful logon notification generation, including the start and end times of the query window and the calculated totals.
- [ ] Test by making controlled login attempts to confirm the notification only appears after a successful login, and that the counts match the figures from the last 24 hours.


---

#### AC-9.3 — Notification of Account Changes (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure people are told, immediately after they successfully sign in, about any important changes made to their account in the previous 24 hours. This matters because if someone changes your password, changes how you prove it is really you, or quietly changes what you can access, you may not notice and could continue using a compromised account without realising.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must, every time a user successfully logs on, check what security-relevant changes were recorded for that user during the past 24 hours and include them in a notification. This includes changes to how multi-step sign-in is set up, any password reset activity, any changes to what the user is allowed to do, and any changes to which groups or roles they belong to. It also includes any changes to security rules or role assignments.

The notification must be based on the organisation’s recorded system information, follow the organisation’s own sign-in notification procedures, and be properly documented, configured, and verifiable using audit records and the organisation’s security plan.

##### IdP sign-in notification digest for security account changes (24h)

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a sign-in “account change digest” that tells the user right after each successful sign-in about any changes to their **security-related account settings or details** that happened during the configured **time period** (set by the organisation; for example, the past 24 hours).

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-9(3) requires the system to **notify the user after a successful sign-in** if the user’s specified **security-related account settings or details** changed during the defined **time period**. This helps users spot unauthorised or unexpected account changes.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Identify the official sources of account changes and map them to event types** in the identity system for the organisation-defined change types. Include at least: **multi-factor authentication (MFA) status changes**, **password reset events**, **account privilege or group membership changes**, and **security policy or role assignments**.
2. **Build a sign-in process in the identity provider (IdP)** that runs after **successful sign-in** and:
   - Finds the user and the sign-in time.
   - Looks up the user’s **official audit/event records** for the period **[sign-in time - time period, sign-in time]**.
   - Keeps only the events that match the defined **security-related** settings/details.
   - Creates a short summary (for example: change type, time, and the person who made the change if available).
3. **Show the notification immediately after successful sign-in** using an in-app/in-session method (for example, a post-login page or banner) that matches the organisation’s existing sign-in notification approach, to reduce the risk of spoofing.
4. **Make it traceable and checkable** by saving, for every notification, the information needed to recreate it (the time window, event identifiers and/or counts, and whether the notification was successfully produced) in an audit trail/security information and event management (SIEM) record.
5. **Record the expected behaviour in the security plan** and keep proof of configuration (IdP configuration exports, the event mapping document, and test evidence) so the organisation can demonstrate compliance.

###### WHO
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Engineer**: builds the IdP sign-in process, event mapping, and notification display.
- **Security Operations / SIEM Engineer**: ensures audit/SIEM logging and keeps evidence.
- **Information Security Manager**: ensures the security plan documentation is completed and approved through governance.

###### Acceptance criteria
- After a successful sign-in, the user receives a notification that includes only **security-related** account changes that occurred within the configured time period (for example, the past 24 hours).
- Notifications are based on **official IdP audit/event records** (not estimated or inferred data).
- The notification is shown **immediately after successful sign-in** using the configured in-session method.
- For each notification, audit evidence exists showing the time window and the underlying event identifiers used to build the digest.
- Security plan documentation and IdP configuration evidence are available for audit.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and get approval for the official mapping between events and changes for multi-factor authentication (MFA) changes, password reset events, privilege and group membership changes, and security policy or role assignments.
- [ ] Set up an identity provider (IdP) sign-in “time hook” or workflow to run after a successful login, and check the user’s activity for the period from **[login time − time period, login time]**.
- [ ] Implement logic to generate the digest so it includes only the security-related characteristics or parameters defined by the organisation.
- [ ] Set up an in-session message that appears right after a user successfully logs on, to show the digest immediately.
- [ ] Record the details of each notification that is generated (the time window, the event identifiers and counts, and whether the notification was successful) in an audit trail and in a security information and event management (SIEM) system so it can be checked and verified.
- [ ] Update the security plan to include the notification design, proof of correct configuration, and the steps to confirm it works in day-to-day operations.


---

#### AC-9.4 — Additional Logon Information (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that, immediately after a successful login, users are shown where their account was last used and what device they are currently using. This is important because if an account is accessed from an unexpected location or device, the user may not notice quickly, which could allow unauthorised access or fraud to continue for longer than necessary.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must display an immediate on-screen message after a successful login. The message must use the organisation’s agreed wording and content and must include:
- the last successful login location, including the internet address and an approximate city or region; and
- the device identifier.

The organisation must also:
- have documented procedures and system design that explain how this information is collected and shown;
- configure the system so it can reliably produce the message;
- keep records that can be checked later to confirm the message was generated and/or displayed; and
- include this approach in its overall system security plan.

##### Post-login notice of last successful location and device ID

- Category: Software
- Priority: High

###### WHAT
Set up the organisation’s identity provider (IdP) and/or the application’s post-login landing flow so that, **immediately after a successful logon**, the user sees an on-screen message containing the additional information defined by the organisation: **“Last successful logon location (IP address and approximate city/region) and device identifier”**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Access Control (AC-9(4)) requires the organisation to **tell the user, after a successful logon**, the additional information specified by **param: ac-09.04_odp**. The guidance explains that “user location” can be worked out from **IP addresses**, and the message should help users understand where and which device their account was last used on.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Only trigger after a successful sign-in**: create a post-login action that runs **only after a successful sign-in** (not after failed attempts). Make sure the message appears on the **first page the user sees after authentication**, before they can access sensitive functions.
2. **Work out the “last successful logon”**: for the signed-in user, look up the **previous successful** sign-in record (not the current one) from the IdP sign-in history or audit records.
3. **Fill in the required details**:
   - **IP address** from the last successful sign-in record.
   - **Approximate city/region** based on that IP address (using the IdP’s location capability or an approved location service).
   - **Device identifier** linked to the last successful sign-in (prefer a stable managed-device identifier from endpoint or device registration; if not available, use an organisation-approved fallback value).
4. **Show the message using the organisation’s wording**: display the notification using the exact content and fields defined for **ac-09.04_odp**.
5. **Make it auditable**: record an event that links together (a) the successful logon, (b) the last-successful details used in the message, and (c) confirmation that the notification was shown.
6. **Document how it works**: update system design and configuration documentation to explain how the last-successful record is selected, how IP-to-city/region is determined, how the device identifier is obtained, and how the message is displayed.
7. **Record it in the system security plan**: include the control implementation and the evidence sources in the system security plan for the application/identity integration.

###### WHO
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Engineer**: implement the IdP post-authentication action, map the required data (claims), and render the message.
- **Application Owner / Security Architect**: confirm the **“first landing page”** behaviour and ensure the message appears before access to sensitive functions.
- **Security Operations / Compliance**: check that audit evidence is produced and kept in line with the organisation’s logging requirements.

###### Acceptance criteria
- After a successful logon, the user receives an on-screen notification **immediately after authentication**.
- The notification includes **IP address + approximate city/region** for the **last successful** logon and includes the **device identifier**.
- The notification text matches the organisation-defined **ac-09.04_odp** wording and fields.
- Audit evidence exists showing the successful logon, the last-successful details used, and that the notification was displayed.
- Documentation and the system security plan are updated to describe the mechanism and evidence sources.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Set up an identity provider (IdP) post-login action that runs only after a successful login, and shows the message on the first page the user lands on.
- [ ] Set up the system to retrieve the user’s previous successful sign-in record (not the current sign-in) to support “last successful logon”.
- [ ] Enable approximate city or region identification from the last successful sign-in IP address, using an approved geolocation method.
- [ ] Map and include a stable device identifier linked to the most recent successful sign-in. Use an organisation-approved fallback value for devices that are not managed.
- [ ] Add application and identity provider (IdP) logging that records (1) successful sign-in, (2) the notification details that were calculated, and (3) confirmation that the notification was shown.
- [ ] Update the system design documentation and the system security plan to explain how the notification is created and what audit evidence is kept.


---

### AC-10 — Concurrent Session Control (Control)

This requirement is about preventing highly privileged accounts from being used in multiple places at the same time. It does this by limiting each privileged system account (such as domain administrators and local administrators) and each service account used by mission-critical applications to a maximum of 2 simultaneous sessions.

This matters because, if these accounts can be used without limits, mistakes or unauthorised access are more likely to spread. That makes misuse harder to detect and increases the damage if an account is compromised.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must:
- clearly define which accounts are covered;
- set the maximum to 2 concurrent sessions per covered account; and
- ensure the systems actively block any attempt to create a third simultaneous session for those accounts.

The organisation must also:
- document the rule and the day-to-day procedures for applying and checking it;
- describe how the system is designed to enforce it;
- configure the relevant settings;
- keep evidence that the settings are in place;
- include this approach in its security planning; and
- be able to show, through testing and records, that the limit is consistently enforced.

##### Enforce 2 concurrent sessions for privileged and service accounts

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set a technical limit of **no more than 2 concurrent sessions per covered account** for the organisation-defined set of **privileged system accounts** and **mission-critical service accounts**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-10** requires: “Limit the number of concurrent sessions for each {{ ac-10_odp.01 }} to {{ ac-10_odp.02 }}.” The guidance clarifies that this applies to **system accounts** (not multiple sessions created by a single user using multiple accounts).

###### HOW (specific approach)
###### 1) Define the covered accounts (authoritative scoping)
- Keep an authoritative list of covered identities in the enterprise identity and access management (IAM) / identity provider (IdP) (for example, Entra ID/Okta groups or account attributes) for:
  - **Privileged system accounts** (for example, domain admins, local admins)
  - **Service accounts** used by **mission-critical applications**
- Make sure the mapping is consistent and repeatable (for example, based on group membership or a clearly defined account attribute), and that it is environment-aware (production versus non-production).

###### 2) Configure the identity provider (IdP) to enforce a limit of 2
- In the central identity provider, set **concurrent session control** for the covered identities to **2 concurrent sessions per account** (per **ac-10_odp.02**).
- Set the **behaviour for a third session** so it is predictable and can be checked later (for example, deny the new session and/or end the oldest session), in line with operational expectations.

###### 3) Ensure the limit is enforced for the real ways users access the applications
- Make sure authentication for the relevant application/admin access routes goes through the IdP (for example, single sign-on (SSO) using OpenID Connect (OIDC) / Security Assertion Markup Language (SAML), or via a bastion / remote desktop protocol (RDP) gateway that checks with the IdP).
- If any access route cannot consult the IdP for session decisions, document this gap and apply an equivalent enforcement method on the access broker / jump host, so sessions for the covered accounts are still limited.

###### 4) Produce audit-ready evidence and monitoring
- Store and/or export IdP configuration evidence (policy definitions, concurrency settings, and group/account mappings) in configuration management (for example, a policy-as-code repository or scheduled configuration exports).
- Turn on logging for both successful sessions and **blocked/terminated** attempts, and send these logs to security information and event management (SIEM).

###### 5) Validate with repeatable tests and a runbook
- Run a controlled test for each covered account type:
  - Session A and Session B succeed
  - Session C is blocked/terminated
- Record the timestamps, identity, and log identifiers.
- Create a runbook for handling legitimate needs for additional concurrent sessions (for example, a temporary approved exception process), including how to confirm the current number of sessions.

###### WHO
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Engineer**: implement IdP concurrency rules and identity-to-group mappings.
- **Application Owner / Security Engineer**: confirm that application access routes are routed through the IdP (or access broker) and that enforcement covers the intended system accounts.
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC) / Monitoring Lead**: ensure logs and alerts exist for blocked/terminated concurrency attempts.
- **Governance, Risk and Compliance (GRC) / Compliance**: check that evidence is complete against AC-10 and that audit readiness is maintained.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every identity in the covered set (per **ac-10_odp.01**), the system prevents a **third concurrent session** so that **no more than 2 concurrent sessions** exist at any time.
- Evidence exists showing:
  - the covered account scoping (groups/attributes)
  - the configured concurrency limit of **2**
  - the configured third-session behaviour
  - configuration export/policy records
  - logs showing blocked/terminated attempts
- Repeatable test results confirm the behaviour for privileged system accounts and mission-critical service accounts in the relevant environment(s).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and keep an up-to-date, official identity provider (IdP) mapping for the privileged system accounts and mission-critical service accounts that are in scope, using account groups and/or account attributes.
- [ ] Set up the identity provider’s (IdP) session control so that each covered account can have no more than 2 active sessions at the same time.
- [ ] Set consistent third-session behaviour (either deny access or end the session) and document what operations should expect to happen.
- [ ] Check that all relevant application and administrator access routes for the covered accounts go through the identity provider (IdP) — or through an access broker that applies the same access limits.
- [ ] Turn on and send logs about session creation and attempts to create concurrent sessions that were blocked or terminated to the security information and event management (SIEM) system for audit and monitoring.
- [ ] Export and store configuration evidence (policy settings and identity scoping) in configuration management and policy-as-code, and run a repeatable test to confirm that Session A and Session B succeed, and that Session C is blocked or terminated.


---

### AC-11 — Device Lock (Control)

This requirement ensures that when someone steps away from a work computer or work app, it is no longer accessible to anyone nearby. Without this, a passer-by could open files, view sensitive information, or keep using the user’s already logged-in work session, creating a real risk of data exposure or misuse.

The organisation must automatically lock the user’s device after 15 minutes of inactivity, and it must prevent any further access while the device is locked. The device must stay locked until the user returns and re-joins access using the organisation’s normal sign-in method, such as entering their usual password or personal code.

The organisation must also make sure that locking is not used as a substitute for logging out when users are required to end their session, for example at the end of the workday.

##### Enforce 15-minute inactivity device lock with re-authentication

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set managed device (and, where applicable, managed applications) settings so that a user’s device automatically locks after **15 minutes of inactivity**, stops any further access while it is locked, and requires the user to sign back in using the organisation’s **normal sign-in method** to regain access.

###### WHY (control requirement)
