AC-11 requires **device locks** to start after a set period of inactivity (organisation-defined), to **stop logical access** when users step away, and to ensure that locking is **not an acceptable replacement for logging out** when logout is required.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Set the inactivity timer to 15 minutes** on managed devices so the operating system/application locks after **15 minutes of inactivity** (ac-11_odp.02).
2. **Require re-authentication when unlocking** so that unlocking the device uses the same sign-in checks as normal sign-in (for example, password/PIN, smart card, or sign-in supported by single sign-on), and ensure there is **no option to resume without signing in** or any equivalent bypass.
3. **Ensure the locked state blocks access** by using the operating system’s secure lock screen/secure desktop, so active sessions cannot be used while the device is locked.
4. **Stop weak “nearby/auto-unlock” behaviours** from undermining the requirement for normal re-authentication (for example, disable any setting that would unlock without going through the standard sign-in process).
5. **Apply settings centrally and control changes** using your endpoint management platform (for example, Microsoft Intune, Jamf, or another tool), and regularly check that the lock timer and unlock requirements stay compliant.
6. **Reinforce that locking is not a substitute for logout** through device/application configuration and operational policy: make sure end-of-day or policy-required logout still happens (for example, turn off persistent sessions for sensitive applications where policy requires logout).

###### WHO
- **Endpoint/Workplace Engineering Lead**: sets up and maintains centrally managed device lock settings.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Lead**: ensures unlocking uses the normal sign-in method and that no bypass routes exist.
- **Security Compliance/Assurance**: checks evidence (configuration reports, audit logs) and monitors for changes that drift from the requirement.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Devices are configured to **lock after 15 minutes of inactivity** (ac-11_odp.02).
- Unlocking requires **normal sign-in re-authentication** (no unlock bypass/resume without sign-in).
- While locked, users cannot access the session or applications (lock screen/secure desktop enforced).
- Endpoint management reports show the settings are applied to the correct device groups and remain compliant over time.
- Evidence shows that device lock is **not used instead of required logout** (for example, policy/training plus technical safeguards for applications where logout is mandated).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Set the endpoint operating system to lock the session after 15 minutes of inactivity.
- [ ] Require a normal sign-in (password, personal identification number (PIN), smart card, or single sign-on (SSO)) to unlock the device, and prevent unlocking without signing in.
- [ ] Disable or limit any “nearby unlock” or “auto-unlock” options that would get around the normal re-authentication process.
- [ ] Apply the configuration to all in-scope user devices using the organisation’s endpoint management platform.
- [ ] Set up ongoing compliance checks and alerts to detect changes (for example, the lock timer is not set to 15 minutes, or an unlock bypass option is enabled).
- [ ] Publish and put into practice guidance that device lock does not replace end-of-day logout or any logout required by policy. Also check that sensitive applications follow the logout requirement.


---

#### AC-11.1 — Pattern-hiding Displays (Enhancement)

This requirement ensures that when someone locks their computer or phone, anything that was previously on the screen is immediately hidden from view and replaced with a harmless display. Without this, a passer-by could quickly look at the screen and read sensitive information that was left open, such as parts of a document, email, or other controlled material, even though the device is “locked”.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must configure devices so that locking immediately conceals the earlier screen content and shows only an approved, publicly viewable display, such as a pattern, a screen-saver style image, a photograph, a solid colour, a clock or battery indicator, or a blank screen.

The organisation must document how the lock function works, record the relevant device settings, and explain how this behaviour is built into the system. The organisation must also be able to provide evidence that the screen remains hidden and does not briefly show (“ghost”) the previous content during or after locking.

##### Enforce approved lock-screen pattern-hiding on managed endpoints

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up managed devices so that when a user session is locked, anything that was previously visible on the screen is immediately hidden. It must be replaced with an approved screen shown to the public (for example: a pattern or screen-saver image, a photograph, a solid colour, a clock or battery indicator, or a blank screen).

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-11(1) requires that information visible before a device is locked is concealed by the device lock and replaced with a publicly viewable image. The guidance allows only certain approved image types and requires that controlled unclassified information is not shown while the device is locked.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Standardise how devices lock** using the operating system’s built-in lock-screen features (avoid custom overlays that could fail during the lock process).
2. **Apply lock-screen settings through device management** (for example, mobile device management (MDM) or unified endpoint management (UEM)) to:
   - Set the lock screen to an **approved type** (pattern/screen-saver style image, photograph, solid colour, clock/battery indicator, or blank).
   - Turn off or restrict any **lock-screen previews** or widgets that could show sensitive information.
3. **Strengthen the lock transition** to prevent “ghosting” or parts of the previous screen showing through:
   - Make sure the lock transition fully covers the previous screen content (no status details, quick-glance views, or notification previews while locked).
   - Check that the approved lock image appears immediately after locking and stays consistent for the whole locked period.
4. **Ensure locking happens reliably** for both manual lock and inactivity-based lock (using policy and configuration), so concealment occurs whenever the session is locked.
5. **Produce audit-ready evidence**:
   - Save configuration exports or baseline reports from the management platform.
   - Run repeatable tests on representative device setups: show a dense sensitive document, trigger lock, and confirm that nothing readable or recognisable remains (including in the first seconds after locking).
6. **Monitor for changes to settings**:
   - Alert if any devices drift from the approved lock-screen settings (for example, an unapproved lock image type, or previews enabled).

###### WHO
- **Endpoint/Workplace Engineering Lead**: owns operating system and device-management configuration, and maintains baselines.
- **Security Engineering (Compliance)**: sets the requirements for “no ghosting” and reviews the evidence.
- **Service Desk/Operations**: supports rollout, fixes non-compliant devices, and keeps test records.

###### Acceptance criteria
- On a representative managed device, after triggering **manual lock** and **inactivity lock**, the previously visible content is **not readable or recognisable** from a normal viewing distance.
- The approved publicly viewable lock display appears **immediately** after locking and remains throughout the locked state with **no ghosting or partial reveal**.
- Lock-screen settings are applied centrally and allow only **approved image types** as required by the control guidance.
- Evidence is available for: (1) the configuration baseline/export from the management platform, and (2) test results showing concealment (including checks in the first seconds after locking).
- Drift monitoring identifies non-compliant devices and flags them within the organisation’s defined remediation timeframe.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Choose and record the approved lock-screen display options allowed for AC-11(1): pattern/screen-saver, photograph, solid colour, clock/battery indicator, or a blank screen.
- [ ] Set up a centrally managed device policy using mobile device management (MDM) and unified endpoint management (UEM) to enforce the approved lock-screen appearance, and turn off lock-screen previews and widgets that could show sensitive information.
- [ ] Set up devices so they automatically lock the session when a user locks it manually and when there is no activity, in line with organisational standards.
- [ ] Check the lock change on sample endpoint builds to confirm there is no “ghosting” or image bleed-through, and that the approved image shows up immediately after the lock is applied.
- [ ] Export and keep endpoint configuration baseline reports from the management platform as evidence for audits.
- [ ] Set up compliance monitoring and alerts for devices when their lock-screen settings change from the approved configuration, and fix any devices that are not compliant.


---

### AC-12 — Session Termination (Control)

This requirement is about automatically ending a user’s access session when it is no longer needed—for example, after 30 minutes of inactivity, immediately if an account is locked or suspected to be compromised, and during the system’s defined out-of-hours period for non-privileged users. Without this, someone could continue using an unattended or incorrectly accessed account longer than intended, increasing the risk of unauthorised actions, data exposure, or damage before anyone notices.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly define what counts as inactivity, what events mean “account locked” and “suspected compromise”, set the out-of-hours schedule for non-privileged users, and ensure the system automatically applies these rules. When a session ends, all activity linked to that session should stop, except for any tasks the user has specifically set up to continue.

The organisation must also document the process, include it in its access rules, keep records of the relevant settings and design, maintain a list of the trigger events, and log when session endings happen and the reason why.

##### Enforce AC-12 session termination via IdP session policies

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up automatic sign-out for users in the application’s identity system (identity provider (IdP)) so that sessions end when:
1. the user has been inactive for 30 minutes,
2. the account is locked,
3. suspected compromise is detected, and
4. the organisation’s off-hours rules apply to non-privileged users.

###### WHY (control requirement)
Control AC-12 requires that the system **automatically ends a user session after {{ insert: param, ac-12_odp }}**. This means ending **user sign-in sessions** (not simply disconnecting network access). Session termination must happen based on organisation-defined triggers, including **inactivity**, **events related to incidents or compromise**, and **time-of-day restrictions**. The organisation-defined parameter **ac-12_odp** means:
- **after 30 minutes of user inactivity**,
- **immediately** when an account is locked or suspected compromise is identified, and
- **during system-defined off-hours** for **non-privileged users**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Use the enterprise IdP for all sign-ins** (OpenID Connect (OIDC)/Security Assertion Markup Language (SAML)) for every interactive way users access the application (web/mobile/portal, and API user consoles where applicable). The application should rely on the IdP’s session and token status, rather than keeping its own long-running sessions.
2. **Set the IdP idle timeout to 30 minutes** for interactive user sessions. Map “inactivity” to the IdP’s inactivity signals (for example, no authenticated user actions or requests) so the IdP ends the session after 30 minutes.
3. **End sessions immediately when an account is locked** by connecting IdP/account status events (lockout/disable) to **token/session cancellation and sign-out** for affected users.
4. **End sessions immediately when suspected compromise is detected** by connecting security monitoring signals (security operations centre/security orchestration, automation and response (SOC/SOAR) or automated risk signals) to IdP actions that cancel sessions (for example, disable the account, revoke refresh tokens, and invalidate active sessions) for the organisation’s defined “suspected compromise” condition(s).
5. **Apply off-hours session ending for non-privileged users** using IdP conditional access/session rules with time limits and group/role targeting (exclude privileged roles/groups). Apply this to the application’s interactive sign-in sessions.
6. **Make the application follow IdP session changes**: update the application so that when the IdP cancels or expires a session/token, the application must stop the user session and must not allow continued access using cached sessions.
7. **Capture audit evidence showing the reason**: ensure IdP logs record the termination time and reason (idle timeout vs lockout vs compromise vs off-hours) and send them to security information and event management (SIEM) for auditability.

###### WHO
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Engineer**: implement IdP session settings, conditional access rules, and token/session cancellation integrations.
- **Application Owner/Engineer**: update application session handling so it relies on IdP session/token status and ends access when the IdP cancels sessions.
- **SOC/SOAR Lead**: define and run the mapping of the “suspected compromise” trigger to the IdP session cancellation actions.

###### Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
- For a non-privileged user, an interactive session is ended by the IdP after **30 minutes of inactivity** (no authenticated user actions), and the application blocks further access without re-authentication.
- When a user account is **locked/disabled**, all active logical sessions for that user are ended **immediately** (within the organisation’s operational tolerance), and the application requires re-authentication.
- When the organisation-defined **suspected compromise** condition is triggered, the user’s active logical sessions are ended **immediately** and access is blocked.
- During **system-defined off-hours**, non-privileged users cannot keep active sessions; sessions are ended according to the configured schedule.
- Audit logs show the **termination time** and **trigger reason** for each termination event and are available for compliance review.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Confirm that every way a user can interact with the application signs in through the organisation’s identity provider (OpenID Connect (OIDC) or Security Assertion Markup Language (SAML)), and where possible remove or disable any separate, long-lasting application sessions that do not rely on the identity provider.
- [ ] Set the identity provider (IdP) interactive session idle timeout to 30 minutes to enforce the ac-12_odp inactivity trigger.
- [ ] Set up identity provider–driven immediate sign-out (session revocation) when a user account is locked or disabled for affected users.
- [ ] Use security operations centre (SOC) and security orchestration, automation and response (SOAR) “suspected compromise” alerts to revoke identity provider (IdP) sessions and tokens for the affected users.
- [ ] Set time-based access and session rules to end user sessions outside working hours for non-privileged user groups or roles.
- [ ] Update application session handling so access ends when the identity provider (IdP) session or token is revoked or expires, with no continued access through cached sessions.
- [ ] Enable and send identity provider (IdP) audit logs to the security information and event management (SIEM) system. These logs must record the time a user session ended and the reason it ended, so they can be used as evidence.


---

#### AC-12.1 — User-initiated Logouts (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring people can end their own signed-in sessions whenever they choose, whether they are using a work computer, a password-protected website, or a signed-in database connection. Without an easy logout option that users can trigger themselves, someone could accidentally leave access open, or a session could stay active longer than intended. This increases the risk that unauthorised people could use the same access to view or change information.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must provide a clear logout option that normal users can select whenever they need to for each of these signed-in session types. When users choose logout, the session must actually end, so they cannot use it again unless they sign in again.

The organisation must also have documented procedures for how logout works, ensure the system is set up to support it, provide a message to the user confirming the session has ended, and record logout and session-ending events in its audit records so the action can be checked later.

##### User-initiated logout across web sessions and SSO-backed clients

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Add a user-controlled “Log out/Sign out” option for every authenticated communication session the application uses. When a user selects “Log out/Sign out”, the authenticated session must be ended, so the user cannot keep using it without logging in again.

###### WHY (control requirement)
This requirement comes from NIST AC-12(1) enhancement: “Provide a logout capability for user-initiated communications sessions whenever authentication is used to gain access to {{ insert: param, ac-12.01_odp }}.” Here, {{ insert: param, ac-12.01_odp }} includes local workstations, password-protected web services, and authenticated database sessions. The approach below covers the application’s authenticated web and application programming interface (API) sessions, and any single sign-on (SSO) sessions the application controls. It ensures logout is available when the user requests it and that it actually ends the authenticated session.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Standardise the logout user experience and trigger**: Add a user-facing “Log out/Sign out” action in the application interface (including any account/profile menu). This should start the identity provider (IdP) logout process (OpenID Connect (OIDC) relying party (RP)-initiated logout and/or Security Assertion Markup Language (SAML) single logout (SLO), where applicable), then redirect the user to a public landing page.
2. **End the application’s session data**: When logout is requested, invalidate the application’s server-side session (for example, remove or invalidate entries in the session store). Also clear any session-related items stored in the user’s browser (for example, session cookies), using the correct domain/path settings and the `HttpOnly` and `Secure` protections.
3. **Stop token-based access**: If the application uses OpenID Connect (OIDC) or OAuth access tokens and refresh tokens, ensure logout causes token invalidation or replacement in line with the identity provider (IdP) settings (for example, revoke refresh tokens where supported, and ensure refresh cannot be used to silently restore access).
4. **Make web and API logout behave consistently**: For browser and API use, ensure that after logout the application will not accept the user’s existing browser session cookies and any bearer tokens (for example, by relying on token expiry plus revocation/denial lists where supported, and by rejecting requests that do not include a valid active session).
5. **Show user confirmation**: After logout completes, show a confirmation page or message stating that the session has ended.
6. **Audit logout and session ending**: Record audit events for logout/session termination, including at least: user identifier, session identifier (or token/session reference), time, relying party/client identifier, and source internet protocol (IP) address. Ensure these events are sent to the organisation’s security information and event management (SIEM) and logging platform.
7. **Document behaviour and test it**: Update system design documentation and operational runbooks to explain logout behaviour, expected identity provider/application interactions, and troubleshooting steps. Include evidence that logout prevents continued use (for example, automated tests).

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application owner / lead engineer**: implement the logout button/action, session invalidation, token handling, and API enforcement.
- **Identity/access management (IAM) engineer**: configure identity provider (IdP) logout behaviour (OIDC/SAML logout, token revocation/rotation behaviour).
- **Security engineer**: define the audit event requirements and verify evidence is sufficient for audit readiness.
- **Operations / site reliability engineering (SRE)**: maintain runbooks and monitor logout-related failures.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A user can start logout from the application user interface at any time during an authenticated session.
- After logout, the user cannot access authenticated pages or API endpoints using the same browser session cookies.
- If token-based access is used, refresh-based re-login does not work after logout (in line with the identity provider (IdP) supported revocation/rotation behaviour).
- A confirmation message is shown to the user after logout.
- Logout/session termination events are recorded and visible in the security information and event management (SIEM) system with the required fields (user, session/token reference, time, client, IP address).
- Automated tests show that requests made after logout are denied until the user logs in again.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Add a user-facing **“Log out / Sign out”** button that starts the identity provider (IdP) sign-out process for the application.
- [ ] When a user logs out, end the application’s server-side session and remove all session cookies from the browser (making sure the cookies have the correct scope and security settings).
- [ ] Set up and enforce the end of user sessions and tokens so that, after logout, a refresh cannot restore access again (based on what the identity provider supports).
- [ ] Add API enforcement to block requests after a user logs out, by rejecting any requests that use invalidated session cookies and/or revoked access tokens.
- [ ] Show a logout confirmation message or page after the session has been ended successfully.
- [ ] Send audit records for logout and session end events to security information and event management (SIEM), including: user ID, session or token reference, date and time, client or application ID, and source IP address.
- [ ] Create automated tests to prove that access is blocked after logout until the user re-authenticates.


---

#### AC-12.2 — Termination Message (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure people can clearly tell when their login session has ended. In everyday terms, if a system simply disconnects you without a clear message, it may not be obvious whether you were properly logged out. This can cause confusion, mistakes, or the risk that someone else could use a session you thought had finished.

To meet this requirement, the organisation must ensure that whenever a user’s authenticated session ends, the system displays an explicit logout message that clearly confirms the session has ended, rather than only showing that the connection was lost.  

For browser-based access, the message must be shown after the session has ended. For file transfer sessions such as File Transfer Protocol (FTP), the message must be sent and displayed as the final message before the session is closed.

The organisation must also have documented procedures, define how the system behaves for different session types, set the correct system settings, and keep records showing that session termination and the logout message happened as required.

##### Explicit logout/termination message after session end (web + FTP)

- Category: Software
- Priority: High

###### WHAT
Set up a clear logout message that confirms an authenticated communications session has ended.  
- **Web access:** show the message only after the authenticated session has been ended.  
- **FTP sessions:** send the logout message as the very last message before the session is closed.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-12(2) (Enhancement) requires that users receive an explicit logout message that confirms the end of authenticated communications sessions. It also notes timing differences: web logout messages can be shown after the session ends, while FTP typically sends the logout message as the final message before closing the session.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define session-type behaviour** in the application and session management design:  
   - **Web:** first end the server-side session and revoke or expire the relevant tokens and cookies. Then show a post-logout page/message only after the server confirms the session has ended.  
   - **FTP:** configure the FTP server to send an explicit termination/logout reply as the final protocol message before closing the control connection.

2. **Web implementation:**  
   - Make sure logout actions perform **server-side session invalidation** (not just clearing cookies in the browser).  
   - Show the termination message from the server after invalidation, using a post-logout page that checks the session is no longer valid (for example, by confirming the session or token revocation status) before displaying the message.  
   - For **single sign-on (SSO)** using **OpenID Connect (OIDC)** and **Security Assertion Markup Language (SAML)**, coordinate logout so that the identity provider (IdP) and application session are ended before redirecting to the termination message page. Use correlation identifiers to link IdP session-end events to the application’s logout page.

3. **FTP implementation:**  
   - Configure the FTP service to send an explicit logout/termination reply (based on what the FTP server supports) immediately before closing the session.  
   - Ensure the server does not abruptly close the connection without sending the final termination reply for authenticated sessions.

4. **Audit evidence:**  
   - Record an event for **session termination**:  
     - **Web:** server-side invalidation and/or completion of IdP logout  
     - **FTP:** authenticated session end and final reply sent  
   - Record an event showing when the **termination message was displayed or sent**, including session identifiers and timestamps.  
   - Ensure logs can be linked together (for example, using correlation identifiers) to prove the correct timing: message after termination for web; final message before close for FTP.

5. **Operationalise:**  
   - Add runbook steps for troubleshooting when the message is not shown or sent (for example, partial logout, token revocation failure, or FTP connection drops), and document the expected behaviour for each session type.

###### WHO
- **Application owner / engineering lead:** implement web logout coordination and control when the post-logout message is shown.  
- **Infrastructure/DevOps engineer:** configure the FTP server to send the final message and ensure logging is in place.  
- **Identity and access management (IAM)/SSO administrator:** align IdP logout and session-ending behaviour and redirect flows.  
- **Security/compliance analyst:** review evidence and confirm acceptance testing results.

###### Acceptance criteria
- **Web:** After a user clicks logout (or the session ends), the termination message is shown only after the server confirms the session has been invalidated. If someone tries to access the termination page while the session is still valid, the message must not be shown (or access must be blocked).  
- **FTP:** For authenticated FTP sessions, the server sends the explicit logout/termination reply as the final protocol message before closing the connection (no silent disconnect for normal logout).  
- **Evidence:** For both web and FTP, audit logs show (1) the session termination time and (2) the time the message was displayed or sent, with correlation that allows verification of the correct order.  
- **Regression:** Automated tests cover both session types and confirm correct ordering for normal logout and common failure scenarios (for example, token already expired, network retry).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Document how session logouts work for different connection types (web versus File Transfer Protocol (FTP)), including the required timing of the logout message in relation to when the session ends.
- [ ] Set up server-side invalidation of web sessions, and only show the “post-logout” termination message after the session invalidation has been confirmed.
- [ ] Coordinate single sign-on (SSO) sign-out for OpenID Connect (OIDC) and Security Assertion Markup Language (SAML) so the identity provider (IdP) and application session are fully ended before redirecting to the sign-out confirmation page.
- [ ] Configure the File Transfer Protocol (FTP) server to send a clear logout/termination message as the final response before it closes the authenticated session.
- [ ] Add linked audit records for when a session ends and for when a message is shown or sent (including date and time stamps and session identifiers).
- [ ] Set up and run automated checks to confirm the correct sequence of messages: the web message must be sent after termination, and the final FTP message must be sent before the disconnect.


---

#### AC-12.3 — Timeout Warning Message (Enhancement)

This requirement is about giving people a clear warning before their sign-in session ends, so they are not suddenly cut off. Without a specific warning, users could lose work, miss important deadlines, or be confused about why they were logged out. This can also lead to mistakes or unsafe behaviour if people try to work around the problem.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must configure the system so that, during an active session, it shows a clear message that the session will end in 5 minutes, using the organisation’s configured 5-minute timing. The message must appear before the logout happens, be easy for a typical user to understand, and ideally encourage the user to continue the session.

The organisation must also have documented procedures that explain how sessions are ended and when the 5-minute warning is shown. It must keep records and documentation that prove the warning timing and that sessions end after the expected period.

##### Session end warning message aligned to configured timeout

- Category: Software
- Priority: High

###### WHAT
Implement a clear, user-facing warning during an active signed-in session that the session will end in **{{ insert: param, ac-12.03_odp }}** (the time period configured by the organisation). Make sure the warning is shown **before** the session ends.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-12(3) enhancement requires: **“Display an explicit message to users indicating that the session will end in {{ insert: param, ac-12.03_odp }}.”** The guidance also states this is intended to improve usability by telling users that the session is about to end, and that the time until the session ends is based on the parameters defined in AC-12.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Use the same source that controls session length and logout/session ending** (for example: the identity provider (IdP) session policy, the application session manager, or the gateway session policy). Configure the system so the warning is triggered exactly **ac-12.03_odp (default 5 minutes)** before the session ends.
2. **Add a warning user interface element** (for example, a modal, banner, or page) that appears when the remaining session time reaches the configured threshold. The message must be clear and plain language, for example:
   - “Your session will end in **5 minutes**.”
   - Include a clear prompt to continue (for example, “Click to stay signed in” / “Continue session”).
3. **Implement the “continue” behaviour** so it leads to a legitimate session extension or reauthentication that matches the organisation’s session management design (i.e., do not just hide the warning). Ensure the extension does not break the configured session policy.
4. **Show the warning while the user is still signed in**, and ensure it appears before the actual logout/session ending event.
5. **Create audit evidence** by recording both:
   - the event/time when the warning is shown (or when the remaining-time threshold is reached), and
   - the event/time when the session ends.
   Send these logs to the organisation’s security information and event management (SIEM) or logging platform to support verification that the time difference is approximately **ac-12.03_odp**.
6. **Document the configuration and process**: record where the timeout and warning offset are set, how the warning is triggered, and how the session ends (including any “continue”/reauthentication steps). Keep configuration records (policy exports/configuration snapshots) for audit.

###### WHO
- **Application Owner / Product Owner**: responsible for the user experience and correct behaviour of the warning and “continue” flow.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Engineer / Platform Engineer**: responsible for configuring session length and the warning offset in the IdP/session layer.
- **Security Engineer**: ensures audit logging, evidence requirements, and documentation meet auditability expectations.
- **QA/Test Lead**: checks timing accuracy and behaviour across the supported client types.

###### Acceptance criteria
- When a user is signed in, the system displays an explicit message stating the session will end in **{{ insert: param, ac-12.03_odp }}**.
- The warning appears **before** the session ends, and the measured time between the warning threshold being reached and the session ending is within an agreed tolerance (for example, ±30 seconds) of **ac-12.03_odp**.
- The “continue” action results in a valid session extension or reauthentication that matches the configured session policy.
- Audit logs (or equivalent evidence) exist showing the warning timing and session ending timing for test cases.
- Documentation exists describing how the warning is triggered, how the session ends, and the configured **ac-12.03_odp** value.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Identify which system controls the session length for logout (identity provider (IdP), application, or gateway) and confirm that it is governed by the AC-12 settings.
- [ ] Set the session warning timing so the warning appears exactly {{ insert: param, ac-12.03_odp }} before the session ends.
- [ ] Show a warning banner or pop-up to users that says their session will end in {{ insert: param, ac-12.03_odp }} and includes a prompt to continue.
- [ ] Set up the “continue” action so it extends the session or re-checks the user’s identity in a way that follows the policy (not just dismiss the warning).
- [ ] Add logging to record the time when the warning threshold is reached or shown, and the time when the session ends, and send these records to the security information and event management (SIEM) system.
- [ ] Create and keep audit-ready proof of configuration (policy exports and configuration snapshots), and document how sessions are ended and how warnings are issued.
- [ ] Carry out a controlled test to measure the time between a warning and a logout, and confirm that it matches {{ insert: param, ac-12.03_odp }} within the agreed tolerance.


---

### AC-14 — Permitted Actions Without Identification or Authentication (Control)

This requirement is about ensuring that, before anyone has proved who they are or logged in, only a clearly defined set of actions can be carried out on a system. Examples include viewing public information pages, receiving inbound calls on a mobile phone, receiving facsimiles, and performing emergency maintenance using a protected physical bypass switch. Without this clear list, unauthorised people could try to use the system, which could lead to information being exposed, services being disrupted, or emergency access being misused.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must identify exactly which actions are allowed without authentication (that is, without identity checks or login) and confirm that each one supports the organisation’s mission and day-to-day business needs. The organisation must then record in the system’s security plan both (1) the exact list of these permitted unauthenticated actions and (2) a plain-language explanation for why identity checks and login are not required for each action, making sure that normal restricted activities are not included.

##### Define and document permitted unauthenticated actions per system

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Create and keep an explicit, system-specific list of **user actions that may happen before the user is identified and authenticated**, limited to the organisation-approved set (for example: **viewing public information pages; receiving inbound calls on a mobile phone; receiving facsimiles; emergency maintenance using a protected physical bypass switch**). For every action on the list, record a clear reason for why identification and authentication are not needed.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-14 requires the organisation to confirm that identification and authentication are not needed for the specified user actions, and to **define and document** those actions (including any limited bypass situations) in a way that stops unauthorised use of features that normally require identification and authentication.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the list of actions allowed without identification/authentication** for the specific application/system boundary, using the organisation’s defined input **ac-14_odp (user actions)** as the starting point, and confirm there are no other unauthenticated actions.
2. For each allowed action, document:
   - the exact action name and its limits (what the user can do, and what they cannot do),
   - the expected result (for example, “view public pages only”),
   - the **reason** explaining why identification/authentication is not required for that action,
   - clear exclusions (for example, “no access to content that requires authentication, no data retrieval beyond public content, no administrative functions”).
3. **Record the decision in the system security plan** (or the equivalent system documentation the organisation uses) so auditors can check the allowed actions and reasons.
4. **Check that controls work using test evidence** for each action:
   - unauthenticated web access is limited to the public routes/pages,
   - inbound call handling is “receive-only” and does not expose processes meant for authenticated users,
   - fax handling does not allow unauthenticated retrieval of sensitive documents,
   - emergency maintenance via the protected physical bypass switch is documented and creates traceable audit records when used.
5. Put in place a **change process** so that any future change to public routes, call handling, fax handling, or emergency maintenance triggers a re-check of the AC-14 unauthenticated action list and reasons.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **System Owner / Application Owner**: accountable for the unauthenticated action list and reasons.
- **Information Security Officer / Compliance Lead**: reviews and approves the documented reasons and exclusions.
- **Engineering Lead (App/Platform/Operational Technology (OT) as applicable)**: ensures the system’s behaviour matches the documented allowed actions and provides test evidence.
- **Operations/Facilities (for the physical bypass switch)**: ensures the bypass procedure and audit evidence are followed in practice.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The system security plan includes an explicit list of allowed unauthenticated actions that matches the organisation-defined **ac-14_odp** set (or explicitly “none” if that applies).
- Each allowed action has a documented, plain-language reason and clear exclusions that prevent access to features that normally require authentication.
- Evidence exists (for example, test results, screenshots, or log extracts) showing that unauthenticated users can only do the allowed actions and cannot access authenticated-only functions.
- Emergency bypass use (physical switch) is documented and produces traceable audit evidence when used.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] List all actions that unauthorised users can take for each system, starting from **ac-14_odp**, and confirm that no further actions are allowed.
- [ ] For every action that is allowed without authentication, document: the scope (what it covers), the expected result, any explicit exclusions (what it does not include), and a plain-language reason for why it is permitted.
- [ ] Update the system security plan to include the list of actions that are allowed without authentication, along with the reasons for each.
- [ ] Run tests that try both failing and succeeding outcomes for each allowed action without logging in, and keep evidence suitable for audit.
- [ ] Document the emergency maintenance bypass procedure and confirm that audit records are created when the bypass is used.
- [ ] Add a change-control step that requires re-approval of the AC-14 unauthenticated action list whenever the related routes, user journeys, or bypass mechanisms are changed.


---

### AC-16 — Security and Privacy Attributes (Control)

This requirement is about making sure every piece of information has the correct labels that say how it must be handled, who it relates to, and how long it must be kept—from the moment it is created until it is deleted. Without this, confidential or regulated information could be shared with the wrong people, kept for too long, sent without the right protection, or used without the required permission—leading to legal, financial, and reputational harm.

The organisation must ensure that, in enterprise email, document repositories, operational technology systems, operational control workstations, and application programming interface (API) gateways, the system attaches and keeps the approved security and privacy labels with the information:
- while it is stored
- while it is being processed
- while it is being sent

The labels must use only approved values for:
- information classification (Confidential or Secret)
- export control status (Yes or No)
- retention period (30–365 days)
- encryption requirement (Required or Optional)
- access domain

Where relevant, the labels must also include privacy details about:
- whether it contains personal data
- whether it is a specific health identifier
- whether consent or authorisation is granted
- whether the person is an employee, contractor, or customer

The organisation must record and audit any changes to these labels, and review whether both the security and privacy labels are still suitable at least every 90 days.

##### Authoritative security/privacy labels bound to content with audit

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up an application feature that attaches **approved security labels** and **approved privacy labels** to information when it is **stored, processed, and sent**, keeps those labels attached throughout the information’s life, and logs **label attachment and any label changes** in a way that can be checked later.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-16 requires that security and privacy details (metadata) are **linked (bound)** to the people and systems involved (active and passive entities) so the organisation can enforce security and privacy rules (for example: access control, controlling how information can move, retention limits, allowed uses of personal data, and identifying personal information). The guidance also requires that label types and values are meaningful and relevant (only approved values/ranges), and that the way labels are applied supports trust and enforcement.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the approved label structure and allowed lists** for the systems covered, using the organisation’s agreed settings:
   - Security labels (types/values): *Information classification; export control (ITAR/EAR); retention category; encryption requirement; access domain* with values/ranges such as *Confidential/Secret; ITAR/EAR Yes/No; Retention 30–365 days; Encryption Required/Optional*.
   - Privacy labels (types/values): *Personal data (PII) indicator; HIPAA identifier flag; consent/authorisation; data subject role* with values such as *PII Yes/No; HIPAA Yes/No; consent Granted/Not granted; role Employee/Contractor/Customer*.
2. **Create an authoritative “label binding” service/library** within the application (or through an enterprise metadata or information rights management (IRM) integration) that:
   - Checks label inputs against the allowed lists (rejects or corrects invalid values).
   - Attaches labels to content at **creation** and **ingestion** (for example: API upload, document creation, message creation) and stores them as part of the content’s metadata.
   - Keeps labels through business processes (for example: versioning, transformations, exports) by carrying the label metadata along with the content.
3. **Enforce label staying power and accuracy**:
   - Stop labels being overwritten or removed during normal processing.
   - Require higher-level authorisation to change labels (for example: only a designated role/service can change security/privacy attributes).
   - When the application sends data, include the label metadata in the sending context (for example: message headers / API context / IRM envelope) so later systems can enforce the rules.
4. **Set up audit logging for label attachment and changes**:
   - For every label attachment and label change, record an unchangeable audit entry that includes: the content/object identifier, the label type(s), the values before and after, the time, and who/what made the change (user/service account).
   - Send audit entries to the enterprise security information and event management (SIEM) system for storage and correlation.
5. **Run label applicability reviews every 90 days**:
   - Set up a scheduled governance process that checks whether the security and privacy labels still apply to stored/active content, using the organisation-defined frequency (*every 90 days*), and records the result (for example: unchanged / updated / exceptions with reasons).

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Owner / Product Security Lead**: responsible for defining how labels are used and how enforcement works.
- **Software Engineers**: build the label attachment, label carrying through processes, validation, and audit logging.
- **Data Protection Officer (DPO) / Privacy Lead**: approves the privacy label types/values and the review process.
- **Security Operations / SIEM Admin**: ensures audit entries are received, stored, and used for alerts.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The application only allows **approved** security/privacy label values for the configured systems (invalid values are rejected or corrected to approved values, with an audit record).
- For content created/received through the application, labels are **attached at creation**, **remain through processing**, and are **included in the sending context**.
- Any label change (including corrections) is recorded in an unchangeable audit log with the values before and after, who made the change, and the time.
- A scheduled process runs every 90 days and produces evidence that security and privacy label applicability was reviewed and the outcomes were recorded.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the approved security and privacy data rules (schema) and the approved lists (allow-lists) for the application’s data flows that are in scope
- [ ] Set up label binding when content is created or added, using strict checks to ensure labels only use allowed values and stay within the permitted ranges.
- [ ] Ensure label information (metadata) is carried through and kept unchanged across all processing steps, version updates, and exports.
- [ ] Add controlled authorisation for label changes, and prevent normal workflows from blanking or overwriting labels.
- [ ] Set up permanent (unchangeable) audit records for every label being linked and every label being changed, including the details of what changed (before and after), who made the change, the time it happened, and the object identifier, and send these records to security information and event management (SIEM).
- [ ] Set up and run a scheduled 90-day process to check whether security and privacy labels apply, and record the results.


---

#### AC-16.1 — Dynamic Attribute Association (Enhancement)

This requirement ensures that the correct security and privacy rules automatically stay with the right people, accounts, and information as it is created, updated, combined, or moved. Without this, confidential or sensitive information could end up with the wrong access permissions, or personal data could be handled in the wrong way—raising the risk of unauthorised disclosure, inappropriate sharing, or keeping more personal information than allowed.

To meet this requirement, the organisation must use its Department of Defense (DoD) security classification and access authorisation rules (Secret or Confidential) and its DoD privacy notice and data minimisation rules (aligned to the Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act (HIPAA), the General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR), and the Cybersecurity Maturity Model Certification (CMMC)) to automatically apply the correct security and privacy labels to:

- information system users and service accounts  
- data stores such as files, databases, and message queues  
- the people represented in records (for example patients, employees, and contractors)  
- the personal data items those records relate to (for example HIPAA identifiers, GDPR personal data fields, and consent records)

The system must also keep these labels accurate when access rights change, when data is reclassified, and when information is moved or combined. Proof of these changes must be recorded in system audit logs.

##### Event-driven security/privacy label recalculation for data and identities

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up an automated, event-driven process that automatically assigns (and updates) security and privacy information to:

- **Subjects**: information system users and service accounts  
- **Objects**: data items (files, database records, message content)  
- **Privacy subjects/objects**: people represented in records and personal data items (for example, HIPAA/GDPR identifiers and consent records)

The process must apply these attributes **in line with** the organisation’s **security policies** and **privacy policies** whenever information is **created and combined** (and also when relevant policy-related details change).

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-16(1)** requires that security and privacy attributes are **dynamically assigned** to the organisation-defined subjects/objects **as information is created and combined**, based on the organisation’s security and privacy policies.

This is intended to stop incorrect or out-of-date attributes when:
- information is brought together or merged,
- access permissions change,
- security category/classification changes,
- privacy and data-minimisation context changes depending on the situation.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
###### 1) Define a standard attribute model and mapping
- Create a standard structure for **security attributes** (for example, classification such as Secret/Confidential and access-authorisation tags) and **privacy attributes** (for example, HIPAA/GDPR categories, minimisation flags, and the status of consent records).
- Define the **authoritative mapping** from the organisation’s policies to the resulting attributes (including the **rules for “created and combined”** situations).

###### 2) Build a policy-as-code “attribute service”
- Turn the organisation’s **security policies** and **privacy policies** into version-controlled, testable policy code.
- Allow attributes to be recalculated in a consistent way for the affected users and data items.
- Add logic for how attributes should be combined when data is aggregated or merged (for example, the resulting security classification and the resulting privacy context based on the combined inputs).

###### 3) Recalculate when authoritative events happen
Connect to existing enterprise systems so the process can react to events such as:
- **Identity and authorisation changes** (role/entitlement updates in identity and access management systems) to update security attributes for users and service accounts.
- **Data lifecycle events** (record create/update, file creation, message publish/consume, changes to reclassification workflows) to update security attributes for data items.
- **Aggregation and combination events** (ETL jobs, document merges, record joins, message fan-in/fan-out, report generation) to recalculate attributes for the combined outputs.
- **Privacy-relevant changes** (linking/unlinking personal data fields, changes to consent record status, changes to data-minimisation context) to recalculate privacy attributes for individuals and personal data items.

###### 4) Save results and keep audit evidence
- Store the calculated attributes back in the relevant systems (for example, storage metadata, row/record attributes in databases, message headers/metadata, and identity attribute stores).
- Record audit evidence for each recalculation, including: **what changed**, **which policy version was used**, **which event triggered it**, and the **before-and-after** attribute values.

###### 5) Ensure correct behaviour when access is requested (to prevent bypass)
- Make sure downstream access checks use the dynamically assigned attributes (for example, authorisation decisions should use the attribute metadata rather than cached or out-of-date labels).
- If direct enforcement is not possible, use an enforcement gateway that checks with the attribute service before allowing access.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Solution owner / Application security architect**: responsible for the attribute model, policy-as-code design, and integration approach.
- **Identity and integration engineers**: implement event subscriptions and write-back to identity and data platforms.
- **Data platform/database engineers**: implement metadata storage and aggregation/combination hooks.
- **Security operations / compliance**: verify audit evidence and policy version controls.

###### Acceptance criteria
- When information is **created and combined**, the system recalculates and applies the **correct security and privacy attributes** to the resulting data items according to the organisation’s policies.
- When **access authorisations** change, security attributes for affected users and service accounts are updated automatically, without manual work.
- When privacy-relevant context changes (for example, consent record status or how personal data fields are combined), privacy attributes for individuals and personal data items are updated accordingly.
- For every attribute change, the system produces audit evidence that includes the trigger event, policy version, and before-and-after attribute values.
- Application access decisions (or the enforcement gateway) use the dynamically assigned attributes, with no reliance on stale labels.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define a standard security and privacy attribute structure that covers the subjects and objects defined by the organisation.
- [ ] Create version-controlled “policy as code” that turns the organisation’s security and privacy rules into machine-readable form, including the rules for how “created and combined” outputs are put together.
- [ ] Set up event subscriptions between identity and access management (IAM) or identity providers (IdPs) and data platforms so that user attributes are recalculated when access permissions change and when data is updated or removed.
- [ ] Set up repeatable (deterministic) re-calculation rules for both security attributes and privacy attributes, for each individual record and for each personal data object.
- [ ] Update the relevant identity and data stores (metadata, headers, and row attributes) with the computed attributes, and make sure any later access checks use these updated values.
- [ ] Enable audit logging for every recalculation, including the trigger event, the policy version, and the attribute values before and after the change.


---

#### AC-16.2 — Attribute Value Changes by Authorized Individuals (Enhancement)

This expectation is about ensuring that only the right people can change important access and privacy settings, and that every such change can be traced back to who made it and when. Without this, someone could quietly change who is allowed to view information, or change how personal data is handled, which could lead to unauthorised access, privacy breaches, or decisions being made using incorrect settings.

To meet this requirement, the organisation must keep an up-to-date list of named individuals (and any approved automated processes acting on their behalf) who are allowed to set or change security-related and privacy-related attribute values. It must also have clear, documented rules and procedures for how these changes are requested, approved, and carried out. The organisation must configure its systems to block unauthorised attempts, and ensure that the system records every successful change in audit logs, including the person (or approved process) responsible and the time the change was made.

##### Authorised attribute changes via step-up admin workflow with audit trail

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a controlled way for authorised people (and explicitly approved automated processes acting for them) to set or change security and privacy attribute values. This must stop unauthorised attempts and ensure that every successful change is recorded for audit purposes, with clear accountability for who made it.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The AC-16(2) enhancement requires the system to allow authorised people (or processes acting for them) to set or change the relevant security and privacy attribute values. It also requires limiting who can create or modify these values, because they directly affect access to organisational information and how privacy is handled.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Define where attributes can be changed**: List all security and privacy attributes in the application (for example, access level, data-sharing consent, privacy handling category) and the exact application programming interface (API), user interface (UI), or administration endpoints that can change them.
2. **Create clear authorisation groups/roles**: In the enterprise identity provider, create dedicated roles/groups for **Security Attribute Changers** and **Privacy Attribute Changers**. Link each attribute (or attribute category) to the minimum role allowed to change it.
3. **Require extra verification for attribute changes**: Require privileged step-up authentication (for example, phishing-resistant multi-factor authentication (MFA)) for any request that changes an attribute value. Block the change if this extra verification is not completed.
4. **Use an approved admin process to carry out changes**: Send attribute changes through an approved workflow (for example, a ticket/workflow system integration) that records the reason for the change and the identity of the approver, then performs the change only after approval. For “processes acting for individuals”, require the workflow to link the automated execution to the authorised individual who requested or approved it.
5. **Apply detailed checks inside the application**: For every attribute change request, enforce authorisation checks on the server side (role-based access control (RBAC) and attribute-based access control (ABAC)) using the dedicated roles and the attribute-to-role mapping. Reject all unauthorised attempts.
6. **Record audit evidence for every successful change**: For each successful security/privacy attribute change, create an audit record that cannot be altered, including: the actor identity (user or approved process identity), the affected item, the attribute name, the values before and after, the date and time, the request/correlation identifier, and the workflow/ticket reference.
7. **Send audit records to SIEM and keep them**: Send audit records to the organisation’s security information and event management (SIEM) and logging platform with integrity protections (for example, restricted administrator access to logs; write-once/append-only where available) and keep them for the period required by enterprise logging standards.

###### WHO
- **Application Owner / Engineering Lead**: Implements server-side enforcement, workflow integration, and audit record creation.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Administrator**: Creates and maintains the dedicated roles/groups and the step-up authentication policies.
- **Security and Privacy Officers (or delegated governance owners)**: Define who owns each attribute and the approval rules for security versus privacy attributes.
- **Service Desk / Change Management**: Runs the approved workflow/ticket process used to request and approve changes.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Only members of the dedicated **Security Attribute Changers**/**Privacy Attribute Changers** roles (or explicitly approved workflow-bound processes acting for them) can successfully change the configured security/privacy attributes.
- Any attempt to change an attribute by a non-authorised identity is rejected by the application (no attribute value changes).
- Every successful attribute change creates an audit record containing: actor identity, date and time, affected item, attribute name, before/after values, and the workflow/ticket reference.
- For automated processes, audit records can be traced back to the authorised individual who requested or approved the change (workflow binding can be verified).
- Audit events are sent to SIEM and retained with integrity protections (no unauthorised modification of audit records).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an inventory and classification of all security and privacy attributes, and identify the administrative and application programming interface (API) endpoints that can change them.
- [ ] Create separate identity and access management (IAM) roles or groups for “Security Attribute Changers” and “Privacy Attribute Changers”, and link each role to the relevant attribute category.
- [ ] Set up step-up authentication (phishing-resistant multi-factor authentication (MFA)) for all privileged attribute write operations.
- [ ] Integrate changes to attributes into an approved process that records who requested and who approved, and ensures the action is carried out by the authorised person.
- [ ] Carry out authorisation checks on the server for every request to change an attribute, and block any unauthorised attempts.
- [ ] Set up permanent (immutable) audit records for every successful change to an attribute, recording who made the change, when it happened, the value before and after, what was changed, and the related workflow reference.
- [ ] Send audit events to security information and event management (SIEM) with integrity protections, and check that audit logs are kept for the required period and that access controls are in place.


---

#### AC-16.3 — Maintenance of Attribute Associations by System (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the system always keeps the correct security and privacy labels attached to the right people and the right information, and that these label links cannot be quietly altered or corrupted. If this is not in place, someone or an automated process could be wrongly treated as allowed (or wrongly blocked), confidential or sensitive information could be shared or kept incorrectly, and required export or privacy handling could be avoided without anyone noticing.

To meet this, the organisation must clearly define which security and privacy labels matter (for example, security clearance, need-to-know, health and data-protection tags, and export-control handling), which users, service accounts, and privileged roles they apply to, and which datasets, files, and policy or configuration items they must be linked to. The organisation must also ensure the system uses these labels for automated decisions, and that any change to the label links—or to the underlying “known good” versions of key configuration and policy items—is detected and flagged.

Finally, it must document how labels are applied, how the system is built and configured to keep them correct, include this approach in security and privacy plans, assign responsibility to relevant staff and developers, and keep evidence that integrity checks and baseline comparisons are working.

##### Signed attribute assertions and baseline-verified bindings for subjects/objects

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a trusted, authoritative mechanism that keeps the organisation-defined **security and privacy attributes** ({{param:ac-16.3_prm_1}}) correctly linked to the organisation-defined **people and systems** ({{param:ac-16.3_prm_2}}). Ensure that all decision-making points use only these maintained links when making automated decisions.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-16(3) requires the system to **keep the links and their accuracy** between {{param:ac-16.3_prm_1}} and {{param:ac-16.3_prm_2}} so that attribute links can be used as the basis for automated policy actions (for example: access control decisions, information sharing/disclosure decisions, and retention expiry). The guidance specifically calls for integrity checks and “known good” reference baselines.

###### HOW (specific approach)
###### 1) Define and version the attribute links
- Create a controlled attribute structure for the organisation-defined **security attributes** ({{param:ac-16.03_odp.01}}) and **privacy attributes** ({{param:ac-16.03_odp.02}}).
- Create a controlled mapping that links **people and systems** ({{param:ac-16.03_odp.03}} and {{param:ac-16.03_odp.05}}) and **resources/data** ({{param:ac-16.03_odp.04}} and {{param:ac-16.03_odp.06}}) to those attributes.
- Store each link as a versioned record: (person/system identifier, resource identifier, attribute set, time period it is valid for, issuing authority, link version).

###### 2) Issue tamper-evident, signed attribute statements
- Implement an **Attribute Authority** service that issues short-lived, **digitally signed** statements for the attribute links for people and systems and for resources/data.
- Decision points must be able to check these statements without trusting the requester (they must verify the signature).
- The signed content must include the link version and the validity time window, so decision points can recognise outdated or invalid links.

###### 3) Make decisions using only the maintained attributes
- Update authorisation and policy decision components (for example: API gateway, service authorisation layer, data access layer) so automated decisions are based only on the verified signed statements.
- For security configuration items and policy items ({{param:ac-16.03_odp.04}}), ensure the enforcement layer checks that the resource’s attribute link matches the required security attributes before allowing access or policy changes.

###### 4) Detect integrity problems using “known good” baselines
- Maintain a “known good” baseline for:
  - the attribute link records (or their authoritative source), and
  - the security configuration/policy items that depend on those links.
- Use an integrity monitoring mechanism to detect changes that differ from the baseline (for example: file/config integrity monitoring for on-premises repositories; signed baseline manifests for cloud configuration/policy artefacts).
- If a mismatch is detected (link version drift, signature verification failure, or baseline deviation), take a safe response: deny or limit automated actions and raise an alert for investigation.

###### 5) Operationalise and provide proof
- Document the attribute link lifecycle (creation, approval, publication, revocation) and the integrity monitoring process in the organisation’s security/privacy governance records.
- Assign responsibility to the application owner and security/privacy engineering roles for maintaining the Attribute Authority, baseline manifests, and enforcement configuration.

###### WHO
- **Security architect / IAM and policy engineer**: defines the attribute structure and link model; implements the Attribute Authority and the enforcement integration.
- **Application owner / security engineering**: ensures enforcement points use verified statements and apply safe-fail behaviour.
- **Configuration management / security operations**: maintains “known good” baselines and integrity monitoring; investigates and triages alerts.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Enforcement points successfully verify signed attribute statements for both security and privacy attributes and refuse decisions when verification fails.
- Attribute links for the defined people/systems and resources/data remain consistent with the authoritative source; any unauthorised change is detected through baseline comparison/integrity monitoring.
- Automated policy actions (access control, information sharing/disclosure, retention expiry) are demonstrably driven by the maintained attribute links.
- Evidence is available for: (1) the attribute structure and link definitions, (2) signing/verification configuration, (3) baseline manifests and integrity monitoring results, and (4) alerting and safe-fail outcomes when deviations are detected.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the approved structure (schema) for security and privacy attributes, and how those attributes are linked to the identifiers for authorised people (subjects) and resources (objects).
- [ ] Set up an attribute authority that issues short-lived, digitally signed statements confirming the link between a person (subject) and the relevant data or resource (object).
- [ ] Set up enforcement points (authorisation and policy decision steps) so automated decisions on access, information sharing, disclosure, and retention use only verified information.
- [ ] Create and publish versioned “known good” baseline settings for attribute bindings and for security configuration or policy objects that are monitored for integrity.
- [ ] Set up integrity monitoring to detect any changes from the expected baseline, and configure safe “fail-closed” behaviour—deny or restrict access and raise an alert—when a mismatch is detected.
- [ ] Produce evidence suitable for audit: records of the full document lifecycle, logs showing signature checks were successful, baseline lists of expected files, and a history of integrity monitoring alerts.


---

#### AC-16.4 — Association of Attributes by Authorized Individuals (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the right people can correctly mark information and accounts with the correct protection level and privacy meaning, and that the system keeps those marks attached to the correct records. Without this, sensitive material could be marked incorrectly or not marked at all, which could lead to unauthorised access, mishandling of regulated data, and confusion about who is allowed to see what.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must set out:
- which security labels are allowed (PUBLIC, CONFIDENTIAL, SECRET, plus compartment codes);
- which privacy tags are allowed (HIPAA role and GDPR role for people, and HIPAA PHI and GDPR personal data for information); and
- which users and service accounts are allowed to assign or change these labels and tags.

The system must allow authorised people (or automated processes acting on their behalf) to apply the tags to the correct targets, including files, directories, and network ports in regulated use, and on-premises document management items. It must also prevent invalid or unsuitable combinations.

For privileged users, the system must help them choose the right options using prompts or automated categorisation where selected, check that choices are valid, and record every change in an audit trail so the full history can be traced.

##### Authorised label/tag association with validation and audit trail

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Build application capability to link **authorised individuals (or authorised processes acting on their behalf)** to organisation-defined **security attributes** (for example: PUBLIC/CONFIDENTIAL/SECRET plus compartment codes) and **privacy attributes** (for example: HIPAA/GDPR role tags for subjects; HIPAA PHI/GDPR personal data for objects) for **authorised subjects** (users/service accounts) and **authorised objects** (files/directories/network ports and on-prem document management items).

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-16(4) requires the system to provide the capability to associate **{{ insert: param, ac-16.4_prm_1 }}** with **{{ insert: param, ac-16.4_prm_2 }}** by **authorised individuals (or processes acting on their behalf)**. This includes support for prompting/automation and ensuring **valid combinations** of security/privacy attributes.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create an attribute catalogue** in the application (or an integrated metadata service) that defines:
   - Allowed **security labels**: PUBLIC, CONFIDENTIAL, SECRET plus compartment codes.
   - Allowed **privacy tags** for **subjects**: HIPAA role (covered entity workforce) and GDPR role (data controller/processor).
   - Allowed **privacy tags** for **objects**: HIPAA PHI and GDPR personal data.
   - Allowed **valid combinations** (for example: SECRET requires at least one compartment; object privacy tags must match the object type).
2. **Add eligibility controls** so only the defined **subjects** (users/service accounts) and **objects** (files/directories/network ports; document management items) can be selected for attribute linking.
3. **Require authorisation for attribute linking** using enterprise identity integration:
   - Only users in an “Attribute Assigner” role/group (or an equivalent attribute-based access control (ABAC) policy) can create, change, or remove links.
   - For automated processes, require them to run under an **authorised service identity** that is mapped to the same “Attribute Assigner” policy.
4. **Provide user prompts and checks**:
   - When an authorised user links attributes, show controlled selection lists (drop-downs) for security labels and privacy tags.
   - Check combinations on the server at the time of linking, and reject invalid combinations with clear, actionable error messages.
5. **Support automated categorisation (where applicable)**:
   - Use policy-driven classifiers (for example: rules/templates/data loss prevention (DLP) signals) that propose or apply labels/tags only when the policy conditions are met and the resulting combination is valid.
   - Record whether the link was created by a user or by automation.
6. **Ensure links point to the correct official records**:
   - Use unchangeable internal identifiers for the target subject/object (for example: subjectId/objectId) and store the links against those identifiers.
7. **Create audit-ready records for every change**:
   - Log the actor identity (user or service identity), the target subject/object identifiers, the attributes added/removed, the time of the change, and whether it was user-driven or automated.
   - Send logs to the organisation’s security information and event management (SIEM) system and keep them according to the organisation’s logging and retention policy.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Security Architect**: defines the attribute catalogue, combination rules, and authorisation model.
- **Application Owner / Engineering Lead**: builds the user interface (UI)/application programming interface (API), validation logic, and data storage model.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Administrator**: maintains the “Attribute Assigner” role/group and service identity mappings.
- **Security Operations / Security Operations Centre (SOC)**: ensures audit records are sent to the SIEM and monitored.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Only members of the authorised assigner role/group (or authorised service identities) can link the defined security/privacy attributes to the defined subjects/objects.
- The system blocks invalid attribute combinations (enforced on the server) and returns consistent, predictable error responses.
- Links are stored against the official subject/object identifiers and can be retrieved for verification.
- Every create/change/remove action for a link produces an auditable record that includes actor identity, target identifiers, the attributes changed, and whether the action was user-driven or automated.
- Automated categorisation applies only valid combinations and can be traced to an authorised process identity.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and implement a list of approved security labels and privacy tags, including which combinations are allowed for each type of object.
- [ ] Set up server-side authorisation checks so that only authorised assigners (users or authorised service identities) can create, change, or remove attribute associations.
- [ ] Add user interface (UI) and application programming interface (API) workflows that prompt authorised users to choose from controlled options for security labels and privacy tags.
- [ ] Check that each combination is valid at the moment it is linked, and reject any invalid set of attributes with clear error messages.
- [ ] Keep links consistent using the official subject and object identifiers, and prevent links that are left without a match or are mapped to the wrong item.
- [ ] Set up audit logs for every change to an attribute association, including who made the change (actor), what it was applied to (target identifiers), which attributes were changed, the date and time (timestamp), and whether it was triggered by a user or automatically. Then send these logs to security information and event management (SIEM).


---

#### AC-16.5 — Attribute Displays on Objects to Be Output (Enhancement)

This expectation is about making sure that whenever the system prints or displays a document on a screen, it clearly explains what the information is, who it belongs to, and how it should be handled—so that sensitive information is not accidentally shown to the wrong person.
