Without this, someone could view or photograph a screen, or read a printed page, and gain access to confidential or privacy-related information they are not allowed to see.

To meet this, the organisation must ensure that every output item includes the labels **Classification**, **Handling Caveats**, **Data Owner**, **System/Network**, **Release/Distribution**, and **Privacy Notice ID**, written in a way that a non-technical reader can understand. The system must show the full values for these labels only when the viewer has permission to see unmasked information; otherwise it must show masked (redacted) values.

The organisation must document this approach in clear procedures, ensure the system is built and configured to follow them, assign responsibility to the right staff, and keep records proving that the correct labels were used and that the system showed masked versus full values as required.

##### Human-readable security/privacy attributes on all outputs

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement an attribute-aware output display method that, for every object the system sends to output devices (printed pages, screens, PDFs, exports), shows the required **security and privacy attribute labels** in a human-readable form. It must show **full attribute values only when the subscriber has permission to view them unmasked**; otherwise it must show **masked/redacted values**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-16(5) requires the system to **display security and privacy attributes** in human-readable form on **every object** it sends to output devices, using the organisation-defined **instructions** and **naming conventions**. This guidance is intended to reduce risks such as **shoulder surfing** by ensuring full values are shown only when unmasked access is permitted.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Define the output attribute set and the label text exactly** using the organisation’s naming conventions:
   - **Classification**
   - **Handling Caveats**
   - **Data Owner**
   - **System/Network**
   - **Release/Distribution**
   - **Privacy Notice ID**
2. **Create one shared output-rendering component** (library/service) that all output channels must use (web user interface views, mobile screens, server-generated PDFs, report exports, and print templates). This component must:
   - Display the six labels on every output artefact.
   - Convert stored attribute values into **human-readable text**.
   - Apply subscriber-aware masking at the time of display:
     - If `unmasked_access=true` for the current subscriber/session/device context, display **full values**.
     - If `unmasked_access=false`, display **masked/redacted values** (for example, `REDACTED` or a deterministic partial pattern).
3. **Determine unmasked access from the authorisation context** (for example, a role-based access control (RBAC) or attribute-based access control (ABAC) decision from the enterprise identity provider (IdP) session claims). Make sure this decision is checked when the output is rendered, not only when the data is stored.
4. **Prevent bypass through engineering controls**:
   - Add continuous integration/continuous delivery (CI/CD) checks (template checks or unit/integration tests) to ensure no output template or export path can display without using the shared renderer and without including the required labels.
5. **Create audit evidence for compliance**:
   - Record an audit event for each output object including: output identifier, subscriber identity, attribute set version, and `display_mode` (`full` versus `masked`).
   - Keep logs according to the organisation’s logging and retention policy so auditors can confirm the correct behaviour.

###### WHO
- **Application owner / engineering lead**: builds the shared renderer and ensures it is used across all output channels.
- **Security architect / identity and access management (IAM) engineer**: defines and validates the `unmasked_access` authorisation logic and how it maps to the subscriber/session context.
- **Compliance/audit lead**: reviews audit evidence and test results to support AC-16(5).

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every supported output device type (screens, printed pages, and equivalent artefacts such as PDFs/exports), the output includes the six required labels with human-readable text.
- When the subscriber has **unmasked access**, the output shows **full attribute values**.
- When the subscriber does **not** have unmasked access, the output shows **masked/redacted values** and does not reveal full values anywhere in the artefact (including headers/footers/side panels).
- All output channels use the shared renderer (no bypass routes are identified by CI/CD checks or tests).
- Audit logs exist for each output object showing `display_mode=full|masked` and the subscriber identity used to make the decision.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Set up a shared output-rendering component that automatically adds the six required labels to every output deliverable.
- [ ] Set up subscriber-aware permission checks so the system can decide the value of `unmasked_access` when the information is displayed (rendered).
- [ ] Use deterministic masking so full attribute values are shown only when `unmasked_access=true`; otherwise, display masked or redacted values.
- [ ] Make sure the correct renderer is used for every output path by using continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) template checks and automated tests.
- [ ] Add audit logging for each output, recording the subscriber’s identity, the version of the attribute set, and the display mode (full or masked).
- [ ] Document the process for generating and hiding (masking) the data, and get approval (sign-off) from the application owner and the security and compliance stakeholders.


---

#### AC-16.6 — Maintenance of Attribute Association (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the right people have the right permissions for the right accounts and data, and that those permissions stay correct over time. In everyday terms, the organisation must correctly record:

- which staff accounts and job roles are allowed to do what  
- which information and systems need which level of protection  
- what personal information is involved  
- what it may be used for  
- how long it must be kept  

Without this, sensitive information could be accessed or handled incorrectly, and personal data could be used or kept in ways that break organisational policy or the law.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must:

- set clear rules for linking security and privacy labels to the correct people and data  
- assign responsibility to the appropriate information security and privacy staff (and system developers where needed)  
- document the steps for doing this  
- give staff practical ways to set up and maintain these links in line with the organisation’s rules for attributes and its privacy policies

##### Attribute association maintenance via authoritative attribute policy engine

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up an authoritative, version-controlled rules engine that ensures staff keep— and, where possible, automatically keep— the link between the organisation’s defined **security and privacy attributes** and the organisation’s defined **subjects and objects**, in line with the **Attribute Management Standard** and the associated privacy policies.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-16(6) enhancement requires: **“Require personnel to associate and maintain the association of {{ac-16.6_prm_1}} with {{ac-16.6_prm_2}} in accordance with {{ac-16.6_prm_3}}.”**
The guidance explains that maintaining these attribute links is the responsibility of **individual users (not the system)**. In practice, we use automation to reduce “drift” over time, while keeping **human responsibility and approval** for exceptions and any changes to the mapping.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Turn the attribute model and mapping rules into a format computers can use**, based on the Attribute Management Standard and privacy policies. This includes:
   - Security attributes for subjects: *data classification, system role, access authorisation level* (ac-16.06_odp.01)
   - Security attributes for objects: *data classification, handling caveats, encryption requirement* (ac-16.06_odp.02)
   - Privacy attributes for subjects: *type of personal data, consent/processing basis* (ac-16.06_odp.03)
   - Privacy attributes for objects: *category of personal data, retention/disclosure constraints* (ac-16.06_odp.04)
   - Defined subjects and objects (ac-16.06_odp.05–ac-16.06_odp.08)
2. **Use authoritative sources to fill in the model**, including:
   - Identity and access management (identity and access management (IAM)) (users, service accounts, OT operator identities and roles)
   - Human resources and records systems (representations of data subjects)
   - Data catalogues / configuration management database (configuration management database (CMDB)) / asset inventory (information assets, databases)
   - OT tag registry / OT asset inventory (OT data points)
3. **Reconcile records and detect drift**:
   - Continuously (or on a defined schedule), compare the **expected attribute links** (from authoritative sources plus the mapping rules) against the **current links** stored in the attribute store.
   - Create remediation actions when mismatches are found (for example: a role change without an updated access authorisation level; a dataset classification change without an updated encryption requirement).
4. **Keep humans responsible for exceptions and changes**:
   - Require named staff (for example, Information Security/Privacy Data Steward) to approve mapping changes and exceptions through a workflow.
   - Keep records of approvals, reasons, and supporting evidence in an audit trail.
5. **Use the maintained links at the points where controls are enforced**:
   - Make sure the attribute store drives downstream enforcement (for example, conditional access/authorisation decisions and data handling controls), so links are not only documented but also actively used.
6. **Provide evidence suitable for audits**:
   - Keep version history of mapping rules and attribute links.
   - Produce periodic reports showing reconciliation results and how exceptions were handled.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Information Security Data Steward / Attribute Manager**: owns the mapping rules, approves exceptions, and checks reconciliation results.
- **Privacy Officer / Privacy Data Steward**: owns privacy attribute mappings and approves privacy-related exceptions.
- **IAM / Data Governance Engineer**: builds the integrations, runs reconciliation jobs, and ensures links are passed to enforcement points.
- **Application Owner** (where required): ensures the application uses the attribute links consistently.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The organisation-defined security and privacy attributes are represented in the attribute policy engine exactly as defined in the Attribute Management Standard (ac-16.6_prm_1) and are mapped to the organisation-defined subjects and objects (ac-16.6_prm_2).
- The system reconciles expected and current links and identifies drift.
- Any exception or mapping change must be approved by authorised staff and recorded in an audit trail.
- Attribute links are passed to the enforcement points used by the application (or its integrated authorisation/data handling services), and audit evidence is available (version history, approvals, reconciliation reports).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Turn the Attribute Management Standard and privacy policies into a computer-readable set of attribute definitions and mapping rules for the agreed security and privacy attributes.
- [ ] Use trusted sources (identity and access management (IAM), human resources (HR), data catalogue, and operational technology (OT) inventory) to fill in the people or systems (subject and object identities) and the supporting details (metadata) used to link attributes.
- [ ] Set up reconciliation jobs to compare the expected and current attribute links, and create remediation tasks when changes (drift) are detected.
- [ ] Set up an approval process so authorised staff approve exceptions and mapping changes, with full audit records of all actions.
- [ ] Link the attribute store to the application’s (or integrated) permission and data-handling controls so that the required associations are applied in practice.
- [ ] Produce regular evidence packs that show the rule versions used, the results of reconciliation checks, and any approved exceptions, for audit and ongoing assurance.


---

#### AC-16.7 — Consistent Attribute Interpretation (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure every part of your systems understands the same meaning when it receives information about (1) who is allowed to access something and (2) how data is allowed to move. If different components interpret these “rules of the road” differently, automated decisions could accidentally give access that should be blocked or allow information to move where it shouldn’t—leading to real privacy and security failures.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly define, in writing, the exact meaning of every security and privacy attribute used across distributed components, for both access decisions and data-movement decisions. It must also publish one organisation-approved guide so everyone follows the same interpretation.

The organisation must ensure relevant components are configured to apply these rules, document how they are used for access control and data-movement enforcement, assign named responsibility to the appropriate staff and developers, keep evidence from configuration records and audit logs, include this approach in security planning and the privacy policy, and verify through testing or review that components behave consistently.

##### Shared security/privacy attribute semantics with contract validation

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Create one single, authoritative meaning for every **security** and **privacy** attribute that is sent between distributed system components, and make sure every component makes access and information-sharing decisions using that same meaning.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-16(7) enhancement requires a **consistent meaning** for security and privacy attributes sent between distributed system components, so that **automated access enforcement** and **information-flow enforcement** decisions are the same across the whole environment.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **List and categorise attributes**: Identify all security and privacy attributes used for automated access enforcement and information-flow enforcement across the application’s distributed components (for example, API gateways, services, identity and access management (IAM) integrations, messaging, and service-to-service calls). Record where each attribute is created, sent, and used.
2. **Define a standard “attribute contract”**: For each attribute, document the standard name, data type, allowed values, meaning, priority rules, and how to handle edge cases (unknown/empty/invalid values, and conflicts). Include clear examples for both **access enforcement** and **information-flow enforcement** meanings.
3. **Publish one shared interpretation guide**: Create one organisation-approved, human-readable guide and a versioned machine-readable file (for example, JSON Schema, OpenAPI extensions, or a policy input schema) that represents the standard contract.
4. **Validate the contract and use “fail-closed” behaviour**: Update every component that receives attributes to check them against the contract and reject or quarantine requests with attributes that do not match (where operationally possible). Ensure the component uses the **interpretation version** from the shared guide.
5. **Use the same interpretation for access and information-flow decisions**: Configure and implement access enforcement and information-flow enforcement so they both refer to the same interpretation file (a shared library or a policy-as-code module), rather than using separate local interpretations.
6. **Keep evidence and control changes**: Record enforcement decisions along with the received attribute values and the interpretation/policy version used. Add continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) checks (contract tests) to stop releases that change meanings without updating the shared guide.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Security Architect / Security Engineering Lead**: owns the attribute contract and interpretation guide.
- **Platform/Integration Engineers**: implement contract validation and use the shared policy/interpretation in gateways/services.
- **Application Owners**: approve the meanings needed for business and privacy requirements.
- **DevOps/Release Managers**: ensure CI/CD contract tests and version control are enforced.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A complete inventory exists for all security/privacy attributes used in automated access and information-flow enforcement.
- The organisation-approved interpretation guide and machine-readable contract are published and versioned.
- All distributed components that receive attributes validate against the contract and apply the same meanings for both access and information-flow enforcement.
- Enforcement logs include the attribute values and the interpretation/policy version used.
- CI/CD contract tests show that attributes that do not match the contract are rejected/quarantined (or handled under a documented exception), and that meanings stay consistent across releases.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Identify and list all security and privacy settings used to automatically control access and to control how information moves between distributed components.
- [ ] Define a single, standard set of rules for the information used to make both access decisions and flow decisions. This includes the attribute names, data types, allowed values, what each value means, which rule takes priority when there is a conflict, and how to handle unusual or edge-case situations.
- [ ] Publish one organisation-approved interpretation guide and a version-controlled, machine-readable contract document.
- [ ] Implement contract validation in every component that uses attributes. Ensure there is documented “fail closed” or “quarantine” behaviour when an attribute does not meet the required standard.
- [ ] Update the access control and traffic-flow enforcement rules so they use the shared interpretation resource (the shared library or policy module) instead of their own local definitions.
- [ ] Add continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) contract tests and release checks to prevent the meaning from changing over time, without updating the shared interpretation guide.
- [ ] Set up enforcement logging to record the attribute values that were received and the interpretation or policy version that was used.


---

#### AC-16.8 — Association Techniques and Technologies (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that the labels describing how sensitive data is, and how it may be used, are securely linked to the data itself so they cannot be changed without detection. Without this, someone could alter the labels to gain access they should not have, or to send personal information through the wrong processing steps, which would weaken both privacy commitments and access rules.

To meet this requirement, the organisation must use a defined method to cryptographically bind (securely link) the security and privacy labels to each item of information. This should be done by embedding signed label details in the data’s metadata, with the signing keys protected using hardware-based safeguards supported by a hardware root of trust. The system must verify the signed security label every time access is requested, and verify the signed privacy label every time the data is processed. Only after these checks should the system use the verified labels to make automated access and data-handling decisions.

The organisation must document the approach, set up and record the required system behaviour, keep evidence in audit records, include it in both security and privacy plans, and assign clear responsibilities to information security and privacy staff, as well as to system developers.

##### JWS-bound security/privacy labels verified at access/processing

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement a cryptographic link between security and privacy labels and the information they describe. Do this by placing **signed metadata (JSON Web Signature (JWS))** inside the object’s metadata, using signing keys protected by a **hardware security module (HSM)-backed hardware root of trust**. Make sure the system **checks the security-label JWS when access is requested** and **checks the privacy-label JWS when the data is processed** before any automated access enforcement or any data movement/processing decisions are made.

###### WHY (control requirement)
National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Access Control (AC)-16(8) requires implementing the organisation-defined method for linking security and privacy attributes to information. The guidance explains that this linking can be achieved by **cryptographically binding attributes to information using digital signatures**, and that keys can be protected using **hardware roots of trust**. Verification must happen at the right decision points so automated enforcement actions can trust the labels.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Define the label-linking method and format**: Create a standard label format for both security and privacy attributes (for example: label identifiers, version, issuing authority, object identifier/hash, and any required claims). Use a standard JSON format to avoid differences that could affect signature checking.
2. **Create and store signed label metadata**:
   - Build a label-signing component/service that produces **two JWS tokens**: one for the **security label** and one for the **privacy label**.
   - Store the JWS tokens in **object metadata** (or in a separate linked metadata record using the object hash, if the storage system cannot reliably store metadata).
3. **Use HSM-protected keys**:
   - Set up signing keys in an **HSM-backed key management service (KMS)/HSM cluster** with **non-exportable private keys**.
   - Ensure signing is performed through the HSM interface, and record the key identifiers/aliases used for each signature.
4. **Verify at access time (security label)**:
   - Put an enforcement point in front of data access (for example: an application programming interface (API) gateway/data access proxy/policy decision point).
   - For every access request, take the security-label JWS from the metadata, verify the signature using the trusted public key/certificate chain, check the label details (including the object binding using the object identifier/hash), and only then apply the access policy decision.
5. **Verify at processing time (privacy label)**:
   - Perform verification at the points where data enters processing/data flows (for example: workflow/orchestration gateway, extract-transform-load (ETL)/stream processing entry, export/share service).
   - Verify the privacy-label JWS before allowing routing, transformation, export, or any downstream processing.
6. **Fail safely and record evidence**:
   - If either verification fails (invalid signature, invalid label details, object binding mismatch, expired/unknown key), treat the label as **not trusted** and deny/block the relevant access or processing.
   - Write audit logs that include: object identifier, verification result (success/failure), label values used (when successful), key identifier, and the decision (allow/deny/block).

###### WHO (roles responsible)
- **Information Security Architect / Data Governance Lead**: define the label format, standardisation rules, and how decision points map to those labels.
- **Application Security Engineer / Platform Engineer**: implement JWS signing, metadata storage, and the verification enforcement points.
- **Security Operations / security information and event management (SIEM) Engineer**: ensure audit logging, retention, and alerts for verification failures.
- **Privacy Officer / Privacy Engineering**: confirm privacy label meaning and the requirements for enforcing at processing time.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every protected object, the system stores **security-label JWS** and **privacy-label JWS** in object metadata (or equivalent separate metadata linked by object hash).
- Signing keys are **protected by an HSM** (non-exportable private keys; signing performed via the HSM interface).
- At **access time**, the enforcement point verifies the security-label JWS and denies access when verification fails.
- At **processing time**, the processing gateway verifies the privacy-label JWS and blocks processing/routing when verification fails.
- Audit records exist for every verification attempt, including the result and key identifier, and are kept according to the organisation’s logging requirements.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define a standard security and privacy label system, and an object-binding claim (an object identifier or hash), for signing using JSON Web Signatures (JWS).
- [ ] Set up a label-signing service that creates security-label JSON Web Signatures (JWS) and privacy-label JSON Web Signatures (JWS) using hardware security module (HSM)-backed keys that cannot be exported.
- [ ] Store the signed JSON Web Signature (JWS) tokens in the object’s metadata when the object is created or updated (or, alternatively, store them in a separate “sidecar” metadata record linked to the object by its hash).
- [ ] Check access time at the point where data access is enforced, and block or deny access if verification of the security label fails (using a JSON Web Signature (JWS)).
- [ ] Set up a check that confirms processing time at the point where workflows, extract-transform-load (ETL), and exports enter the system, and stop any routing or processing if the privacy label JSON Web Signature (JWS) verification fails.
- [ ] Set up audit records for every verification attempt, including the result, the key identifier, and the label values used, and send this information to a security information and event management (SIEM) system.


---

#### AC-16.9 — Attribute Reassignment — Regrading Mechanisms (Enhancement)

This requirement ensures that any change to the security and privacy labels attached to information is made only through a trusted, approved re-labelling process. Without this control, the organisation could accidentally or incorrectly treat sensitive information as less protected (or more protected than it should be), which could result in unauthorised access, incorrect handling, or privacy breaches.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must use an approved data-labelling workflow that includes:
- a formal policy-exception request
- re-tagging that can be verified
- complete records of what was changed and when

Before the process is used, it must be validated for security labels using automated checks against the approved exception and test examples. It must also be validated for privacy labels by confirming the labels follow the required format and by running tests that compare what different users can access before release.

Only authorised staff and developers should make these reassignments, using documented procedures and keeping audit records.

##### Validated, ticketed label regrading service with signed retags

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement a single-purpose, approved **regrading mechanism** (a Label Regrading Service/workflow) so that **changes to security and privacy attributes** for information happen **only** through this mechanism. The mechanism must be **checked and verified** using the organisation’s defined methods/processes for security and privacy attributes, and it must perform **cryptographic re-tagging** with **complete audit records**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-16(9) requires that security and privacy attribute changes are made **only via regrading mechanisms that are verified using** the organisation-defined method/process parameter **ac-16.9_prm_1**. The guidance also requires that regrading mechanisms are **trusted**, **single purpose/limited function**, and verified because attribute changes can directly affect **policy enforcement**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create an approved regrading workflow** integrated with policy-exception ticketing: every regrade request must reference an approved exception ticket (including scope, expiry, and reason) and be limited to the defined label reassignment use case.
2. **Implement cryptographic re-tagging**: after verification, the service issues integrity-protected regraded label tokens (for example, signed label metadata) that include the exception ID, label version, and a validation evidence hash.
3. **Verify security attribute reassignment** using the organisation-defined verification approach in **ac-16.09_odp.01** (automated rule checks against the approved policy exception and security test vectors) before any re-tag is released.
4. **Verify privacy attribute reassignment** using the organisation-defined verification approach in **ac-16.09_odp.02** (schema conformance checks and differential access-control tests before release).
5. **Apply single-purpose/limited-function controls** in the service: restrict the interface to label reassignment only (no general data transformation), require authenticated/authorised callers, and block bypass routes (for example, direct database label edits).
6. **Create audit logging** for every regrade: record who/what/when/why, the exception ticket link, input/output label values, validation run identifiers, and pass/fail outcomes; send logs to the enterprise security information and event management (SIEM) system and retain them without alteration.
7. **Put governance into practice**: publish runbooks and technical design covering the mechanism, how validation evidence is produced, and how downstream enforcement points must rely only on the signed regraded labels.

###### WHO
- **Application/Platform Owner**: responsible for deploying and running the Label Regrading Service.
- **Security Engineering / Identity and Access Management (IAM) & Data Governance team**: owns the verification logic, exception-ticket integration, and cryptographic signing key management.
- **Compliance/Information Security**: approves the policy exception workflow and confirms that the evidence meets audit expectations.
- **Developers/Integration engineers**: integrate downstream systems so they consume only signed regraded labels.

###### Acceptance criteria
- All security/privacy attribute changes are carried out **only** through the regrading service workflow; no other method exists for label reassignment.
- For each regrade request, the service performs **security verification** (automated rule checks against the approved exception plus security test vectors) and **privacy verification** (schema conformance plus differential access-control tests) and stops the process on failure.
- Regraded labels are cryptographically integrity-protected and include the exception ID and validation evidence hash.
- Audit logs are produced for every attempt (successful or failed) and are immutable, searchable, and linked to the exception ticket and validation run.
- The service is clearly **single-purpose/limited-function** (label reassignment only) and can be invoked only by authorised roles.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Design and implement a dedicated “label regrading” service that does only label reassignment.
- [ ] Integrate the service with the policy-exception ticketing process so every request refers to an approved exception, with the exception’s scope and expiry date enforced.
- [ ] Before each retag release, carry out automated security checks against the approved exception list and a set of pre-approved security test cases.
- [ ] Before releasing a retag, carry out privacy checks by confirming the data matches the required format (schema conformance checks) and by running tests that verify access controls behave correctly (differential access-control tests).
- [ ] Add cryptographic re-tagging using signed, integrity-protected label tokens, including an exception identifier and a validation evidence hash.
- [ ] Set up tamper-proof audit records for every regrade attempt, and send the logs to the organisation’s security information and event management (SIEM) system, linked to the relevant ticket and including the validation results.
- [ ] Update the downstream enforcement points so they only accept regraded labels that have been digitally signed, and block and alert on any direct label changes outside the service.


---

#### AC-16.10 — Attribute Configuration by Authorized Individuals (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that only the right people can decide which labels and rules the system uses to describe who is allowed to access information and how that information should be handled. If unauthorised staff could create or change these labels and values, they could accidentally or deliberately give themselves or others access they should not have, or incorrectly classify sensitive information. This could lead to real compliance and privacy failures.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must give authorised individuals the ability to define or change the types and values of both access-related (security) and privacy-related attributes. These attributes must be able to be linked to both people and information. The organisation must also have clear written procedures and documented rules that state who is authorised, ensure the system settings enforce those permissions, and keep records of changes so any attempt to alter attributes can be traced back to the person who made the change.

##### Authorised-only security/privacy attribute schema configuration

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a controlled, auditable way for authorised people to define or change the **type and value** of **security and privacy attributes** that can be linked to **subjects and objects**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-16(10) enhancement requires the system to limit who can create or change the **type and value** of **security and privacy attributes** that can be linked to **subjects and objects** to **authorised people only**. Because attribute details can directly affect access and privacy outcomes, the controls must be enforced technically and recorded for audit purposes.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create dedicated authorisation roles** for attribute setup (separately for security attributes and privacy attributes) and assign them to individuals in the enterprise identity provider (IdP).
2. **Require phishing-resistant multi-factor authentication (MFA) and strong session controls** to access the attribute configuration user interface (UI) and application programming interface (API) (for example, FIDO2/passkeys where feasible; conditional access for admin consoles).
3. **Limit configuration changes at the application/API level**: only the authorised roles can create, update, or delete attribute **types** and **values**; everyone else must be blocked when the request is made.
4. **Treat attribute schemas as version-controlled configuration**: keep the intended schema state in a controlled repository and require validation (policy-as-code or equivalent rule checks) before release.
5. **Use an approval process for schema changes** (for example, ticket/approval plus automated validation) and **prevent direct emergency edits in production**.
6. **Record tamper-evident audit events** for every schema change, including: the person who started the change, the time, the attribute type/value changed, the old and new values, the approval/ticket reference, and whether the change was allowed or blocked.
7. **Restrict subject/object linking** so the application only allows links using the currently deployed, authorised schema set; reject any unknown or not-configured attribute types/values.

###### WHO
- **Application Security Lead / Identity and Access Management (IAM) Engineer**: define roles, IdP mappings, and admin authentication requirements.
- **Application Owner / Engineering Lead**: implement API/UI enforcement, schema versioning, and linking restrictions.
- **Security Operations / Security information and event management (SIEM) Engineer**: set up audit event forwarding, retention, and alerts.
- **Change Manager / Governance Lead**: ensure the approval workflow and change records match organisational processes.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Only users in the designated authorised roles can create, update, or delete security and privacy attribute types/values; all other attempts are technically blocked.
- Admin configuration actions require phishing-resistant multi-factor authentication (MFA) and meet the defined session controls.
- Every schema change generates an audit record showing the initiating identity, old/new values, and the approval reference; audit events are sent to SIEM and kept according to policy.
- Subject/object linking screens and APIs reject attribute types/values that are not in the currently deployed authorised schema set.
- Production schema changes are made only through the approved, validated workflow (no direct emergency hot edits).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and implement separate authorised roles for configuring security attributes and privacy attributes, and map those roles in the organisation’s identity provider (IdP).
- [ ] Require phishing-resistant multi-factor authentication (MFA) and conditional access for all attribute configuration user interface (UI) and application programming interface (API) endpoints.
- [ ] Set up checks for application programming interface (API) permissions so that only authorised roles can create, update, or delete attribute types and values.
- [ ] Store attribute schemas as version-controlled configuration and automatically check they meet the required rules (for example, using policy-as-code or an equivalent method) before deployment.
- [ ] Set up an approval process for database schema changes and stop making direct emergency changes in the live production environment.
- [ ] Create tamper-evident audit records for every database schema change, including who made the change, when it was made, what changed, the old value, the new value, and the approval reference, and send them to security information and event management (SIEM).
- [ ] Limit attribute-to-field associations to the currently deployed authorised schema set, and reject any unknown types or values.


---

### AC-17 — Remote Access (Control)

This requirement is about ensuring that only approved ways of connecting from outside the organisation are allowed to reach your systems. It also requires that every such connection is set up and used in a controlled, documented way. Without clear rules and prior approval, staff or devices could connect using the wrong method, bypass safeguards, or gain access before anyone has checked that the connection is safe and appropriate. This increases the risk of unauthorised access and data loss.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must:
- List every type of remote access it allows (for example, dial-up, broadband, or wireless).
- For each type, write clear, plain-language rules covering who may use it, what is allowed and prohibited, the exact connection and configuration requirements, and practical guidance on how to implement it correctly.
- Ensure each remote access type is formally approved before any connections are allowed.
- Be able to produce records showing remote access activity for review.

##### Authorised remote access types registry with gateway enforcement

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Create and maintain an auditable, per-system register of **every way the organisation allows remote access**, including the **restrictions**, **required connection and configuration settings**, and **implementation guidance** for each type. Make sure that **no remote access type is enabled for a system until it has been formally authorised**, and that remote access activity can be evidenced.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-17 requires that remote access (access via external networks such as the Internet) is controlled so that only **approved remote access types** are allowed, with **authorisation before remote access is permitted**, and with enough assurance that encrypted virtual private network (VPN) protections are set up correctly. It also recognises that encrypted VPNs can affect monitoring and availability, so the solution must support controlled access and auditability.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Build a Remote Access Types Register** (the single source of truth) for each application/system and environment (Production, Test, Disaster Recovery). Each entry must state:
   - Remote access type (for example: encrypted VPN profile, zero trust network access (ZTNA) app access, vendor support through an approved jump host, wireless administration access if applicable)
   - Allowed user groups/populations and any required roles
   - Allowed source networks/locations and any time windows
   - Required authentication method(s) (linked to the organisation’s identity system)
   - Required encryption/tunnel settings (for VPN types) and any features that are not allowed (for example: split tunnelling unless explicitly approved)
   - Connection and configuration requirements (gateway profile settings, client requirements, and ports/protocols where applicable)
   - Usage restrictions and prohibited behaviour
   - Implementation guidance (runbook) and a verification checklist
2. **Put enforcement controls at the remote access entry point** so unauthorised remote access types cannot connect:
   - Configure the VPN concentrator, ZTNA gateway, jump host, or bastion host to allow only the approved connection settings and authentication methods defined in the register.
   - Add identity-based enforcement (for example, conditional access) that requires the user/device/session to match the authorised remote access type and security posture.
3. **Create an authorisation process for each remote access type** that must be approved before enabling the matching gateway and identity controls for that system/environment. The approval record must link:
   - The register entry (remote access type)
   - The system/environment
   - The approver(s)
   - The effective date/time and expiry (if applicable)
4. **Produce audit evidence** by ensuring gateway and authentication logs record, at minimum: user identity, remote access type/profile, connection start time, and source endpoint/network details. Send logs to the security information and event management (SIEM) system for retention and review.
5. **Identify configuration changes that drift from the plan** by running automated checks on a regular basis to confirm that enabled gateway and identity controls match the register; raise alerts for differences and block changes that are not linked to an approved authorisation record.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Owner / System Owner**: responsible for the per-system register and ensuring only authorised remote access types are permitted.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Engineer / Security Engineer**: implements identity conditional access and gateway enforcement.
- **Network/Platform Engineer**: implements VPN/ZTNA/jump host configuration templates and drift detection.
- **Security/Compliance**: checks that the evidence is sufficient and approves the authorisation process.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each system/environment, the Remote Access Types Register lists all permitted remote access types, with documented restrictions, connection/configuration requirements, and implementation guidance.
- No remote access type is enabled in gateway/identity controls without a corresponding, pre-dated authorisation process record linked to the register entry.
- Gateway/identity logs allow identification of the remote access type/profile, user identity, connection start time, and source endpoint/network.
- Automated drift checks confirm that enabled configurations match the register; deviations trigger alerts and are corrected.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a separate “Remote Access Types Register” for each system. It must list every remote access type that is allowed, including any restrictions, required settings, and step-by-step implementation instructions (runbooks).
- [ ] Set up gateway controls (virtual private network (VPN), zero trust network access (ZTNA), jump host, or bastion) so that only approved connection profiles and approved authentication methods from the register are allowed.
- [ ] Set up identity-based conditional access so that remote sessions are only allowed when they match the authorised remote access type and meet the required security posture.
- [ ] Set up an approval process for remote access. Each type of remote access must be approved and time-stamped before the matching gateway and identity rules are enabled for each environment.
- [ ] Set up and test audit logging so it records remote access type and profile, the user’s identity, the time the connection started, and details of the source endpoint and network. Then send this information to security information and event management (SIEM).
- [ ] Automate checks for configuration changes by comparing the currently enabled gateway and identity settings against the approved register, and alert or block any unauthorised changes.


---

#### AC-17.1 — Monitoring and Control (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that every time someone connects to your systems from a distance, the organisation automatically checks that the connection is allowed and records what happened. Without this, unauthorised people could try to break in, or staff could connect in ways that do not follow company rules, and you might only find out after damage has already occurred.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must use automated tools to continuously monitor remote connections from all supported device types, including servers, laptops, workstations, smartphones and tablets. It must also automatically enforce the organisation’s remote access rules by allowing or blocking connection sessions based on those rules. In addition, it must automatically produce clear records of the monitoring activity and of any connections that were allowed or blocked, using the agreed details, so that later reviews can demonstrate compliance.

The organisation should be able to show that these monitoring and blocking features are enabled in current settings, work in practice, and are reviewed by the responsible people.

##### Policy-enforced remote access with automated allow/deny logging

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Put in place automated controls that (1) **monitor** remote access sessions as they are started and (2) **allow or block** remote access automatically based on the organisation’s remote access rules.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-17(1)** requires the organisation to **use automated controls to monitor and manage remote access methods**. The guidance says monitoring and control should help detect attacks and ensure remote access rules are followed by checking connection activity across systems (for example: servers, laptop computers, workstations, smart phones, and tablets). Remote access audit records are required under **AU-2**, and the details of which remote access events must be recorded are defined in **AU-2(a)**.

###### HOW (specific approach)
Use an **identity-first remote access gateway that enforces policy** (for example: ZTNA/secure access proxy/VPN with policy enforcement) connected to the organisation’s identity provider (IdP) and signals about whether the user’s device is in an approved state. Configure the enforcement point to:
1. **Check every remote access attempt/session** against the remote access rules at the time the session is created (and re-check when the platform supports it).
2. **Automatically allow or block** the session based on the rules (for example: approved user groups/roles, required multi-factor authentication (MFA), device compliance status, source network/IP/region limits, time-of-day limits, and how sensitive the target system/service is).
3. **Record audit events for both outcomes** (allowed and blocked/denied), including the remote access method, target system/service, user identity, and any available information about the source device/IP.
4. **Send structured audit events** into the organisation’s security information and event management (SIEM) and logging system, so the remote access audit event format matches the organisation’s **AU-2/AU-2(a)** requirements.

###### Implementation steps (technology-agnostic but concrete)
- **List and standardise remote access entry points** (VPN, remote desktop protocol (RDP)/secure shell (SSH) jump hosts, remote web portal, ZTNA applications, mobile access) and confirm which ones are in scope for **AC-17(1)**.
- **Connect all entry points to the IdP** so authentication and permission decisions are handled centrally (single sign-on (SSO) plus required multi-factor authentication (MFA)).
- **Set conditional access/session authorisation** on the enforcement point to apply remote access rules (allow/deny/restrict) using IdP information and device compliance signals from the endpoint/device management platform.
- **Turn on audit logging at enforcement time** on the gateway/IdP for remote access attempts, ensuring events are created for both successful and denied sessions.
- **Match and validate event details** against the organisation’s required remote access audit event information (**AU-2/AU-2(a)**), and ensure logs include the result (allowed vs blocked/denied) and connection context.
- **Set up automated alerts and detections** for repeated denied attempts, unusual source/device compliance signals, and remote access to sensitive targets; ensure alerts are based on the same audit events.

###### WHO (roles responsible)
- **Security Architect / Identity and Access Management (IAM) Lead**: designs the policy model, IdP/gateway integration, and enforcement logic.
- **Network/Security Engineering**: builds and configures the remote access gateway/ZTNA/VPN enforcement and logging.
- **Security Information and Event Management (SIEM)/Monitoring Engineer**: ensures audit event forwarding, field mapping, retention, and detection rules.
- **System Owner / Authorising Official**: confirms the remote access policy rules and approves which entry points are in scope.

###### Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
- Every remote access method in scope routes through the policy-enforcing enforcement point (gateway/IdP integration) and **automatically denies** sessions that do not meet the remote access rules.
- For both **allowed** and **blocked/denied** remote access attempts, the system generates **audit events** that are sent to the SIEM and include, at minimum, remote access method, target system/service, user, and outcome; source device/IP is included where available.
- The audit event format and required fields **match the organisation’s AU-2/AU-2(a) remote access audit event definitions**.
- Demonstrated test: an unauthorised user and a device that is not compliant attempt remote access and are **blocked automatically**, with the corresponding allow/deny audit events visible in the SIEM within the expected time for ingestion.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] List all ways people can access systems remotely, and identify which ones fall within the scope of AC-17(1).
- [ ] Connect every remote access entry point to the organisation’s identity provider (IdP) so users are authenticated and authorised centrally.
- [ ] Set up rules for conditional access and session authorisation to automatically allow or block remote access based on the remote access policy.
- [ ] Integrate information about the security state of each device so the enforcement point can check whether the device meets compliance requirements and apply the correct controls.
- [ ] Turn on and check audit logging at the time of enforcement for both permitted and blocked or denied remote access attempts.
- [ ] Map the fields from remote access audit events to the organisation’s remote access audit event definitions in AU-2 and AU-2(a), and then check that the mapping is correct in the security information and event management (SIEM) system.
- [ ] Set up security information and event management (SIEM) alerts for denied login attempts and unusual remote access patterns, using the same audit events.


---

#### AC-17.2 — Protection of Confidentiality and Integrity Using Encryption (Enhancement)

This requirement is about protecting remote connections to your systems. It ensures that the information people send and receive is kept secret and cannot be changed while it travels over the internet. Without this protection, an attacker could intercept remote connections, read sensitive information, or alter messages, which could lead to fraud, data leaks, or unauthorised changes.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must use approved, secure encryption for every remote access session. This should use a recognised secure remote method, such as a private network connection and/or a secure web connection, and it must be set up correctly. The organisation must record which encryption methods are used and how they are configured, keep the relevant evidence and audit information showing encryption was active, and ensure remote access instructions and staff procedures follow the organisation’s access rules.

The organisation should also maintain design and security planning documents that clearly explain how encryption protects remote access.

##### Enforce VPN/TLS-only remote access with approved cryptography

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement and enforce cryptographic protection for **all remote access sessions** to the application and its administrative interfaces using **approved methods** (for example, **virtual private network (VPN)** and/or **transport layer security (TLS)**). This must ensure **confidentiality and integrity** from end to end.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) access control (AC)-17(2) enhancement requires: **“Implement cryptographic mechanisms to protect the confidentiality and integrity of remote access sessions.”** Guidance notes that **VPN** and **TLS** are examples of technologies used to secure communications.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define approved remote access routes** in the access control standard (for example: “VPN required for all remote access to internal networks and administrative functions”; “TLS only for any remote web/application programming interface (API) access endpoints”).
2. **Enforce VPN-first / TLS-only connections** so remote users cannot reach application or administrative endpoints through unencrypted or non-approved routes:
   - Block direct internet-to-internal network access; allow only traffic that passes through the VPN gateway or an approved TLS reverse proxy/entry point.
   - For any web/API remote access, require **HTTPS** using modern TLS and disable **HTTP**.
3. **Apply approved cryptography consistently across environments**:
   - **VPN**: use an approved VPN method (for example, internet protocol security (IPsec) with internet key exchange version 2 (IKEv2), or a TLS-based VPN) with minimum protocol, cipher, and key-exchange strength, plus certificate validation.
   - **TLS**: enforce a minimum TLS version, strong cipher suites, and certificate validation; enable HTTP Strict Transport Security (HSTS) for web where applicable.
4. **Add identity and session controls for remote access**:
   - Require enterprise identity authentication (preferably phishing-resistant multi-factor authentication (MFA)) at the VPN/TLS entry point, using conditional access where available.
   - Ensure session re-checks or session termination match policy changes (for example, account revocation or risk-based re-authentication).
5. **Create audit-ready evidence**:
   - Enable logging at the VPN gateway and/or TLS termination layer to record session start/stop, user identity, source internet protocol (IP) address, and the negotiated cryptographic settings where available.
   - Send logs to a security information and event management (SIEM) system and retain them according to the system security plan.
6. **Document the cryptographic methods and configuration**:
   - Keep a configuration baseline and system design/security plan evidence showing which cryptographic methods are used for remote access and how they are configured.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Security Architect / identity and access management (IAM) and network security engineer**: define approved remote access routes and cryptographic baselines.
- **Network engineer / cloud platform engineer**: implement VPN/TLS enforcement and cryptographic configuration.
- **Application owner / DevOps engineer**: ensure administrative and remote endpoints are reachable only via the approved TLS entry point.
- **Security operations centre (SOC) / SIEM administrator**: verify logging, alerts, and retention.

###### Acceptance criteria
- All remote access to application and administrative functions is only possible via the approved VPN and/or TLS entry points; attempts to bypass are blocked.
- VPN and/or TLS endpoints are configured with approved cryptographic settings (minimum TLS version/ciphers; approved VPN method and strong key exchange).
- VPN/TLS logs show that encrypted sessions are established and include user identity and session details for every remote access session.
- Documentation exists (baseline plus system design/security plan evidence) linking remote access methods to the approved cryptographic configuration.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and publish the approved remote access routes in the access control standard, limiting remote access to either a virtual private network (VPN) only for internal and administrative access and/or transport layer security (TLS) only for remote web and application programming interface (API) access.
- [ ] Set up network, firewall, and internet-entry (ingress) rules to block direct access from the public internet to internal network ranges and administrative pages, except through the virtual private network (VPN) gateway or an approved Transport Layer Security (TLS) termination point.
- [ ] Strengthen the virtual private network (VPN) and transport layer security (TLS) settings to meet the organisation’s approved encryption standards, including the minimum supported protocol versions, approved cipher suites, certificate checking requirements, and the strength of the key exchange.
- [ ] Require users to prove their identity when accessing remotely (integrate with the organisation’s identity provider and require multi-factor authentication and conditional access in line with policy)
- [ ] Send and centralise virtual private network (VPN) and transport layer security (TLS) termination logs into the security information and event management (SIEM) system, including session start and end times, user identity, source internet protocol (IP) address, and the cryptographic settings agreed during the connection where available.
- [ ] Maintain configuration baseline documentation and keep evidence of the system design and security plan showing which cryptographic methods are used for remote access and how they are configured.


---

#### AC-17.3 — Managed Access Control Points (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that anyone connecting to your systems from outside the office does so only through approved access points that your organisation controls and monitors, rather than finding a direct route in. Without this, attackers could bypass your protections, reach your systems from the open internet, and potentially steal data, disrupt services, or take over accounts.

To meet this expectation, your organisation must:
- set clear written rules and step-by-step procedures for remote access, including which access points are approved; and
- keep up-to-date design and configuration records showing how remote access is routed through those approved points.

You also need:
- a current, complete list of every approved access point used for remote access; and
- audit records showing that the routing is actually being followed; and
- a system security plan that describes and supports this approach.

##### Centralise remote access via managed gateways with enforced routing

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Ensure all remote access to the in-scope application’s administrative and management functions goes through a small, organisation-approved set of managed network access points (for example, virtual private network (VPN) concentrators and/or zero trust network access (ZTNA) secure remote access web proxies). The target systems must only allow remote administrative connections from these approved access points (or from their controlled identity and session context).

###### WHY (control requirement)
National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Special Publication (SP) 800-53 Access Control (AC)-17(3) (Enhancement) requires that remote access is routed through authorised and managed network access points. The guidance states that limiting the number of access points reduces the attack surface.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the approved access points set**: create and maintain an official register of approved gateways (host name/internet protocol (IP) address, purpose, owner, environment scope, and monitoring/log sources). Keep it up to date through change management.
2. **Prevent bypass by enforcing network routing**: update network security groups and firewall rules so that inbound administrative ports to the target systems are allowed only from the gateway networks (or gateway security groups). Block direct internet-to-target administrative access.
3. **Require identity checks at the entry point**: connect the gateways to the organisation’s identity provider (IdP) and apply conditional access and phishing-resistant multi-factor authentication (MFA) (for example, FIDO2 security keys/passkeys where available). Ensure session setup is controlled at the gateway.
4. **Secure and standardise gateway settings**: apply secure configuration baselines and, where feasible, configuration-as-code for gateway policies, allowed destinations, and authentication/session settings; require peer review for changes.
5. **Limit access on the target side**: for each target layer (administration services, management endpoints, jump hosts), apply source restrictions (IP allowlists/security group rules) that match the approved gateway register.
6. **Centralise and keep evidence**: send gateway logs and firewall allow/deny logs (including authentication/session events) to the security information and event management (SIEM) system. Set up alerts to detect any attempted direct-to-target administrative access.
7. **Document the design and operating model**: update system design and network diagrams, and the system security plan, to show that remote access enters only through the approved gateways, including expected log sources and retention periods.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Network Security Engineer / Cloud Network Engineer**: implement firewall and security group rules, and enforce routing.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Engineer**: connect gateways to the IdP, configure conditional access, and implement phishing-resistant MFA.
- **Application Owner / Platform Owner**: ensure administrative endpoints are isolated and reachable only via the gateway.
- **Security Architect / Governance, Risk and Compliance (GRC)**: maintain the approved access points register, update the system security plan, and validate audit evidence.
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC) / SIEM Analyst**: ensure logs are collected, retained, and monitored with appropriate detections.

###### Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
- An up-to-date register exists listing all approved managed access control points used for remote access, including owners and monitoring/log sources.
- Network controls prevent direct remote administrative access from the internet to the target systems; only traffic originating from the approved gateways is allowed.
- Gateway authentication uses the enterprise IdP with conditional access and phishing-resistant MFA (where supported).
- Gateway and firewall logs show remote administrative sessions originate via the approved gateways; SIEM collection and retention are confirmed.
- System design documentation and the system security plan explicitly describe gateway-only remote access routing and the associated log sources.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and keep an official list of approved managed access control points, including their gateway identifiers, owners, environments, and log sources.
- [ ] Set up firewalls and security groups so that administrative access ports on the target systems can be reached only from the approved gateway networks or security groups.
- [ ] Connect the gateways to the organisation’s identity provider and apply conditional access rules, using phishing-resistant multi-factor authentication (MFA), for remote administrative access.
- [ ] Apply secure gateway configuration standards and manage any changes using configuration-as-code, with peer review before deployment.
- [ ] Limit access to management endpoints on the target system by allowing only approved source addresses, using an allowlist that matches the gateway’s registered entries.
- [ ] Send the internet gateway and firewall logs into the security information and event management (SIEM) system, and set up alerts to detect any direct attempts to access the target for administrative purposes.
- [ ] Update the system design diagrams and the system security plan to show that remote access is only possible through the managed gateways.


---

#### AC-17.4 — Privileged Commands and Access (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that when someone logs in from another location to carry out high-privilege work or view sensitive security information, it is done only for clearly approved reasons and in a way that can be checked later. Without this, remote access could be misused to make unauthorised changes, conceal wrongdoing, or steal information needed to protect the business—turning a helpful support route into a serious weakness.

The organisation must permit remote access to run privileged commands and to view security-relevant information only when there is explicit approval for specific purposes: incident response, fixing vulnerabilities, privileged maintenance, and security monitoring or audit evidence. Privileged commands must be limited to emergency maintenance, applying patches, making configuration changes, or running security tools to produce audit evidence. Viewing security-relevant information must be limited to incident response support, vulnerability remediation, and security monitoring for Confidential and Secret data.

Every remote action must create clear records showing who did what, when, and why. The organisation’s security plan must also document the reasons for using remote access.

##### Purpose-bound remote privileged access with evidence-rich approvals

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a controlled process for **remote use of privileged commands** and **remote access to security-relevant information**. Allow it only for clearly approved reasons, and make sure every remote action produces evidence that can be checked later.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The AC-17(4) enhancement requires limiting remote use of privileged commands and remote access to security-relevant information to reduce the risk created by remote access. The organisation’s approved reasons for needing remote access must be enforced, and remote actions must be traceable for later review.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Match approved reasons (purpose) to what people are allowed to do (entitlements)**, based on the organisation’s approved needs:
   - Remote privileged commands are limited to: **emergency privileged maintenance, patching, configuration changes, and access to security tools to collect audit evidence** (ac-17.04_odp.01).
   - Remote access to security-relevant information is limited to: **support for incident response, fixing vulnerabilities, and security monitoring for Confidential and Secret data** (ac-17.04_odp.02).
2. **Route all remote privileged access through a privileged access management (PAM) gateway (or equivalent remote administration broker)** that enforces:
   - **Request → approval → time-limited access**.
   - The person requesting access must choose a **purpose** that matches the allowed needs; approval must be recorded before access is granted.
   - **Session details** must include purpose, approval/ticket ID, requester identity, target system, and the time period.
3. **Limit what can be done during the session**:
   - For privileged command sessions, enforce rules so only the permitted categories of actions can be carried out (for example, patching/configuration change tools and security tools used to collect audit evidence).
   - For security-relevant information sessions, enforce permissions so only the permitted categories of information can be accessed (for example, incident/vulnerability/security monitoring views for Confidential/Secret).
4. **Create evidence for both command use and information access**:
   - Turn on **session recording** and **command-by-command auditing** for privileged command sessions.
   - Turn on **access logging** for security-relevant information (for example, dashboard/query/application programming interface (API) access logs), including user, target, purpose, and time.
   - Send logs to the organisation’s central logging platform/security information and event management (SIEM) system, with protections to prevent tampering and with retention periods suitable for audit.
5. **Record the reason for remote access in the system security plan**:
   - Explain how the system enforces the allowed purposes, how evidence is produced, and how approvals are linked to sessions.
   - Ensure the plan explicitly references the organisation’s approved needs (ac-17.04_odp.01/ac-17.04_odp.02).
6. **Run governance and handle exceptions properly**:
   - Define who can approve each purpose (for example, incident manager for incident response, change manager for patching/configuration, security operations lead for security monitoring).
   - For any emergency exception, require documented justification and compensating controls, while still producing evidence.

###### WHO
- **System Owner / Application Owner**: updates the system security plan with the remote access rationale and the purpose-to-entitlement mappings.
- **Privileged Access/PAM Administrator**: sets up PAM rules, command allow-lists, session recording, and logging integrations.
- **IT service management (ITSM)/Service Desk (Process Owner)**: ensures approvals are captured with the purpose and the ticket/approval IDs.
- **Security Operations / Incident Response Lead**: approves purposes related to incident response, vulnerability remediation, and security monitoring.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Remote privileged command execution is only possible through the PAM/remote administration broker and only when there is an approval with a purpose matching **ac-17.04_odp.01**.
- Remote access to security-relevant information is only possible when there is an approval with a purpose matching **ac-17.04_odp.02** and the user has the required permissions.
- For every remote session, evidence exists showing: **who**, **what privileged commands or information were accessed**, **when**, **where (target system)**, and **why (purpose + approval/ticket ID)**.
- Session/command/access logs are centralised, protected against tampering, and available for auditor review.
- The system security plan documents the rationale and enforcement approach for AC-17(4), aligned to the organisation’s approved needs.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] For each approved remote command with elevated privileges and each security-relevant information need, link it to the specific privileged access management (PAM) entitlement and the correct purpose code.
- [ ] Set up a remote privileged access management (PAM) workflow that requires a user request, approval, and time-limited access. Record the reason for access and the approval or ticket reference in the session details (metadata).
- [ ] Set up approved “command allow-lists” and role-based restrictions so that, during approved remote sessions, only the permitted categories of privileged commands can be run.
- [ ] Enable session recording and record commands for privileged sessions, and log access to security-relevant information views and queries.
- [ ] Add privileged access management (PAM) and session/access logs to the organisation’s central security information and event management (SIEM) and logging system, with protections to keep the records trustworthy and with retention that supports audit requirements.
- [ ] Update the system security plan to explain why remote access is used, how the system’s purposes are matched to the access, and how proof (evidence) is created and linked to the relevant approvals.


---

#### AC-17.6 — Protection of Mechanism Information (Enhancement)

This requirement is about keeping the instructions for how remote access works securely controlled, so only the right people can use them and only for approved purposes. Without this, someone could obtain the information needed to connect from outside the organisation, then misuse it or pass it on, which could lead to unauthorised access to systems and sensitive information.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly identify what remote-access information it holds and protect it from unauthorised use or sharing. It should ensure that the rules for who can access what, the step-by-step remote access procedures, the system security plan, and any relevant agreements or behaviour rules all explicitly include these protection requirements.

Access to this information must be limited to authorised staff who set up or monitor remote access, and to authorised system users who genuinely need to know. The documentation must be kept up to date and easy to use.

##### Remote access mechanism info vault with role-based access controls

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Create and run a controlled storage location (“vault”) for all information that explains how remote access works (for example: VPN/Remote Desktop Protocol (RDP)/jump-host connection instructions, configuration details, diagrams, and troubleshooting steps). Treat this information as sensitive and allow access only for authorised roles with a clear need to know.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST Access Control (AC)-17(6) (enhancement) requires: **“Protect information about remote access mechanisms from unauthorised use and disclosure.”** The guidance also says remote access requirements should be reflected in **rules of behaviour (PL-4)** and **access agreements (PS-6)** where applicable. This approach protects the mechanism information itself by limiting who can access it and controlling how it is shared.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Identify and list** all remote access mechanism information used across the organisation (documents, runbooks, screenshots, network route diagrams, connection details, broker/jump-host steps, firewall/network address translation (NAT) change notes, and troubleshooting procedures).
2. **Store everything centrally** in a dedicated repository with appropriate confidentiality controls (for example, document management/vault) and ensure the repository is the single, trusted source of truth.
3. **Use role-based authorisation** through the organisation’s enterprise identity provider (IdP): set up groups based on need to know (for example, remote access implementers/monitors, security operations, and specific system users who need troubleshooting access).
4. **Restrict access at the repository level**: require multi-factor authentication (MFA), limit viewing/downloading/exporting, disable public sharing and unauthorised link creation, and apply time limits to access where possible.
5. **Deliver the information through an approved process** (for example, a ticketing/IT service management (ITSM) request that grants time-limited access or provides a vault link), and avoid sending connection instructions/configuration details via email or chat attachments or forwarding outside the process.
6. **Keep records and ensure information stays current**: enable detailed access logs for the repository, require periodic review/attestation by content owners, and ensure updates follow change management so the information remains accurate and usable.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Information Security (or Security Governance)**: set classification and handling rules, and approve which roles are authorised.
- **IT Operations / Network & Security Engineering**: keep the remote access mechanism information accurate and up to date.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM)/Platform Team**: set up IdP group mappings, multi-factor authentication (MFA)/conditional access, and repository access policies.
- **Service Management (ITSM) Owner**: implement the approved request and delivery workflow.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A complete inventory exists of remote access mechanism information assets, and all authoritative content is stored in the controlled repository.
- Only authorised roles with a need to know can access the repository content; unauthorised users cannot view, download, or share the mechanism information.
- Access to mechanism information is provided through the approved workflow (no uncontrolled email/chat distribution of connection instructions or configuration details).
- Repository access events (viewing/downloading/sharing attempts) are logged and kept in line with the organisation’s logging requirements.
- Content owners review and update mechanism information on an agreed schedule, and changes can be traced to the organisation’s change management records.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] List all information assets used for remote access (documents, runbooks, diagrams, connection steps, and troubleshooting guidance) and mark each one as sensitive.
- [ ] Set up a dedicated, secure storage area (vault) for information about the remote access method, and configure how that information is kept confidential.
