- [ ] Set up role groups based on who needs access (people who implement and monitor systems, the security operations team, and authorised system users) and link each group to the organisation’s identity provider (IdP).
- [ ] Apply repository access rules, including multi-factor authentication (MFA) and conditional access, restrict users’ ability to view, download, or export data, disable public sharing and prevent users from creating uncontrolled share links.
- [ ] Use an approved IT service management (ITSM) process for delivering access within a set time or by using a vault link, and do not allow mechanism information to be sent as email or chat attachments.
- [ ] Turn on and keep audit logs for access to repositories and attempts to share them, and set a regular schedule for reviewing and formally confirming content, with clear records showing what changed and when.


---

#### AC-17.9 — Disconnect or Disable Access (Enhancement)

This requirement is about being able to quickly stop remote access to your systems when it is no longer safe. If remote access cannot be turned off quickly enough, an attacker or an unauthorised person could continue using your systems, or access could continue after a staff member leaves or a device is compromised. This could cause real disruption to the business and financial loss.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must have a working way to disconnect or disable remote access within 15 minutes of starting the action. This must be supported by clear, written rules and step-by-step instructions. The instructions must:

- name who is responsible for making the change  
- explain exactly what to do for each type of remote access  
- require the organisation to record who made the change and when it was done, so it can be checked later  

The organisation must also keep records of how the system is designed and configured to show it supports this fast shut-off, and keep audit records proving the action was completed within 15 minutes.

##### Emergency remote-access kill switch with 15-minute disablement

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a capability to **disconnect or disable remote access** to the system using a **pre-set, operator-activated “kill switch”** at every remote-access control point (for example: VPN/remote access gateway, firewall rules, ZTNA application rules, bastion/jump host access). This must block remote access **within the time period defined by the organisation**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-17(9)** (Enhancement) requires: **“Provide the capability to disconnect or disable remote access to the system within {{ insert: param, ac-17.09_odp }}.”** The guidance notes that the required speed depends on mission and business importance, and it must prevent remote access immediately and in the future.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Identify and document remote access methods** for the application/system (for example: VPN, ZTNA application access, SSH/RDP via bastion, remote support tools) and link them to the **exact control points** where access can be blocked.
2. For each control point, **set up an emergency “deny” rule** (use “policy as code” where possible) that blocks the relevant remote access method(s) to the target system(s). Make sure each rule has a **stable identifier** so audit records can be matched reliably.
3. Implement an **operator-activated kill switch** that turns on the emergency deny rules through an orchestration/runbook action (for example: “start playbook”), not by ad-hoc manual changes. Limit use to **authorised roles** (least privilege) and require **step-up authentication** for the kill-switch action.
4. Define an **emergency process** that allows activation without normal lead times (where your governance allows), while still requiring: a ticket/reference, the reason for activation, and the operator’s identity.
5. **Measure and confirm the disablement time**: start a timer when the kill switch is triggered; confirm remote access fails using a controlled test account/session; record the start and end times and the rule identifiers used.
6. **Centralise audit evidence**: ensure logs from the control points (VPN/Firewall/ZTNA/bastion) and from the orchestration system are sent to security information and event management (SIEM)/log management. Retain: operator identity, start/end timestamps, rule identifiers, and the runbook/playbook execution ID.

###### WHO
- **Security Operations / SOC Lead**: owns the kill-switch design, the verification approach, and the audit evidence requirements.
- **Infrastructure/Network Engineering Lead**: implements the control-point rules and orchestration integrations.
- **Authorised System Administrators / Privileged Operators**: activate the kill switch during emergencies.
- **GRC/Compliance Owner**: ensures the emergency process and evidence retention meet organisational governance.

###### Acceptance criteria
- All remote access methods mapped to control points are **covered** by an emergency deny capability.
- When the kill switch is triggered, remote access is **disabled within 15 minutes** (per **ac-17.09_odp = within 15 minutes**) during repeatable testing.
- The kill-switch action is limited to **authorised roles** and requires step-up authentication.
- Audit evidence shows **who initiated** the kill switch, **when it started**, **when it finished**, and **which rule identifiers** were applied.
- The runbook/playbook and emergency process are documented and can be executed consistently by authorised operators.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] List all ways people can remotely access the application or system, and for each one, identify where access is controlled (the enforcement point).
- [ ] Create emergency “deny” rules for each enforcement point, using stable identifiers so audit records can be matched reliably.
- [ ] Set up an operator-initiated emergency shutdown runbook (playbook) that uses role-based access control (RBAC) and requires step-up authentication.
- [ ] Define and document an emergency process that records the operator’s identity, the reason for the action, and a reference to what was done.
- [ ] Run a time-limited check to confirm remote access is disabled within 15 minutes, and record the start and end times.
- [ ] Set up security information and event management (SIEM) and log retention so that it keeps records of kill-switch execution IDs, the operator’s identity, policy identifiers, and enforcement-point logs.


---

#### AC-17.10 — Authenticate Remote Commands (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that remote “admin” instructions sent to your on-premises servers and operational technology gateways are genuine, not altered, and only carried out once. Without these checks, an attacker could try to send unauthorised commands, change a legitimate command while it is being sent, or resend an old command so the system repeats an action—potentially leading to serious disruption, damage, or compromise of sensitive information.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must set up approved remote management routes so that both sides prove they are trusted using digital certificates. Each command must also include (1) a per-command integrity check to confirm it has not been tampered with and (2) a sequence number that increases steadily. The receiving systems must verify these checks before executing anything, reject any command with an incorrect or missing check, and refuse any command that is out of sequence or a repeat.

The organisation must document how this works, define the procedures, configure the systems to enforce it, record the results in audit logs, include it in the security plan, and assign clear responsibility for implementation and oversight.

##### Authenticate remote admin commands with mTLS, HMAC, and sequence checks

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement cryptographic authentication for **remote administrative commands** sent to **on-premises servers and operational technology (OT) gateways** over **approved management channels**, using **mutual Transport Layer Security (mutual TLS (mTLS))**, a **message authentication code (HMAC) per command**, and **monotonically increasing sequence numbers**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Access Control (AC)-17(10) enhancement requires: “Implement {{ ac-17.10_odp.01 }} to authenticate {{ ac-17.10_odp.02 }}.” The guidance explains this protects against **unauthorised commands** and **replaying previously authorised commands**, ensuring the system:
- accepts commands in the **intended order**,
- executes **only authorised commands**, and
- **rejects unauthorised or replayed** commands.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Define and enforce approved management channels** for remote administration to on-premises servers and OT gateways (for example: ZTNA/jump host routes, dedicated administration network segments, or allow-listed management endpoints). Only these routes may reach the command intake points.
2. **Set up mutual TLS (mTLS)** between the remote command sender (administration client / management service) and the command intake endpoint(s) on the on-premises servers/OT gateways (or a dedicated command broker/agent, if used). Require certificate checks (trusted certificate authority (CA), hostname/subject alternative name (SAN) validation, and certificate revocation/rotation according to your public key infrastructure (PKI) process).
3. **Package each remote command in an authenticated command envelope** that includes:
   - a **sequence number** (increasing for each command stream, scoped by sender identity and target),
   - a **command identifier** (to support audit linking),
   - the **command payload** (formatted in a consistent way for integrity),
   - and an **HMAC** calculated over (the formatted payload + sequence number + relevant session/context fields).
4. **Check everything before execution** at the receiving endpoint:
   - confirm the mTLS session and authorisation context,
   - verify the **HMAC** using the pre-agreed shared secret(s)/key(s) (or derived keys tied to the mTLS identity/session, as defined in your design),
   - enforce **sequence acceptance**: accept only if `sequence_no` is **strictly greater** than the last accepted value for that sender/target stream; otherwise **reject** (out-of-order/replay).
5. **Reject and log failures with clear reasons** (for example: “HMAC mismatch”, “sequence out of order/replay”, “invalid certificate”, “unknown sender/target stream”) and ensure rejected commands are **not executed**.
6. **Use the same mechanism for both** on-premises servers and OT gateways by implementing the receiving-side checks in the server/OT gateway command handler/agent (or an adapter that verifies the authenticated envelope and forwards only after verification).

###### WHO (roles responsible)
- **Security Architect / Compliance Lead**: confirm the design meets AC-17(10) and is documented for audit.
- **Platform/OT Engineering Lead**: implement receiving-side checks (mTLS, HMAC, sequence enforcement) on on-premises servers and OT gateways.
- **Public Key Infrastructure (PKI)/Identity Team**: manage certificate trust, rotation, and revocation processes used by mTLS.
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC)/Security Information and Event Management (SIEM) Engineer**: ensure audit events and rejection reasons are sent to monitoring with appropriate retention.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Remote administrative commands to on-premises servers and OT gateways are accepted **only** when:
  1) the connection uses **mTLS** with valid, trusted certificates, **and**
  2) the command envelope includes a valid **per-command HMAC**, **and**
  3) the **sequence number** is strictly increasing for the relevant sender/target stream.
- Any command that fails mTLS validation, HMAC verification, or sequence checks is **rejected and not executed**, with an audit log entry recording the **specific rejection reason**.
- Evidence exists (design documentation, configuration, and test results) showing the mechanism is implemented consistently for both **on-premises servers** and **OT gateways**, and is integrated with approved management channels.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Set the approved communication routes and limit network connections so that only those approved routes can reach the command intake points for on-premises servers and operational technology (OT) gateways.
- [ ] Set up mutual Transport Layer Security (mutual TLS) for the remote command intake endpoints, and configure which certificate authorities are trusted, how certificates are checked, and how certificates are revoked and rotated.
- [ ] Define and implement a message “envelope” format that includes: a standardised (canonical) version of the message content, a separate hash-based message authentication code (HMAC) for each command, and a sequence number that only increases over time, tracked separately for each sender and target stream.
- [ ] Set up checks on the receiving side to confirm mutual Transport Layer Security (mTLS), verify the hash-based message authentication code (HMAC), and reject any command if its sequence number is out of order or has already been received.
- [ ] Record an audit log entry for every command that is accepted and every command that is rejected, including the specific reason for each rejection (for example, a failed HMAC check, a repeated or out-of-order sequence, or a failed certificate check).
- [ ] Carry out end-to-end tests that replay and attempt to tamper with data for both on-premises servers and operational technology (OT) gateways, and record the results as compliance evidence.


---

### AC-18 — Wireless Access (Control)

This requirement is about making sure any wireless connection to your systems is set up correctly and is only allowed when it has been formally approved. Without this, someone could connect using an unapproved device or wireless method, bypass intended restrictions, or gain access through a poorly set-up wireless link. This could lead to unauthorised access, data exposure, or disruption.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must first identify every type of wireless connection it will use (such as Wi‑Fi, Bluetooth, and other wireless links). It must then document clear setup rules, clear connection rules (including what is allowed and what is not), and step-by-step guidance for each type.

The organisation must also name specific people who are responsible for managing wireless connections and for oversight. Finally, the system must only allow each type of wireless connection after the organisation has recorded and approved it for the specific purpose. It must also keep records of wireless connection activity so later checks can confirm that all connections matched the approvals.

##### Authorise-then-enable wireless access with mutual authentication

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Put in place an auditable, enforced process and configuration approach so that every wireless connection type used in the environment (for example, Wi‑Fi using 802.11x, Bluetooth, and any microwave/packet radio links used for connectivity) is only turned on after formal approval for a specific purpose. Configure it to use authentication that protects the person/device being authenticated and requires both sides to prove their identity to each other (mutual authentication).

###### WHY (control requirement)
Control AC-18 requires that wireless technologies used (including microwave, packet radio, 802.11x, and Bluetooth) use authentication that provides authenticator protection and mutual authentication. It also requires that wireless access is controlled so that only approved wireless access is allowed, with auditable evidence showing which connections were made.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create a wireless access catalogue** covering each wireless technology in use (Wi‑Fi 802.11x service identifiers/profiles, Bluetooth use cases/pairing modes, and any microwave/packet radio connectivity used for access or backhaul). For each entry, define the **purpose**, **which user/device/service identities are allowed**, **which authentication method(s) are required**, and **any usage restrictions**.
2. **Set configuration requirements for each technology** that directly translate into settings that can be enforced on wireless controllers/gateways (for example: disable legacy or unauthenticated modes; require enterprise authentication with mutual authentication such as EAP-TLS where applicable; for Bluetooth require authenticated pairing and controls over device identity). Make sure the documented requirements explicitly include **authenticator protection** and **mutual authentication**.
3. **Use an “authorise-then-enable” workflow**: for every wireless access catalogue entry, create an approval record in the organisation’s governance, risk and compliance (GRC) or ticketing system (purpose, scope/site, validity period, and approving authority). This workflow must produce a machine-readable policy (for example, the profile/service identifier enablement settings) that is the only source of truth for turning on wireless access.
4. **Enforce this at the wireless enforcement points** (wireless controllers, network access control (NAC) / 802.1X gateways, Bluetooth management platform, and any relevant radio gateway configuration): only approved profiles are pushed and enabled. Unauthorised profiles must not be broadcast or accepted. Use configuration baselines so that mutual authentication settings cannot be bypassed.
5. **Log and keep records of wireless connection activity** (connection/association, authentication attempts, successful authentications, and failures). Ensure the logs can be linked to the relevant approval record (for example, using the service identifier/profile identifier, site, and validity period). Send events to security information and event management (SIEM) for audit-ready evidence.

###### WHO
- **Wireless/Network Engineering Lead**: maintains the wireless access catalogue and the configuration mappings.
- **Information Security (identity and access management (IAM)/Network Security) Manager**: approves the authentication requirements (mutual authentication/authenticator protection) and checks policy templates.
- **GRC/Compliance Owner**: runs the approval workflow and ensures evidence retention requirements are met.
- **Operations/Platform Team**: operates the enforcement points and ensures the policy deployment process is followed.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For each wireless technology used, there is an approved catalogue entry specifying required authentication that provides **authenticator protection** and **mutual authentication**.
- No wireless access profile (Wi‑Fi service identifier/Bluetooth pairing mode/radio connectivity profile) is enabled in production without a current approval record.
- Wireless controllers/gateways are configured so that only approved profiles are enabled; unauthorised profiles are not broadcast or accepted.
- Wireless connection/authentication events are logged, retained, and can be traced back to the relevant approval record (by profile/service identifier and validity scope).
- A sample audit confirms that an enabled wireless access profile has an approver record and that the connection logs match that approval.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a wireless access catalogue that covers every wireless technology used for access, including Wi‑Fi (IEEE 802.11), Bluetooth, and any microwave or packet radio links used for access.
- [ ] Set clear configuration requirements for each technology that explicitly require protection for the authentication process and mutual authentication, and link those requirements to the controller and gateway settings.
- [ ] Set up an “authorise first, then enable” process that creates a machine-readable wireless policy from approved authorisation records, including the purpose, scope, and validity period.
- [ ] Set up wireless enforcement points so only authorised device profiles are enabled and broadcast, and unauthorised profiles are blocked.
- [ ] Enable and centralise logging of wireless connection and authentication attempts, including both successes and failures. Send these logs to security information and event management (SIEM), using identifiers that link each event to the related authorisation record.
- [ ] Carry out a test audit to confirm that a switched-on wireless profile has a current authorisation record, and that the connection records can be traced back to it.


---

#### AC-18.1 — Authentication and Encryption (Enhancement)

This expectation is about making sure that anyone trying to connect to your systems over wireless is properly checked, and that any information sent over that wireless connection is protected so it cannot be read or altered by others. Without strong checks, an unauthorised person could join your wireless network, pretend to be a legitimate user, or capture sensitive data while it is being sent through the air.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must set up wireless access so that both people and the devices they use are strongly verified. This means only authorised users and approved devices can connect. It must also use strong encryption so wireless data stays unreadable to outsiders while it is being transmitted.

The organisation should document how wireless access is implemented and what restrictions apply, keep the relevant design and configuration records, retain audit records showing wireless access activity, and include the wireless authentication and encryption requirements in its security planning. This helps the right teams maintain the protections and demonstrate them over time.

##### WPA3-Enterprise 802.1X with user+device auth and strong encryption

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up all organisation-managed wireless connections so they require **strong authentication** for both **people (users)** and **devices**, and so all wireless data is protected using **strong encryption**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-18(1) enhancement requires: “Protect wireless access to the system using authentication of {{ insert: param, ac-18.01_odp }} and encryption.” The organisation-defined parameter **ac-18.01_odp = users, devices** means both interactive users and connecting devices must be authenticated, and wireless data must be encrypted.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Standardise wireless security mode**: enable **Wi‑Fi Protected Access 3 Enterprise (WPA3-Enterprise)** (or **WPA2-Enterprise** only if WPA3 is not feasible) and turn off older or weaker options (for example: open networks, **Wired Equivalent Privacy (WEP)**, **WPA/WPA2-Personal**).
2. **Use IEEE 802.1X for authentication**: configure each production wireless network name (SSID) to use **IEEE 802.1X** with **Remote Authentication Dial-In User Service / Authentication, Authorisation, and Accounting (RADIUS/AAA)**, so a device can only connect after it successfully authenticates.
3. **Authenticate users**: connect 802.1X user authentication to the organisation’s central identity system (**identity provider (IdP)**) and require **phishing-resistant multi-factor authentication (MFA)** for people logging in.
4. **Authenticate devices**: authenticate devices using **device certificates** (or equally strong device credentials) issued and managed through the organisation’s certificate and identity process, and link each certificate identity to an “approved device” record.
5. **Authorise and limit access**: apply access rules in **RADIUS/AAA** (for example, based on user group and device certificate details) and connect authenticated sessions to the correct network area (for example, **virtual local area network (VLAN)**/**virtual routing and forwarding (VRF)**) so only intended systems can be reached.
6. **Keep evidence that can be checked**: store wireless controller/access point configuration exports, RADIUS/AAA policy settings, certificate issuance/approval records, and RADIUS authentication logs (success and failure) for audit purposes.

###### WHO
- **Network Engineering Lead**: sets up **WPA3-Enterprise/WPA2-Enterprise** and **IEEE 802.1X** on wireless controllers/access points.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Lead**: connects 802.1X user authentication to the enterprise IdP and ensures multi-factor authentication (MFA) requirements are met.
- **Security Engineering / Public Key Infrastructure (PKI) Owner**: manages device certificate issuance/approval and links certificates to devices.
- **Security Operations (SOC) / Security Information and Event Management (SIEM) Admin**: ensures authentication logs are sent to the SIEM and kept for investigation.

###### Acceptance criteria
- All organisation-managed SSIDs used to access the system require **IEEE 802.1X** and **WPA3-Enterprise (or WPA2-Enterprise)**; no open or legacy wireless modes remain enabled for those SSIDs.
- A connection attempt without valid **user credentials** is blocked.
- A connection attempt with valid **user credentials** but without a valid **approved device certificate** is blocked (or limited to a non-production/restricted network according to policy).
- Wireless data is protected using strong encryption negotiated by **WPA3/WPA2-Enterprise** (confirmed through controller/access point security checks).
- An evidence pack exists: exported wireless configuration, RADIUS/AAA policy configuration, device certificate issuance/approval workflow records, and retained RADIUS authentication logs covering both successful and failed attempts.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Enable Wi‑Fi Protected Access 3 Enterprise (WPA3-Enterprise) (or Wi‑Fi Protected Access 2 Enterprise (WPA2-Enterprise) where required) and turn off open or older wireless modes for all network names (service set identifiers, SSIDs) used to access systems.
- [ ] Configure each network name (SSID) so that devices must use IEEE 802.1X with Remote Authentication Dial-In User Service (RADIUS) / authentication, authorisation, and accounting (AAA). If authentication fails, block the device from connecting.
- [ ] Set up 802.1X user sign-in with the organisation’s identity provider (IdP) and require phishing-resistant multi-factor authentication (MFA) for user logins.
- [ ] Set up device-certificate based sign-in and keep an approved-device list that is used when granting access through authentication, authorisation and accounting (AAA).
- [ ] Apply authorisation rules for authentication, authorisation and accounting (AAA) to assign authenticated users and devices to the correct virtual local area network (VLAN) or virtual routing and forwarding (VRF), and limit access to only the required resources.
- [ ] Export and securely store the wireless controller/access point settings and the RADIUS (Remote Authentication Dial-In User Service) and AAA (authentication, authorisation and accounting) policy settings as evidence for audits.
- [ ] Send and keep Remote Authentication Dial-In User Service (RADIUS) authentication logs (both successful and failed logins) in the security information and event management (SIEM) system, with suitable retention periods and alerts.


---

#### AC-18.3 — Disable Wireless Networking (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that any built-in wireless features on new devices are switched off before the devices are issued and put into use, unless wireless is genuinely needed for an essential business purpose. If wireless is left switched on by default, it can give attackers an easier way to interfere with or access systems from nearby, which can result in data loss, service disruption, or unauthorised access.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must:
- identify which devices have built-in wireless capabilities
- clearly document when wireless is allowed for essential missions
- ensure that for all other cases the wireless features are disabled before deployment, using the device’s built-in settings rather than temporary workarounds

The organisation must also:
- keep written rules, design and configuration records showing how wireless is disabled
- keep evidence and audit records proving the wireless was switched off before deployment
- assign named responsible staff who are trained or authorised to follow the documented process

##### Disable embedded Wi‑Fi/Bluetooth before device issuance (default-off)

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Turn off the built-in wireless features (for example, Wi‑Fi and Bluetooth) in system components before they are issued and deployed, unless an approved essential mission or function exception applies.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) AC-18(3) enhancement requires that wireless networking features built into system components are disabled **when they are not intended for use** and **before issuance and deployment**. This reduces the risk of adversaries exploiting wireless attack routes.

###### HOW (specific approach)
Set up a repeatable “default-off” process to disable wireless for all managed endpoint and embedded device types, using tightly controlled, time-limited exceptions.

1. **Create an agreed wireless capability baseline for each device type** (for example, laptops, desktops, rugged endpoints, embedded computers). This should list which radios are present (Wi‑Fi, Bluetooth) and which disablement methods are available (for example, firmware settings such as UEFI/BIOS, operating system settings, and/or device management tools).
2. **Define when an exception is allowed** for “essential mission or function” use (for example, specific operational roles or time-limited field activities). Require documented approval by the system owner and information security (including the expiry date and the scope of the exception).
3. **Apply the disablement as early as possible**:
   - Use a **golden build / provisioning image** that sets Wi‑Fi and Bluetooth to disabled/off where supported.
   - If firmware controls are available, set **UEFI/BIOS** options during provisioning to disable WLAN/Bluetooth (or equivalent radio disablement).
   - For devices where only operating system or management toggles are supported, enforce the setting through enterprise device management configuration profiles.
4. **Stop settings from changing after build** by using ongoing compliance checks in the endpoint management platform (or equivalent), so devices that are not exceptions are automatically returned to the wireless-disabled state.
5. **Produce audit-ready proof for each issued device**:
   - Save the configuration compliance results (Wi‑Fi/Bluetooth disabled state),
   - Save deployment/issuance records linked to the asset identifier,
   - Save change records and approvals for any exception that enables wireless (including expiry).
6. **Run verification in practice**: carry out automated checks at the end of provisioning and periodic sampling (where firmware evidence cannot be fully automated) to confirm wireless remains disabled for non-exception devices.

###### WHO
- **Infrastructure/Endpoint Engineering Lead**: owns the wireless baseline and the golden build/provisioning configuration.
- **Information Security (or Security Governance) Officer**: approves and maintains the exception criteria and approval process.
- **Service Desk/Operations (Device Issuance Owner)**: ensures devices are issued only after the required compliance evidence is recorded.
- **Configuration Management/Platform Owner**: maintains device management policies and compliance reporting.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For all non-exception devices, evidence exists showing Wi‑Fi and Bluetooth are disabled **before issuance and deployment**.
- Exceptions are granted only when documented essential mission or function criteria are met, with recorded approver(s), scope, and expiry.
- Ongoing compliance reporting shows that wireless-enabled settings do not remain on non-exception devices (or are automatically corrected).
- Audit records include: wireless baseline/configuration documentation, deployment/issuance records, and per-device configuration compliance outputs (or a documented sampling method where full automation is not possible).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Maintain an inventory of managed device types, and record which built-in radios (Wi‑Fi and Bluetooth) are present and how they can be turned off (using the computer’s firmware, the operating system, or device management tools).
- [ ] Publish an exception policy that defines the criteria for what counts as an “essential mission or function”, who must approve requests, what is covered, and when approvals expire for enabling wireless.
- [ ] Update the “golden” build and provisioning process to turn off Wi‑Fi and Bluetooth by default, including any firmware settings where this is supported.
- [ ] Set up endpoint management to ensure all non-exception devices are kept in a wireless-disabled state, and automatically fix any changes that drift away from this setting.
- [ ] Set up an approval step (“issuance gate”) that checks configuration compliance evidence and deployment or release records before any devices are sent to users.
- [ ] Set up regular checks (automated where possible; where firmware evidence is limited, use documented sampling) and keep records of the results for audit.


---

#### AC-18.4 — Restrict Configurations by Users (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that only the right named people can make independent changes to your wireless settings, such as Wi‑Fi configuration. Without this, someone who is not meant to could quietly weaken security, disrupt services, or redirect connections, creating opportunities for unauthorised access or outages.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must keep a clear, up-to-date list of the specific users who are allowed to independently configure wireless networking, and it must explicitly approve each person on that list. It should define what “independently configure” covers in practice, and then enforce this through its written rules for system access, its documented procedures for how wireless is implemented and used (including any restrictions), and the way the wireless systems are set up.

The organisation must also keep supporting design and configuration records, maintain audit logs showing who made wireless changes, and include this restriction in its overall security plan so that changes can be checked and verified.

##### Authorised Wi‑Fi configurators register with RBAC enforcement

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Create an official, explicitly approved list of named users who are allowed to independently make changes to wireless network settings. Clearly define what “independently make changes” includes, and enforce this authorisation in the wireless management system so only those users can make configuration changes. Make sure audit logs record the identity of the person who made each wireless configuration change.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Access Control (AC)-18(4) enhancement requires the organisation to **identify and explicitly authorise users allowed to independently make changes to wireless network settings**. Guidance states that enforcement is achieved, in part, through the **access control mechanisms** used in organisational systems.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define what “independent configuration” covers**: document which wireless capabilities are included (for example, SSID/VLAN mapping, radio profiles, Wi‑Fi Protected Access (WPA) settings for WPA2/3, RADIUS/WPA enterprise settings, captive portal templates). Also state what is excluded (for example, read-only viewing, routine end-user actions, and any emergency “break-glass” process).
2. **Create and maintain an official register**: keep a controlled register that lists each authorised user’s identity details (full name, username/identity provider (IdP) subject, employee/contractor ID), the authorisation date, review date, approver, and the in-scope capability tags.
3. **Enforce using identity-based role access control (RBAC)**: connect the Wi‑Fi controller and admin portals to the enterprise identity provider (IdP) using single sign-on (SSO). Match the register’s scope tags to RBAC roles/groups (for example, WIFI-SSID-ADMIN, WIFI-RADIUS-ADMIN). Configure the wireless management system so only those RBAC roles can access configuration functions; all other users are limited to read-only access or blocked.
4. **Require strong authentication for configurators**: require phishing-resistant multi-factor authentication (MFA) for any RBAC role that allows configuration changes (where supported by the wireless platform and IdP).
5. **Ensure auditability and reconciliation**: configure the Wi‑Fi controllers to log each configuration change with the exact authenticated identity (IdP subject/username). Run a periodic reconciliation report that compares the “changed-by” identities in the wireless audit logs against the authorised configurators register.
6. **Align governance documents**: update the system security plan and wireless configuration/procedure documents to reference the register, the defined scope, and the RBAC enforcement approach.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Wireless/Network Security Lead**: owns scope definition, register governance, and reconciliation reporting.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Administrator**: sets up IdP groups/roles and MFA requirements.
- **Wi‑Fi Platform Administrator**: configures controller/admin portal RBAC and audit logging.
- **Governance, Risk and Compliance (GRC)/Compliance Owner**: ensures evidence is kept and that the system security plan references how the control is implemented.

###### Acceptance criteria
- A controlled register exists and includes **explicit authorisation** for each named user allowed to independently configure in-scope wireless capabilities.
- The wireless management system enforces RBAC so that **only** identities mapped to authorised roles can access configuration functions (others cannot independently change wireless settings).
- Wireless configuration change audit logs record the **exact authenticated identity** for each change.
- A reconciliation process shows that every “changed-by” identity in the audit logs appears in the authorised configurators register for the relevant period.
- The system security plan and wireless procedures reference the register, the scope definition, and the enforcement approach.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and document the wireless features that are included in scope and that can be set up independently.
- [ ] Set up a controlled, authorised configurators register that lists named users, includes scope tags, records the approver, and sets review dates.
- [ ] Connect the Wi‑Fi controller and admin portals to the organisation’s identity provider (single sign-on (SSO)), and link the Wi‑Fi register scope tags to role-based access control (RBAC) roles and groups.
- [ ] Configure the wireless management system so that only authorised role-based access control (RBAC) roles can use configuration functions. Everyone else must be limited to read-only access or denied access.
- [ ] Turn on and keep audit logs for all wireless configuration changes, including the authenticated identity from the identity provider (IdP).
- [ ] Create a regular reconciliation report that compares the “changed-by” identities recorded for wireless changes against the authorised configurators register.
- [ ] Update the system security plan and the wireless configuration and procedure documents to reference the register, scope, and enforcement.


---

#### AC-18.5 — Antennas and Transmission Power Levels (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure your building’s wireless internet signals stay within the areas you control. You do this by choosing the right antenna types and setting the correct transmission strength for each wireless access point. If you do not, signals could carry beyond your organisation’s controlled areas, allowing unauthorised people to pick them up from outside and potentially access your systems or data.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must select antennas for each access point with the specific aim of reducing signal reception outside the boundary, and record the reasons for each choice. It must also calibrate and document the transmission power settings so the signals are unlikely to travel outside the boundary. This should be based on results from regular wireless surveys that measure the local radio environment and take account of other nearby radio systems.

The organisation should apply the agreed antenna and power settings consistently, keep evidence of the survey results and decisions, and ensure any changes are approved and can be traced back to the authorising records.

##### RF survey-led antenna selection and calibrated Wi‑Fi power baselines

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Choose suitable radio antennas for each wireless access point (WAP) and adjust (calibrate) each WAP’s transmission power so that Wi‑Fi signals are unlikely to be received outside organisation-controlled boundaries.

###### WHY (control requirement)
Control AC-18(5) requires the organisation to **select radio antennas and calibrate transmission power levels** to **reduce the likelihood** that wireless signals can be received outside organisation-controlled boundaries. It also calls for **regular wireless surveys** to understand the radio environment (including other systems operating nearby) before taking mitigating actions.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Create a complete WAP inventory** including physical location (coordinates/floor plan), mounting details, and which boundary section each WAP is intended to cover (for example: perimeter wall, car park edge, loading bay).
2. **Carry out an initial wireless survey focused on boundaries** (on-site active survey plus spectrum analysis) in areas close to the boundary. Include nearby radio transmitters (neighbouring Wi‑Fi, licensed and unlicensed radios, microwave links, and others) and, where possible, capture differences by time of day/shift.
3. **Select antennas to reduce signal leakage outward**: for boundary-facing edges, use directional or sector antennas, and where supported, antennas that can use beamforming. Record the reason for each antenna choice, linking the antenna type and orientation to the boundary section it protects.
4. **Calibrate transmission power for each WAP** to the **lowest power that still meets coverage needs within the boundary**, using the measured results from the survey. Record the exact power settings (for example, controller/firmware power in dBm or an equivalent value), the radio characteristics, and the measured basis used for the calibration.
5. **Create an auditable baseline configuration** for antenna selection and transmission power (controller configuration exports and/or infrastructure-as-code records). Apply changes through formal change management so antenna and power settings cannot be altered without approval.
6. **Schedule periodic surveys** (at a defined frequency and after significant environmental changes such as building work, new nearby tenants, or antenna replacement) and re-check that the calibrated power and antenna choices still reduce out-of-boundary reception.

###### WHO
- **Network Engineering Lead / WLAN Administrator**: maintains the WAP inventory, leads antenna selection and power calibration, and owns the controller baseline configuration.
- **Information Security / Compliance Lead**: sets what evidence must be provided, and approves any decision to accept risk if boundary leakage cannot be fully prevented.
- **RF Survey Vendor or Internal RF Engineering Team**: performs the boundary-focused wireless surveys and provides the measured results.

###### Acceptance criteria (audit-ready)
- For each WAP that serves areas close to the boundary, there is documented evidence of:
  - the **selected antenna model/type and mounting orientation**, including the rationale for reducing boundary leakage;
  - the **calibrated transmission power setting(s)** and the **measured survey results** used to determine them;
  - an **approved configuration baseline** (controller export and/or infrastructure-as-code records) showing the antenna and power settings.
- A **boundary-focused wireless survey** is completed initially and repeated **periodically** according to an organisation-defined schedule.
- Change records show that any antenna or power changes are **approved, traceable, and consistent** with the baseline.
- Survey evidence shows that out-of-boundary reception is **reduced** (measured results are recorded and retained), consistent with the control intent.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Build and keep an official wireless access point (WAP) inventory, including location tags for sites near boundaries and details of how each unit is mounted
- [ ] Carry out an initial wireless survey focused on the coverage boundary, including an active site check and spectrum analysis, and assess nearby radio systems.
- [ ] Choose antennas that support direction, sector coverage, and beamforming for each boundary segment, and record the reasons for your choice.
- [ ] Adjust each boundary-adjacent wireless access point’s (WAP’s) transmit power to the lowest level that still works, based on the measured survey results, and record the dBm/power setting profile.
- [ ] Create an auditable baseline for wireless local area network (WLAN) settings (for example, controller exports and/or infrastructure-as-code) that locks antenna and power settings.
- [ ] Put change-management controls in place so that any changes to the antenna or power settings must be approved, and so that the baseline and supporting evidence are updated.
- [ ] Arrange regular wireless signal checks and re-check the antenna and power settings after major changes in the environment.


---

### AC-19 — Access Control for Mobile Devices (Control)

This expectation is about making sure company phones and tablets are set up and used in a controlled, consistent way, even when staff are working away from the office. Without clear rules and enforced connection checks, an unapproved device could access company systems, or a device could be left unprotected and become a way for data theft, fraud, or disruption.  

To meet this expectation, the organisation must define and keep up to date clear rules for organisation-owned mobile devices. These rules must cover what settings the devices must have and how they must be protected when they are in controlled areas and when they are outside them.  

The organisation must also provide step-by-step guidance for users and administrators. This should include what protective software is required, how often security updates must be applied, how to check for suspicious or changed software, and how to turn off unnecessary device features.  

Finally, the organisation must only allow mobile devices to connect to its systems when it has explicitly approved that device’s connection. It must be able to prove this using records and audit logs.

##### MDM-enforced mobile device authorisation and conditional access

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Set up organisation-controlled rules for mobile phone and tablet access so that only devices that are explicitly authorised and enrolled can connect to organisational systems. Also ensure devices follow secure settings and protective behaviour both inside and outside controlled areas.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-19 requires protection and control of mobile devices using policy and behaviour, backed by technical controls. This includes identifying and verifying devices and users, requiring protective security software, scanning for malware, checking that security updates are installed (patches), and—where applicable—checking that the operating system has not been tampered with. It also requires explicit authorisation for mobile device connections and the ability to audit and review access activity.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the mobile device access policy and off-site rules** for organisation-controlled devices (for example, company-issued only, or bring-your-own-device with explicit opt-in). Include what users must do to physically protect devices when outside controlled areas (screen lock, encryption, no unapproved apps, and what to do if the device is lost or stolen).
2. **Set up a mobile device management (MDM) baseline** to control device settings (iOS and Android). Enforce required settings such as a device passcode policy, encryption, restrictions on installing or removing unapproved apps, and turning off unnecessary hardware or features where possible.
3. **Require protective security software and scanning** through MDM. Require the organisation’s mobile security app, enable malware scanning where supported, and block access if the security app is missing, disabled, or out of date.
4. **Enforce security update and patch compliance**. Configure MDM to check whether the operating system is up to date with security updates, and require the security app to be at least a minimum supported version. Deny access if devices do not meet the defined security baseline.
5. **Implement device identification and verification** using the MDM enrolment identity (device ID, serial number, or UDID) and link it to the organisation’s identity provider.
6. **Use conditional access to allow access only after checks**. Only permit access to organisational apps, virtual private network (VPN), and network resources when the device is enrolled and meets the MDM compliance requirements. Apply stricter conditions for off-site use (for example, require a VPN and stronger authentication).
7. **Keep audit evidence**. Store records showing device enrolment/authorisation, compliance check results, and access attempts (both successful and failed), including attempts from devices that are not compliant or not authorised.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Security Architect / identity and access management (IAM) Lead**: designs conditional access rules and maps device compliance to allowed access.
- **Mobile Device Management (MDM) Administrator**: sets up MDM baselines, enforces the security app, and runs compliance checks.
- **Service Owner / Application Owner**: ensures mobile access routes (apps, application programming interfaces (APIs), VPN, and network) are protected by the conditional access policy.
- **Cybersecurity Operations / security operations centre (SOC)**: confirms alerts and log retention for device compliance and access outcomes.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Only devices that are **explicitly enrolled and authorised** in the MDM can access organisational mobile resources.
- Devices must meet the enforced **MDM compliance baseline** (protective software present and enabled, malware scanning enabled where supported, and operating system/security update compliance) to be granted access.
- Access attempts from **devices that are not enrolled or not compliant** are denied and recorded in retained audit logs.
- Off-site access is subject to **stricter conditions** (for example, VPN requirement and stronger authentication) and this is clearly enforced through conditional access.
- Audit evidence is available for: device enrolment/authorisation, compliance check results, and access success/failure events.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and get approval for the organisation-controlled mobile device policy, including how users should behave when working away from the office and what to do if a device is lost or stolen.
- [ ] Set up mobile device management (MDM) baseline settings for iPhone and Android devices to require device encryption, a screen lock and passcode, and limits on apps that are not managed by the organisation.
- [ ] Set up and keep an up-to-date list of approved devices in mobile device management (MDM), using each device’s ID or serial number, and make sure only devices on that approved list can access systems.
- [ ] Use mobile device management (MDM) to deploy and enforce required mobile protection software. Where supported, include malware scanning, and block access if the protection software is turned off or out of date.
- [ ] Set up mobile device management (MDM) checks to confirm devices meet the required operating system and security update/patch standards, and block access if a device does not meet the minimum security baseline.
- [ ] Link mobile device management (MDM) compliance information with the identity provider, and use conditional access to allow only compliant, authorised devices to connect. Apply stricter rules for off-site access, such as requiring a virtual private network (VPN).
- [ ] Enable and keep audit logs for mobile device enrolment and authorisation, the results of compliance checks, and whether attempts to access mobile resources succeed or fail.


---

#### AC-19.4 — Restrictions for Classified Information (Enhancement)

This requirement is about preventing people from bringing normal mobile phones and tablets into secure areas in a way that could accidentally reveal sensitive, classified information. Without these controls, an allowed device could connect to the wrong systems, copy or store sensitive material, or use wireless features in a way that bypasses safeguards—creating a real risk of unauthorised access or data leakage.

The organisation must only allow unclassified mobile devices in classified facilities when the Authorising Official has specifically approved this. Even then, the device must never be connected to any classified system. Any connection to unclassified systems must be approved by the Authorising Official. The device must also not use built-in or external phone or network connections, or wireless features such as Wi‑Fi, Bluetooth, or mobile data.

The Information System Security Officer and the Security Operations Centre incident response team must carry out random checks of permitted devices and any stored information. If any classified information is found, the organisation must follow its incident response plan.

Any connections for classified mobile devices must be limited in line with the approved classified mobile device connection policy.

##### Allowlisted unclassified mobile devices with enforced no-wireless/no-classified

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up an **Authorising Official–approved list (allowlist)** of specific **unclassified mobile devices** that are allowed to enter **classified facilities**. Then apply technical controls so those devices **cannot connect to any classified system** and **cannot use wireless or modem features** (for example: **Wi‑Fi, Bluetooth, mobile data, or tethering**). Any connection from these devices to **unclassified systems** must only be possible when the **Authorising Official** has explicitly approved it.

###### WHY (control requirement)
**AC-19(4) (Restrictions for Classified Information)** requires controls for handling classified information. In particular, it requires that you:  
1. Define the **security officials** responsible for reviewing and inspecting **unclassified mobile devices** and any stored information.  
2. Define **security policies** that restrict **classified mobile devices** from connecting to **classified systems**.  
3. Enforce restrictions that prevent unauthorised exposure of classified information through mobile devices, including:  
   - prohibiting classified connectivity, and  
   - limiting wireless/modem use on permitted devices.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create a device allowlist linked to Authorising Official approval**: Keep an accurate inventory of the unclassified mobile devices that are permitted (for example: **International Mobile Equipment Identity (IMEI)**/serial number and/or a **device certificate**/**mobile device management (MDM) identity**). Record the **Authorising Official approval reference** for each device and each facility.
2. **Control facility entry and device registration**: Link facility entry (for example: **badge**, **quick response (QR) code**, or **asset scan**) to the allowlist so only approved devices can be registered and used in classified areas. Devices not on the allowlist must be denied entry or sent into a controlled quarantine process.
3. **Technically enforce “no classified connectivity”**: Use **network separation** and policy controls (for example: **zero trust network access (ZTNA)** / **secure access service edge (SASE)** policy, firewall rules, and routing controls) so allowlisted mobile devices have **no route** to classified network areas or endpoints. Block all attempts to reach classified **Internet Protocol (IP)** ranges/**fully qualified domain names (FQDNs)** and block any attempt to create **virtual private network (VPN)** connections or tunnels into classified zones.
4. **Technically enforce “no wireless/modem use” on permitted devices**: Use **mobile device management (MDM)**/**unified endpoint management (UEM)** (or an equivalent enterprise device management tool) to disable or lock down **Wi‑Fi, Bluetooth, cellular/mobile data, and hotspot/tethering** on the permitted unclassified devices while they are in classified facilities. Ensure the settings are enforced at the **device level** (not based on the user) and can be audited.
5. **Require Authorising Official approval for any unclassified connectivity**: Use **conditional access** so the device can only reach explicitly approved unclassified destinations (for example: approved apps, websites, or file services) when a valid approval token/reference is present (using **policy-as-code** or an equivalent approach). Deny all other unclassified destinations by default.
6. **Provide evidence for random checks and incident triggers**: Ensure logs record:  
   (a) whether the device is on the allowlist,  
   (b) facility entry/registration events,  
   (c) MDM configuration compliance (wireless/modem disabled state), and  
   (d) network session denials to classified zones.  
   This supports random checks by the **Information System Security Officer (ISSO)** and the **Security Operations Centre (SOC)**, and enables rapid incident response if classified data is found.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Information System Security Officer (ISSO)**: Owns allowlist governance, readiness for inspections, and the evidence requirements.
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC) incident response team**: Owns monitoring and alerts for denied attempts to connect to classified systems, and supports incident handling.
- **Authorising Official**: Approves which unclassified mobile devices are permitted and which unclassified destinations are permitted.
- **IT/Operational Technology (OT) security engineering (MDM/network/security teams)**: Implement MDM/UEM enforcement and network/conditional access controls.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Only **Authorising Official–approved** unclassified mobile devices can be registered and used in classified facilities (verified by facility entry controls plus allowlist checks).
- Allowlisted devices cannot establish any network connection to classified network areas/endpoints (verified by test attempts and logged denials).
- On permitted devices, **Wi‑Fi, Bluetooth, cellular/mobile data, and hotspot/tethering** are disabled/locked via MDM/UEM while in classified facilities (verified by device compliance reports).
- Access to unclassified destinations from permitted devices is denied by default and only allowed when an **Authorising Official approval reference/token** is present (verified by conditional access testing).
- Evidence exists to support random ISSO/SOC inspections (device compliance logs, facility registration logs, and network denial/session logs) and to trigger incident response when classified data is detected.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Set up an Authorising Official–approved list of permitted unclassified mobile devices for each classified facility, based on their International Mobile Equipment Identity (IMEI), serial number, and certificate.
- [ ] Link facility entry and registration to the device allowlist, so any device that is not approved is blocked or placed in quarantine.
- [ ] Set up network segmentation and zero trust network access (ZTNA), secure access service edge (SASE), and firewall rules to block all connections from approved (allowlisted) devices to classified areas, and to stop tunnelling or virtual private network (VPN) access into classified networks.
- [ ] Set mobile device management (MDM) and unified endpoint management (UEM) rules to disable and lock Wi‑Fi, Bluetooth, mobile data, and hotspot/tethering on approved devices when they are inside classified facilities.
- [ ] Set up conditional access so that access to unclassified destinations is allowed only when an Authorising Official approval reference or token is provided. Block access to all other destinations by default.
- [ ] Enable and keep audit records for allowlist status, facility registration, mobile device management (MDM) compliance, and network or session blocks. This supports random checks by the information system security officer (ISSO) and the security operations centre (SOC), and helps with incident response.


---

#### AC-19.5 — Full Device or Container-based Encryption (Enhancement)

This requirement is about protecting sensitive information on work mobile phones and tablets by keeping it inside encrypted “containers”, rather than leaving it exposed. Without this, a lost or misused device could reveal private files, records, or specific data, or allow unauthorised changes—damaging confidentiality and trust.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must use container-based encryption on all mobile devices and endpoints managed by the Department of Defence (DoD). It must clearly state which specific data areas are covered (for example, files, records, or data fields), and configure the encryption so it protects both secrecy and resistance to tampering.

The organisation must document how the encryption is set up and what protection mechanisms are used. This must be included in the system design and the system security plan. It must also ensure the device management system produces audit records that show encryption is enabled and record when encryption-related changes are made.

Finally, specific named individuals must be responsible for setting up the encryption, monitoring it, and managing changes, supported by clear procedures and instructions.

##### MDM/MAM container encryption for DoD-managed mobile endpoints

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement **container-based encryption** on **Department of Defense (DoD)-managed mobile devices and endpoints** by using the organisation’s **mobile device management (MDM)** and **mobile application management (MAM)** platform to encrypt selected data areas (for example, managed-app data containers such as files, records, or fields within approved apps). This ensures the encrypted containers provide **confidentiality** (only authorised people can read the data) and **integrity** (the data cannot be altered without detection).

###### WHY (control requirement)
National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) Access Control (AC)-19(5) enhancement requires: **“Employ {{ param, ac-19.05_odp.01 }} to protect the confidentiality and integrity of information on {{ param, ac-19.05_odp.02 }}.”** The guidance states that container-based encryption offers a **more detailed approach** by encrypting selected data structures on **mobile devices**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define what will be encrypted** in MDM/MAM policy terms: identify which **managed-app data containers** (for example, app document storage, email attachments, specific file types, or protected app data areas) must be encrypted. Clearly document what is **in scope** versus **out of scope**.
2. **Set up container encryption** to enforce **confidentiality and integrity** for the selected containers using the MDM/MAM platform’s supported encryption and data protection options (for example, managed app data encryption with integrity protection; disable or limit plain-text backup/export where supported).
3. **Apply the policy to DoD-managed endpoints**: roll out the container encryption policy to the defined group of **DoD-managed mobile devices and endpoints** using device compliance groups and conditional access, so only compliant devices can access the managed apps/data.
4. **Ensure the configuration can be checked**: record the exact MDM/MAM policy configuration (including scope and encryption settings) in configuration management records, and reference it in the **system security plan**.
5. **Capture and keep audit evidence**: centralise MDM/MAM audit logs into the **security information and event management (SIEM)** system (or an equivalent logging platform). Ensure logs record events such as **policy applied**, **encryption enabled/disabled**, and **changes to encryption-related settings**.
6. **Assign named operational responsibility**: appoint specific named roles (for example, MDM/MAM administrator and security configuration owner) for initial setup, monitoring compliance, and approving changes to encryption scope/settings.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **MDM/MAM Administrator**: implements and maintains the container encryption policies.
- **Information Security / Security Configuration Owner**: approves encryption scope/settings and ensures evidence is captured.
- **Compliance/Audit Liaison**: ensures the system security plan and audit evidence meet assessment needs.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Container-based encryption is **enabled via MDM/MAM** for the defined **DoD-managed mobile devices and endpoints**.
- The encrypted scope is **documented** and matches the MDM/MAM policy configuration (selected app data containers/files/records/fields).
- The configured container encryption provides **confidentiality and integrity**, as shown by MDM/MAM configuration and/or platform documentation.
- The **system security plan** references the container encryption method and settings.
- Audit logs show encryption/policy status and **encryption-related changes** for the managed endpoints.
- Named roles are assigned for configuration, monitoring, and approval of changes.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Identify and document the population of mobile devices and endpoints managed by the Department of Defense (DoD) that are covered by the mobile device management (MDM) and mobile application management (MAM) deployment.
- [ ] Define which managed-application data containers, files, records, and fields are encrypted, and document this encryption scope in the configuration documentation.
- [ ] Set up mobile device management (MDM) and mobile application management (MAM) rules to use encryption inside the approved (in-scope) containers, so the data is protected for both confidentiality and integrity.
- [ ] Apply the encryption settings to the specified device groups and require compliance checks before users can access managed applications and data.
- [ ] Enable mobile device management (MDM) and mobile application management (MAM) audit logs for encryption and policy setup, including any encryption-related configuration changes, and send those logs to the security information and event management (SIEM) system.
- [ ] Update the system security plan to reference the container encryption method, its scope, and the baseline configuration for the security policy.
- [ ] Assign named roles for managing encryption policy, monitoring compliance, and approving changes to the encryption scope.


---

### AC-20 — Use of External Systems (Control)

This requirement ensures that when people use outside systems to access the organisation’s services or handle organisation information, it is done safely and according to clear, agreed rules. Without this, sensitive information could be exposed, changed, or lost because outside providers or devices may not protect data in the same way, and issues may not be noticed or reported quickly.

The organisation must only allow access from external systems when the access and data-handling rules match the trust agreements it has with the owners of those external systems. These agreements must be written and must cover:
- which applications can be used
- the maximum level of information allowed (up to the Secret maximum)
- the access rules
- what activity must be recorded
- how incidents must be reported within 24 hours

For any approved use of external systems, those systems must enforce strong protections, including:
- encryption when data is sent and when it is stored
- requiring a second factor to sign in
- keeping audit records
- managing security weaknesses

The organisation must also prohibit:
- personally owned devices
- contractor-managed systems that are not managed by the organisation
- public cloud services
unless an approved authorisation boundary is in place.

##### External system trust agreements with enforceable access limits

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Put in place a controlled, auditable way to allow external systems to access organisational systems. This includes: (1) written terms and conditions (“trust agreements”) agreed with each external system owner, (2) a clear list of which applications are allowed and a maximum information security level the external system may handle (“Secret max”), and (3) technical and operational safeguards to protect external-system access (for example, encryption while data is moving and when stored, multi-factor authentication (MFA), audit logging, and vulnerability management), with incidents reported within 24 hours. Also clearly forbid specific types of external systems (for example, personally owned devices, contractor-managed systems that are not managed by the organisation, and public cloud services without an approved authorisation boundary).

###### WHY (control requirement)
Control AC-20 requires that external systems used to access organisational systems are governed by specific terms and conditions. At a minimum, these must cover: the types of applications that can be accessed; the highest security category of information that the external system may process, store, or transmit (up to the organisation’s “Secret max” ceiling); and the organisation’s ability to apply restrictions if trust terms cannot be agreed. The guidance also expects the organisation to define the safeguards it will require for external systems based on the trust relationship, and to define and block prohibited external system types.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create a “External System Trust Agreement” template** and require it for every external system type/owner relationship. Include the organisation’s minimum required content: allowed applications, the “Secret max” data classification limit, access rules, required audit logging, and breach/incident notification within 24 hours.
2. **Define and publish an external-system allow-list** that links each permitted external system type to: (a) the specific applications/application programming interfaces (APIs) it may access, and (b) the maximum data classification it may process (“Secret max”). Access must be granted only when the external system is on the allow-list and the user/session is authorised.
3. **Enforce access using technical safeguards that match the trust agreement**: require encryption while data is moving and when stored, require multi-factor authentication (MFA) for access (using phishing-resistant methods where possible), and ensure audit logging is enabled and sent to the organisation’s security information and event management (SIEM) / logging platform. Include vulnerability management expectations (for example, patching frequency and security scanning) in the trust agreement annex, and confirm them using evidence.
4. **Apply explicit prohibitions** for the organisation-defined prohibited external system types through both policy and technical controls (for example, block personally owned devices using device checks; block unmanaged contractor-managed systems by requiring managed devices and/or approved access points; block public cloud use unless an approved authorisation boundary exists).
5. **Run ongoing evidence and monitoring**: keep a register of external systems and their associated trust agreements; carry out periodic reviews (at least aligned to your access review schedule) and check for configuration changes to confirm the external system still meets the required safeguards.
6. **Define how incidents are handled for external-system access**: ensure the trust agreement includes the 24-hour notification requirement, and ensure the organisation’s incident response process can record, assess, and escalate external-system events with clear responsibility.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Information Security / Governance Lead**: owns the trust agreement template, the prohibited types list, and the external-system register.
- **IT / Identity Engineering Lead**: implements multi-factor authentication (MFA), device checks, and access controls.
- **Application Owner / System Owner**: defines which applications/APIs are allowed and the “Secret max” ceiling for each application.
- **Third-Party Risk / Procurement**: ensures trust agreements are signed with external system owners and that evidence is collected.
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC) / Incident Response Lead**: ensures the 24-hour notification process is in place and that security information and event management (SIEM) logging/alerts are working.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every permitted external system type/owner relationship, there is a **signed written trust agreement** that includes: allowed applications, the “Secret max” data limit, access rules, required logging, and **incident/breach notification within 24 hours**.
- Access to organisational systems from external systems is only possible using **allow-listed applications/APIs**, and only for data up to the **Secret max** ceiling.
- Technical safeguards are in place for **encryption while data is moving and when stored**, **multi-factor authentication (MFA)**, and **audit logging** (with logs available for audit).
- **Prohibited external system types** are blocked by policy and technical controls (no access route exists for personally owned devices, unmanaged contractor-managed systems, or unapproved public cloud use).
- External systems have evidence of **vulnerability management**, and the organisation can show ongoing compliance (for example, records of periodic reviews and configuration evidence).
- Incident response records show the organisation can meet the **24-hour notification** requirement for external-system events.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and get approval for a template for an external system trust agreement. The template must set out which applications are allowed, the maximum limits for secrets, the access rules, the logging that must be recorded, and a requirement to notify incidents within 24 hours.
- [ ] Create and keep an external-systems register that links each external system type and owner to its signed trust agreement and the controls it claims to have.
- [ ] Create an allow-list of approved applications and application programming interfaces (APIs), and enforce a maximum limit on how much secret data each application can access during the access approval workflow.
- [ ] Define which external system types are not allowed, and enforce policy checks so that personally owned devices, systems managed by contractors without approval, and unapproved public cloud services cannot be used.
- [ ] Provide evidence that data is protected while it is being sent and while it is stored, that multi-factor authentication (MFA) is used, that audit logs are kept, and that vulnerabilities are managed. Keep this evidence for audit purposes.
- [ ] Update the incident response playbook so that, when an incident happens, you notify and escalate to the relevant external systems within 24 hours.


---

#### AC-20.1 — Limits on Authorized Use (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that staff can only use an external service to access your systems or handle your information when the external provider has been properly checked or formally agreed. Otherwise, an external platform may not provide adequate protection or privacy, which could allow sensitive information to be leaked, changed, or used in ways your organisation did not intend. This could result in financial loss, legal issues, and reputational damage.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must allow only authorised people to use external systems to access, process, store, or send organisation-controlled information after either:
- verifying that the external system has the required security and privacy protections in place, as set out in the organisation’s own policies and plans; or
- maintaining an approved written agreement with the organisation that hosts the external system.

The organisation must also keep the relevant evidence or the approved agreement on file, so it can be shown when access is granted.

##### Assurance-gated external system access with audit-ready evidence

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up an approval process and technical safeguards so that authorised people can use an external system to access the organisation’s systems and/or processes, and to store or send organisation-controlled information **only after** the organisation has either: (1) **checked and confirmed** that the external system has the required security and privacy protections, or (2) kept an approved written agreement covering the connection and/or use of the external system.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-20(1) Enhancement – Limits on Authorized Use** requires that authorised people may use external systems **only after** the organisation has verified the required external protections (through independent assessment, attestation, or other suitable methods) **or** has kept an approved agreement.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create and maintain an External System Register** for every external system, vendor, or service that could be used for access or for processing, storing, or sending organisation-controlled information. Include: purpose, types of information, data flows, environments, user groups, hosting organisation, and risk level.
2. **Create an External Assurance Checklist** based on the organisation’s security and privacy policies and (where relevant) security and privacy plans. Use the checklist to decide whether the external system’s protections are sufficient for the organisation-controlled information and the intended activities (access, process, store, transmit).
3. **Add an onboarding step to the access request process** that creates an “External System Assurance Record” for each external system. The approval must be blocked unless the assurance record is in one of the allowed states:
