   - **Verified**: proof is provided and reviewed (for example, independent assessment reports, attestations, assurance packs) showing coverage against the checklist; or
   - **Approved Agreement**: an approved written agreement is on file, is still in date, and matches the external system and the specific activities and data flows.
4. **Collect and review proof** in a traceable way. Store proof details such as provider, report/attestation period, scope/coverage statement, checklist mapping, reviewer, review date, and the confidence/risk decision. The proof review must be carried out by a defined role (for example, Security/Privacy Assurance), and the decision must be recorded.
5. **Apply the decision using technical controls** such as conditional access or zero trust network access (ZTNA), or an equivalent approach. Access to the external system (and/or to organisation-controlled data through that external system) must only be possible when the assurance record is marked **Approved/Verified** (or when the approved agreement route is selected). Make sure the controls apply to all relevant user types (employees, contractors, partners, and service accounts) who can initiate use of external systems.
6. **Keep audit-ready records** that link each external system to the assurance method used (verification versus agreement), the approval date, and the supporting evidence/agreement documents, so auditors can confirm compliance at the time access was granted.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Information Security Assurance Lead** (owns the assurance checklist, proof review criteria, and approval decisions)
- **Privacy Officer / Privacy Assurance (where applicable)** (checks privacy-related checklist items and proof)
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) / Platform Engineering** (implements conditional access or ZTNA enforcement linked to the assurance record status)
- **Service/Vendor Management** (maintains approved agreements and ensures scope matches)
- **System Owners** (start the external system onboarding and provide the intended data flows and information types)

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every external system that can be used to access, process, store, or send organisation-controlled information, there is an External System Assurance Record.
- External system access is not granted unless the assurance record is **Approved** through either: (a) verified required protections with documented evidence coverage, or (b) an approved, in-date, scope-matching written agreement.
- Evidence/agreement documents and their link to the assurance record are kept and can be retrieved for audit.
- Conditional access / ZTNA (or an equivalent control) blocks access when the assurance record is missing, expired, or not approved.
- The onboarding workflow and technical enforcement cover all relevant activity types: access, process, store, and transmit.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an external systems register that lists all external systems used to access, and to process, store, or transmit organisation-controlled information.
- [ ] Publish an external assurance checklist that matches the organisation’s security and privacy policies and plans.
- [ ] Set up an onboarding process that prevents approval for external-system access unless an assurance record exists and has been approved.
- [ ] Set out what proof (evidence) is required and what counts as an acceptable result for independent assessments and attestations, and link the evidence to the checklist.
- [ ] Set up conditional access and zero trust network access (ZTNA), or an equivalent control, so that access is allowed only when the assurance record status is **Approved** or **Verified**.
- [ ] Store and keep audit-ready assurance records that link each external system to the verification evidence or the approved agreement, including the dates and the reviewer names.


---

#### AC-20.2 — Portable Storage Devices — Restricted Use (Enhancement)

This expectation is about tightly controlling company USB sticks and other approved portable storage devices when they are connected to other people’s computers or external computers. The goal is to stop them being used to spread harmful software or move data to places where it should not go. Without these controls, a device could bring viruses or other malicious code into a new system, start unwanted software as soon as it is connected, or allow information to be copied onto removable media that has not been approved.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must allow only authorised staff to use these devices for approved work tasks, and only on external systems that have been given written permission for that use. The devices must be encrypted, checked for threats immediately before and after each use, blocked from copying data to any unmanaged removable media, and configured so that automatic start-up behaviour is disabled when the device is connected.

The organisation should also keep clear written procedures, policies, and records of system settings. These records should include named responsibilities and agreements that set out when and how these devices may be used.

##### Workflow-controlled encrypted USBs with scan-before/after and no auto-run

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a controlled portable-storage programme for devices owned and managed by the organisation (for example, encrypted USB drives). Only authorised people may use these devices for **approved mission tasks** on **external systems where there is written permission**, using a process that requires **encryption**, **malware checks before and after use**, and **turning off auto-run**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST Special Publication 800-53 Access Control (AC)-20(2) (enhancement) requires limiting how authorised people use organisation-controlled portable storage devices on external systems, using the organisation-defined setting **AC-20.02_ODP (“restrictions”)**. This setting requires: **use only for approved mission tasks; only on systems with written authorisation; no copying to unmanaged media; encrypt the devices; scan before and after use; disable auto-run**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Standardise and encrypt devices**: Provide only organisation-controlled portable devices that are centrally managed, with encryption enabled by default (for example, hardware-encrypted USB drives or centrally managed operating-system encryption). Keep records showing encryption is active.
2. **Disable auto-run and verify**: Configure devices so auto-run/auto-play cannot start software when connected. Keep and retain evidence that auto-run has been disabled for audit purposes.
3. **Use written authorisation and mission task through a workflow**: Create an IT service management (ITSM) or secure portal workflow that records: the user’s identity, the approved mission task reference, the external system identifier, and the written authorisation reference (for example, ticket or approval ID). The workflow must only release the device for the specified external system and time window.
4. **Scan before and after use using a controlled kiosk/managed endpoint**: Require devices to be checked out and connected only through an approved scanning kiosk (or managed endpoint). This must perform malware scanning **immediately before** the device is connected to the external system, and again **immediately after** the device is returned/disconnected. If scanning fails, quarantine or block the device and raise an incident.
5. **Stop copying to unmanaged media in practice**: Configure the external system session to prevent copying from the organisation-controlled device to unmanaged removable media (for example, limit removable media types or USB write access for the session, or use endpoint controls that block writing to other removable drives). If the external system cannot be controlled, the workflow must block use until an acceptable technical restriction is confirmed.

###### WHO
- **Cybersecurity / IT Security team**: set device standards, define scanning and quarantine criteria, and define approval requirements.
- **IT Operations / Service Desk**: run the device check-out/check-in workflow and keep evidence.
- **External system owners (IT/Operational Technology (OT)/third-party management)**: provide written authorisation and implement the required removable-media restrictions for the session.
- **Endpoint and identity administrators**: implement kiosk/managed endpoint scanning and configure auto-run settings.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Only centrally issued encrypted portable devices are used for external-system sessions.
- Auto-run/auto-play is disabled on devices, with configuration evidence retained.
- Every external-system use is linked to an approved mission task and a written authorisation reference captured in the workflow.
- Malware scanning happens immediately **before** connection and immediately **after** disconnection/return; failed scans lead to quarantine/blocking.
- External systems used for the workflow have confirmed technical restrictions that prevent copying to unmanaged removable media for the session.
- Audit evidence exists for: device encryption status, auto-run disabled setting, workflow approvals, scan times/results, and quarantine actions.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Set the approved standards for encrypted portable devices and publish the approved build and configuration baselines, ensuring automatic start-up is disabled.
- [ ] Set up an information technology service management (ITSM) and secure portal process that records the user’s identity, the approved mission task, the external system identifier, and the written authorisation reference.
- [ ] Set up a secure “scanning kiosk” or managed endpoint that checks for malware as soon as a device is connected, and again as soon as the device is returned.
- [ ] Set up devices so that auto-run or auto-play cannot start software when they are connected, and keep records of the configuration so it can be checked during an audit.
- [ ] Set up workflow controls so devices can only be checked out by explicitly authorised external systems, and only during approved time windows.
- [ ] Coordinate with the owners of external systems to put in place controls that stop users copying data to unmanaged removable storage, and record confirmation.


---

#### AC-20.3 — Non-organizationally Owned Systems — Restricted Use (Enhancement)

This requirement is about preventing staff from using other people’s systems, or their own personal devices, to handle the organisation’s information unless it is done under strict control. Without this, sensitive information could be exposed, lost, or accessed by the wrong people because outside systems may not protect information to the same standard, and personal devices are harder to manage and investigate if something goes wrong.

The organisation must clearly define what counts as a “non-organisation-owned” system or component. It must only allow such systems to process, store, or send organisational information after getting prior approval, using security protections the organisation has already reviewed and agreed. Access must be limited to Confidential or Secret information only. Personally owned devices must not be used to process Classified information.

The organisation must put these rules into written procedures and agreements. It must document how the systems are set up to enforce the limits, assign named responsibilities, and keep records showing who used which system or device and when.

##### Approval-gated external/non-owned system access with data-class limits

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up an approval step and technical safeguards to ensure **non-organisationally owned systems or components** (including **other organisations’ systems** and **personally owned devices**) are used to process, store, or transmit **organisational information** only. The exact limits are defined in the organisation parameter **ac-20.03_odp (restrictions)**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-20(3)** requires that non-organisationally owned systems or components are restricted to organisational information using **{{ insert: param, ac-20.03_odp }}**. The guidance also expects restrictions such as **getting approval before use**, **having approved security measures in place before connecting**, and **limiting what types of information, services, or applications can be accessed**. It also highlights legal and forensic considerations for personally owned devices.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
###### 1) Define and put “non-organisationally owned” into practice
- Keep a controlled set of categories in the access request process for:
  - Other organisations’ systems or components (for example, vendor software-as-a-service not under your organisation’s tenant, contractor-managed platforms, third-party remote support tools)
  - Personally owned devices (bring your own device (BYOD))
- Make sure the request captures the **system/component identity**, **owner**, **purpose**, **how it connects**, and the **intended information classification**.

###### 2) Require prior approval and link permission to approved security measures
- Create an IT service request (IT service management (ITSM)) that **cannot be completed** unless it has pre-approval from:
  - Information Security (approval of security measures)
  - System/Network owner (operational acceptance)
- Require the request to reference an **approved security measures checklist** (for example, encryption in transit/at rest where applicable, least privilege, logging and monitoring, secure configuration baseline, and expectations for vulnerability and patch status).
- Save the approval records (approval decision, checklist sign-off, and any legal or usage agreement references) as audit evidence.

###### 3) Enforce the data classification limits defined by the organisation
- Make the permission outcome match **ac-20.03_odp**:
  - **Allowed only with prior approval, approved security measures, and access limited to Confidential/Secret information**
  - **Personally owned devices are not allowed for processing Classified information**
- Technical enforcement examples (choose what fits your environment):
  - Identity provider conditional access rules that block access when the request is not approved and/or when the device is identified as BYOD for the Classified scope.
  - Data access controls (for example, application-level authorisation and/or data loss prevention (DLP) or label-based controls) that prevent handling of Confidential/Secret versus Classified information outside the approved scope.

###### 4) Require clear usage terms for non-owned systems/components
- For each approved connection or use, require agreement to usage terms covering:
  - Allowed purposes (process/store/transmit organisational information only)
  - Prohibited actions (for example, no Classified processing on personally owned devices)
  - Expectations for incident and forensic support for personally owned devices (handled through legally approved terms)

###### 5) Produce audit-ready evidence
- Ensure the process records:
  - Who requested access, which system/component was used, the approval decision, the approved security measures, and the maximum allowed information classification.
  - The technical enforcement status (for example, conditional access policy applied, account/device assignment created).
- Keep logs detailed enough to show that any non-approved or non-compliant uses were blocked.

###### WHO
- **Information Security**: approves the security measures and the maximum data classification.
- **System/Network/Application Owner**: approves operational suitability and the connection method.
- **Service Desk/ITSM Owner**: runs the request process and ensures evidence is captured.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) team**: implements conditional access and enforces authorisation.
- **Legal/General Counsel (as needed)**: reviews usage terms for personally owned devices, including forensic expectations.

###### Acceptance criteria
- No non-organisationally owned system/component is authorised to process, store, or transmit organisational information without recorded **prior approval**.
- Every authorisation includes evidence of **approved security measures** and a **maximum allowed data classification** of **Confidential/Secret only**.
- Access requests involving **personally owned devices** for **Classified** processing are automatically blocked or rejected.
- Audit evidence exists showing: request details, approvals, enforcement applied (for example, IAM policy/authorisation), and enforcement logs proving that non-compliant attempts were denied.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an IT service management (ITSM) service request workflow for systems and components that are not owned by the organisation, and for bring your own device (BYOD). The workflow should record the system’s identity, its purpose, how it connects, and the data classification being requested.
- [ ] Define and publish a controlled classification (taxonomy) for systems and components that are not owned by the organisation, and link each category to the required approval route.
- [ ] Set up approval checkpoints so access cannot be authorised unless Information Security and the system or network owner have signed off on the approved security controls.
- [ ] Set up identity and access management (IAM) conditional access and application and data permissions to allow access only to Confidential and Secret information, and to prevent personally owned devices from being used for Classified processing.
- [ ] For every approved use of a non-owned system or component, require acceptance of the legally approved usage terms, including expectations for incident handling and digital forensics for bring your own device (BYOD).
- [ ] Store approval records and enforcement evidence (checklist sign-off, highest security classification, applied policies, and denial logs) so they are available for audit retention.


---

#### AC-20.4 — Network Accessible Storage Devices — Prohibited Use (Enhancement)

This requirement is about preventing staff systems from using any shared storage that can be reached over a network from outside your organisation. This includes online storage provided through public, hybrid, or community cloud services. If this is not done, sensitive information could be copied, accessed, or exposed through external storage locations that are harder to control. That increases the risk of unauthorised access, data loss, or misuse by partners or other third parties.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly state in its access rules and supporting procedures that network-accessible storage devices are not allowed in external systems. This includes items such as network-attached storage, storage networks, network file shares, and cloud object storage endpoints. The organisation must also reflect this ban in system design, configuration settings, and its security plan.

The organisation must keep an up-to-date list of the specific storage targets that are prohibited. It must ensure that any external connection or processing agreements include the same prohibition, and it must keep audit records showing that the prohibited storage was not used.

##### Block and evidence prohibition of external network storage use

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Stop using **all network-accessible storage devices** (network-attached storage (NAS), storage area networks (SAN), server message block/network file system (SMB/NFS) network shares, and object storage endpoints) **in external systems**, including **online storage in public, hybrid, or community cloud**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) access control (AC-20(4)) enhancement requires: **“Prohibit the use of {{ insert: param, ac-20.04_odp }} in external systems.”** The guidance explains that “external systems” includes **online storage services in public, hybrid, or community cloud**. This reduces the risk that data is copied to, or accessed through, storage locations that are harder to control and audit.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the prohibited scope** in the access control policy and the system security plan using the organisation-defined parameter **ac-20.04_odp**: “all network-accessible storage devices (NAS, SAN, SMB/NFS shares, object storage endpoints) in external systems”. Make sure it explicitly includes **public/hybrid/community cloud online storage**.
2. **Create and maintain a Prohibited Storage Target Registry** that lists the specific storage endpoints/shares/buckets/containers treated as “external-system network storage” for this application (for example: approved external software-as-a-service (SaaS) storage domains, partner cloud buckets, public object storage endpoints, and SMB/NFS share patterns). Assign an owner and a process for updates.
3. **Block prohibited storage by default** so it cannot be used:
   - Set up **outbound (egress) controls / firewall rules / proxy policies** to block outgoing connections from the application and its managed services to prohibited storage endpoints (using hostname/IP/fully qualified domain name (FQDN), and where possible protocol/port such as SMB/NFS).
   - Apply **storage access allow-listing using identity and access management (IAM)**: remove broad storage permissions and require explicit, time-limited approval for any exception (exceptions must still follow the organisation’s risk acceptance process).
   - For cloud services, use **policy-as-code / service control policies** to stop the creation or mounting of external file/object storage from non-approved situations.
4. **Stop interactive and service access paths** by integrating with enterprise identity and access management:
   - Use conditional access to restrict access to storage consoles/application programming interfaces (APIs) from non-approved devices or contexts.
   - Ensure service accounts used by the application cannot obtain credentials/roles that allow access to prohibited external storage.
5. **Produce audit evidence**:
   - Turn on logging for storage-related actions (for example: mount attempts, object storage API calls, SMB/NFS connection attempts, and blocked outbound (egress) events).
   - Match the logs to the Prohibited Storage Target Registry and produce regular compliance reports showing **no successful use**, and include **blocked attempts** where relevant.
6. **Update external connection/processing agreements** (where the application connects with partners or suppliers) to include the same prohibition on network-accessible storage in external systems, ensuring the contract matches the technical controls.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Owner / Security Architect**: owns the policy wording, updates to the system security plan, and the enforcement design.
- **Infrastructure/Cloud Security Engineer**: implements outbound blocking (egress), proxy rules, and IAM allow-listing, plus policy-as-code.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Lead**: implements conditional access and service account restrictions.
- **Security Information and Event Management (SIEM)/Monitoring Engineer**: ensures audit logging, log correlation, and reporting.
- **Third-Party/Legal/Procurement**: ensures the contract clauses in connection/processing agreements include the requirement.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The access control policy and system security plan explicitly prohibit **ac-20.04_odp** (all network-accessible storage devices) in **external systems**, including **public/hybrid/community cloud online storage**.
- A maintained **Prohibited Storage Target Registry** exists, with an owner and an update process.
- Technical controls block prohibited storage by default (no successful mounts/connections/API access to prohibited endpoints from the application environment).
- Audit evidence is produced: logs show **blocked attempts** and/or no successful access to prohibited storage targets, matched to the registry.
- External connection/processing agreements include the prohibition clause that matches the technical and policy requirements.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Update the access control policy and the application system security plan to clearly forbid **ac-20.04_odp** in external systems, including public, hybrid, and community cloud online storage.
- [ ] Set up and keep a **Prohibited Storage Target Register** (endpoints, network shares, storage buckets, and storage containers) with clear named owners and a controlled change process.
- [ ] Set up outbound controls (egress, proxy, and firewall “deny” rules) to block access to prohibited storage targets for Server Message Block (SMB), Network File System (NFS), and object storage. Block by storage target name (fully qualified domain name (FQDN)) and by connection type (protocol) and port.
- [ ] Use identity and access management (IAM) allow-listing for storage access, so application workloads and service accounts cannot connect to external storage endpoints that are not permitted.
- [ ] Set up conditional access and service account restrictions to stop interactive logins and automated workloads from accessing prohibited storage consoles and application programming interfaces (APIs).
- [ ] Set up and centralise logging for storage access attempts and blocked outbound (egress) network events, then match the results against the prohibited registry to support audit reporting.
- [ ] Add the storage restriction clause to external connection and processing agreements for any partner or vendor integrations that could involve storing data outside your organisation.


---

#### AC-20.5 — Portable Storage Devices — Prohibited Use (Enhancement)

This requirement is a strict rule: staff must not plug the organisation’s own USB drives or other organisation-managed portable storage into systems that the organisation does not directly control. The real-world risk is that these devices can bring in malware or copy sensitive information to partner, customer, contractor, or other external environments—sometimes without anyone noticing until harm has already occurred.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly define what counts as an external system and publish a complete ban in its access rules and supporting procedures, so staff know what is allowed and what is not. The organisation must also enforce the ban in practice. This should include using system settings to block portable storage where possible, and using documented processes—such as training and approvals—for any data movement that is genuinely needed.

Named roles must be responsible for this, including system and network administrators and information security staff. The organisation must also reflect the ban in its system design and security planning, include it in connection or processing agreements, and keep audit records showing the rule is being followed.

##### Deny organisation USB use on external systems via endpoint policy

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Put in place a complete ban on authorised people using organisation-controlled portable storage devices (for example, USB memory) on systems outside the organisation. Apply this using technical safeguards where possible, supported by documented procedures.

###### WHY (control requirement)
AC-20(5) Enhancement requires: **“Prohibit the use of organisation-controlled portable storage devices by authorised individuals on external systems.”** Guidance also says this must be a **complete prohibition**, and it should be enforced using **technical methods and/or non-technical (process-based) methods**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define what counts as an “external system”** in the access control policy and supporting procedure (for example: any system not directly owned or managed by the organisation; partner/customer/contractor environments; customer virtual machines accessible via the internet; third-party managed devices).
2. **Apply a default “deny” on devices that can reach external systems** by using enterprise endpoint management and device controls to block **USB mass storage/removable media** (and related storage types such as USB storage devices) when the device is in an “external access” situation (for example: connected via a virtual private network (VPN) to external networks, or placed in an “external access” device group).
3. **Stop organisation-controlled portable media being used on external systems** by making sure the deny rule applies specifically to organisation-issued portable storage (where the device identity can be managed). If this is not possible, apply a blanket deny of removable storage for the “external access” device group.
4. **Offer an approved alternative way to move data** if it is needed (for example: organisation-approved secure file transfer tools, encrypted uploads to approved repositories, or controlled application-to-application transfer). Document that USB-based transfer is not allowed.
5. **Record and keep evidence**: capture attempts to connect devices, the block/deny result, the user identity, the device identity, and the time. Send this information to the security information and event management (SIEM) system for audit-ready reporting.
6. **Manage exceptions (if any)** through a documented approval process that includes a security review and records the reason. Exceptions must not weaken the “complete prohibition” unless they are explicitly approved.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Information Security (policy owner):** defines “external system”, publishes the ban and the exception process.
- **Endpoint/IT Operations (technical implementer):** sets up device-control rules in Microsoft Intune, Jamf, VMware Workspace ONE (or equivalent) and ensures enforcement for “external access” situations.
- **System/Network Administrators:** manage device group assignments and the logic that determines “external access” status.
- **SIEM/Monitoring team:** ensures logs are collected, formatted consistently, and kept for audit purposes.

###### Acceptance criteria
- There is a written policy/procedure that defines **external system** and states a **complete prohibition** on using organisation-controlled portable storage on external systems.
- Device controls are configured so that, for devices in the **external access** context, **USB mass storage/removable storage is denied** (block events are recorded).
- Any approved alternative data transfer method is documented and used instead of USB transfer.
- Audit evidence is available showing **block/deny events** (including user/device/time) for attempts to use organisation-controlled portable storage on external systems.
- If exceptions are used, they are documented with security approval records and do not remove the ban without authorisation.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and publish what “external system” means in the access control policy and the supporting procedure.
- [ ] Create an “external access” device-control policy for endpoints that blocks USB mass storage and other removable media.
- [ ] Apply the “deny” rule only when the external-access situation meets certain conditions (for example, the virtual private network (VPN) security status or the external-access device group).
- [ ] Set up logging for attempts to connect removable media, including any blocked or denied outcomes, and send those event records to the security information and event management (SIEM) system.
- [ ] Document and publish approved non-USB secure ways to transfer any data that needs to be moved.
- [ ] Set up an exception process that requires information security approval and proper recordkeeping, so the prohibition remains the default option.


---

### AC-21 — Information Sharing (Control)

This expectation is about making sure people share sensitive information only with the right partners, for the right reasons, and under the correct legal and access rules. Without this, an organisation could accidentally disclose classified or contract-sensitive material to someone who is not properly authorised, or share it without the required agreements. This could cause serious legal, financial, and reputational damage.

To meet this requirement, the organisation must give authorised staff a way to check that the access granted to a sharing partner matches the specific restrictions attached to the information. This includes any special access programme or compartment rules, and any non-disclosure agreement requirements.

The organisation must also provide clear, policy-based guidance that uses the information’s sensitivity labels, carries out automated eligibility checks, and prompts the user to confirm the required agreement and any special access conditions before sharing. Where automation is not sufficient, the organisation must define when staff must use judgement, and require them to record the decision and the reasons for it.

##### Policy-based sharing workflow with NDA/SAP access-match prompts

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a sharing process that can be independently checked later (audited) by doing two things:  
(1) attach machine-readable labels (classification tags) and required sharing limits to every item that is shared, and  
(2) check that the chosen sharing partner is authorised to receive and use the information under those required limits (including rules governed by non-disclosure agreements (NDAs) and by SAP/compartment identifiers).  

If needed, the process must require mandatory confirmations/validations (for example, NDA/SAP/compartment confirmation) and must not allow sharing until those checks are completed.

###### WHY (control requirement)
Control AC-21 requires that sharing of restricted information is governed based on formal decisions, supported by security and privacy risk assessments and applicable laws and policies. It also requires defining when users may exercise judgement, and defining automated or manual support that helps users make correct sharing and collaboration decisions.  

This solution meets those requirements by matching the partner’s access authorisations to the information’s required access and use limits, and by providing policy-based guidance using classification tags plus automated checks and mandatory NDA/SAP/compartment validation prompts.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define an information-to-restriction mapping model**: create a controlled set of classification tags and link each tag to the required sharing restrictions (what actions are allowed such as view, edit, or download; what types of sharing partners are permitted; and whether NDA and/or SAP/compartment validation is required). Include clear handling for special access programme and compartment identifiers.
2. **Attach tags and restrictions to information items**: ensure every item that can be shared (documents, records, datasets, or messages) includes the required metadata (classification tags plus restriction details). Apply this when the item is created or received, using existing classification tools or enforced metadata in the application.
3. **Create and maintain a partner eligibility and authorisation directory**: keep records of sharing partners (individuals, groups, organisations) and whether they are eligible for NDA and SAP/compartment access. Connect this to identity and access management (IAM) and entitlement sources, and to contract and NDA records, so the workflow can validate eligibility.
4. **Implement the sharing request workflow with access-match checking**: when a user requests sharing, the workflow must:
   - work out the information’s required restrictions from its tags and attributes;
   - compare those restrictions to the selected partner’s authorisations and eligibility for the requested access type;
   - produce an auditable result (pass or fail), including which rule(s) were checked.
5. **Require mandatory validations and prompts**: if the information’s restrictions mean the sharing is governed by an NDA and/or SAP/compartment, require the user to complete mandatory confirmations (for example, selecting/confirming the correct NDA reference and SAP/compartment authorisation evidence) and block approval until the validations pass.
6. **Allow defined user judgement with recorded reasons**: only in the organisation-defined situations where judgement is permitted, allow a guided manual override. This must require the user to provide a reason and references (for example, a ticket or approval identifier). Record the reason, the decision, and the authorisation evidence used.
7. **Produce audit-ready evidence**: ensure the workflow records (logs) include:  
   (a) the information item identifier and the tags used,  
   (b) the partner selected,  
   (c) the requested access type,  
   (d) the automated access-match results,  
   (e) whether the NDA/SAP/compartment prompts were completed, and  
   (f) any manual override reason.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Owner / Product Owner**: owns the workflow requirements and ensures it is built into the application.
- **Information Security (Policy/Governance, Risk and Compliance (GRC)) Lead**: defines the classification-to-restriction mappings and the situations where user judgement is allowed.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) / Entitlements Engineer**: integrates the partner eligibility and authorisation sources.
- **Contract/NDA Governance Owner**: ensures NDA and SAP/compartment references and eligibility records are accurate.
- **Security Operations / Compliance**: reviews audit logs and ensures evidence is retained.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For any sharing request involving classified or contract-sensitive information (including SAP/compartment and NDA-governed items), the workflow must perform an automated access-match check against the information’s required access and use restrictions, and must block sharing when authorisations do not match.
- NDA and SAP/compartment validations must be mandatory (required prompts/confirmations) and cannot be skipped for items that require them.
- All sharing decisions (including manual overrides) must produce complete, tamper-evident audit records that include the evaluated tags/restrictions, the basis for partner eligibility, and the reason where user judgement is used.
- The workflow must use the organisation-defined automated mechanisms and user-judgement parameters for classified and contract-sensitive data.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the classification labels and link each label to the access and use rules that apply, including requirements for a non-disclosure agreement (NDA) and for SAP systems and compartments.
- [ ] Apply machine-readable labels and access restrictions to every piece of information in the application that can be shared.
- [ ] Connect the sharing process to identity and access management (IAM) and entitlement records, and to the contract and non-disclosure agreement (NDA) repositories, so that partner eligibility for NDA and SAP access to the relevant compartments can be confirmed.
- [ ] Carry out an access match check that compares what each partner is authorised to do with the restrictions required for the type of access requested.
- [ ] Set up mandatory non-disclosure agreement (NDA) checks and SAP and compartment validation prompts, and prevent sharing until the required checks are completed.
- [ ] Create a controlled manual override route only for the organisation-approved situations where discretion is allowed. Require a clear reason for each use and keep a record of the evidence.
- [ ] Enable tamper-evident audit logging for each sharing decision, including the tags used, the results of automated checks, whether the prompt was completed, and the reason for any override.


---

#### AC-21.1 — Automated Decision Support (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure information is shared only when the right people ask for it, and that the system automatically and consistently checks that the receiving partner is allowed to receive that specific information. Without this, staff could accidentally (or improperly) share sensitive material with the wrong organisation, or share more than they should, which could cause legal, financial, and reputational harm.  

To meet this expectation, the organisation must put in place policy-based processes and approval checkpoints so that sharing decisions are only triggered by users who are authorised to make those decisions. Every request must be automatically checked against two things: (1) the partner’s current permission to receive information, and (2) the specific access limits attached to the information being shared.  

The organisation must also keep up-to-date records of who is allowed to make decisions, which partners are allowed to receive information, and what restrictions apply. The system must be configured to enforce these rules without any manual workarounds, and the organisation must document how the automated checks work, so there is evidence that the system’s decisions were based on the correct, up-to-date information.

##### Policy-driven sharing workflow with partner and data restriction checks

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement an automated, policy-led workflow that enforces information-sharing decisions by **authorised users**. Before any sharing takes effect, the workflow must check both:
1) the **sharing partner’s permissions**, and
2) the **access limits set for the specific information** being shared.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-21(1)** enhancement requires using **automated checks** to enforce information-sharing decisions made by authorised users, based on:
- the **sharing partner’s permissions**, and
- the **access limits on the information** being shared.

These automated checks must prevent unauthorised sharing or outcomes that do not follow policy.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the policy inputs and decision rules**
   - Create a machine-readable policy model that links each shareable information item (or data classification/category) to **access limits** (for example: which partner types are allowed, required approval level, allowed recipients/groups, expiry, and handling requirements).
   - Create a machine-readable partner authorisation model that links each **sharing partner** (external tenant/vendor/service principal) to the **permissions** they have for each information category.
2. **Limit decision-making to authorised users**
   - Use role-based access control (RBAC) so only users in a dedicated **“sharing decision-maker/approver”** role can start and/or approve sharing requests.
   - Remove or disable any direct “grant sharing” ability for non-decision-makers (including any user interface or application programming interface routes), so decisions must go through the workflow.
3. **Check automatically before sharing is activated**
   - Send all sharing requests through an approval workflow engine (or equivalent enterprise workflow capability) that performs automated checks:
     - Confirm the requested partner is permitted to receive the requested information category.
     - Confirm the information’s access-limit rules are met (for example: required approval tier, time limits, and allowed handling requirements).
   - Only after the automated checks succeed should the system carry out the sharing action (for example: create the share link/access control list, grant access, or trigger downstream sharing).
4. **Create system-generated decision records (audit evidence)**
   - For every decision, record: the partner identity, the information identifier/category, the policy rules checked, the result (allow/deny), the approver identity (if applicable), timestamps, and correlation identifiers for traceability.
   - Send decision logs to the organisation’s logging and security monitoring tools for auditability.
5. **Keep policy data up to date and authoritative**
   - Set up an ownership process for maintaining partner permissions and the mappings to information access limits (including change control and regular review) so automated decisions always use current data.
   - Ensure the workflow configuration is version-controlled and released through the organisation’s standard release/change management process.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Owner / Data Governance Lead**: defines information access-limit rules and owns the policy model.
- **IAM/Access Governance Lead**: defines and maintains partner permissions and the decision-maker roles.
- **Security Engineering / Platform Team**: implements workflow enforcement, prevents bypass routes, and ensures audit logging.
- **Compliance/Audit Liaison**: checks that the evidence is sufficient and supports audit readiness.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every sharing request, the system automatically checks both **(a) partner permissions** and **(b) information access limits** before any sharing is activated.
- Only users in the configured decision-maker/approver roles can start and/or approve sharing outcomes; unauthorised users cannot bypass the workflow through the user interface or application programming interface.
- Denied requests are blocked and recorded with decision evidence.
- For each allow/deny decision, the system produces an auditable decision record that includes partner identity, information category/identifier, the evaluated policy/rules, the outcome, the approver (where relevant), and timestamps.
- Decision logs are kept and available for audit (for example, through security monitoring and log retention) and can be traced back to the sharing action.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create a machine-readable map that links shareable information categories or objects to the rules that control who can access them.
- [ ] Create a machine-readable list that maps each sharing partner to the permissions they have for each information category.
- [ ] Create separate identity and access management (IAM) roles for decision-makers and approvers, and limit their permissions to the minimum needed (least privilege).
- [ ] Set up a workflow checkpoint so every request to share information must first pass automated checks for partner approval and information access limits before sharing is enabled.
- [ ] Remove or disable direct sharing options in the user interface (UI) and application programming interface (API) so that only decision-makers can grant access, preventing others from bypassing the process.
- [ ] Create system-generated records of decisions that show the rules that were checked, the partner identity, the information identifier and category, the result, and the date and time.
- [ ] Connect decision logs to security information and event management (SIEM) and logging, and check that audit records can be traced from each request through to the point where sharing is activated.


---

#### AC-21.2 — Information Search and Retrieval (Enhancement)

This requirement ensures that when people search for documents or information, they only see content they are officially authorised to access, and only in the approved situations for sensitive material. Without this, someone could accidentally or deliberately find restricted **Secret** or **Confidential** content, or share it with the wrong customer, partner, country, or programme. This could create serious legal and contractual risk, including breaches of export controls and other compliance obligations.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly define who may receive **Secret** and **Confidential** information and the approved limits for sharing it. It must then configure its search service to filter results according to the user’s permissions and the item’s sharing rules.

The organisation must also follow the applicable export-control and compliance requirements for **Secret** and **Confidential** data, keep documented procedures and design information that explain how these restrictions work, and retain records of searches and the decisions made. This allows the organisation to review and demonstrate what was shown or blocked.

##### ABAC policy-as-code filtering for Secret/Confidential search results

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up information search and retrieval services that enforce the organisation-defined **information-sharing restrictions** (ac-21.02_odp) by filtering search results at the time of the search. Filtering must be based on: **(1) user authorisation** and **(2) approved data-sharing boundaries** for Secret/Confidential information. This includes enforcing **International Traffic in Arms Regulations (ITAR) / Export Administration Regulations (EAR)** and **Cybersecurity Maturity Model Certification level 2 and level 3 (CMMC-L2/L3)** constraints.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-21(2) (Enhancement) requires: **“Implement information search and retrieval services that enforce {{ insert: param, ac-21.02_odp }}.”** The guidance also requires that search services **identify the information system resources relevant to a user’s information need**, while ensuring only permitted resources are returned.

###### HOW (specific approach)
1. **Create an authoritative metadata record model** for every Secret/Confidential item indexed by the search service. This must include: classification (Secret/Confidential), ITAR/EAR tags, required CMMC level (L2/L3), and sharing-boundary attributes (for example: programme/customer/partner/country boundary).
2. **Define user/recipient attributes** in the identity and access layer (or a dedicated authorisation service) that are sufficient to check sharing restrictions. This includes clearance/authorisation, permitted programmes, permitted countries/partners, and CMMC eligibility for external recipients.
3. **Use attribute-based access control (ABAC) with policy-as-code** built into the search service. For each search request, the service must evaluate the request against rules derived from ac-21.02_odp. For example: “if an item has ITAR/EAR tag X, only users with permit(X) may see it”; “if the item boundary does not match the user’s approved boundary, withhold it”.
4. **Apply filtering at the time of search and at the time of results use**: the search service must make the ABAC decision before returning results, and any later retrieval or export that starts from those results must use the same enforcement decision.
5. **Compile policies into a restriction list held by the system** (generated from the policy-as-code and authoritative metadata). The search service must use this consistently across environments (development/testing/production).
6. **Record enforcement decisions for audit purposes**: capture the search context, the user identity, the policy decision outcome (allowed/withheld/filtered), and reason codes (for example: boundary mismatch, ITAR/EAR not permitted, CMMC level not satisfied). Send these logs to the organisation’s security information and event management (SIEM) system.
7. **Put change control in place**: version policies, require review and approval for policy changes, and run automated tests that confirm expected allow/deny outcomes for representative Secret/Confidential items.

###### WHO
- **Application Owner / Search Service Owner**: ensures the search service includes ABAC enforcement and reuses the same decisions for retrieval/export.
- **Information Security / Compliance (Export Control and CMMC governance)**: defines and approves the sharing-boundary rules and the ITAR/EAR/CMMC mapping.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Team**: sets up and maintains user/recipient attributes and assurance levels.
- **Security Engineering / Platform Team**: implements policy-as-code, restriction-list compilation, and audit logging.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For Secret/Confidential items, the search service returns **only** results where **both** user authorisation and approved sharing boundaries match the item metadata.
- Requests that breach ITAR/EAR or CMMC-L2/L3 constraints must result in **withheld/filtered** outcomes (no exposure through ranking, snippets, or metadata).
- Enforcement decisions must be applied **consistently** to both search results and any subsequent retrieval/export actions started from those results.
- Audit logs must show, for each request, the **decision outcome** and **reason code**, and be available for review in the SIEM.
- Policy changes must be versioned, reviewed, and validated by automated tests covering boundary mismatch and ITAR/EAR/CMMC denial scenarios.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and document the rules for what can be shared under “Secret” and “Confidential” classifications, including the boundaries by programme, customer, partner, and country. Then link these rules to the relevant legal and compliance requirements, including the International Traffic in Arms Regulations (ITAR), the Export Administration Regulations (EAR), and the Cybersecurity Maturity Model Certification levels 2 and 3 (CMMC-L2 and CMMC-L3).
- [ ] Create and label indexed content with its security classification, International Traffic in Arms Regulations (ITAR) and Export Administration Regulations (EAR) tags, the required Cybersecurity Maturity Model Certification (CMMC) level, and boundary attributes.
- [ ] Set up user or recipient authorisation details in the identity layer, including clearance or authorisation level, which programmes they are allowed to access, which countries or partners they are allowed to work with, and whether they are eligible for the Cybersecurity Maturity Model Certification (CMMC).
- [ ] Set up attribute-based access control (ABAC) rules as code, integrated with the search service, so that every search request is checked and results are filtered when the query is run.
- [ ] Ensure that any later retrieval or export of data from search results uses the same attribute-based access control (ABAC) decision, and cannot be used to bypass filtering.
- [ ] Create and deploy a restriction list stored within the system, based on approved policies and official metadata, with version control.
- [ ] Set up audit records for all search queries and the decisions made about them (allowed, withheld, or filtered). Include a reason code for each decision, and send these records to security information and event management (SIEM).


---

### AC-22 — Publicly Accessible Content (Control)

This requirement is about stopping private or confidential information from being accidentally published on your organisation’s public website or other public-facing pages. Without it, staff could post sensitive material such as private personal details, internal documents, confidential contract information, or proprietary information. This could lead to legal issues, reputational damage, and harm to customers or employees.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must:
- appoint specific people who are authorised to publish public content,
- train them so they clearly understand they must not include any non-public information, and
- put a process in place to review every proposed item before it is posted, to confirm it contains nothing that should remain private.

After content goes live, the organisation must check the public-facing content every day, promptly remove anything non-public that is found, and keep records of these checks and actions.

##### Authorised public publishing workflow with daily review evidence

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Set up a controlled process for any content that becomes publicly available on organisation-controlled systems. This must ensure that only **authorised and trained individuals** can publish, that a **review before publication** is carried out and recorded, and that publicly available content is **checked every day** to quickly spot and remove any information that should not be public.

###### WHY (control requirement)
Control AC-22 requires management of **publicly accessible content** on systems controlled by the organisation, where the public is **not authorised** to access information that is not meant for public release (including protected and proprietary information). The guidance explains that publicly accessible content is usually available without needing to identify or log in, and that the organisation must control the people who make such information public.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the scope** of “publicly accessible systems” covered by AC-22 (for example: public website pages, public downloads, public knowledge bases, public landing pages, and public-facing portals).
2. **Keep an authorised publisher list** (named individuals or roles) as the single source of truth for who can submit content for publication. Include the date of the last training/authorisation.
3. **Use technical publishing controls** in the content management system (CMS) or web platform:
   - Publishers can create drafts only.
   - Only designated reviewers/approvers can publish to the live (production) site.
   - Publishing must be linked to a **pre-publication approval record** (for example, a ticket reference or sign-off reference) created before the publish time.
4. **Use a mandatory pre-publication checklist** that publishers must complete for every item before it can be approved. This checklist must cover common types of non-public information, such as personal data, internal documents, confidential contract terms, credentials, system or network details, incident details, and proprietary information.
5. **Check publicly accessible content every day** at a frequency of **daily** (as defined by the organisation). Use a repeatable checklist focused on higher-risk areas (news/updates, downloads, “latest” sections, frequently asked questions, and pages that are often edited). Record proof of completion in a ticketing or governance, risk and compliance (GRC) log.
6. **Remove quickly and record remediation**: if non-public information is found, immediately remove it or roll back to the last known good version. Then record what was removed, when, by whom, and the outcome of the fix.
7. **Check for authorisation changes**: periodically (for example, monthly), compare the authorised publisher list against the CMS permissions and alert on any differences.

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Content Governance Lead / Web Content Owner**: owns the authorised publisher list, the checklist, and the daily review process.
- **CMS/Web Platform Administrator**: sets up role-based access control (RBAC), workflow controls, and evidence capture.
- **Authorised Publishers**: complete the pre-publication checklist and submit items for approval.
- **Approvers/Reviewers**: carry out the pre-publication review and approve publication.
- **Information Security / Compliance**: audits the evidence and checks the process meets AC-22 expectations.

###### Acceptance criteria
- Only people on the authorised publisher list can submit content for publication in the CMS/web platform.
- Every live (production) publish action has a linked pre-publication approval record created before the publish time.
- A documented pre-publication checklist is required and completed for every published item.
- Proof of daily review exists for the publicly accessible content scope, at the required **daily** frequency.
- Any non-public information found is removed or rolled back promptly, with records that can be audited showing what/when/who and the remediation.
- CMS permissions match the authorised publisher list, with no unauthorised accounts able to publish to the live (production) site.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and document the scope of organisation-controlled public-facing systems covered by AC-22.
- [ ] Create and keep an authorised publisher list that names specific people and shows each person’s last training and authorisation dates.
- [ ] Set up content management system (CMS) and web role-based access control (RBAC) so that only approved users can publish to the live (production) site, and other publishers can only create draft content.
- [ ] Set up a mandatory pre-publication checklist and require a recorded approval reference before anything can be published.
- [ ] Assign a reviewer role to carry out and record a daily review of publicly accessible pages using a repeatable checklist.
- [ ] Create and practise a documented plan to remove or roll back any discovered non-public information, with records that can be audited.
- [ ] Carry out a regular check to confirm that content management system (CMS) publishing permissions still match the approved list of authorised publishers.


---

### AC-23 — Data Mining Protection (Control)

This requirement is about preventing people from repeatedly querying sensitive databases to gradually uncover confidential information, even if they are allowed to ask questions in the first place. Without these protections, someone could automate many queries to identify patterns or extract exact details, which could expose personal data or other highly sensitive secrets, including through misuse by insiders.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must first clearly identify which database records and which specific fields are classed as Confidential or Secret. It must also define and document which types of data analysis are genuinely authorised, including a process to check authorisation before any data mining starts.

The organisation must then limit how often and how many queries can be run against that sensitive data. It must allow only the minimum access needed for each authorised task, and block or restrict requests that go beyond the permitted scope.

It must also monitor for unusual query behaviour, alert the right people so they can investigate, keep audit records for review, and—where feasible—use privacy-preserving output methods to make repeated querying harder to exploit.

##### Policy-enforced query gateway for protected data mining

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a central, policy-controlled query gateway (or service layer) that controls every access to **protected data storage objects** (database records and database fields that contain **Confidential/Secret** information). The gateway must allow only **authorised data-mining methods**, limit how often queries can be made (**rate limiting/query throttling**), enforce **least-privilege** rules for what each user can query, and **detect and alert** when queries look unusual. Where possible, it must send analytics requests to **differential privacy (DP)**-enabled query methods.

###### WHY (control requirement)
Control AC-23 requires the organisation to **use the agreed methods for the agreed protected data storage objects** to **detect and protect against unauthorised data mining**. The guidance specifically expects controls such as **limiting the number and frequency of database queries**, **limiting the types of results returned**, **least-privilege query controls**, **informing staff when unusual database queries/access happen**, and **using differential privacy where feasible**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create a protected-data map**: keep an official catalogue that links each protected database **record/field** (Confidential/Secret) to the correct gateway policy identifier.
2. **Define authorised data-mining methods as “policy-as-code”**: build an approved list of query templates/operations (for example, allowed aggregations, required filters, allowed ways to combine tables). These must match the organisation’s approved methods, including **rate limiting/query throttling**, **least-privilege query controls**, **unusual-behaviour alerts**, and **differential privacy where feasible**.
3. **Enforce least-privilege when queries are made**: the gateway must reject or change any query that tries to expand access (for example, requesting unauthorised columns/fields, removing required filters, asking for too many results beyond policy limits, or using query patterns linked to data extraction).
4. **Apply rate limiting and query throttling per user identity and per protected object**: set and adjust limits for interactive users versus service accounts. Slow down requests based on how often queries are made and which protected objects are targeted, to make extraction harder.
5. **Detect and alert on unusual, data-mining-like behaviour**: collect structured information (user identity, query pattern, which protected objects are targeted, estimated number of results, and query frequency). Send alerts to the **security information and event management (SIEM)** and **security orchestration, automation and response (SOAR)** tools when behaviour differs from normal patterns (for example, repeated high-frequency access to the same protected fields/records).
6. **Use differential privacy where feasible**: for analytics/reporting that use protected fields, route requests to **differential privacy (DP)**-enabled stored procedures/engines or DP-wrapped endpoints. Ensure the gateway blocks direct/raw extraction paths for protected fields when DP is required.
7. **Audit and keep evidence**: record every allowed, denied, and throttled query, including the policy decision details. Link these records to the relevant identity/session information for later review.

###### WHO
- **Application owner / data platform owner**: responsible for the protected-data map and for defining the authorised data-mining policy.
- **Security engineering (identity and access management (IAM)/SIEM integration)**: builds the gateway enforcement, sets up the unusual-behaviour detection rules, and configures alert routing.
- **Database administrators / data engineers**: implement DP-enabled analytics endpoints where feasible and help with the approved query template list.
- **Privacy/legal counsel (as required)**: checks that data-mining activities over protected objects are authorised.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The gateway enforces the AC-23 methods for the defined protected objects: **rate limiting/query throttling**, **least-privilege query controls**, and **unusual query alerts** are clearly active for Confidential/Secret database fields/records.
- Attempts to query unauthorised protected fields/records are **rejected or changed** and result in **audit logs**.
- Repeated high-frequency query attempts against protected objects are **throttled** and trigger **SIEM/SOAR alerts** when thresholds or normal baselines are exceeded.
- For at least one representative analytics use-case involving protected fields, the system produces **DP-enabled output** (or formally documents why DP is not feasible for that specific use-case, with compensating controls).
- Evidence is available showing: policy configuration, gateway decision logs (allowed/denied/throttled), and alert rule coverage for unusual query behaviour.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and keep up to date a protected-data catalogue that links Confidential and Secret database records and fields to gateway policy identifiers.
- [ ] Set up a query gateway (service layer) that is the required route for every query that targets protected data storage objects.
- [ ] Encode approved data mining methods as pre-approved (allowlisted) query templates, and enforce required conditions using policy-as-code.
- [ ] Set rate limits and query throttling thresholds for each user identity and each protected resource, and enforce these limits at the gateway.
- [ ] Apply the principle of least privilege to database queries, so that any query trying to access unauthorised data (fields or rows) or returning an excessive number of results is rejected or rewritten.
- [ ] Connect query activity data to security information and event management (SIEM) and security orchestration, automation and response (SOAR), and set up alerts for unusual query patterns or unusual query frequency aimed at protected objects.
- [ ] Enable differential privacy for analytics endpoints where it is practical, and ensure the gateway sends analytics that use protected fields through differential privacy (DP) controls.


---

### AC-24 — Access Control Decisions (Control)

This requirement is about making sure that, every time someone tries to use a system or access a resource, the organisation’s permission decision is made first, and access is only allowed or blocked after that decision. If this is not done, there is a risk that people could gain access when they should not, or that sensitive actions could be carried out before the organisation has properly checked whether the person is authorised. This could lead to data loss, fraud, or disruption.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must create clear, documented procedures that explain how authorisation decisions are made for every access attempt, and how those decisions are applied before access is enforced. It must also configure the system so that this “decision first” sequence happens for all relevant types of access, including logins, files or shared folders, applications, and administrative actions. Finally, it must keep audit records showing the outcome of each access request, so the organisation can demonstrate what happened.

##### Enforce decision-before-access via PDP/PEP for every request

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT must be done
Ensure that for every access request, the organisation’s **access control decisions** are **checked and applied before access is allowed to take effect** (i.e., the system must not carry out the requested access until it has made an explicit allow/deny decision).

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-24** requires that **access control decisions (ac-24_odp.02)** are **checked for each access request before access is enforced** (ac-24_odp.01). This helps prevent unauthorised access and reduces the risk of actions happening before authorisation is assessed.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
Set up a **request-time authorisation flow** with a clear separation between:
- a **Policy Decision Point (PDP)** that decides whether the request is authorised, and
- one or more **Policy Enforcement Points (PEPs)** that block or allow the request based on the PDP decision.

###### Implementation approach (typical enterprise multi-tier / API / microservices)
1. **Define what the decision needs and what it returns**: for each request, collect the requester identity (from the enterprise identity provider), the resource being accessed, the action being requested (for example, read/write/admin), and relevant context (for example, environment and device/workload details). The PDP must return an explicit decision (allow/deny) and a decision identifier.
2. **Route all access paths through a PEP**: configure the application/API gateway, service middleware, or service-mesh component so that **every** access request (including administrative endpoints and privileged actions) is intercepted before any business logic runs or any content is delivered.
3. **Only enforce after the PDP decision is received**: the PEP must **not** report success, return data, or carry out privileged actions until the PDP decision is received. If the PDP cannot be reached or returns no decision, default to **deny** (or use a documented compensating control that matches organisational policy).
4. **Tie the decision to the exact request**: include the request identifier and the PDP decision identifier in the enforcement path so the enforcement outcome can be traced back to the exact decision made for that request.
5. **Keep audit records for each access request**: log (at minimum) request ID, requester identity, resource, action, PDP decision outcome, and enforcement outcome. Centralise logs to the security information and event management (SIEM) system and keep them for the period required by organisational logging rules.
6. **Document the decision-before-enforcement process**: create a procedure that explains the authorisation workflow—where the PDP and PEP sit, what counts as an “access request”, and how the system ensures decisions are made before enforcement across both user and administrative functions.
7. **Test the sequence**: use automated tests to confirm the PDP decision is obtained before enforcement (for example, denied requests must not reach business logic; allowed requests must include the PDP decision identifier).

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Security Architect / Security Engineering Lead**: designs the PDP/PEP approach and the authorisation model.
- **Application Owner / Engineering Lead**: implements PEP interception and enforcement gating across all endpoints.
- **Identity and Access Management (IAM) Lead**: ensures the PDP receives authoritative identity and attribute information (claims).
- **Security Operations Centre (SOC) / SIEM Engineer**: ensures audit logging, correlation, and retention.
- **Compliance/Assurance Lead**: checks that evidence and procedures are complete for AC-24.

###### Acceptance criteria
- For 100% of defined access request types (including administrative actions), the system obtains an explicit **PDP allow/deny decision** before any enforcement occurs.
- For denied requests, no business logic execution or data delivery occurs (verified through integration tests and request tracing).
- Audit logs exist for each access request containing: request ID, requester identity, resource, action, PDP decision outcome, and enforcement outcome, and can be correlated end-to-end.
- The documented procedure clearly states how decisions are made and applied before enforcement, matching the implemented request flow.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the decision information and outcomes for the personal data processing (PDP) process—subject, resource, action, and context—and set out the rules for when the system should allow or deny the request (the allow/deny decision contract).
- [ ] Set up a policy enforcement point (PEP) at every access route (API gateway, middleware, and service mesh) so requests are stopped and checked before they reach business logic.
- [ ] Set up request-time gating so enforcement either returns a success response or carries out the required actions only after a policy decision point (PDP) decision is received.
- [ ] Set a “fail closed” behaviour (deny access) when no Policy Decision Point (PDP) decision is available, or document and implement an approved compensating control.
- [ ] Add audit logs that record, for every access request, the request ID, the decision made by the policy decision point, and the result of enforcing that decision.
- [ ] Document the process for deciding before access is granted, covering both standard access and administrative access.
- [ ] Create automated tests that confirm the decision is made before any enforcement happens, for both “allow” and “deny” scenarios.


---

#### AC-24.1 — Transmit Access Authorization Information (Enhancement)

This requirement is about protecting the permission information that one part of your organisation sends to the systems that actually allow or block access. If this is not protected end-to-end, an attacker could intercept the permission message, alter it, pretend it came from a trusted decision-maker, or send it to the wrong destination. This could result in unauthorised access or incorrect denials.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must package the authorisation details into signed authorisation tokens. These tokens must clearly state:
- who is requesting access (the subject),
- what is being accessed (the resource),
- what the requester wants to do (the action),
- and any security or privacy attributes needed to make the decision.

The signed tokens must be sent over a mutually authenticated encrypted connection, where both sides prove they are the correct parties. The connection must also include extra checks to ensure only the expected certificates are accepted, and it must protect messages so any tampering is detected.

The access enforcement points must verify the token signature before using it to allow or block access. They must also keep evidence through system records and documented design and configuration settings.

##### Secure transmission of signed authorisation tokens to API enforcement

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a federated authorisation process where **access authorisation information** (a token) is sent **securely** from the place that makes the access decision to the **systems that enforce access control decisions** (the federated application/API enforcement points). The information sent must be protected from **tampering, impersonation, and compromise** by using **mutual Transport Layer Security (mutual TLS)** and **message/token signing**.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-24(1) requires that **{{ param:ac-24.01_odp.01 }}** is sent using **{{ param:ac-24.01_odp.02 }}** to **{{ param:ac-24.01_odp.03 }}** so that the enforcement points can **enforce access control decisions** based on **authorisation information that can be checked and has not been altered**.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the access authorisation token format** used as the {{ param:ac-24.01_odp.01 }}. The token must include, at minimum, **subject, resource, action**, and the required **security/privacy attributes** needed for later decisions.
2. **Sign the token** using a managed signing key (for example, a key managed by **Key Management Service (KMS)** or stored in a **Hardware Security Module (HSM)**). Use a signed authorisation token format (for example, **JSON Web Signature (JWS)**/**JSON Web Token (JWT)** with a signature) so the enforcement point can check that it is genuine and unchanged.
3. **Send tokens using mutual TLS** between the authorisation decision service and the federated API enforcement points, configured for **mutual authentication**.
4. **Use certificate pinning** for the partner system(s), so the client/enforcement integration only trusts the expected certificate/public key(s). Use a controlled certificate rotation process to avoid service disruption.
5. **Verify before making an access decision**: at the federated API enforcement point, use a required processing sequence: (a) confirm the mutual TLS connection is valid, (b) verify the token signature, (c) check the token details (subject/resource/action/security/privacy attributes) and that it is intended for the correct audience/target enforcement point, then (d) apply allow/deny decisions.
6. **Reject and record failures**: if signature checking, token detail checking, or mutual TLS peer checking fails, deny access and record evidence that can be audited (for example, correlation ID, failure reason, enforcement point identity).

###### WHO (role responsible)
- **Solution/Platform Architect**: defines the token structure, trust boundaries, and integration approach.
- **API/Gateway Engineering Lead**: implements the enforcement point verification logic and mutual TLS/certificate pinning configuration.
- **Security Engineering / Identity and Access Management (IAM) Team**: manages signing keys (KMS/HSM), certificate lifecycle, and pin rotation.
