- **Operations/Site Reliability Engineering (SRE)**: ensures deployment, monitoring, and readiness for incident response.

###### Acceptance criteria
- The enforcement point makes access decisions only after **successful token signature verification** and **successful mutual TLS peer validation**.
- Tokens sent to enforcement points are **signed** and include **subject, resource, action, and security/privacy attributes**.
- **Certificate pinning** is enabled for the integration and prevents connections to unexpected peers.
- If any verification fails (mutual TLS or token signature/claims), the enforcement point **denies access** and produces **audit logs** showing the verification result.
- Evidence exists (configuration exports and sample logs) showing the above in a test environment and at least one production-like integration path.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the format for the signed access authorisation token, including the token’s subject, the resource it applies to, the action it allows, and the security and privacy information that must be included.
- [ ] Use key management service (KMS) or hardware security module (HSM) managed keys to digitally sign tokens (JSON Web Signature (JWS) / JSON Web Token (JWT), or an equivalent method), and ensure the token is only accepted for the intended audience or target by binding that information at the enforcement point.
- [ ] Set up mutual Transport Layer Security (mutual TLS) between the authorisation decision service and the federated application programming interface (API) enforcement points, using strict certificate checks.
- [ ] Set up certificate pinning so the system only trusts the expected peer certificate or public key, and put in place a controlled process to rotate (change) the pinned value when needed.
- [ ] Set up enforcement-point checks to confirm the other party’s identity using mutual Transport Layer Security (mTLS) and to verify the token’s signature and claims before making any allow or deny decision.
- [ ] Add “deny if verification fails” behaviour and record audit logs for the results of token verification and mutual Transport Layer Security (mTLS) checks, using correlation identifiers to link related events.


---

#### AC-24.2 — No User or Process Identity (Enhancement)

This expectation is about making access decisions without relying on who the person is, or on which system process is acting on their behalf. Instead, the organisation should decide whether someone can **read**, **write**, or **run** something using only agreed information about the item itself—for example, its resource classification label, how the data is intended to be handled, and the access purpose code. Alternatively, the organisation can use where the item sits in the system and the type of access needed. Where privacy is involved, the organisation should use only the privacy purpose and the data minimisation category.

If this is not done, the wrong people could be granted access because decisions quietly depend on personal identifiers. Privacy could also be weakened by collecting and using identity details that are not necessary.

To meet this requirement, the organisation must:
- define and document these “identity-free” access decision rules;
- configure systems so the rules are enforced exactly as written;
- ensure staff and developers follow the documented approach; and
- keep clear records showing decisions were based on these attributes, not on user identity or process identity, including in the organisation’s security and privacy plans where relevant.

##### Identity-free ABAC decision engine using purpose/classification attributes

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Implement an authorisation decision process where every “allow” or “deny” decision is based **only** on security and privacy attributes defined by the organisation (for example: a resource classification label, a data handling category, and an access purpose code; and/or an enclave or zone plus the required access mode; and/or a privacy purpose plus a data minimisation category). The decision rules must **not** use, rely on, store, or look up any user identity or the identity of the process acting on behalf of the user.

###### WHY (control requirement)
NIST Special Publication 800-53 Access Control (AC)-24(2) enhancement requires enforcing access control decisions based on **ac-24.2_prm_1** (identity-free security or privacy attributes) so that decisions **do not include the identity of the user or the process acting on behalf of the user**. The guidance explains that in some cases identity is not needed and is costly to send, and that policies based on labels and similar approaches (including label-based, role-based access control (RBAC), and attribute-based approaches) may omit user identity.

###### HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the identity-free decision attributes** in the application’s policy model using **ac-24.2_prm_1** (resource classification label, data handling category, access purpose code; optionally privacy purpose and data minimisation category; optionally enclave/zone and required access mode). Make sure these are the **only** inputs used for “allow” or “deny”.
2. **Create a trusted “access context” input** for requests (for example: signed JSON Web Token (JWT) claims, mutual Transport Layer Security (mTLS) authenticated service intent, or an internal policy-context service) that contains **only** the identity-free attributes needed by the policy. Protect the context so it cannot be altered.
3. **Implement policy-as-code with identity fields explicitly excluded**: configure the policy engine (for example: an application programming interface (API) gateway, a service mesh authoriser, or the application authorisation layer) so rule checks use **only** the identity-free attributes. Add automated checks in continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) to block changes if the policy code refers to identity fields (for example: user id, subject, actor, or process id) in any “allow” or “deny” rule.
4. **Apply this consistently at the same enforcement point** (API gateway / service mesh / application authorisation middleware) so all services and endpoints use the same identity-free decision logic.
5. **Produce audit evidence for attribute-based decisions**: record the exact identity-free attribute values used for each decision (classification label, data handling category, purpose code; and/or enclave/zone and access mode; and/or privacy purpose and minimisation category). Identity may be recorded for auditing and incident response, but it must be clearly separated from the decision inputs and must not be used by the policy engine.
6. **Document the approach in the security (and privacy, if applicable) documentation**: record the identity-free attribute definitions, where they come from, how integrity is ensured, and how the enforcement point prevents identity from influencing “allow” or “deny” decisions.

###### WHO
- **Application Security Architect / Security Engineer**: defines the attribute model and policy-as-code rules; ensures identity-free enforcement.
- **Platform/API Gateway or Service Mesh Owner**: implements the enforcement point and validates the request access context.
- **Developers**: implement the policy code and CI/CD checks that prevent identity references.
- **Compliance/Assurance Lead**: checks that audit evidence and documentation meet Access Control (AC)-24(2).

###### Acceptance criteria
- For every “allow” or “deny” decision, the policy engine evaluates **only** the attributes defined in **ac-24.2_prm_1** (and any selected identity-free privacy/enclave/access-mode attributes) and **never** references user/process identity fields.
- CI/CD policy checks prevent merges where “allow” or “deny” logic references identity fields.
- Audit logs for authorisation events include the identity-free attribute values used for the decision and show that identity is not part of the decision basis.
- A documented enforcement design exists showing how the trusted access context is integrity-protected and how the enforcement point applies the identity-free rules consistently across the application.

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the authorisation settings for identity-free access exactly as described in **ac-24.2_prm_1** (and any selected privacy, secure-enclave, and access-mode settings), and link each resource and application programming interface (API) to those same values.
- [ ] Set up a trusted access-context system that protects data integrity and provides only identity-free information to the policy enforcement point.
- [ ] Configure the policy engine and rules so that any allow or deny conditions can use only attributes that do not identify a user or process, and do not include any user or process identity fields.
- [ ] Add continuous integration and continuous delivery (CI/CD) checks that stop a build from completing if the policy code uses identity details (for example, subject or user identifiers, actor identifiers, or process identifiers) in any “allow” or “deny” rules.
- [ ] Apply access checks consistently at the point where requests enter the system (the API gateway, service mesh, and application authorisation layer), so every request follows the same decision process without relying on user identity.
- [ ] Enable audit logging for authorisation decisions. This should record the attribute values used in each decision, without recording any user identity (separate from any user identity logging).
- [ ] Update the security documentation (and privacy documentation, if used) to explain: attribute definitions, where the information comes from, the controls used to keep it accurate and unaltered, and how evidence is produced.


---

### AC-25 — Reference Monitor (Control)

This requirement is about having one dedicated “gatekeeper” inside the system that checks every time before any Confidential or Secret information is shared or used, deciding who is allowed to access it. Without this, access rules can be applied inconsistently, accidentally skipped, or an attacker could try to change how decisions are made so unauthorised people gain access.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must apply the Department of Defence access rules for Confidential and Secret data. This includes making access decisions based on roles and attributes, and using the principle of least privilege (only the minimum access needed). These decisions must be enforced by a tamper-resistant gatekeeper that is always consulted for access decisions and cannot be bypassed through other routes.

The gatekeeper must be small and clearly defined so its behaviour can be thoroughly analysed and tested. The organisation must keep documented evidence showing that it makes the correct allow-or-deny decisions, records the outcome, and blocks and logs unauthorised attempts to interfere with it or its rules.

##### Tamper-resistant always-invoked reference monitor for DoD data

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

###### WHAT
Set up a dedicated “reference monitor” that checks every attempt to access Department of Defense (DoD) Confidential and Secret information. It enforces the organisation’s access rules (role-based access control (RBAC) and attribute-based access control (ABAC) using least privilege) and returns a clear allow or deny decision.

###### WHY (control requirement)
Control AC-25 requires a reference monitor for the organisation-defined access rules (parameter: **ac-25_odp**) that is:
- **Tamperproof** (prevents the checking mechanism from being compromised)
- **Always used** (prevents people or systems from bypassing it)
- **Small enough to be analysed and tested** (so its coverage and correctness can be verified)

###### HOW (specific approach)
###### 1) Define the mediation boundary (small, limited)
- Choose one enforcement point that all Confidential/Secret access must pass through (for example, operating system/kernel mediation for labelled resources, or a single data-access service/library that every data path uses).
- Limit the reference monitor’s responsibilities to: (a) check the inputs needed for the decision, (b) decide allow or deny, and (c) produce an auditable record of the decision.

###### 2) Ensure always-invoked mediation for Confidential/Secret information
- **Label or partition** Confidential/Secret data so only labelled items are eligible for checking (for example, labelled file paths/volumes, container/workload labels, or database row/object classification enforced through a single access wrapper).
- Make sure every attempt to access those labelled items is routed through the reference monitor at the point of access (not at the user interface or application programming interface layer). Block any direct or alternative routes that could access the data without going through the reference monitor.

###### 3) Enforce least privilege using policy evaluation (RBAC/ABAC)
- Put the user or system identity and relevant attributes (role, clearance, group membership, workload identity) into the reference monitor’s decision context.
- Put the data’s classification (Confidential/Secret) and any relevant data attributes into the decision context.
- Apply the organisation’s DoD access rules and return an **allow/deny** decision.

###### 4) Make the monitor resistant to tampering and able to be verified
- Protect the reference monitor code/configuration and the policy information using integrity controls (for example, signed policy packages, restricted administrator access, unchangeable deployment artefacts, and runtime integrity checks).
- Prevent disabling or bypassing by enforcing mediation at the lowest practical layer and by hardening configuration (default to deny for unlabelled or unknown items).

###### 5) Demonstrate completeness through analysis and testing evidence
- Build a test setup that runs the reference monitor using representative combinations of user/system, data, and requested actions, including cases that should be denied and edge cases.
- Provide evidence that the monitor is small and testable (for example, a limited amount of code, unit and integration test coverage for the decision logic, and documented test cases).
- Record decision outcomes in an audit trail that includes: the subject, the data label/classification, the requested action, the decision (allow/deny), and correlation identifiers.

###### WHO
- **Security Architect / Identity and Access Management (IAM) Architect**: defines the mediation boundary, how policy inputs are turned into decision context, and what must be audited.
- **Platform/Infrastructure Engineering (Operating System/Cloud)**: implements the always-invoked enforcement mechanism and the hardening measures.
- **Application Engineering (if a data-access wrapper is used)**: ensures every Confidential/Secret data path calls the reference monitor and removes any bypass routes.
- **Security Assurance / Governance, Risk and Compliance (GRC)**: checks the test evidence, completeness, and whether the audit record is sufficient for AC-25.

###### Acceptance criteria
1. **Mediation coverage**: Every access to Confidential/Secret-labelled items is checked by the reference monitor; attempts that do not pass through the monitor are blocked.
2. **Correct decisions**: For a defined test set based on the DoD RBAC/ABAC least-privilege rules, the monitor returns the expected allow/deny outcomes.
3. **Tamper resistance**: Integrity protections prevent unauthorised changes to the monitor and policy inputs; bypass attempts are detected and denied.
4. **Always invoked**: Monitoring shows that every relevant access attempt produces a reference monitor decision record.
5. **Verifiable completeness**: The decision logic is small and bounded, supported by repeatable unit/integration tests with documented evidence.
6. **Audit evidence**: Audit logs include the required decision fields and can be linked back to access attempts (subject/data/action/decision).

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Choose and document the single “mediation boundary” (the reference monitor enforcement point) that controls access to Confidential/Secret data along all access paths.
- [ ] Set up confidential and secret object labels and classifications so that all eligible objects can only be accessed through the mediation boundary.
- [ ] Implement role-based access control (RBAC) and attribute-based access control (ABAC) policy checks using: the user’s identity and attributes, the target item’s classification and attributes, and the requested action; then produce an allow or deny decision.
- [ ] Protect the reference monitor and policy files with integrity safeguards (for example, signed policy bundles, limited administrator permissions, and checks that confirm they have not been altered while the system is running).
- [ ] Prevent any “always-on” mediation from being bypassed by blocking alternative or shortcut routes, and set rules to deny access by default for any unlabelled or unknown objects.
- [ ] Create a repeatable test setup and produce evidence that the monitor’s decision-making logic is small, has been reviewed, and is covered by unit tests and integration tests.
- [ ] Set up audit logging so that every access attempt that is checked records: the user or system (subject), the item being accessed (object label or classification), what action was requested, the decision made, and the related reference identifiers.
